There was, he knew now, a future in the motion-picture studios for his great horse &ldquo;Umpqua,&rdquo; and perhaps one for himself as well. But he shied at becoming an actor and hated to double for the handsome cowboy Apollos of the screen; and to hang around the studios merely as the owner of a wonderful horse, letting the spirited animal earn his living for him, did not fit his idea of a career. As a matter of fact, he had never desired a future in Hollywood under such circumstances and the immediate necessity for earning money was over. Nance, his sister, was perfectly well again after her operation and would soon be married. So in demand, indeed, had Umpqua been that Lance found himself with at least enough cash in hand to last him until he could find a job more to his liking. He found himself leaning toward a horseback ride through southern California, across Arizona, perhaps into New Mexico. To be sure the cattle business was practically ruined, but the desert ranges and purple uplands of Arizona, or the silver grassed valleys of New Mexico, about which he had read so much, would be vastly wilder and infinitely freer than the old pastures he had ridden, and surely there would be some kind of a job for a husky chap who was fond of animals. There was a singular zest in the thought of new adventure in a harder country than he had known. But the truth was, Hollywood did not let go its grip so easily. And why? Lance knew that he had no ambition to beat the movie game. Still he admitted the fascination of the gay bright whirl of the picture world. Reducing this down to the lure of feminine charm seemed another step in the right direction. Lance dubiously admitted to himself that he was afraid he had more than the ordinary male&rsquo;s weakness for the fair sex. But hell! he thought in self-defense, just think of Hollywood&rsquo;s thousands of extras, and more thousands of girls unable to find even jobs as extras, many of them exceptionally beautiful, all of them pretty! Here was a case where it was hard to break away. And he ruefully recalled the three girls to whose attraction he had succumbed&mdash;Coretta&mdash;and Virginia&mdash;and lastly Maurine. Only last night Maurine had faced him, a little pale, with dark eyes steady. &ldquo;Lance, you&rsquo;ve been swell to me,&rdquo; she had said. &ldquo;I could lie to you, but I won&rsquo;t. At last I&rsquo;ve got a break. You know what that means. I must make the grade.... Sure I love you. String along with me, darling, and when I&rsquo;m a star....&rdquo; That was Hollywood, but it was not Lance Sidway. Looked at now, in this serious moment, it seemed a deciding factor. &ldquo;It&rsquo;d happen again,&rdquo; muttered Lance, sadly. &ldquo;And I might fall worse. It&rsquo;s coming to me.... I&rsquo;m through!&rdquo; And he arose with springy step to gaze at the hazy Sierra Madres beyond which barrier there was an unsettled land. He strolled through a long terrace of roses, sensitive to their color and fragrance. They were beautiful, but he liked wild flowers best. Meanwhile he was revolving in mind the problem of riding Umpqua out of California. The horse was fat and needed work. He would not care much for the asphalt roads; perhaps, however, from Palm Springs south Lance could keep mostly to soft ground. Once in condition again, Umpqua was good for fifty miles a day without turning a hair. Lance emerged from the Museum park, and presently, strolling along, he found himself on the edge of the University campus. Students of both sexes were in evidence, some chatting in groups, others moving along with books under their arms. These bareheaded boys and girls in their colorful sweaters, young and full of the joy of life, aroused memory and regret in Lance. After high school he had attended college in Corvallis for nearly a year, and outside of freshman miseries, which now seemed sweet, he had done well in his studies and better in athletics. But financial trouble had intervened and Nance&rsquo;s illness... both of which had sent him to Hollywood. This college atmosphere was something that he liked. If only his father had not died, to leave Nance and him orphans! Lance cast off the sadness. His sister was well&mdash;happy&mdash;and he had the greatest horse in the West and a new adventure in that West before him. Pretty lucky, Lance thought he should be telling himself! Reaching a cross street Lance halted to absorb more of the flash of color on the campus. He sauntered up the cross street toward some shade trees. On that side there were more students. He heard bantering voices and gay high-pitched laughter. The shrill sound of a siren disrupted his attention, as well as that of the students. Wheeling, Lance saw a bright topless roadster turning the corner from the main street. Its driver was a bareheaded girl with hair bright as spun gold. At the moment she withdrew her extended left arm. Behind her raced the car with the loud horn. It caught up with her. One of the two occupants, surely policemen, yelled for her to stop. The young woman took her time about it, and passing Lance, finally halted at the first shade trees where half a dozen students had congregated. Lance had not far to go to reach them and he strolled along, curious, expectant, and a little angry at the gruff yell of the officer. Lance was in time to hear: &ldquo;Why didn&rsquo;t you stop?&rdquo; &ldquo;I have stopped,&rdquo; replied the girl, coolly. Lance joined the group of students who had advanced to the curb. From all points on the campus others were coming, some on the run. Then Lance saw the girl at close range. Many a time in the studios and on the locations had he sustained a shock of masculine transport, but he had never seen a motion-picture star who in his opinion could hold a candle to this girl. &ldquo;Majesty, what do these cops want?&rdquo; queried a tall young fellow, stepping out. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not sure, Rollie,&rdquo; she replied, with a laugh. &ldquo;But I think they want to chase me off the streets.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the idea, officer?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah?&rdquo; queried the student, insolently. &ldquo;Yeah! She was makin&rsquo; forty-five on the turn an&rsquo; she didn&rsquo;t even hold out her hand.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lay off us kids, can&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; asked another, plaintively. &ldquo;Aren&rsquo;t there enough drunk drivers to keep you busy?&rdquo; &ldquo;Looks a little steamed up himself.&rdquo; Good-natured cat calls and boos sounded from back of the circle of students, crowding closer and denser. They sensed events. Lance gathered that the officers did not fail to grasp something inimical to their own well-being at the moment. &ldquo;Give the girl a ticket, Brady, and let&rsquo;s get going,&rdquo; advised the one at the wheel. A storm of protest went up from the foremost line of students. Rollie, who evidently had some distinction on the campus, yelled for them to shut up. &ldquo;Beat it, you flat-feet!&rdquo; he called, sharply. &ldquo;You hang around here and run us fellows ragged. But lay off the girls. Get that? We won&rsquo;t stand for it.&rdquo; &ldquo;You shut up or I&rsquo;ll run you in,&rdquo; said Brady angrily, as he began writing the summons. &ldquo;Madge, is it coming to you?&rdquo; Rollie asked. &ldquo;Not this time, Rollie, I swear,&rdquo; she replied. &ldquo;I did run away from him some days ago. But today I wasn&rsquo;t making twenty.&rdquo; &ldquo;You tell that to the Judge,&rdquo; said Brady, coldly. &ldquo;An&rsquo; you&rsquo;re interferin&rsquo; with an officer of the law.&rdquo; &ldquo;Law, hooey! Only when there&rsquo;s nothing in it for you. Get the hell out of here!&rdquo; The crowd of students surged over the sidewalk and pulled the officer from the running board of the car. He made the mistake of raising his fist, and striking himself free he shouted to his companion: &ldquo;Send a riot call!&rdquo; At that juncture a motorcycle policeman roared up to make a fringe of students in the street hop out of his way. Brady hoarsely repeated his order to him, and with his comrade, both swinging their arms, cleared a space. Lance had been shoved off the pavement by the pushing of the students, all of them roused now and full of devilment. Rollie appeared to be the only one who took the affair seriously. The girl, Madge, acted as if she were enjoying the proceedings. But her violet eyes were ablaze. Rollie leaped on the running board and leaned close to speak low to her. Then Brady turning with red visage and bristling front jerked Rollie down. &ldquo;Young woman, get away from that wheel,&rdquo; he ordered, opening the door. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll take you for a little ride.&rdquo; &ldquo;Like hell you will,&rdquo; she rejoined, her voice as ringing as a bell. And she snapped the door shut. Brady&rsquo;s attention veered to a charging crowd of students who pushed the officers&rsquo; car down the street, while another gang, yelling like Indians, rushed the truck of a fruit and vegetable vendor who had happened along. They halted him, and in gold and red streams they spilled the mounds of oranges and tomatoes into the street. Another moment saw the air full of colored missiles. Their target was the offending car of the law. A smashing of windows and clinking of glass mingled with the derisive yells of the assailants. Then the driver, who had attempted in no gentle manner to drive the students from crowding Brady, turned to roar at the slingers. A huge soft tomato struck him squarely in the face. That elicited a howl of fiendish glee from the armed force in the street. A hail of oranges and tomatoes not only halted his belligerent rush but blinded him, swamped him, knocked him flat. At the moment, then, a blare of sirens announced the arrival of reinforcements. Lance hung by the girl&rsquo;s car while the students, numbering hundreds by now, rushed pell-mell into the street, whooping like a lot of Indians. What was left of the vendor&rsquo;s ample supply of oranges and tomatoes disappeared from his truck as if by magic to take swift form of a colorful barrage right at the charging policemen. For a while they were held back, but as the supply of ammunition began to diminish, they forged forward, eventually to drive the students out of the street upon the campus. But it was not an onslaught such as Brady and his man had attempted. The students were having a wonderful time, but the officers, plainly disgusted and angered though they were, did not resort to violence. Against three hundred crazy students they could do nothing save harangue them off the street. Lance, keenly enjoying the whole performance, was suddenly startled by a cry from the girl in the car. And as he wheeled he leaped off the curb. Brady had opened the car door. &ldquo;Move over, Blondie,&rdquo; he ordered, a rude hand on her shoulder. &ldquo;You dirty bum! Don&rsquo;t you dare paw me!&rdquo; It appeared to Lance that the officer&rsquo;s action exceeded his authority. Even if it had not, the girl&rsquo;s poignant outburst and the flash of her magnificent eyes would have been enough for Lance. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m drivin&rsquo; you to the station,&rdquo; declared Brady, shoving at her. &ldquo;You are not!&rdquo; she cried, starting the engine. &ldquo;Get off or I&rsquo;ll spread you along the street.... I&rsquo;ll drive to...&rdquo; Lance snatched the policeman&rsquo;s hand off the car and as he turned in surprise Lance hit him a not-too-gentle blow on his rather protruding abdomen. A gasping expulsion of breath followed the sodden drumlike sound. Brady began to sag. Lance reached up with powerful hand, pulled him off the running board, and then with a vicious swing of fist at the convulsed visage he laid the officer neatly in the street. In action as swift, Lance vaulted into the car. &ldquo;Step on it!&rdquo; he yelled. And almost before the words passed his lips the little car shot ahead. Lance&rsquo;s knees came up hard under the dash. A shrill blast from the horn sent several students leaping for their lives. Then the car ate up the open street, to whirl at the corner, and sped on, describing swift half-circles in the traffic. Lance&rsquo;s hat went flying, and as the car grazed a trolley his hair stood up stiff. Though scared as he had never been, Lance&rsquo;s heart strangled a cowboy yell in his throat and his blood beat thick in his ears and he was possessed by a wild elation. The car whirled off the thoroughfare into a quiet street, on which the houses blurred in Lance&rsquo;s sight. Another turn, then block after block on a traffic-congested street, then a break in the speed&mdash;and at last a parking place! &ldquo;Whew!&rdquo; exploded Lance, catching his breath. &ldquo;We&rsquo;d have shaken them&mdash;if they had chased us.&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell, wasn&rsquo;t it?&rdquo; rejoined the girl, with an amazing coolness. And she uttered a low laugh. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll tell the world. Say, but you can drive,&rdquo; burst out Lance, turning to look at her. With steady beautiful hands, and shapely coral-tinted finger tips she was taking a gold monogrammed cigarette case from her purse. &ldquo;Thanks. Have a cigarette?&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t mind.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did I scare you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes&mdash;but it was a great ride.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, we got the best of those cops anyway, and now we&rsquo;re just two fugitives from justice.&rdquo; All this time Lance was gazing at the girl, conscious of a mounting exhilaration. To find pleasure in the beauty of women had been the only debt he owed Hollywood. But this visual experience seemed a magnifying of all former sensations. &ldquo;Oh, your hand!&rdquo; she exclaimed, in sudden solicitude. Then Lance became aware that he was opening and shutting his right hand, the knuckles of which were bruised. It was a big brown member, matching his brawny wrist. &ldquo;Bunged my fist&mdash;a little,&rdquo; he said, awkwardly. &ldquo;Nothing much.&rdquo; &ldquo;No?&mdash;I wonder what that cop thought. I&rsquo;ll never forget his face. I was looking at it when you socked him. Did that tickle me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Then I&rsquo;m glad,&rdquo; returned Lance, beaming at her. &ldquo;You see he was sore at me. He&rsquo;s caught me before. Last time I made eyes at him, you know, and let him think.... I had a date and was late. Next time he spotted me I ran away from him. Today he must have been laying for me.&rdquo; &ldquo;So that was it? Big fathead! You&rsquo;ll get hauled up for this. I&rsquo;m sorry. But I had to slug him.... I was looking at you when he...&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t be sorry. You made me your friend for life. Rollie was mad, but he wouldn&rsquo;t have done that.... You&rsquo;re not a college man?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. I went one year at Corvallis. Then... but that wouldn&rsquo;t interest you. I&mdash;I&rsquo;d better be going.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t go yet,&rdquo; she replied, detaining him with a hand on his sleeve. &ldquo;Indeed I am interested. You&rsquo;re not going to walk out on me after such a romantic adventure. Are you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Why, Miss Madge&mdash;I&mdash;you... of course, that&rsquo;s up to the lady.&rdquo; &ldquo;It always should be, even if it isn&rsquo;t. Tell me about yourself. I&rsquo;ll bet you&rsquo;re from Hollywood. You have that cut.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? You don&rsquo;t mean movie actor?&rdquo; inquired Lance, quickly. &ldquo;No? Too bad! You&rsquo;re handsome enough to be one. My sorority sisters will be jealous. I&rsquo;ll have you out to the house to meet them.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;d be swell. But I&rsquo;m afraid it&rsquo;s not possible. Thank you.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re not married?&rdquo; &ldquo;I should say not.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nor in love. I know how that malady affects them,&rdquo; she replied, flippantly. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll come, won&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re very kind. I&mdash;I have to say no.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, of all things. A turn-down from a cavalier who fought for me! ... It doesn&rsquo;t happen, at least never yet.... They always say: &lsquo;How about a date?&rsquo;... What&rsquo;re you doing out in Hollywood?&rdquo; &ldquo;I own a horse. He&rsquo;s been in pictures, not I. Oh, I&rsquo;ve had to ride him a few times, doubling for these actors. I hated that. It&rsquo;s almost as tough on me as letting them ride him.&rdquo; &ldquo;A wonderful horse. How thrilling! I love horses.&rdquo; &ldquo;As much as cars?&rdquo; &ldquo;More. We have a ranch and some Arabians.... What&rsquo;s his name?&rdquo; &ldquo;Umpqua.&rdquo; &ldquo;Umpqua? Must be Indian?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, it is. Means swift.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then he can run?&rdquo; &ldquo;Run!&mdash;See here, little lady, Umpqua is as swift as the wind.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet I&rsquo;ve a horse that can beat him.&rdquo; Lance laughed. Here apparently was a real western girl. It did not detract from the dazzling glamour of her. &ldquo;Is he pretty&mdash;beautiful&mdash;grand, or what?&rdquo; she continued. &ldquo;All of them. Umpqua has Arabian blood,&rdquo; replied Lance, warming to her interest. That seemed to put him on her level. &ldquo;He is big and rangy. Mottled black with white feet and nose. Bright soft eyes. Spirited but gentle. And this Hollywood game hasn&rsquo;t done him any good. That&rsquo;s why I&rsquo;m going to quit it and leave this place. Ump is too fine, too sweet a horse for Hollywood.&rdquo; &ldquo;You love him, don&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; she said, softly, as if she understood. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll say I do. Why, he saved Nance&rsquo;s life.... Nance is my sister. Umpqua was given to me when he was a colt. He&rsquo;s cowboy bred. On the Oregon range near Bend. And no horse ever had ten years&rsquo; better breeding.... Well, Nance and I were left alone. We lost the ranch. I had to quit college. She fell ill. It was necessary to have special treatment for her&mdash;operations and all&mdash;to save her life. So to earn the money I brought Umpqua to Hollywood where I had been assured of a job. And did he make good? I&rsquo;m telling you.&rdquo; The girl&rsquo;s eyes were bright with interest. &ldquo;Splendid. And your sister&mdash;Nance?&rdquo; &ldquo;Just fine now. She&rsquo;s going to be married soon.&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell!... Oh, wouldn&rsquo;t I love to see Umpqua? But I wouldn&rsquo;t dare. I&rsquo;d want to buy him. I always try to buy everything I like. And you&rsquo;d hate me. That wouldn&rsquo;t do at all.... Cowboy, are you leaving town? Wouldn&rsquo;t you like... couldn&rsquo;t we meet again?&rdquo; &ldquo;Why&mdash;I&mdash;I... hope to see you again,&rdquo; stammered Lance. &ldquo;We have a lot in common. Horses and ranches&mdash;and things,&rdquo; she went on, consulting her wrist watch. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s see. If I don&rsquo;t get pinched and haled into court, I can cut psych. Say two-thirty, here, tomorrow. Will that be convenient?&rdquo; &ldquo;Okay by me,&rdquo; replied Lance, and opened the door to step out. &ldquo;Thank you for all you did. Good-by till tomorrow. And be careful. Don&rsquo;t forget you punched a cop. They&rsquo;ll be looking for you if they can remember what you look like. I won&rsquo;t forget.&rdquo; Lance stood there rooted to the spot, watching the bright car and golden head flash out of sight. Then expecting to come down to earth with a dull thud, he found himself in the clouds. He soared while he hunted for a westbound trolley and the long ride out seemed only a few moments. Riding a block past his street augured further of his mental aberration. He strode on, out of the main zone of buildings, into the hills, and up the canyon where he had lodgings with a man who rented him a little pasture and stall for his horse. Lance went into the alfalfa-odorous barn. Umpqua nickered at him. &ldquo;My God, Ump!&rdquo; said Lance, as he put his arm over the noble arched neck and laid his cheek against the glossy mane. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve fallen like a ton of bricks. Hardest ever!&mdash;No, old pard, not a movie extra or even a star. But a college girl. Another blonde, Ump! Only this one has them all backed off the lot.... So that&rsquo;s what was wrong with me when I sat dumb in her car?&rdquo; Contact with Umpqua brought Lance down to reality and to the fact that he was leaving Hollywood. Against his sober judgment he would keep the date with the girl, which would be a last sentimental gesture before he rode out toward the open ranges and to the life he was meant for. Lance packed and tagged his outfit, walked downtown to an express office, and checked it to be sent for later. Then he cashed his last check from the studio. It was still only midafternoon. On the boulevard he dropped into a movie theater and sat through two pictures, no details of which he could have even faintly remembered afterward. Then he went to a restaurant for his supper. Even his usually keen appetite did not return to break his abstraction. Thereafter he strolled up the boulevard, knowing it would be the last time. There was a premi√®re at the Chinese, heralded aloft by great searchlight beams, streaking up and sweeping across the heavens. Hollywood&rsquo;s main thoroughfare blazed with colored lights. Cars hummed to and fro, halted for the signals, rolled on again. Lance stood on the corner of Vine Street, absorbing the flash, the glitter, the roar, the vivid life of the strange city. There was a little sadness mixed with his varied feelings and he could not quite analyze the cause. He did not really want this life. Then a shining black limousine sped noiselessly by. Lance caught a fleeting glimpse of a lovely fair girl, radiant in white, lying languorously back against the black-clad shoulder of her companion. That was Hollywood. How many times Lance had seen the same sight, always with a vague envy! He let that glimpse be the last to intrigue him, and striding back to his room, he went to bed. There, wide-awake, he lay in the dark, remembering, wondering, feeling more clearly than at any time since his adventure. A vivid and entrancing picture of the girl appeared etched against the blackness. Her face floated there, exquisitely fair. It was oval, crowned by shining golden hair, which waved back from her broad low brow. Slender arched eyebrows marked large intent eyes, wide apart, dark, the color of violets and singularly expressive with a light of friendliness, of frank interest. The whole face had a flash, of which fixed and changeless beauty was only a part. Feature by feature the girl&rsquo;s face appeared to Lance with a clearness which astonished him. Lance shut his eyes to blot out this memory picture. But it made no difference. She was there, in his mind, on his heart. Never in all his life had he yearned for anything so dearly as to kiss those red lips. That dragged him rudely out of his trance. It would be wise not to see the girl again. With a pang he abandoned the idea. Majesty.... Madge, that student Rollie had called her. The first name suited her. Who was she? Where was that ranch with the Arabian horses? Somewhere in California, no doubt. That girl had class. Yet there was nothing the least snobbish about her. Too lovely, too kind and sweet to be a flirt! No need. She was rich, of course, Lance thought, remembering her clothes and her car! He remembered, too, the jeweled monogram on her cigarette case, but could recall only the letter M. And Lance rolled over to go to sleep. Aw! What the hell? He was always mooning over some pretty dame, especially a blonde, and here he had what was coming to him. Forget it, cowboy, and hit the trail. All the same he dreamed of her and upon awakening in the morning, he began to waver in his resolution. Why be a sap? She had been grateful. He would want to know how she fared with the police and the college authorities. She would keep the date and wait for him. Lance, in the broad light of day, while he made his final preparations to leave, thought better of his resolve not to meet her. Treat a swell girl like that&mdash;stand her up on a promised date&mdash;a girl who loved horses&mdash;it just was not in him. And all the rest of the morning, at lunch, and when he took the bus downtown, he was conscious of a tingling expectance, a heat in his veins, a glamour over everything. It amazed Lance extremely that he could not immediately find the parking place where he was to meet the girl. He had been so balmy, he thought, that he had scarcely known whether he was walking or riding. It was a good thing that he had come downtown so early. After wandering around, up one street and down another, at last he found the vacant lot which had been utilized to park cars. He was still a quarter of an hour ahead of time. An attendant, observing Lance loitering around, told him he could sit in one of the cars if he were waiting for someone. Lance promptly availed himself of this permission; in fact he took a back seat in a car standing against a building. Lance did not believe she would come at all; if she did he wanted to see her before she spied him. The buoyancy usual with Lance at a rendezvous seemed to be wanting here. This was a tremendous occasion. He could see a large blue-handed clock in a tower some distance away, and watching this, as the half hour neared, he gave way more and more to inexplicable feelings. If she came, that would be proof she liked him, and maybe.... Why not postpone his departure for Arizona? A few days or even weeks would not make any particular difference. If she wanted to see him, take him to the house to meet her friends, perhaps go out to see Umpqua&mdash;how could he ever resist that? He had always been a fool over girls. With this one he would be serious and assuredly she had only a passing fancy or interest in him. Or she might have been one of these beautiful dames who had to have a new flame every day. Maybe he had better just wait to have a farewell look at her, and not let her know he was there. But suppose she really had been struck with him! That was possible. It had happened once. In this case there would never again be any peace away from the glad light of those violet eyes. &ldquo;Gosh! I was a dumbbell for coming,&rdquo; he muttered, kicking himself. &ldquo;She&rsquo;s late now.... She won&rsquo;t come&mdash;and am I glad?&rdquo; Nevertheless he lingered there, sliding down in the seat, watching with hawk eyes the passing cars, slowly succumbing to a pang in his breast. At a quarter to three he gave up hope. Then a bright tan roadster flashed into sight. It slowed and turned in. The driver was a girl in blue. But her blue hat did not hide a gleam of gold. She had come! Lance&rsquo;s heart gave a leap and his blood gushed through his veins. Then a seven-passenger car, shiny black in hue, flashed into sight, slowed and stopped outside the turn. From it leaped a slender young man, noticeably well-dressed. He waved the car on with sharp gesture and came hurrying, his piercing gaze on the blonde girl. Lance saw her sweep a quick glance all around the parking place. She was looking for him, and the disappointment she expressed was so sweet and moving to Lance that it would have drawn him out of his hiding place but for the mien of the newcomer. She had halted at an angle from Lance&rsquo;s position, perhaps a dozen steps distant, and scarcely had she dismissed the polite attendant when the other man caught up to lean over the side of her car. He did not remove his soft gray hat. He had a remarkably handsome visage, pale, chiseled as if from marble, a square chin and ruthless mouth, and light gray eyes sharp as daggers. He reminded Lance of someone he knew. &ldquo;On the lam, eh, Madge? You certainly gave that driver of mine a run,&rdquo; he said, with an air of cool effrontery. &ldquo;Hello, Bee. What do you mean&mdash;on the lam?&rdquo; she replied. &ldquo;Trying to run away from me again.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. I was in a hurry to keep a date. I&rsquo;m too late. He&rsquo;s come and gone. Damn old Fuzzy-Top! It was his fault.&rdquo; &ldquo;Was your date with Fuzzy-Top?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. You don&rsquo;t seem to understand my college talk any better than I do your gangster expressions. Fuzzy is one of my profs.&rdquo; Gangster! Lance sustained a sudden shock. So that was it. This young man bore a remarkable resemblance to the picture star, Robert Morris, in his racketeer roles. What could the girl possibly have to do with a gangster? Plenty, thought Lance, considering that she had the imperious look of one who had an insatiable thirst for adventure. &ldquo;Madge, I haven&rsquo;t said nothing yet,&rdquo; replied the fellow, with a laugh. &ldquo;Saying it with flowers is not my way. How about cocktails? Take me for a ride.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bee, I told you I had a date,&rdquo; she protested. &ldquo;With a perfectly swell fellow. I&rsquo;m crazy about him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? He doesn&rsquo;t seem so crazy about you. Dish the date and let&rsquo;s go places.&rdquo; With that the cool gentleman walked around the front of her car, and opening the door he slipped in and slammed it shut. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve got a nerve,&rdquo; she retorted. &ldquo;Didn&rsquo;t you tell me that was what you liked about me?&rdquo; &ldquo;I suspect I did. You were something new, Bee.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks. You&rsquo;re a new twist on me. All women are flirts. But I went for you in a big way. And you went out with me, didn&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. A couple of times. If you recall I met you at the Grove one afternoon for tea. We danced. And one other time at the Biltmore, where we quarreled because you were pretty raw.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cooled on me, eh?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not exactly. You still pack a thrill. But you&rsquo;re a little too&mdash;too...&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, no broad ever made a sucker of Bee Uhl yet,&rdquo; he rejoined, with a crisp ring in his voice. &ldquo;Mr. Uhl, you&rsquo;re quite beyond me,&rdquo; said the girl, with a smile that disarmed her aloofness. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m afraid you&rsquo;re going to make me regret my&mdash;well, shall we call it playful indiscretion? I never took you for a gentleman, but I thought you a good sport. If I&rsquo;m not mistaken the favors of our little flirtation were yours.... Where can I drop you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, Beauty, you hate yourself, don&rsquo;t you? Well, I can take it. But the Honey Bee is not through buzzing around yet.... Let me off corner Seventh.&rdquo; In another moment they were gone, leaving Lance in a queer state of mind. He hardly knew what to think, or why he had not made his presence known. Presently his romance burst like a pricked bubble. But his relief did not equal his regret. He would not be seeing Madge again. If her apparently friendly contact with a gangster had caused her to fall somewhat in his hasty estimation, that did not seem to make any difference. Almost he sympathized with Honey Bee Uhl. That was a cognomen. Lance wondered what it signified. Then his sympathy veered to the spirited girl. He seemed to grasp that it would be impossible for her to have any fun, at least with men, to follow any natural bent of conquest or coquetry, to play around and look for what and whom she wanted from life, without leaving havoc in her wake. A girl as beautiful as she was, radiant with such an intense and fatal charm, would have to go into a nunnery, or else expect a fall of Troy around her. No doubt she desired that very thing. Lance congratulated himself on his great good fortune in avoiding the meeting, yet when it was too late he wanted it otherwise. * * * * * In less than two hours Lance was riding Umpqua along the hilly backroads of Hollywood. He was on his way and saw the last of the town from a bridle path high upon a foothill. He knew every bit of soft road under the slope of the mountains and avoided the asphalt wherever possible. At nine o&rsquo;clock, some twenty miles out of the city, he called it a day and sought lodgings for himself and Umpqua. Up at dawn he made San Bernardino by nightfall and the next day Banning. This entrance to the desert pass he welcomed as an event. From there on he could keep his horse almost altogether off the paved roads. That night Lance was so tired he went to sleep when his head touched the pillow. On the following morning he headed down San Gorgonio Pass toward the great gray valley of the southernmost California desert. He knew that arid country, having been to Palm Springs and Indio with motion-picture companies. Still, sight of the rolling wasteland with its knolls of mesquite and flats of greasewood, and the irregular barren mountains zigzagging the horizon, afforded him keen pleasure. How different this country from the golden pastures and black hills and swift streams of Oregon! Lance could not have conceived a greater contrast. And by noonday the June heat of the desert was intense. Sweat oozed out of his every pore and Umpqua was wet. But this heat was what both horse and rider needed. They were heavy from underwork and overeating. By midafternoon Lance reached a little station on the railroad above Indio, where he halted for the night. He slept on a spread of hay under a cottonwood tree; and when the red sun peeped over the Chocolate Mountains next morning Lance felt that the comfort and the lure of Hollywood had been left far behind. From that point he began a leisurely journey down the long sun-baked desert. Mecca, the Salton Sea, Niland were each marked by hitching up another hole in Umpqua&rsquo;s cinch. But the great horse, once off the automobile roads and loosened up by the heat, soon showed his sound bottom and his love of the open. He knew they were headed for new ranges. Lance struck the five-mile stretch of sand dunes at sunrise, and he marveled at the smooth mounds with their knifelike crests, the scalloped vales between the dunes, the opal hues changing and playing across the sands. Umpqua did not like this region where his hoofs sank to his fetlocks. The flinty levels beyond, black and red with polished gravel, the sparse tufts of greasewood and cactus, the volcanic peaks, and finally the dusky arrowwood-bordered road to the Colorado River&mdash;these kept Umpqua on his easy ground-covering gait. Lance&rsquo;s first sight of the red river justified what he had anticipated&mdash;a sullen swirling muddy flood, inimicable to horse and rider. And Yuma at night struck Lance favorably, with its wide main street and bright lights, its giant Indians and stealthily stepping Mexicans. He was across the river and this was Arizona. That fact roused Lance at dawn. On his way again he appeared to have Arizona burst upon him in a blaze of brilliant sunlight that flooded vast wastes of barren soil and meager patches of grass, and ranges of ragged mountains asleep in the sunrise, and dim mesas and escarpments in the distance, and ghosts of purple domes hauntingly vague. Lance was a man of the open, but the great distances, the vastness, the endless reach of wasteland allured while it repelled him. He rode on, and dust, heat, wind were his portion. Ranches, service stations, hamlets stretched lonesomely across the desert. He had lost track of days and miles beyond Yuma by the time he reached Florence. Tombstone with its preserved buildings of a hard frontier past, Bisbee with its great mines and bustle, Douglas, an enterprising and progressive town marked Lance&rsquo;s long ride across southern Arizona. Lance meant to strike off the main highway and railroad somewhere beyond Douglas into the ever-increasing rugged grandeur and beautiful valleys of this Arizona land. But his money, which he had thought would hold out for a much longer period, had dwindled to almost nothing, and it was now necessary that he stop and look for work. A rest would do Umpqua good. Lance found a Mexican who owned a small pasture outside of town and here he left the horse. In a pinch he could pawn his watch or gun, but he would have a try at finding work before resorting to that. Lance accosted men in service stations and stores without any success. What he wanted to encounter was a cowboy. But this type appeared remarkably scarce. One man, evidently a cattleman, laughed gruffly at Lance: &ldquo;Wal, son, thet kind of two-laiged critter has been aboot washed up on this range.&rdquo; &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t say. What by?&rdquo; queried Lance, blankly. &ldquo;I reckon by hard times. North of heah a ways there&rsquo;s some cattle left. But down heah the only successful business is bootlegging.&rdquo; That discouraged Lance, and he strolled around, slowly succumbing to the need of pawning his watch. Walking in high-heeled cowboy boots was not exactly a joy. It was noon and Lance was hot. Presently he heard voices near at hand, and turning discovered that he had halted close to a big black car, from which issued sharp voices. A second glance at that car struck him singularly. How like the black car that had followed the girl Madge to the parking place where he had chosen to avoid meeting her! With a pang he realized he had not thought of her for days. He was in another world. But this car!... Shiny black, without a gleam of metal anywhere, a fine high-priced machine, it certainly resembled.... &ldquo;Hey, buddy, come here,&rdquo; called a voice that shot through Lance. A young man, with pale face and eyes like gimlets, was leaning out of the front seat opposite the driver. Lance recognized him immediately. The young man Madge had designated as a gangster and who had called himself Honey Bee Uhl. &ldquo;Hello, yourself,&rdquo; he replied. &ldquo;You look sort of on the loose.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, I look just what I am,&rdquo; Lance replied. &ldquo;No offense. We&rsquo;re loafing here for a guy, and I just wanted to be friendly. Care for a drink?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not till I have a feed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Broke?&rdquo; &ldquo;Flat as a pancake. And I can&rsquo;t find a job in this slow burg.&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, buddy, there&rsquo;s plenty jobs for the right guys. Can you drive a truck?&rdquo; &ldquo;Mister, I could drive two trucks,&rdquo; retorted Lance, boastfully. &ldquo;Yeah? Well, how&rsquo;d you like to grab a century?&rdquo; &ldquo;Uhuh! Sounds good to me. I&rsquo;d pull almost any kind of a job for that much dough. Only I&rsquo;d want to be sure I was going to get it,&rdquo; laughed Lance. &ldquo;Exactly. It&rsquo;s okay. Now who are you and what have you been doing?&rdquo; &ldquo;You never heard of me, mister,&rdquo; said Lance, evasively. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;ll say I&rsquo;ve been beating it from L. A.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dicks after you?&rdquo; Lance laughed grimly and looked blankly silent, and averted his face somewhat from the piercing scrutiny bent upon him. &ldquo;Come clean with me, buddy, if you want your luck to change. What you been doing in L. A.?&rdquo; &ldquo;Are you asking me, mister?&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, what the hell? I&rsquo;m hungry.... I beat it out of Portland ahead of Latzy Cork,&rdquo; hazarded Lance, remembering the name of a shady underworld character who had recently been eluding the police on the coast. &ldquo;That racket, eh?&rdquo; flashed Uhl, snapping his fingers. And with his eyes like gray fire he turned in the seat to his companions. Lance took advantage of this moment to make certain that he would recognize the driver of the car, and the three hard-faced individuals in the back seat, if he ever saw them again. At the side of the one farthest toward the road Lance espied the muzzle of a machine gun. &ldquo;Cork may have been spotting me. What do you think, Dipper?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not a chance, Bee. He&rsquo;s been in Frisco and north for two months,&rdquo; replied the one addressed. &ldquo;We don&rsquo;t know that,&rdquo; said Uhl, doubtfully, and turning again he pulled out a roll of bills, the wrapper of which bore the denomination one hundred. &ldquo;Here&rsquo;s your dough, buddy.... You&rsquo;re on the spot. But the only risk you run is if you double-cross me.&rdquo; &ldquo;If I undertake the job, I&rsquo;ll be straight,&rdquo; interposed Lance. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s how you strike me.... See the big canvas-covered truck across there, back of the service station? Well, she&rsquo;s your bus. You&rsquo;re to take her to Tucson. She&rsquo;s empty, but you drive slow, as if she was loaded heavy. See? You&rsquo;ll be held up sooner or later, probably after dark outside of Tucson. That&rsquo;ll be okay. You&rsquo;re dumb. You just drove the truck over and you don&rsquo;t know me. See?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know whether I see or not,&rdquo; rejoined Lance, dubiously. &ldquo;Who&rsquo;ll hold me up&mdash;and why?&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, you won&rsquo;t need to fake being dumb. All you got to do is stop when you&rsquo;re held up. See? You don&rsquo;t know nothing.&rdquo; &ldquo;Does that truck belong to you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes.&rdquo; &ldquo;Rumrunner?&rdquo; &ldquo;I told you it was empty,&rdquo; snapped Uhl. &ldquo;Is it a go?&rdquo; &ldquo;You bet,&rdquo; declared Lance, taking the proffered money. &ldquo;What&rsquo;ll I do when I get to Tucson?&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll be on the main highway. Stop at the first service station on the edge of town. Right-hand side.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then what?&rdquo; &ldquo;If I don&rsquo;t meet you someone else will.&rdquo; &ldquo;Suppose these holdup gents take the truck away from me?&rdquo; &ldquo;That won&rsquo;t be your loss.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss,&rdquo; interposed the sallow-faced Dipper, &ldquo;this husky bird is packin&rsquo; a rod.&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, are you telling me? I hope he turns cowboy with it on those dopes.... Buddy, if you turn this trick there&rsquo;ll be more.&rdquo; &ldquo;This one doesn&rsquo;t strike me so hot,&rdquo; declared Lance, tersely. &ldquo;But one at a time. I&rsquo;m on my way.&rdquo; As Lance strode off, carefully pocketing the money he heard Uhl say: &ldquo;Dip, if he comes through we&rsquo;ll take him on.&rdquo; &ldquo;No gamble, Boss. That fellow will do....&rdquo; Lance passed on out of earshot. At the station he said to the operator: &ldquo;That truck ready?&rdquo; Upon being informed that &ldquo;she&rsquo;s all set,&rdquo; Lance climbed into the driver&rsquo;s seat and took a look. The machine was a fine make. As he moved out of the station yard he observed that the big black car had gone. Lance did not look to see what had become of it. A block away he turned into the highway and got through Douglas without a stop. Once beyond the town he opened up to twenty-five miles an hour and faced the north with a grim realization that he was in for an adventure he never would have hazarded but for a blonde college girl named Madge who had intrigued him. &ldquo;Queer setup,&rdquo; soliloquized Lance, now giving rein to his conjectures. &ldquo;I hit the bull&rsquo;s-eye with that crack about Latzy Cork.... Racket? Wonder which racket? Cork was suspected of most everything up north.... Anyhow I got away with it.... And this Bee Uhl. He&rsquo;s a crook all right. From Chi.... I get it. Chicago, of course. He doesn&rsquo;t seem to care who knows it. And this big truck must have to do with bootlegging. Over the border, maybe. Or up from some harbor on the Gulf.... Nothing to me. It gets my goat, though, what this slick bozo had to do with that girl.&rdquo; Lance reflected presently that he ought to have that circumstance mastered. Madge&rsquo;s own words testified to that. She had flirted with Uhl, obviously for the thrill of it. She certainly knew he was a gangster. Perhaps that was the secret. A girl like her must be besieged by admirers, importuned and bored until she was tired of them. Still Uhl was handsome, and perhaps his hard and insolent way might have appealed to the girl. Assuredly that had been the inception of the affair. Lance felt glad to convince himself that she had realized her mistake, and in a thoroughbred but kindly way had made it clear to Uhl. Lance did not need to bring back the vision of her. That was limned on his memory. No use faking it, he thought&mdash;he had fallen in love with her at first sight. That was all right. But he wished the thought and beauty and charm of her would not stick so tenaciously. He could not banish her and again came the regret that he had not stood right out like a man to meet her that day. He could at least have spared her the encounter with Uhl. All at once Lance had a disturbing thought. Uhl, gangster, racketeer, bootlegger, might have another slant to his crookedness. He might be a kidnaper. That seemed reasonable enough, and the idea grew on Lance. The girl must belong to a rich California family. Her style, her patrician air, her talk of a ranch full of Arabian horses, surely these attested wealth. And that might explain Bee Uhl&rsquo;s interest in her. With the near approach of repeal of prohibition these bootleggers must work up other rackets. Already there had been a nationwide activity in kidnaping. &ldquo;Goofy or not, I take it as a hunch,&rdquo; muttered Lance, with finality. &ldquo;Believe me, I&rsquo;ll get a line on that slicker with his roll of centuries, if I can.&rdquo; And in the stress of the moment Lance thought that if he did verify such suspicion of the gangster, it would not be beyond him to go back to Los Angeles to warn the girl. Then the realization of his sudden tumult of delight made him look aghast at himself. &ldquo;Quit your romancing, kid,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;This is hard pay. And I&rsquo;m on a job I should have passed up.&rdquo; But nothing he thought or reasoned out changed the essential sentiment and presagement of the situation. The way accidents and circumstances fell across his path and what came of them had taught him to believe any strange and far-reaching adventure could befall him. Cars and trucks going and coming passed Lance now and then, the southbound traffic being the heavier. Lance did not see the big black automobile belonging to Uhl. Once he looked back down a league-long stretch, half expecting to discover the car following. But he did not. Driving a truck did not permit of close attention to the desert scenery, which had been his pleasure while riding Umpqua. However, that labor and his concentration on the peculiar circumstances leading to this ride, certainly made the time fly. Almost before he knew it, he was climbing the tortuous grade through Bisbee, keeping keen lookout for the holdup he had been told to expect. About midafternoon he went through picturesque Tombstone on the outskirts of which he halted for gasoline. This necessitated his breaking the hundred dollar bill Uhl had given him. The service station man, a westerner of middle age, glanced from the bill to Lance with keen blue eyes. &ldquo;Seen bills like this before&mdash;an&rsquo; also that truck you&rsquo;re drivin&rsquo;. How aboot yore company?&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t savvy,&rdquo; returned Lance, gruffly. &ldquo;What you mean by company?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, usually thar&rsquo;s two or three trucks like this one strung along. Reckon you&rsquo;re new to...&rdquo; &ldquo;To what, mister?&rdquo; interrupted Lance. &ldquo;Wal, I ain&rsquo;t sayin&rsquo;,&rdquo; responded the operator, in cool evasion. &ldquo;Yeah? Well, as a matter of fact, I&rsquo;m damn new at this job.&rdquo; That little wordy byplay roused Lance anew to the possibilities that might be thickening ahead of him. Thereafter he kept keen as a whip, increasing his speed a little. It was almost dark when he passed Mescal, a desert hamlet, and he did not halt to appease thirst or hunger. He wanted to get this job over. The desert night was soft and balmy, cooling as the radiation of the day&rsquo;s heat passed away. Jackrabbits and coyotes leaped across the road, gray in the flash of his lamps. The headlights of cars grew from pin points in the blackness to yellow orbs, rushing at him and passing by, to leave the distant road dark again. The dry odor of dust and desert growths clogged his nostrils. Under favorable circumstances Lance would have liked closer acquaintance with that desert. The spectral arms of cactus and the dense thickets of mesquite accentuated the lonesomeness. Some miles beyond Vail there appeared to come a brightening to the north. Soon Lance made that out to be the lights of Tucson, miles away still, but clear in the rarefied atmosphere. Lance rolling along at forty miles or more an hour, began to feel an edge for the expected holdup. Every time he caught the gleam of headlights behind he prepared for the order to halt. But so many cars passed him the next hour and so bright grew the illuminated horizon that he began to believe he might reach the first service station on the right without being stopped. Presently a car came up behind and held its place for a couple of miles. Lance anticipated that this was the one, and he forced himself to be ready. He slowed down to thirty, then to twenty. The car kept behind him, somewhat to the left. At length it slipped up alongside Lance. &ldquo;Hey, you driver. Halt!&rdquo; rang out a hoarse voice. Lance shut off, and applying the brakes, screeched to a stop. &ldquo;Okay!&rdquo; he yelled, complying with the order. Two men leaped out and a door clicked. The car moved on ahead to come to a standstill in front of the truck. Lance&rsquo;s door was jerked open. Light flooded his cab. Over an extended gun he caught indistinct sight of two faces, the foremost of which was masked. Lance heard footsteps running back behind his truck and the clank of bolts. &ldquo;Bud, j&rsquo;ever see this one before?&rdquo; queried the bandit with the gun. &ldquo;Nope. Another new one,&rdquo; came the laconic answer. &ldquo;Who are you?&rdquo; followed the demand. &ldquo;Arizona cowpuncher,&rdquo; replied Lance. &ldquo;Broke. Agreed to drive this truck.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who hired you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know. Five men in a black car at Douglas.&rdquo; From behind clanked the hinge and there was a slap of canvas. &ldquo;Empty, by God!&rdquo; cried a hoarse voice, in anger. Footsteps preceded the appearance of two more men, one of whom Lance managed to distinguish despite the blinding flashlight. &ldquo;Henny, we&rsquo;re tricked. He&rsquo;s made suckers out of us again. This truck is empty.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ah, hell no!&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, hell yes! It&rsquo;s a cattle car. As late as yesterday, when we picked them cars up, this one was full of steers. The other one had the...&rdquo; &ldquo;Shut up!&rdquo; yapped the leader, pounding his gun on the door. &ldquo;Hey, driver, how many trucks like this have you lamped lately?&rdquo; &ldquo;Off and on I&rsquo;ve seen a good many,&rdquo; rejoined Lance, glibly. &ldquo;Three in a row day before yesterday.&rdquo; &ldquo;Goin&rsquo; which way?&rdquo; &ldquo;North, out of Douglas.&rdquo; &ldquo;Driver, is there a short cut to El Paso without goin&rsquo; through Douglas?&rdquo; queried the leader, sharply. &ldquo;Yes, at Benson,&rdquo; replied Lance, readily. &ldquo;Poor road, but passable.&rdquo; The leader snapped off his flashlight. &ldquo;Beat it, cowboy, wherever you&rsquo;re goin&rsquo;. An&rsquo; tell your boss we&rsquo;re onto his racket.&rdquo; &ldquo;Henny, if there&rsquo;s a short cut, no matter how bad, we might head off that car,&rdquo; rasped the man Bud. He had a bitter raucous voice that Lance would remember. The four bandits ran to pile into their car. &ldquo;Turn an&rsquo; step on it!&rdquo; ordered the leader. In another moment their car was roaring east on the highway, and Lance had a clear road ahead. Relieved, and more interested than ever, he threw in his clutch and sped on toward Tucson. Lance saw that he had been used merely to throw this gang off the track. The cattle slant to the business seemed a trick that would take more than one carload of bandits to beat. The run from that lonely stretch of road to the service station designated was accomplished in short order, Lance driving at a fast clip. The truck appeared to ooze along as smoothly as a limousine. Hardly had Lance come to a halt in the station yard when two men in dark garb, slouch hats pulled down, hurried out to meet him. Lance was ready for them, and opening the door he stepped out with a long whew of relief. &ldquo;Hello. Am I glad to see you? Take her away,&rdquo; he said, vociferously. &ldquo;Dey stick you up?&rdquo; queried one, tensely, while the other leaped into the seat. &ldquo;You bet. About five miles out. You should have heard Bud and Henny cuss to find her empty. I gave them a bum steer.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? An&rsquo; how bum?&rdquo; &ldquo;They asked me if there was a short cut to El Paso and I told them yes, at Benson. I heard it was some road. They&rsquo;ll get lost.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;ll go hot with the boss. How much&rsquo;d you dole out for gas? He forgot thet, an&rsquo; told me to square it.&rdquo; Lance named the sum, which was handed to him in a five dollar note and no change wanted. &ldquo;Blick, have we got all night?&rdquo; demanded the man in the driver&rsquo;s seat. &ldquo;Cut it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Keep your shirt on. Honey Bee gave me an order, didn&rsquo;t he?... Driver, yer come through clean. I&rsquo;m to tell you thet if you&rsquo;re hangin&rsquo; round Douglas next run, you can get another job.&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell. I&rsquo;ll hang around, if it&rsquo;s not too long. When&rsquo;s the next run?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know. Mebbe in a month&mdash;mebbe longer.&rdquo; When they had gone Lance went into the service station, aware that his arrival and the short conference had been observed. &ldquo;How far to a hash joint? I&rsquo;m sure starved,&rdquo; he began, genially. &ldquo;Stranger hereabouts?&rdquo; the man returned, with a keen look. &ldquo;Plenty grub places up the street.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks. Yes, I&rsquo;m a stranger. And I don&rsquo;t mind telling you I drove that truck because I was broke. I was held up out here and scared stiff.&rdquo; &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t say. Well, that&rsquo;s not strange considerin&rsquo; the company. You got off lucky.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? What was I up against?&rdquo; &ldquo;Couldn&rsquo;t say.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did you ever see that truck before?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yep, an&rsquo; some more like it. They been comin&rsquo; and goin&rsquo; every six weeks or so.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cattle business must be good when steers get hauled in trucks,&rdquo; commented Lance; then waiting a moment for an answer, which did not come, he strode up the street. In the middle of the second block he found a caf√©, where he obtained his supper. At the next corner there was a hotel. Inquiry brought the information that he could take a bus early next morning for Douglas. Then he went to bed. Events of the day had been thought-provoking, but they did not keep him awake. On the bus the following morning, however, he had nothing to do but think. It took Lance practically all that long ride to reason out the futility of any further interest in Uhl. Lance did not want to drive any more questionable trucks. Aside from an interesting experience, this meeting with Uhl had no warrant to absorb him. It was the singular connection with the college girl that kept him wondering and conjecturing, and thinking that he should warn her somehow. But he did not even know her last name. And to go back to Los Angeles on such a fanciful assumption seemed absurd. Nevertheless his conscience bothered him. That passed, however, leaving Lance with only the increasing pang of regret. When at Douglas he went out to see Umpqua, and quite provokingly he conceived a picture of Madge on his beautiful horse, he almost gave way to rage at his sentimentality. All the same, thought of the girl persisted, and Lance finally reconciled himself to being haunted. Riding northeast from Douglas the Arizona desert land magnified its proportions of color and wildness and rugged grandeur to such a degree that Lance was loath to travel on and turn his back to ranges that dwarfed those he had ridden in Oregon. What a grand country he was entering! Ahead of him were mountains, peaked and lofty, purple in the distance, growing black and gray as league after league he neared them. Lance took his time stopping to ask questions, but the several little hamlets along the way failed to yield much information. He spent one night at Chiricahua, which town appeared to be in the center of a vast green and gray range surrounded by mountains. He had begun to see cattle in considerable numbers, though not one hundredth as many as the country might have supported. He rode on and on over a rolling and lovely valley. Darkness overtook Lance. He had inquired at Apache about towns farther north. He had been told they were few and far between. It looked as though his preoccupation with the solitude and beauty of this upland valley was going to make him spend a night in the open. He did not mind that. The day had been hot and the night still remained warm. However, three hours after sunset he sighted lights ahead and soon entered a place called Bolton. Unlike most of the other towns, this one appeared to be comparatively new and located on both highway and railroad. There was a wide main street with bright lights and many parked cars, stores and caf√©s, a hotel and an inn, a bank and motion-picture theater. Lance rode on through to the outer zone of garages and autocamps. Umpqua, staunch as he was, had begun to tire. Lance was pleased to see several horses tethered beyond a garage off the main street, and next a livery stable. The garage, evidently, also provided the facilities of a service station and was quite modest compared to showy places Lance had passed. &ldquo;Howdy, cowboy,&rdquo; drawled a pleasant voice. &ldquo;Git down an&rsquo; come in.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hello, yourself,&rdquo; replied Lance, greeting a sturdy bow-legged young man who had appeared from somewhere. There was enough light to make out a lean tanned face from which shone narrow slits of eyes, keen and friendly. &ldquo;My Gawd!&mdash;Where&rsquo;d you steal thet hawse?&rdquo; queried this individual. &ldquo;Are you kidding me or is that the way horsemen are greeted here?&rdquo; asked Lance. &ldquo;Shore kiddin&rsquo;, cowboy. We got some grand hawses in this country an&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s why I got fresh. But on the level where did you find him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oregon bred. He was given us when he was a colt. And I raised him.&rdquo; &ldquo;You from Oregon?&rdquo; went on the other, walking around the horse in a way that betrayed a love of horseflesh. It was an open sesame to Lance&rsquo;s friendliness. &ldquo;Yes, rode him all the way.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t tell. I&rsquo;ll be dog-goned.... Wal, lookin&rsquo; him over I ain&rsquo;t so surprised. All hawse, cowboy, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;d trade you my garage for him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sounds cowboy,&rdquo; laughed Lance. &ldquo;Gee, that&rsquo;s bad news. I came to Arizona to find a job with some cattle outfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you&rsquo;re jest outa luck. Cowboys air scarce these days. As scarce as jobs. Plenty of cattle all through heah. An&rsquo; the outfits thinned down to two or three riders. My job for three years before this bust-up hit us was with Gene Stewart. Finest rancher in these parts. Used to run eighty thousand haid. But of late years Gene has lost out. An&rsquo; as I couldn&rsquo;t ride no more for nothin&rsquo; I had to take this place. Pays fair, but I just hate it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t blame you.... How about me bedding down Umpqua in this livery stable?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s Indian. Name of a river in Oregon. Means swift.&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell handle at thet. Shore, this stable is okay. I&rsquo;ll go in with you.... What&rsquo;d you say your name was? Mine&rsquo;s Ren Starr.&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t say yet. It&rsquo;s Lance Sidway.&rdquo; &ldquo;Air you gonna hang about heah a spell?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, if I can find work.&rdquo; The livery stable man turned out to be an old fellow with an unmistakable cattle range air about him. He was almost as enthusiastic over Umpqua as Starr had been. For the first time in a long while Lance began to feel at home with his kind. &ldquo;Ump, old boy, this barn smells good, doesn&rsquo;t it?&rdquo; said Lance, and giving his pet a parting smack he went out with Starr. &ldquo;Where can I eat and sleep?&rdquo; &ldquo;Several places, but outside the hotel, you&rsquo;ll like Mrs. Goodman&rsquo;s caf√©. Nice woman, dotes on cowboys, an&rsquo; runs a swell little chuck house.&rdquo; &ldquo;Won&rsquo;t you come with me? I&rsquo;d like to talk.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, shore. I&rsquo;ve had my supper. But I can always eat. An&rsquo; it&rsquo;s closin&rsquo; time for my place anyway.&rdquo; Presently Lance was ushered into a clean fragrant little shop, with more of a homey than a caf√© look, and introduced to a portly woman of kind and genial aspect. Evidently she had a warm spot in her heart for cowboys. &ldquo;Wal, Oregon, I&rsquo;d shore like to see you stop heah,&rdquo; said Starr, eagerly. Manifestly he had taken to Lance as Lance had to him. &ldquo;All the way up from Apache I&rsquo;ve liked the range more and more.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell, this ain&rsquo;t nothin&rsquo;. You ought to see thet range down along the west slope of the Peloncillo Mountains. Swell deer an&rsquo; antelope huntin&rsquo;. Bear an&rsquo; cougar up high. Trout fishin&rsquo;, oh boy! Grass an&rsquo; sage ranges.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sounds more than swell. Is that where your Gene Stewart runs cattle?&rdquo; &ldquo;Used to, when he had ten outfits. But now he&rsquo;s only got about a thousand haid left, not countin&rsquo; yearlin&rsquo;s an&rsquo; calves. He jest lets them graze around his ranch, with a couple of Mexican kids ridin&rsquo; for him.&rdquo; &ldquo;How far away is his ranch?&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon about thirty by trail. The road runs round an&rsquo; up an&rsquo; down. Cars register forty-two miles. No road work this spring makes tough goin&rsquo;, an&rsquo; I don&rsquo;t mean mebbe.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wonder what chance I&rsquo;d have getting on with him? Wages no object for a while. I want to ride open country, and have a square meal often, with pasture for Umpqua. You see he was raised to fare for himself. Alfalfa and grain would spoil him.&rdquo; &ldquo;My idee of trainin&rsquo; a hawse right.... I&rsquo;ll tell you, Sidway, there&rsquo;s a pretty shore chance for you out with Gene. He needs riders most damn bad. I&rsquo;ll give you a note to him in the mawnin&rsquo;. Thet&rsquo;ll cinch it, if you really want a job for nothin&rsquo; &rsquo;cept board. He&rsquo;ll be glad. Only you gotta approach him sorta careful.... Sensitive fellow, Gene is, but the salt of this range. For two bits I&rsquo;ll sell out an&rsquo; go with you.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;d be swell. Why don&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;d be all right with Mom. She wants to get out of this hot country for a while. But I&rsquo;m makin&rsquo; money an&rsquo; I reckon I ought to save plenty before hittin&rsquo; the trail again.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right, Ren. Thanks for the hunch. I&rsquo;ll go. Maybe we can see each other sometimes. I&rsquo;d like that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Me too. Shore we can. Gene would give you a Sunday off now an&rsquo; then. I&rsquo;d run out after you.&rdquo; &ldquo;What kind of ranch does Stewart own?&rdquo; &ldquo;Darned interesting, Ren. I&rsquo;m going to like Stewart.&rdquo; &ldquo;You shore will, an&rsquo; if you turn out as good as you look&mdash;excuse me bein&rsquo; personal&mdash;Gene is goin&rsquo; to cotton to you. He was grand to me. An&rsquo; I just love him as if he was my dad. He always stops in to see me, hopin&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll come back. But he never says so. He was in town today, worried plain about somethin&rsquo;! He said it was only cause he was losin&rsquo; a few cattle.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cattle thieves?&rdquo; exclaimed Lance, quickly. &ldquo;Rustlers over heah, Sid.&rdquo; &ldquo;No!&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. There&rsquo;s still some rustlin&rsquo; all over. Nothin&rsquo; heah like it used to be. But you see a dozen haid to Gene now means more&rsquo;n a thousand, years ago. He was sore because he couldn&rsquo;t find out how the cattle was stole. An&rsquo; old Nels, the last of thet great outfit of cowboys, couldn&rsquo;t find out either.&rdquo; &ldquo;Too old-fashioned, maybe.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dog-gone, Sid. I had thet very idee.&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, Starr,&rdquo; spoke up Lance, as if with an inspiration. &ldquo;Not so many days ago I drove a big truck from Douglas to Tucson. It was empty but it had been full of steers.&rdquo; Lance related briefly the circumstances that made it necessary for him to earn some money, but he did not go into detail about the men he had met on that adventure. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m a son-of-a-gun!... What kind of a truck?&rdquo; &ldquo;A big one, fine make, and canvas-covered. I took the license number and the name of the owner. Which I suspect is not the name of the right owner.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sidway, you&rsquo;re sayin&rsquo; things,&rdquo; rejoined Starr, growing cool after his excitement. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve seen three or four trucks like thet one pass heah every month or so. One went through north four days ago.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did you pay any particular attention to it?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. Only saw it an&rsquo; was sore as usual &rsquo;cause the driver got gas from one of the other stations. You see them fellers never have bought a gallon of gas from me. Thet&rsquo;s okay, shore. My place is as you saw it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Starr, they passed you up because you were a cowboy.&rdquo; &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t say! Thet&rsquo;s an idee. A hot one.&rdquo; &ldquo;We got something to work on.&rdquo; &ldquo;I should smile. I&rsquo;ll grow curious as hell. Sid, this heah is goin&rsquo; to be most damn interestin&rsquo; to Gene. You tell him pronto. All about them trucks.&rdquo; &ldquo;You bet. And, Starr, if I get in with Stewart I&rsquo;ll send you the receipt and money to send for my baggage in Los Angeles.&rdquo; &ldquo;Glad to fetch it out.... Wal, I reckon you ought to hit the hay. Your eyes look tired. Come on an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll take you to the hotel. Most as reasonable as autocamps, an&rsquo; good.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not much,&rdquo; declared Lance with a laugh. &ldquo;Boy, I&rsquo;ve been in Hollywood for a spell, hobnobbing with the prettiest and slickest girls in the world.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hollywood? My Gawd, what&rsquo;ll you spring on me next? Sid, you are goin&rsquo; to relieve the tedium of my days.... Did you fall for any of them stars?&rdquo; &ldquo;You mean movies? I go every show an&rsquo; sometimes twice.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then you know how safe I&rsquo;ll be on an Arizona ranch.&rdquo; &ldquo;So am I, Ren. It&rsquo;s just swell. See you early tomorrow. Don&rsquo;t forget to write that note to Stewart. Good night.&rdquo; Lance went to bed glowingly excited and satisfied with the day&rsquo;s happenings, and its promise. Particularly was he pleased at finding the ex-cowboy, Starr. Lance thought he would be a fellow to tie to. Luck had attended this adventuring into a new and far country. After an early breakfast next morning Lance made his first Arizona purchases, which consisted of a new riding outfit, and a much needed shaving kit and several other articles. His discarded things he tied in his old coat, so that he could carry the bundle conveniently on his saddle. When he presented himself at Starr&rsquo;s garage, that worthy stared in comical surprise. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo; Sid,&rdquo; he drawled. &ldquo;What you been about? All dolled up. My Gawd! I hadn&rsquo;t no idee you was such a handsome galoot. On thet hawse you&rsquo;ll knock &rsquo;em cold. Say! I&rsquo;ve a hunch you&rsquo;ve either seen or heahed about Gene Stewart&rsquo;s daughter.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope, I haven&rsquo;t. But I needed some clean duds and a shave,&rdquo; explained Lance. &ldquo;Couldn&rsquo;t ask for a job looking like a tramp.&rdquo; &ldquo;I ain&rsquo;t so shore, Sid,&rdquo; returned Starr, doubtfully. &ldquo;Gene likes &rsquo;em tough. Why, with thet red scarf an&rsquo; all you look like Buck Jones.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, I bought the least gaudy outfit that storekeeper had,&rdquo; protested Lance. &ldquo;If you think I should change back to...&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, I was half kiddin&rsquo;. You look okay. In fact you look grand. But no cowpuncher can fool Gene Stewart. He&rsquo;ll see right through you, Sid. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll bet my shop he&rsquo;ll take to you same as I did.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, then, what the hell...&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s it. What the hell will come off if you run plumb into Gene&rsquo;s daughter? She&rsquo;s on her way home. Gene told my mother so yesterday.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, you certainly harp on that subject. Is the heart of Miss Stewart all you&rsquo;re concerned with?&rdquo; queried Lance, facetiously. &ldquo;Hell no! I&rsquo;m jest concerned about what&rsquo;ll happen to you, if she sees you. An&rsquo; in this new outfit you shore stand out from the landscape.&rdquo; &ldquo;So you told me before,&rdquo; replied Starr, dryly. &ldquo;Thet bet&rsquo;s on. Heah&rsquo;s your note to Gene Stewart. I shore hope you turn out half as good as my recommendation.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, Ren. I hope I make good. Now tell me how to find this wonderful ranch.&rdquo; &ldquo;Go out the highway, south, of course. Take the first road&mdash;it&rsquo;s a dirt road&mdash;turnin&rsquo; left. Stick on thet for about five miles, till you come to a bridge over a crick. Lots of green willows. Anyway, it&rsquo;s the first bridge you come to, so you cain&rsquo;t miss it. There&rsquo;s a trail thet follers the crick, on the right-hand side. Hit thet trail, pard, an&rsquo; good luck to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;How&rsquo;ll I know the ranch when I come to it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell! it&rsquo;s the only ranch out there. The house, old Spanish style, sets on a knoll among trees. Walls used to be white. But you can see it from the divide, ten miles away.&rdquo; * * * * * In very short order Lance was out of Bolton on a road that seemed to climb and lose itself in gray obscurity. Umpqua, scenting something out there, the sage and the open, perhaps, settled down to his fast ground-gaining trot. Lance saw on his right where the highway, a shining ribbon, followed the railroad and line of telegraph poles off to the southwest across the desert. On his left, beyond the green willows bordering the brook, an occasional humble ranch, or adobe Mexican house, gave life to a range gradually growing wilder as he proceeded. Ahead of him on the horizon mountain ranges stood up, some bold, others dim. Lance&rsquo;s quick eye caught sight of romping jack rabbits and sneaking coyotes, and the white rumps of deer-like animals he concluded were antelope. Eventually the trail left the brook. Dwarf cedar trees and a line of pale purple marked the zone of sage. Lance was familiar with Oregon and California sage, but neither had the luxuriance and fragrance of this Arizona brand. Umpqua manifestly liked the smell of it. There seemed to be a tang and a zest in this clear air. The sun became hot on Lance&rsquo;s back; heat veils arose like smoke from the ground; the peaks that had been sharp against the blue appeared to dim in haze. Down to the right, toward the road Lance could still see, herds of cattle dotted the gray. The trail, however, headed more to the left, toward rising land, and rugged bits of outcropping rocks red in color, and back clumps of cedars. By noonday Lance calculated that he had covered at least twenty miles, two thirds of the distance to Stewart&rsquo;s ranch; and soon he had surmounted the divide Starr had mentioned. The scene was so splendid that Lance halted to gaze and gaze spellbound. He saw a moving dust line from a car creeping across this vast gray-purple bowl under him which must be the southern end of Bernardino Valley. Rocky areas and clumps of cedars and darker patches of trees relieved the monotony of that range, sweeping away and upward to the mountains that must be the Peloncillos. Then Lance&rsquo;s keen eyes sighted the forested knoll and the old Spanish mansion built by Don Carlos. Ten miles away still, it appeared to stand out with a magnificence that Starr had hinted of. A lake, blue as a gem, shone in the sun, and its circle of green let out a branch that wound down across the gray, to make a wide bend around the rocky ridge Lance had surmounted. This, of course, was the stream he had encountered below. It was a big country. How vast this country must be when here lay only a mountain-walled valley! Heading down the trail Lance thought gravely, yet somehow with exaltation, that he was won forever. He would find or make a home there, and felt that he owed Ren Starr infinite gratitude. * * * * * &ldquo;No savvy. Can&rsquo;t you talk United States?&rdquo; asked Lance, mildly, smiling at the girl. &ldquo;Yes, cowboy, Mr. Stewart is home.&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, cowboy,&rdquo; the rancher greeted Lance, in pleasant deep voice. &ldquo;You got the jump on them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who... what?&rdquo; stammered Lance. &ldquo;Are you Gene Stewart?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, I&rsquo;m Stewart. And who&rsquo;re you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Lance Sidway. I want a job.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fine.... May I ask if you have been recommended by my daughter?&rdquo; &ldquo;No&mdash;indeed, sir,&rdquo; replied Lance, recalling Starr&rsquo;s talk, and suddenly filled with dismay. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know your daughter.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s quite possible. But might not her return home today have something to do with your asking for a job?&rdquo; asked Stewart, with a twinkle in his piercing eyes. &ldquo;It might, judging from the Arizona cowboys I&rsquo;ve met,&rdquo; rejoined Lance, recovering coolness at the fun evidently enjoyed at his expense. &ldquo;But in my case it hasn&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;Indeed? Well, in your case then I&rsquo;ll listen.&rdquo; &ldquo;Here&rsquo;s my letter of introduction,&rdquo; went on Lance, producing it. Stewart opened and read it, suddenly to beam upon Lance. &ldquo;Pard of Starr&rsquo;s, eh? You sure can&rsquo;t be all Ren says. But if you&rsquo;re anyways near as good...&rdquo; &ldquo;Excuse me, Stewart,&rdquo; interposed Lance, hurriedly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not sailing under false colors. Starr doesn&rsquo;t know me any better than you do. Met him only last night! We liked each other right off. He told me you might take me on. Offered a letter of introduction.&rdquo; &ldquo;I see. That&rsquo;s like Ren. Get down and come in.&rdquo; Lance stepped out of the saddle to drop the bridle. Stewart spoke to one of the admiring native lads: &ldquo;Pedro, water him and rub the dust off him.... Cowboy, you&rsquo;ve a grand horse. I can&rsquo;t see a fault in him. Any rancher in the West would give you a job to get a chance to buy him or steal him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Umpqua is swell,&rdquo; replied Lance, as the rancher led him to a seat on the porch of what appeared to be a store. &ldquo;Nels, come out,&rdquo; called Stewart, into the wide-open door of the old building. Receiving no answer he said plaintively: &ldquo;Nels must be out back with my daughter, looking at her horses. Cowboy, you&rsquo;ll have hell keeping that horse.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I see,&rdquo; laughed Lance, thrilled at the intimation that the rancher might take him on. &ldquo;Any girl who loves horses would want Umpqua, naturally. But she&rsquo;ll have to take me with him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Old-time cowboy spirit! I was that way, once.... Where you from?&rdquo; Lance briefly told of his home in Oregon, his experience on the ranges there, modestly enumerating his abilities and skipping the Hollywood experience. &ldquo;Did you ever hear of this range and my ranch?&rdquo; &ldquo;Only from Starr. It&rsquo;d be a great place to work. Please give me a trial, Stewart.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d sure like to,&rdquo; returned the rancher, kindly but gravely. &ldquo;Once I had the best and wildest outfit on the border. But times have changed.... Starr says in his note that wages are no object.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll be glad to work for my board.&rdquo; &ldquo;Are you rich?&rdquo; &ldquo;Lord, no! I have a few dollars in my pocket. And Umpqua. Yes, I should have said I am rich.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sidway, I couldn&rsquo;t let you work for nothing.&rdquo; &ldquo;But sir, if it&rsquo;s money don&rsquo;t let that keep you from hiring me,&rdquo; importuned Lance. &ldquo;Tell me straight. I&rsquo;ll like you the better if you confess you want this job on account of Madge.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge!&mdash;Who&rsquo;s she? Oh, of course, your daughter.... Mr. Stewart, on my honor I swear I never heard of her until Starr raved about her last night.&rdquo; &ldquo;But that might have been enough. You&rsquo;re cowboy brand, all over.&rdquo; &ldquo;It wasn&rsquo;t enough. It wasn&rsquo;t anything. Women are not in my troubles.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t perjure yourself. Girls are always cowboys&rsquo; troubles.... I&rsquo;ll take you on, Sidway, and pay you a few dollars a month till the cattle business looks up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thank you. I&rsquo;ll sure do my best for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did Ren mention he might come back to me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, he did. He wants to. I&rsquo;ll bet he&rsquo;ll come, soon as he saves a little more money.&rdquo; Stewart was interrupted by a sweet high-pitched voice that came from round the corner of the porch, down the lane. Clinking spurs attested to the slow steps of a rider. &ldquo;Well, lass, I never set eyes on thet hawse before,&rdquo; drawled a quaint voice. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll shore hate me when I say he&rsquo;s got yore nags beat to a frazzle.&rdquo; Stewart whispered: &ldquo;Step around and tell her here&rsquo;s one horse she can&rsquo;t buy. It&rsquo;ll be fun.&rdquo; &ldquo;Certainly, sir,&rdquo; replied Lance, dubiously. It was his first order from his boss. Besides he seemed curiously struck by the situation or that sweet voice. As he moved to the corner he heard pattering footsteps. Then a vision flashed into sight to plump squarely into his arms. Lance recognized her. His girl of the campus adventure and the mad ride through the streets of Los Angeles. As she enveloped him, with gay trill, and her red lips came up nearer to meet his in a cool sweet kiss, his breast seemed to cave in. Nels, the old Texan who was the last of his great riders of that earlier and wilder day, sat on the porch of the store, smoking and waiting. Gene seemed to see that white head, and the narrow eagle eyes, the lean lined face, with a rare and shocking sense of their age. Nels must be close to seventy now. And all the West showed its life and havoc under that mild mask of tranquillity. &ldquo;Wal, you&rsquo;re late, Gene, an&rsquo; come draggin&rsquo; along like you was on a bogged hawse,&rdquo; remarked Nels. &ldquo;Yes, old-timer,&rdquo; replied Gene, wearily, as he sat down on the porch, a folded newspaper in his hand. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s on yore chest?&rdquo; &ldquo;Things have gone from bad to worse, Nels.&rdquo; &ldquo;Heahed from Majesty?&rdquo; queried the old cowman, eagerly. &ldquo;Letter and telegram to me. More for Madeline.... Madge is on her way home. For good!&rdquo; &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t say?&mdash;Aw! Then nothin&rsquo; can be bad,&rdquo; replied Nels, settling back with an air of beautiful relief. &ldquo;Bad news from Madge, Nels. But let that go for the moment. There are lesser evils. Lawson has gone into bankruptcy. No hope of the money he owes me. I had banked on that. My creditors are pressing. Money must be raised.&rdquo; &ldquo;Any better market for cattle?&rdquo; &ldquo;Gone down to thirty dollars on the hoof.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, I reckon I&rsquo;d sell.&rdquo; &ldquo;All the herd?&rdquo; queried Gene, in surprise. Nels would be the last cattleman to sacrifice all his stock.
