There was not in Arizona a keener judge of matters pertaining to cattle. &ldquo;Every horn an&rsquo; hide, Gene.&rdquo; &ldquo;But that is an unheard of thing for a rancher to do,&rdquo; protested Stewart. &ldquo;Shore. How aboot these times? Onheard of, ain&rsquo;t they? Never in my life have I seen the like. Lookin&rsquo; far ahaid, Gene, I&rsquo;ll predict thet the day of the big cattleman is over.&rdquo; &ldquo;Unthinkable!&rdquo; ejaculated Stewart. The idea somehow hurt him. &ldquo;What warrant have you for such a prediction?&rdquo; &ldquo;Government interference, sure as shootin&rsquo;. Then the range land grows less an&rsquo; less every year. Last an&rsquo; wust, we already have Argentine meat comin&rsquo; heah to the U. S., cheaper than we can raise it. Gene, we&rsquo;re in for bum years. I&rsquo;ve got a hunch.&rdquo; &ldquo;I always respected your hunches, Nels,&rdquo; replied Stewart, testily. &ldquo;But this seems preposterous.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gene, jest how bad in debt air you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t the nerve to figure it up,&rdquo; replied Stewart, evasively. &ldquo;Wal, if you sold oot at thirty you could pay up, an&rsquo; then tide over ontil good times come again. If we live thet long!&rdquo; &ldquo;I might consider selling half my stock,&rdquo; rejoined Stewart, thoughtfully. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re the boss. An&rsquo; you asked my opinion. I forgot to tell you thet Danny Mains rode up today. He ain&rsquo;t makin&rsquo; oot with his cattle raisin&rsquo;. Been losin&rsquo; too many steers. An&rsquo; Danny is afeared thet the Mexicans air doin&rsquo; the stealin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;But how could a few Mexicans, even if they were crooked, get rid of cattle without leaving any sign?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know, Gene. But there&rsquo;s shore somethin&rsquo; doin&rsquo;. All of Bonita&rsquo;s relatives cain&rsquo;t be good. Some of them won&rsquo;t be good until they&rsquo;re daid. Danny&rsquo;s got a fine wife in Bonita, an&rsquo; shore a dandy girl in their daughter, young Bonita. But thet&rsquo;s aboot all. And he&rsquo;s scared of her relatives. Asked me plumb oot what to do?&rdquo; &ldquo;And what&rsquo;d you say, Nels?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I told him to sell. An&rsquo; when he bucked on thet I advised him to throw in with you. Then if you hired a couple of good cow hands we could beat this game. At least the stealin&rsquo; end. Thet&rsquo;s the profit-eatin&rsquo; cussed part of it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not a bad suggestion, Nels. But what&rsquo;d we pay hired cow hands with?&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, shucks, Gene! It cain&rsquo;t be thet bad with you,&rdquo; complained Nels, plaintively. COLLEGE CAMPUS RIOT CO-ED EXPELLED FOR INCITING RIOT BETWEEN STUDENTS AND POLICE &rdquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll be... !&rdquo; he ejaculated, jerking up his fine white head. Gene had seen those blue eyes flash fire many a time, though hardly ever like this. Nels divined the truth and his affections were attacked. &ldquo;Yes, I&rsquo;m damn sorry to say. But read what it says, Nels.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw!&rdquo; Gene watched that fine lined face as Nels laboriously read the half column in the newspaper. He had seen Nels face death many times and deal it often, with never a gray shade creep over his features nor a convulsive quiver, such as were visible now for a fleeting instant. And he remembered that it was Nels who loved Madge as well as her own father, it was Nels who had long years ago named the imperious child Majesty, who had put her upon a horse and taught her to ride. Nels folded up the newspaper and handed it back. &ldquo;Gene, I&rsquo;d give somethin&rsquo; to throw a gun on the cuss who wrote thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nonsense, Nels. Are you crazy? This is 1932.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why hell yes! And there&rsquo;s more shootin&rsquo; in the U. S. now than when we come first to this range, thirty years ago for you an&rsquo; more for me.... He&rsquo;s a damn liar, Gene.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who? The writer of this article?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. I don&rsquo;t believe a word of thet dirty part. Aboot her bein&rsquo; wild, rich, an&rsquo; as hard a drinker as she was a speed demon. Gene, don&rsquo;t you believe thet an&rsquo; fer Gawd&rsquo;s sake, don&rsquo;t tell Madeline.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sort of sunk, Nels. Kind of a last blow. I don&rsquo;t know what to think. Madge&rsquo;s letter admits it. Honest, right out! And her telegrams say she&rsquo;s on the way home to stay.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh! Thet&rsquo;s the best news I&rsquo;ve heahed fer a long time.&rdquo; &ldquo;It is good news, Nels. It hurts, though. Looks kind of like disgrace is responsible.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw no, Gene. Why, Majesty loves this range, this house where she was born. It&rsquo;s home.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know my own girl,&rdquo; sighed Stewart. &ldquo;Remember, Nels, I haven&rsquo;t laid eyes upon Madge for over three years. You know I was in Mexico the last time she came home. And the summer before that she went to Europe.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I have. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m gamblin&rsquo; on her, Gene. Wild as a young filly, shore she was. But good as gold an&rsquo; as true as steel. When she was heah last I had some jars, you bet. I had to figger oot thet times had changed since you an&rsquo; me ran after girls. We&rsquo;ve stayed right in one spot, Gene, an&rsquo; this old world has moved on.&rdquo; &ldquo;Right. I&rsquo;ll bet you we have it coming to us. Madge said in her letter she was having a crowd of college friends come to visit her.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fine. She did thet last time an&rsquo; I never had such fun.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, you&rsquo;re a hopeless old fool. Madge will have you eating out of her hand. But I&rsquo;m her father!&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m gonna have fun oot of you, Gene.&rdquo; Gene slowly walked up the winding green-bordered path toward the ranch house. He had not told Nels all his worries. As a matter of fact he was both overjoyed at the prospect of Madge&rsquo;s return and greatly dismayed. A crowd of college friends! Mockingbirds and quail and robins and magpies were rustling and chattering in the thick pines. The last rays of the setting sun burned gold on the flowering vines and the open weathered walls and arches of the old Spanish mansion. The fragrance of roses mingled with that of pine, and the soft sage wind from off the range. Gene felt the fact that the grounds, the great adobe structure, were more beautiful than ever. But the evidence of decay struck Gene most forcibly this evening. The trellises were falling down; the planking of the porches had rotted through in places, the weathering of plaster showed the adobe bricks. Passing through the high archway at the rear of the house, Gene entered the patio. It appeared a dusky jungle of dark verdure, running water, drowsy twittering of sleepy birds, and odorous fragrance. A savory smell wafted from the kitchen where he heard the servants talking in their low voices. When Gene crossed the wide porch to enter Madeline&rsquo;s sitting room, the newspapers to which Nels had objected did not show among the large quantity of mail. Madeline had heard his step on the porch and had come to meet him. Love for this patrician woman who as a girl had forsaken the East to make his country and his life hers, and pride in her well-preserved beauty and charm, seemed to be strong and moving emotions at the moment when these disclosures about their only child had to be made. Would Madge be another Madeline? There was gray visible in Madeline&rsquo;s hair and lines had begun to show in the handsome face. But the light in her lustrous eyes appeared as soft and glad as in her youth. &ldquo;Gene!&rdquo; she exclaimed, kissing him. &ldquo;A whole day late!... You look tired&mdash;worried.&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, Madeline,&rdquo; replied Gene, laying the bundles and packets of mail on the table. &ldquo;Yes, I&rsquo;m tired&mdash;and worried. Bad news, Wife. It never rains but it pours. Lawson failed, Madeline. Gone into bankruptcy. No hope of money. I&rsquo;ll have to sell some stock. Nels advises selling all my herd.... That&rsquo;s nothing though. I&rsquo;ve got a big surprise for you. Madge is coming home.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge! Coming home? Why? What has she done now, Gene?&rdquo; rejoined Madeline, quietly. &ldquo;Got herself expelled from college,&rdquo; Gene blurted out, knowing that he should have broken the news more gently, but incapable of the guile necessary to spare his wife&rsquo;s feelings. &ldquo;Oh, no! Not on the eve of her graduation? June eleventh.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. It&rsquo;s tough, but maybe not so bad as it seems. Here&rsquo;s her letter and telegrams to me. Read them before you open yours.&rdquo; Gene went into his office, which adjoined the sitting room, turned on the lights, and laid all his unopened mail, and some business papers upon his table. Then he repaired to his room to wash and change for the evening meal. He took plenty of time about this, his thoughts under the dominance of gloom. Presently Madeline called him to supper, and he found her in the dining room. If he had expected her to be cast down he was agreeably surprised. &ldquo;I ought to be hungry,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Most forgot to eat in town.&rdquo; And he asked Madeline questions pertaining to the ranch during his absence. Nothing had happened. The drowsy languorous summer had come and the tranquil tenor of the lonely range land had not been broken. When Gene had finished a hearty meal he suggested that they go into the sitting room and get it over. &ldquo;Dear, it will never be over until you change your habit of mind,&rdquo; she replied, sweetly. &ldquo;You always look upon the dark side.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madeline, this time of trouble has brought back the Gene Stewart of other and darker days.&rdquo; &ldquo;It should not. You have made me perfectly happy for more than twenty years. Loss of money, for you and me, is nothing.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madeline, I could take my losses without.... But it&rsquo;s yours that distress me. All your life you have had luxury. You were born to it. This last year and more you&rsquo;ve been using your money to pad Madge&rsquo;s bank account. She keeps overdrawing her income and you keep from telling her that her income isn&rsquo;t one with what it was. Now through me and that spendthrift girl of ours you must suffer. When the depression hit us you should have told Madge the truth. How much her income had fallen off. Instead of that you never told her&mdash;made up the difference yourself. And she spends hundreds like a drunken cowboy does dollars. That is what hurts me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gene, I expected the shrinking of capital and income would be only temporary. I still believe, as my lawyer in New York assures me, that we will recover. Madge&rsquo;s capital is intact and eventually her income will grow normal. That was a wise provision of Aunt Helen&rsquo;s. Madge can&rsquo;t spend the capital. And it doesn&rsquo;t make so great a difference that her income has dwindled. But now we should tell her&mdash;if we have the courage!&rdquo; &ldquo;We!&rdquo; expostulated Gene, startled. &ldquo;Not much. Why, I don&rsquo;t know Madge since she grew up. When she was seventeen&mdash;before she left for college I was scared to death of her. You&rsquo;ll have to tell her.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;ll be hard. I&rsquo;m afraid myself of these years she has lived away from us. If I had it to do over I&rsquo;d not have sent her away to college.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, let&rsquo;s forget the financial side of it for the present. You read her letters and telegrams?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. Madge asked me to reserve judgment until I had heard her side. Evidently she became involved in some kind of a college row, for which she was not responsible, but which resulted in her expulsion. She regretted greatly that she could not graduate.&rdquo; &ldquo;Was that all? No regret for the&mdash;the disgrace?&rdquo; &ldquo;She never mentioned disgrace. I don&rsquo;t believe that has occurred to her.&rdquo; &ldquo;Same old Majesty, eh? She couldn&rsquo;t do any wrong,&rdquo; returned Gene, and there was a tinge of bitterness in his tone. &ldquo;What else?&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s easy. Tell Madge she can&rsquo;t have her friends this summer.&rdquo; &ldquo;Could you tell her that?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sure I could,&rdquo; replied Gene, grimly. &ldquo;Very well. That will be a relief. For the rest she wired from L. A. she was leaving. And last evening from Yuma. Gene, don&rsquo;t excite yourself over probabilities. The fact seems that at that rate she may get here tomorrow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madeline, it&mdash;it&rsquo;ll be so wonderful to see her again that I almost don&rsquo;t care what she&rsquo;s done,&rdquo; replied Gene with emotion. &ldquo;Gene, she&rsquo;s our problem. She&rsquo;s a composite of you and me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madeline, not much of me?&rdquo; implored Gene. &ldquo;A very good deal of you, her father.&rdquo; &ldquo;Suppose she inherited some of that wild blood of mine?&rdquo; ejaculated Gene, aghast. &ldquo;If it hadn&rsquo;t been for that, there might have never been any Madge, darling.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lord!&mdash;I always said Madge had your beauty, your sweetness, your intelligence. But if she&rsquo;s got my old devil in her, too&mdash;come out in these modern days of freedom for women&mdash;what, Maddie, what on earth can we do?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know, Gene. Love her, trust her. Make her love her home. Let us agree on that right here, Gene.&rdquo; &ldquo;I promise, Madeline. But I&rsquo;m scared.&rdquo; &ldquo;So am I. But not the way you are. I&rsquo;m scared of a crowd of young college people, just freed from cramping restrictions, let loose upon us here.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madeline, do you remember your young crowd&mdash;that you had come out here from New York just after you bought this place?&rdquo; &ldquo;Helen!&mdash;Why, Maddie, you&rsquo;re crazy,&rdquo; protested Gene. &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t the slightest doubt of that,&rdquo; growled Gene. &ldquo;Perhaps no other kind could ever win Madge.... My husband, why do you always disparage yourself so bitterly?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve failed as a rancher. After raising a herd of eighty thousand head.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, I find it sweet too, Madeline. The past would be enough for me. But there is you&mdash;our home&mdash;and now Madge to think of.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gene, it will all come out right.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sure it will, dear. I&rsquo;m an old croaker. Wish I could be like Nels.... You&rsquo;ll want to read your mail. And I&rsquo;ve a lot of papers to look over.&rdquo; Gene left his wife, conscious of a sense of guilt and remorse. He had not told her all. The deal with Lawson had been made to raise money to pay a mortgage he had secretly placed upon the ranch. Gene had meant to confess this, but could not bring himself to it. Nels had sensed that something was wrong, though the keen old friend had not dreamed that it was so bad. It was insupportable for Gene to think of Madeline and Madge losing this beautiful ranch. Outside he walked the old familiar path under the cotton woods that had been planted there before Don Carlos built the house. The sultry heat of day was wafting away; a fragrant incense of flowers and pine needles filled the air; the irrigation ditch in its stone-walled vine-covered confines murmured on musically like a brook. There was strength and help in this environment, and in the solitude that hung over it. But there was no comfort in Gene&rsquo;s confession that he had not been equipped to cope with these modern days of bewildering changes and upsets in business. Nels was a far better cattleman than he. For fifteen years there had been too much money to spend, and he had spent instead of saving it. Then out of a clear sky, like a thunderbolt, had come the collapse of eastern securities, and the bank that had held the rest of Madeline&rsquo;s fortune. She did not know how poor they really were. Sober reasoning assured Gene that Madge could and probably would save the ranch. Nevertheless telling her of the straits he had brought about seemed absurdly beyond him. Gene made up his mind to sell two thirds of his stock, pay his pressing debts and the interest on the mortgage, then plan and plot somehow to save the situation. With a mighty effort he threw off the depression, and went back into the house, to deceive Madeline with an apparent return of his old cool unconquerable spirit, and presently to bed. In the morning there had come a change. Whether or not the anticipated home-coming of Madge had wrought the magic or a vivid realization of the sweetness of life on this glorious June morning, so rich in song of birds and blaze of purple range and golden sun, Gene did not know. A good sleep and then the light of day always worked wonders. Gene found Danny Mains with Nels, having a cup of coffee in the old cowboy&rsquo;s bachelor quarters which had been his home for twenty-five years. Danny had been one of Gene&rsquo;s wild outfit in those long-past prosperous days. His bow legs, his sturdy build had not altered. But Danny&rsquo;s homely visage betrayed the havoc of the years. &ldquo;Howdy, Boss,&rdquo; he greeted Gene, gladly. Danny had not worked for Gene for a whole decade and more, but he always addressed him in the cowboy vernacular of rider to his employer. &ldquo;I was comin&rsquo; up. Nels an&rsquo; me hev been talkin&rsquo; over my throwin&rsquo; in with you. I like the idee, Boss. Are you goin&rsquo; to sell some stock?&rdquo; &ldquo;Like it, Boss. If the price is goin&rsquo; up, as Nels figgers, why by the time we can round up an&rsquo; drive to the railroad it ought to reach thirty-five dollars a haid.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore it will,&rdquo; drawled Nels, as he sipped his coffee. &ldquo;Danny figgers he has aboot seven hundred haid, probably more. An&rsquo; he aims to sell half of them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, with a lot fewer cattle we can keep count better an&rsquo; mebbe stop this queer rustlin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who&rsquo;s doing it?&rdquo; demanded Gene, angrily. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m damned if I know. I&rsquo;m shore afeared, though, thet some of my wife&rsquo;s lazy kin are mixed up in it some way.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. So Nels said. That ought to be easy to correct.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes? How&rsquo;n hell can I hang her relatives?&rdquo; &ldquo;Danny, we don&rsquo;t need to hang them. Just stop them.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; you knowin&rsquo; greasers for thirty years!... Gene, we&rsquo;re growin&rsquo; dotty in our old age.&rdquo; &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s pull out of it, Danny.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll drink on thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, I can see you&rsquo;ve got it all figured out for us. Spring it pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hire two good cowboys, eh? Where? How? What with?&rdquo; queried Gene, spreading wide his hands. &ldquo;Sech ain&rsquo;t to be had, Boss,&rdquo; declared Danny, hopelessly. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ve an idee,&rdquo; went on Nels. &ldquo;Gene, you an&rsquo; Danny open the store while I clean up heah.&rdquo; Gene took the key with its buckskin string attached, and accompanied by Danny went out by the long-deserted bunkhouse, across the green toward the store. He could hear the whistling of Madge&rsquo;s horses beyond the corrals. &ldquo;Danny, I&rsquo;d closed up this store long ago but for Nels,&rdquo; said Gene. &ldquo;Aw, Boss, you can&rsquo;t do thet. Why, it&rsquo;d kill the old feller. An&rsquo; the store ain&rsquo;t runnin&rsquo; at a loss, is it? All the Mexicans deal with Nels.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, and they owe him plenty. He must restock. And I just can&rsquo;t go deeper in debt.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell no! We&rsquo;ll do somethin&rsquo;, Boss. I&rsquo;m afraid we&rsquo;re down in the mouth. As if I didn&rsquo;t hev enough to pester me without thet girl of mine!&rdquo; &ldquo;Bonita?&rdquo; queried Gene, quickly, with a chord of sympathy. &ldquo;Yes, Bonita. Boss, I&rsquo;m damn ashamed to confess it, but I&rsquo;m afeared she&rsquo;s a no-good little hussy. After all your wife has done for Bonita&mdash;educatin&rsquo; her&mdash;makin&rsquo; a lady out of her&mdash;why, she&rsquo;s jest cussed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Danny, what do you mean?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, I remember, Danny, old pard. It&rsquo;s tough sledding now for us old boys, who can&rsquo;t figure the present and this younger generation. ... I&rsquo;ve a daughter of my own, Danny. An&rsquo; she&rsquo;s due home today or tomorrow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Majesty comin&rsquo;? Aw, thet&rsquo;s grand! Why, Boss, she&rsquo;ll put the life in us. I&rsquo;m sure glad, Boss. This time you gotta make her stay home.&rdquo; &ldquo;Make her?&mdash;Danny, didn&rsquo;t you just admit you couldn&rsquo;t do anything with Bonita?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sure. But what the hell has thet got to do with Madge?&rdquo; &ldquo;I suspect these girls are precisely the same.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lord help us, Boss!&rdquo; Gene unlocked the rickety door of the old supply store and threw it open. The shelves were almost bare. Some print goods, gaudy in color, and glass jars of pink and yellow candy, and gewgaws for children, and a spare supply of tobacco and cigarettes were about all the stock left for Nels. In the wintertime he sat beside the old stove, to smoke his pipe, and feed billets of wood to the fire; and to talk about the past when, at rare intervals, somebody dropped in. &ldquo;Always makes me think we&rsquo;re living in the past,&rdquo; said Gene, coming out to join Danny. &ldquo;Aw, Boss, don&rsquo;t talk as if it was all over,&rdquo; returned Danny. &ldquo;We got a future.&rdquo; At that juncture Nels appeared behind them, his free clinking stride belying his white locks. As he was about to step up on the porch he halted, his keen blue gaze fixed beyond the village, far down on the range. &ldquo;Look!&rdquo; Gene sighted a streak of yellow dust tailing out behind a motorcar. His heart swelled up in his throat to check his utterance. &ldquo;Car. Comin&rsquo; hell-bent fer election!&mdash;Boss, doesn&rsquo;t thet remind you of Link Stevens when he used to drive Madeline&rsquo;s white car across thet sage flat?&rdquo; &ldquo;I should smile. Makin&rsquo; seventy miles an hour. On thet road. My Gawd, some fellers have nerve! He&rsquo;s young an&rsquo; don&rsquo;t give a damn fer his life!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wonder who it can be?&rdquo; queried Gene, under his breath. &ldquo;Important telegrams, I&rsquo;ll bet. Hope it&rsquo;s not bad news. Nels, have you a field glass handy?&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t need none, Gene. Thet&rsquo;s Majesty!&rdquo; rang out Nels. &ldquo;Both.... Look at thet car streak along! Gene, it shore ought to make you feel as young as it does me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Young! Man alive, it makes me a doddering old man,&rdquo; replied Gene, thickly, and he sat down to relieve shaky legs. Somehow he knew that reckless driver was Madge and he wondered why he had not grasped the fact at once. At the same instant he had a resurgence of pride in the girl&rsquo;s spirit and ability. She could drive&mdash;she could ride a horse like an Indian&mdash;she could do anything. &ldquo;Nels, what color is the car?&rdquo; asked Gene, whose eyes had grown dim. &ldquo;Color of a coyote, I reckon. Gene, she had two cars heah last time, both of them black, if I recollect.... Dog-gone, but it does my pore heart good to see Madge eatin&rsquo; up the miles like thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss,&rdquo; interposed Danny, impressively. &ldquo;You an&rsquo; me hev a common cause. Nels has no feelin&rsquo;s. I reckon we oughta git drunk.&rdquo; &ldquo;You said it, Danny.&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, you fellers air blessed among men,&rdquo; put in Nels. &ldquo;Both got purty daughters an&rsquo; you rave aboot yore troubles! I wisht to Gawd they was both mine.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss! Did you see her take thet wash? Must hev forgotten it.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m looking, boys, but I can&rsquo;t see very well.&rdquo; Nels had walked to the end of the porch. Gene could make out only the streaking dust-comet, blurred in his sight. Yet that appeared to grow magically closer. &ldquo;Gene, she&rsquo;s off the wust of the road. Be heah in a few shakes of a lamb&rsquo;s tail.... It&rsquo;s a low open car&mdash;shiny&mdash;with a long front&mdash;a nose like them staghounds Madeline used to have.&rdquo; Presently car and rising dust disappeared under the slope. &ldquo;Heah thet drone? All the same airplane,&rdquo; shouted Danny. Gene heard and thought that his ears had never drunk in such sweet music. Madge&mdash;his kid&mdash;his little girl&mdash;his second Madeline&mdash;come home for good! He heard Danny babbling in his old cowboy manner, and then Nels let out a &ldquo;Kiyi!&rdquo; The drone gave place to hum and then a mellow roar. Then like a flash a tan car shot into sight, passed the village, to turn left at the fork of the road, and speed out of sight up the knoll into the green foliage. &ldquo;Shore, I oughta reckoned on thet,&rdquo; said Nels to himself. &ldquo;Boss, I&rsquo;ll be waitin&rsquo; home when you come down,&rdquo; added Danny. &ldquo;Welcome Majesty home fer me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, I think I&rsquo;ll go up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I should smile.... Tell her my heart ain&rsquo;t as strong as it used to be,&rdquo; drawled Nels, with a hand on his breast. That jest in earnestness troubled Gene Stewart as he made his way up the knoll. The years were flying by. This home-coming of his daughter seemed to mark an epoch in his life and Nels&rsquo;, too. The old cowboy had no kin; he did not remember his age and he could not have worshiped Madge more if she had been his own. Gene fought a disloyal and disturbing thought about Madge. If she turned out to be wild and flighty, undutiful!... But he conquered the incipient fear. As a child she had been warmhearted, loving, imperious and willful as her mother had been. Gene expected to find Madge bewildering, and he walked slowly up the shady path, seeking to prepare himself for he knew not what. His steps, however, led him inevitably up to the house, through the great arch into the patio and on toward the east wing. Before he stepped into the flagstone corridor he heard a strange voice, swift and high-pitched, sweet and happy. That would be Madge. She was with her mother in the living room. Gene took some long strides to reach the wide doorway. He saw Madeline in her big armchair with the girl on her lap. &ldquo;... Mom, darling, I am wild with joy to be home. I have forgotten nothing. I am drunk with the sage. I am....&rdquo; And then Gene stepped into the room. They heard his step. The girl raised a lovely face, flushed and radiant, with great violet eyes that were wet and dim. Gene knew her, yet he did not know her. This Madge had golden hair. &ldquo;Yes&mdash;if you are&mdash;Madge,&rdquo; he replied, a little huskily. &ldquo;Madge, is&mdash;is it really you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Dearest, the years leave their marks,&rdquo; replied Madeline, her voice not quite steady. &ldquo;I think he&rsquo;s handsomer. Can you find me a lover like him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Lord forbid, Madge!&rdquo; laughed Gene. &ldquo;Now stand away and let me look at you.&rdquo; She revolved for his inspection, like the models in fashion shows, and from the crown of her golden head to her suede shoes she appeared to be the ultimate in grace and beauty, in vivid and intense pulsing life. Then her eyes, wide upon him, brought back the child and the girl to prove this lovely young woman his own Madge. It was a profound and moving moment for Gene. &ldquo;Mom said almost the same,&rdquo; rejoined Madge, with a laugh. &ldquo;Both of you have forgotten your darling.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not much,&rdquo; said Gene. &ldquo;Madge, once your hair was chestnut, like mine before it darkened,&rdquo; added her mother. &ldquo;Well, honey bunches, we will waive that question. But really I am disappointed. I was sure you&rsquo;d fall for me hard.&rdquo; &ldquo;Daughter, if you are one hundredth as good as you are lovely, I shall be the happiest father in all the West.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dubious, but eminently satisfactory.&rdquo; Then with striking suddenness she changed from gay to grave. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s get it over, my darling Mom and Dad.&rdquo; It struck Gene that she addressed both of them but looked at him with eyes no man on earth could have doubted. &ldquo;You had my letters and wires. I hope you did not see the L. A. papers.... I was expelled from college, in disgrace. It was hateful&mdash;the publicity. I&rsquo;m sorry I couldn&rsquo;t graduate, for your sakes. For mine, I don&rsquo;t care in the least. I learned all they gave me and yelped for more. I was secretary of the student body and I&rsquo;m a Phi Beta.&rdquo; &ldquo;What does that mean, Madge?&rdquo; asked Gene. &ldquo;Why Dad!&mdash;To belong to the Phi Beta is one of the highest honors any woman can attain in college.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dearest,&rdquo; murmured Madeline, &ldquo;that makes me happy indeed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, what did they expel you for?&rdquo; queried Gene, stern despite his emotion. &ldquo;Dad, I was indirectly to blame for a riot between the students and the police.&rdquo; &ldquo;Indirectly? Does that mean innocently?&rdquo; &ldquo;It certainly does.&rdquo; &ldquo;Okay. Tell us what came off.&rdquo; &ldquo;It doesn&rsquo;t strike me as so terrible,&rdquo; replied Gene, with a reassuring smile. It was certain Madge had no idea she had done the least wrong. &ldquo;What do you think, Madeline?&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge was rather thoughtless and indiscreet.&rdquo; &ldquo;What became of the young man?&rdquo; queried Gene. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d like to shake hands with him.&rdquo; &ldquo;So would I,&rdquo; flashed Madge, her eyes lighting up. &ldquo;I drove him downtown, to a parking place where we chatted. He was the handsomest fellow. Shy. He had no line at all. Oh, I liked him. Made a date with him to meet me right there the next day. But he didn&rsquo;t come, the idiot! Instead, oh, never mind&mdash;that was all of that.&rdquo; &ldquo;If you don&rsquo;t feel badly over it, why should we?&rdquo; asked Madeline, happily. &ldquo;Then we&rsquo;ll forget it. I think you are both darling. I&rsquo;m going to make up for my long absence by loving you to death.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, we can stand some loving,&rdquo; returned Gene, fervently. &ldquo;Are you really going to stay home now?&rdquo; &ldquo;Dad! Don&rsquo;t look so wistful. Oh, how I have neglected you both! But you wanted me educated. You&rsquo;ve had your way. I am, and how!&rdquo; Neither Madeline nor Gene could resist a laugh. &ldquo;You will stay home with us&mdash;at least once in a while?&rdquo; asked her mother. &ldquo;Forever, darling. I&rsquo;ll have my friends come to see me. I wired you to expect a crowd after graduation. What a place this ranch is to entertain city tenderfeet! I&rsquo;ll have the time of my life.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, the ranch&mdash;is run down,&rdquo; said Gene, hesitatingly. &ldquo;Hardly fit now for your friends.&rdquo; &ldquo;But, Dad, it&rsquo;s so western, so Spanish. I adore the atmosphere of years and leisure. Before I left L. A., I bought three truckloads of stuff. Everything under the sun. Mom, I hope you like my modernistic taste. I&rsquo;ll refurnish my rooms, and all those in the west wing. Oh, it&rsquo;ll be swell.&rdquo; &ldquo;Only three truckloads!&rdquo; ejaculated Gene, with a smile at Madeline. He had to laugh. His daughter was amazing, electrifying. He felt shot through and through with new life. The flush on Madeline&rsquo;s lovely face was pleasant to behold. &ldquo;What about your baggage?&rdquo; &ldquo;My car outside is full. And I expressed ten trunks and a lot of bags. They will be at Bolton today. The other stuff comes by freight. I do hope soon. We have only two weeks to get ready for my crowd.... Dad, will you carry in my truck? I brought you both presents galore, when I can unpack. Mom, are my rooms ready?&rdquo; &ldquo;They are clean, my dear, and exactly as you left them.&rdquo; Gene went out in front, conscious of varied emotions. A good deal of the happiness that possessed him was the pent-up delight Madeline had betrayed. &ldquo;Gosh! No wonder this car could travel!&rdquo; ejaculated Gene, sizing up the magnificent machine, new, glittering, apparently all engine. The back seat was packed full of bags and parcels. And there were three beautiful coats, one of them fur. All about the car and its contents reminded Gene of Madeline Hammond when she had first arrived at El Cajon, which was now Bolton. Like mother, like daughter! Still could Madge ever adjust herself to the changed times? She had a fortune but she could not squander her principal. Aunt Helen, wise in her vision, had seen to that. But Madge&rsquo;s income now could not support her present extravagance. Gene thought of these things and many more, in the fifteen trips he made with Madge&rsquo;s baggage. On the last she and her mother met him in Madge&rsquo;s sitting room. &ldquo;Mom! Why should you apologize for my rooms?&rdquo; Madge was saying. &ldquo;They are just swell. I wouldn&rsquo;t change them. Of course the furnishings are rather dingy and old. But I anticipated that.... I&rsquo;ll paint these walls.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll what?&rdquo; asked Gene, incredulously. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll paint them. I bought the paint and brushes.&rdquo; &ldquo;You learned that in college?&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re on, Madge.... I&rsquo;d like, though, that you&rsquo;d run down to see Nels.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels and my horses! Oh, am I happy?... Where&rsquo;s my purse? I don&rsquo;t want Nels to see me such a sight,&rdquo; she babbled. Finding the purse she sat down to open it and take out something shiny on a chain. From this she extracted a powder puff, with which she powdered her nose. This act was performed deftly and while she talked to her mother. But when she took out a small metal tube and began to paint her lips with it, Gene observed that she was careful and quiet. Her lips took on a hue still more scarlet. Madge, bouncing up, encountered her father&rsquo;s nonplused gaze, and she burst into mirth. &ldquo;Why do you do that?&rdquo; he asked, curiously. &ldquo;You old range rider! Why do you suppose?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve no idea, unless you imagine it makes you prettier. Nothing artificial could do that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dad, don&rsquo;t you fool yourself. I just could. I&rsquo;ll make up really for you some day. It&rsquo;s an art.... I suppose, to answer you, that the custom grew popular through motion pictures. The most beautiful stars are those who have the artistry, or do their making up under experts.... Mom, would it interest you to know that I had an offer to go into the movies?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, of course. But it wouldn&rsquo;t surprise me.&rdquo; &ldquo;There was one studio hot after me. It turned out that I had met some official or director at some function, or the Grove, I forget which. He talked me deaf, and phoned the house until I told him where to get off. I was interested, of course. Any girl in the world is keen about the movies. I&rsquo;d like to have taken a fly at it. But&mdash;I decided it would cost more than I&rsquo;d pay.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cost? Why, I read about the big salaries the companies pay their stars.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Dad!&mdash;Mom, isn&rsquo;t he a darling old dumbbell?... I&rsquo;ll be back right away to unpack.&rdquo; She ran out, her high heels clicking. Gene stood there, smiling quizzically at Madeline. Presently he heard the slam of a car door and the burst of engine. &ldquo;Dumbbell? I suppose I am. Poor Nels and I are in for hell.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gene, I&rsquo;m tremendously relieved. Whatever college and city may have done to her these four years she is wholesome and sweet. And oh! so lovely!&rdquo; &ldquo;I liked the way she looked when I spoke of Nels.... Maddie, I think, if she&rsquo;s got a heart, we can stand anything.&rdquo; &ldquo;Be assured then, Gene, and relieved as I am. She is warmhearted. She loves us. She loves&mdash;this home.&rdquo; Madge met him as he entered the living room, and he halted in sheer amazement. She looked like a slim boy. &ldquo;Madge, what kind of a riding outfit is that?&rdquo; Evidently his reaction to her appearance gave her delight. &ldquo;Dad, I have on slacks. Don&rsquo;t you like them?&rdquo; &ldquo;Daughter, I&rsquo;m afraid I&rsquo;ll like anything you wear,&rdquo; he replied, putting his arm around her. &ldquo;Against your better judgment, yes?&rdquo; They entered the dining room, which appeared brighter than for many years. The sunshine sifted through the foliage over the open window. There were roses and Indian paintbrushes and sage in the vases. Madeline had celebrated the occasion by gracing the table with white linen and some of her old silver and china. &ldquo;Swell to be home!&rdquo; exclaimed Madge. &ldquo;Disappointed in&mdash;anything?&rdquo; asked Gene, haltingly. &ldquo;Yes, your horses are okay, Madge,&rdquo; replied Gene. &ldquo;I hope Nels didn&rsquo;t talk too much.&rdquo; &ldquo;He couldn&rsquo;t keep anything from me.... Dad, I&rsquo;ve known for a couple of years that your financial situation was not so hot. Mom told me when I was home last. And of course I&rsquo;ve read about the depression going from bad to worse. Just how bad is it for you?&rdquo; Madge&rsquo;s direct query and the gaze that added more to it were not easy to meet. &ldquo;Pretty tough, Madge&mdash;but I&rsquo;d rather not confess just what a poor businessman your dad is.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gene, it is not your management of the ranch,&rdquo; interposed Madeline. &ldquo;You made it pay expenses until the bottom dropped out of everything.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madeline, that&rsquo;s darn good of you,&rdquo; protested Gene. &ldquo;But it&rsquo;s not so. We had too much money and too many cattle. For ten years we ran behind, a little more every year. Then came the crash... .&rdquo; Gene hesitated, spreading wide his hands, looking from wife to daughter. Nels was not the only one who would find it difficult to lie to Madge Stewart. &ldquo;I get it,&rdquo; she said, soberly, dropping those penetrating eyes. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve always understood Majesty&rsquo;s Rancho was mine. You know, just in a vain and playful way, perhaps. How about that, Dad&mdash;seriously?&rdquo; &ldquo;Of course this ranch is yours&mdash;or will be someday, which is just the same. And a white elephant&mdash;my daughter.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not for little Madge. What do you suppose I went to college for? What did I study economics for?... Dad&mdash;Mom, I tell you I&rsquo;m home for good. I&rsquo;m crazy about my home. It has been swell to have unlimited money. Let me play around this summer&mdash;entertain my friends&mdash;then I&rsquo;ll hop to the job.&rdquo; Gene wanted to find out how many telephone poles were down on the short cut of the line across the valley and over the ridge. From that point on in to Bolton, both the survey of the line and the necessary repair work could be done by truck. Manuel and he met some horses later, and the sum of their report was an agreeable surprise to Gene. Less than a dozen poles were down and there was only one break in the wire. Several days&rsquo; labor, after the new poles had been snaked down from the foothills, would put the telephone in working order again, which Gene saw was important in view of Madge&rsquo;s return and the activity presaged for the summer. Then it would be very necessary to go over the road and make that safe for automobilists. The problem of help occupied his mind. That, added to his other difficulties seemed an insurmountable fact, yet somehow, Madge&rsquo;s presence counteracted it, and made that afternoon sojourn in the colorful and fragrant sage as pleasant as it was serious. Riding back he thought that he could not succumb to gloom and hopelessness. Manuel turned off at the village to get his supper. Bonita, whose sharp eyes always saw everybody and everything, waved a red scarf at Gene, as if he had been a cowboy with whom to flirt. Gene waved back at her. Despite her deviltry, she was lovable. &ldquo;Gosh! there&rsquo;ll be young fellows in Madge&rsquo;s crowd,&rdquo; suddenly exclaimed Gene. &ldquo;What Bonita will do to them! But I&rsquo;ll gamble on her.&rdquo; The rider got down, and introduced himself, saying bluntly that he wanted a job. Gene looked him over, favorably impressed. He seemed under twenty-five, tall and lithe, powerful of limb and shoulder, and he had a strong open countenance and fine hazel eyes, medium dark and very penetrating. His black horse would have been an asset for any cowboy. Gene read a letter of recommendation from Ren Starr, and it was not many moments till he liked Lance Sidway&rsquo;s looks, his words, and had given him a job on the ranch. Meanwhile one of the lads had taken the black horse down the lane for a drink. Gene anticipated results from that procedure and he was tinglingly prepared for Madge&rsquo;s ecstatic squeal. That girl had cowboy blood in her. Then Gene, with malice aforethought, sent the unwilling Sidway in Madge&rsquo;s direction. They met, so precipitously that Madge ran into Sidway and almost fell out of his arms. Astounded, plainly stunned for an instant, Madge&rsquo;s vivid face suddenly flashed radiance. Recognition and rapture were evident in her speaking eyes. Gene, utterly astonished as he was at his daughter&rsquo;s impetuous action, yet did not fail to catch the reaction in the boy. When she ran into his arms he gave a violent start and uttered a gasp. Then at her words of surprise and delight, followed by that impulsive kiss, his face turned a dusky scarlet. It receded as she drew back until he was pale. &ldquo;Yes&mdash;me!&rdquo; she replied, sweetly. &ldquo;You can&rsquo;t be&mdash;be Stewart&rsquo;s daughter?&rdquo; he implored. &ldquo;You can&rsquo;t be Madge&mdash;not Majesty Stewart!&rdquo; &ldquo;I&mdash;I did keep it,&rdquo; he gulped. &ldquo;You did?&rdquo; she echoed, her violet eyes wide and dark. &ldquo;Then you didn&rsquo;t disgrace me by being the first fellow ever to break a date with Madge Stewart?&rdquo; &ldquo;I came&mdash;Miss&mdash;Miss Stewart,&rdquo; replied Sidway, still overcome. &ldquo;I was there&mdash;long before you came. In a car.... I saw you drive in. Then that&mdash;that fellow followed you....&rdquo; &ldquo;You saw him&mdash;heard us?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, I was quite close.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why didn&rsquo;t you jump out and let him see you were my date? He would have acted true to type&mdash;and you could have socked him, too. I&rsquo;m afraid you lost a golden opportunity.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sorry, Miss Stewart,&rdquo; he said, in awkward though sincere regret. &ldquo;But I was&mdash;sort of paralyzed. I never got over it until after you drove away with him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then I do forgive you. What is your name?&rdquo; &ldquo;Lance Sidway.&rdquo; She touched his arm and turned him toward Gene, who had leaned against a post, taking it all in. Gene had no idea how he looked, but he felt highly amused at this clever ruse of Sidway&rsquo;s. &ldquo;Dad, isn&rsquo;t it just darling?&rdquo; said Madge. &ldquo;This is my hero&mdash;the young man whom I told you and Mom about&mdash;who rescued me at the campus riot.... Lance Sidway&mdash;my father, Gene Stewart.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, we haven&rsquo;t been introduced, but we&rsquo;ve met,&rdquo; replied Gene, genially, and he eyed Lance as if he had been taken in a bit. &ldquo;Of course. How silly of me! He just rode in and you...&rdquo; &ldquo;I gave him a job,&rdquo; interposed Gene. &ldquo;What?... To ride for you!&mdash;He is a fast worker.... Lance Sidway, I don&rsquo;t know just what to make of you.&rdquo; &ldquo;That goes for me, too,&rdquo; replied Gene, with a smile which softened the doubt. &ldquo;Mr. Stewart, I am on the spot,&rdquo; burst out Sidway. &ldquo;I told you I&rsquo;d never met your daughter&mdash;never heard of her till I met Starr. How could I guess she was that one?... I did meet her&mdash;as she told you.... I&rsquo;m innocent of...&rdquo; &ldquo;Sidway, don&rsquo;t take it so hard,&rdquo; went on Gene, kindly. &ldquo;I was young once. It didn&rsquo;t turn out just as you planned. But I&rsquo;d have liked you better if you&rsquo;d told me...&rdquo; &ldquo;I did not lie to you,&rdquo; declared the cowboy, with such vehemence that Gene began to feel sorry for him. Then Madge claimed Sidway&rsquo;s attention. &ldquo;I get you, Mr. Lance Sidway. But I&rsquo;ve been kidded by experts,&rdquo; she said, with laughing eyes. She was pleased with his subterfuge. &ldquo;Yeah? You get what?&rdquo; he demanded bluntly, and it appeared that his awkwardness was vanishing. &ldquo;What is so obvious.&rdquo; &ldquo;Miss Stewart, it may look obvious that I knew who you were&mdash;that I deliberately rode out here because of you. But it is not true.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, so you are ashamed to be caught?&rdquo; she taunted. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re a pretty smooth actor, cowboy, but you can&rsquo;t fool little Madge.&rdquo; &ldquo;You may be pretty smart but you&rsquo;re wrong this time,&rdquo; he retorted, and there was plainly resentment in his tone. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s skip it,&rdquo; she returned, without the archness. And at that moment Nels, and the lad leading Sidway&rsquo;s horse, came around the corner. &ldquo;Nels, shake hands with Lance Sidway,&rdquo; interposed Gene, glad to relieve the growing strain between the young couple. &ldquo;He hails from Oregon. And I&rsquo;ve just given him a job.&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy,&rdquo; drawled Nels, and shook hands with the flustered lad. Gene was of the opinion that Sidway had no conception how he was being looked over by the keenest eyes in Arizona. Gene&rsquo;s conclusion was that the cowboy showed up favorably in a most trying situation. When Madge saw the black horse again, her swift reversal of mood eased the situation. Like a true range rider she walked around the black, all eyes, placing a careful yet confident hand on him, and never saying a word until she had made a second circuit. &ldquo;Nels!&rdquo; she importuned, as if wanting him to refute her judgment. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m shore sorry, lass,&rdquo; drawled Nels. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s a grand hawse. He&rsquo;s got Cedar beat all holler.&rdquo; &ldquo;Traitor!&rdquo; she flashed, her eyes blazing purple fire at Nels. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re teasing me. Nels darling, you don&rsquo;t mean that?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, mebbe I&rsquo;m exaggeratin&rsquo; some. But see heah, Majesty. Even if you been away from the range so long, you know a great hawse when you see him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Of course&mdash;if you wish.... The stirrups will be long.&rdquo; Madge swung gracefully up into the saddle and Nels beat the cowboy to her side. Presently the stirrups were laced up to fit Madge and she walked Umpqua across the green square, trotted him up the lane a few hundred yards, paced him a little and loped him back. What a picture the black horse and the golden-haired girl made! Turning away from the sight himself, Gene saw it in Nels&rsquo; worshipful eyes, and then he caught a gleam of the eternal cowboy in Sidway&rsquo;s. Madge sat the saddle as if reluctant to get off, while she patted the arched glossy neck. She was flushed of face. Her eyes were soft, glowing. At that moment Gene experienced the old fullness of love for her in his heart. She was Madeline&rsquo;s daughter, but she was western. Presently she sat up in the saddle, the glamorous spell vanished, and she faced the three men coolly. Gene imagined he could read her mind. &ldquo;His trot does not equal Cedar&rsquo;s, but his pace and lope beat that of any horse I ever rode.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, lass, thet&rsquo;s strong praise from you,&rdquo; declared Nels. &ldquo;Justified, I reckon.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, never go back on your own horses,&rdquo; warned Gene. &ldquo;Me!&rdquo; ejaculated the cowboy, amazed. &ldquo;Nobody can tell me anything about him.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet I can.&rdquo; &ldquo;Go to it.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;ll you bet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, the truth is I haven&rsquo;t anything. Of course I wouldn&rsquo;t bet Umpqua.&rdquo; &ldquo;No? Oh, you&rsquo;re a cowboy. You might some day,&rdquo; she returned, subtly. &ldquo;Umpqua reminds me of something I read&mdash;that an Arab chieftain said. I didn&rsquo;t have to commit it to memory. It just stuck in my mind... &lsquo;If in the course of your life you alight upon a horse of noble origin, with large lively eyes wide apart, and black broad nostrils close together, whose neck, shoulders and haunches are long, his forehead, loins and limbs broad, his back and hip-bones and pasterns short, all covered with soft skin, fine hair, and his lungs wide and powerful, and his feet good, with heels high off the ground&mdash;hasten to buy or trade or steal that horse&mdash;secure him, and always afterward bless Allah for your good fortune!&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if thet doesn&rsquo;t beat me,&rdquo; said Nels, in rapt admiration. &ldquo;Madge, you&rsquo;ve the high sign on us. But it was good,&rdquo; added Gene. &ldquo;You win,&rdquo; chimed in Sidway, reluctantly smiling. &ldquo;It seems superfluous to ask&mdash;can he run&mdash;is he fast?&rdquo; asked Madge, tensely. &ldquo;He can beat your Cedar or any other nag you own.&rdquo; &ldquo;That remains to be seen, cowboy,&rdquo; returned Madge, darkly. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s more,&rdquo; went on Sidway, &ldquo;he&rsquo;s the best cowboy horse ever bred in Oregon.&rdquo; &ldquo;Now yore talkin&rsquo;,&rdquo; said Nels. &ldquo;An Arab is no good to us riders, onless he&rsquo;s a cow-hawse.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, Umpqua can run over rocks as if they were level ground.&rdquo; &ldquo;I seen that from his hoofs.&rdquo; Madge slipped slowly out of the saddle, facing Sidway: &ldquo;No need to ask how you love Umpqua,&rdquo; she said, with a softness rounding the turn of her words. &ldquo;You won&rsquo;t take it amiss if I&mdash;almost insult you?&rdquo; Sidway stared at her, and then with something of a gallant gesture he repudiated any possibility of her doing that. &ldquo;What value do you place on Umpqua?&rdquo; she launched, suddenly keen, vibrant. &ldquo;Value!... None, Miss Stewart.&rdquo; &ldquo;Every horse has a value. Tell me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Diamonds, rubies, gold!&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell! I like you the better for that.... Do you know that you could sell him for five thousand dollars?&rdquo; &ldquo;Humph. A movie star offered me that,&rdquo; returned the cowboy, contemptuously. &ldquo;Yeah?... I&rsquo;ll give you six thousand for Umpqua.&rdquo; &ldquo;No.&rdquo; &ldquo;Seven thousand.&rdquo; &ldquo;No.&rdquo; &ldquo;Eight thousand.&rdquo; &ldquo;No!&rdquo; Sidway&rsquo;s flushed face turned pale, either with anger or some other emotion. &ldquo;Miss Stewart, don&rsquo;t you know that money can&rsquo;t buy everything?&rdquo; he queried, with dignity. &ldquo;Umpqua is all I own in the world. He has saved my life twice. I love him. We raised him from a colt, and all of us loved him.&rdquo; &ldquo;I know I&rsquo;m rotten,&rdquo; she cried, as if forced. &ldquo;But no matter. I love him, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s well. I&rsquo;m glad you do. But&mdash;you can&rsquo;t buy him.&rdquo; &ldquo;I always have what I want,&rdquo; she flashed, imperiously. &ldquo;You probably have had.&rdquo; &ldquo;But be reasonable.&rdquo; Madge stamped her booted foot until her spur jingled sharply. Tears of vexation and disappointment burned out of her big eyes and they glowed and dilated like mystic balls. &ldquo;You admit you&rsquo;re broke. I offer you a small fortune. You can get a start here on the range. Dad and Nels will help you. I will. You can still go on loving Umpqua. You may ride him&mdash;sometimes. You can make me happy. Please, Mr. Lance Sidway.&rdquo; The cowboy gazed at her, listened to her eloquent appeal as if fascinated by something beside and beyond her offer. &ldquo;Really, Miss Stewart,&rdquo; he said, finally. &ldquo;You may be Gene Stewart&rsquo;s daughter, born on this wonderful range, but you don&rsquo;t know cowboys.&rdquo; Madge betrayed that she could not gainsay that, and it seemed a struggle went on in her between realization and selfishness. The latter evidently conquered. Anger at herself for being so little or at him for frustrating her desire burned out that momentary softness. &ldquo;You won&rsquo;t sell him?&rdquo; she queried. &ldquo;I told you&mdash;no.&rdquo; &ldquo;Mr. Sidway, I can&rsquo;t have you riding around here on a finer horse than&mdash;a horse that I want.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;ll be just too bad,&rdquo; returned the cowboy, in a tone which brought a hue to her cheeks that matched the carmine on her lips. &ldquo;You appear to be rather dense. Do I have to tell you I will not have you on this ranch?&rdquo; &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t have to tell me anything, lady. Your father hired me and he&rsquo;ll have to fire me.&rdquo; She looked at Gene with great luminous eyes. &ldquo;Dad!&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, you&rsquo;re unreasonable,&rdquo; replied Gene, coolly, smiling upon her. &ldquo;I need riders badly. Sidway has offered to ride for me at a wage I am ashamed to take advantage of. I couldn&rsquo;t discharge him just because he refuses to sell you his horse. Could I, Nels?&rdquo; Nels showed plainly that he was between the devil and the deep sea. Madge had been his especial joy and treasure all her life, as Gene well knew, and he had always spoiled her. Gene enjoyed his old cowman&rsquo;s extreme discomfiture, but knew he would extract himself somehow. &ldquo;Majesty, I shore know how you feel aboot this hawse,&rdquo; he began, in his slow drawl. &ldquo;But, lass, you&rsquo;re bound to respect Sidway for his feelin&rsquo;s. I reckon you wouldn&rsquo;t be playin&rsquo; the game if you fired him. Shore I never heahed of you bein&rsquo; unfair. You used to give hawses to cowboys. An&rsquo; I reckon, if yore happiness actooly depends on ownin&rsquo; Umpqua, wal, in the nature of things on the range, you know, he&rsquo;ll jest naturally drift yore way.&rdquo; The persuasive cool voice of the old cattleman, the significant content of his last words, spread oil upon the troubled waters. &ldquo;Very well, Mr. Sidway, you stay,&rdquo; said Madge, loftily. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sorry if I was unfair. But I will have that horse.&rdquo; The dark passionate glance she bent upon Sidway had infinite and unknown possibilities. &ldquo;Thank you, Miss Stewart. But I do not want to remain under false pretenses. You will not have Umpqua.&rdquo; &ldquo;I accept your challenge. If you don&rsquo;t show yellow and ride off&mdash;we&rsquo;ll see.&rdquo; Then she smiled upon him without malice or resentment, and wheeled to start up the path. &ldquo;Hey, Madge, you&rsquo;re forgetting your car,&rdquo; called Gene. &ldquo;What&rsquo;ll we do with it?&rdquo; She turned to call back in a sweet high-pitched voice: &ldquo;Mr. Lance Sidway can use it to bed down his darling Umpqua.&rdquo; Then she was gone into the foliage. &ldquo;Whoopee,&rdquo; sighed Gene, and Nels came back with: &ldquo;Dog-gone, old pard! Seems like old times when you fust came to Majesty&rsquo;s Rancho, before thet lass was borned.&rdquo; Sidway had been lengthening his stirrups with swift hands. Presently he turned with pale face and hazel eyes shadowed. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll be on my way. Thank you, Gene Stewart,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Hold on, Sidway. You wouldn&rsquo;t let my daughter&rsquo;s taunt. ...&rdquo; &ldquo;No, it&rsquo;s not that, altogether. I know you didn&rsquo;t believe me&mdash;that I didn&rsquo;t come here on&mdash;on Miss Stewart&rsquo;s account. And under the circumstances I don&rsquo;t want to stay.&rdquo; &ldquo;No, I didn&rsquo;t believe you,&rdquo; rejoined Gene, seriously, searching the troubled face. &ldquo;See heah, cowboy,&rdquo; interposed Nels, descending from the porch with clinking slow steps. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t ride off hot-haided. Air you on the level? You didn&rsquo;t know this was Majesty&rsquo;s Rancho an&rsquo; thet the lass you done a favor for in Los Angeles was Madge?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, I did not,&rdquo; replied Sidway, forcibly. After a keen scrutiny of Sidway&rsquo;s face the old cowman turned to Gene: &ldquo;Boss, he&rsquo;s tellin&rsquo; the truth. Don&rsquo;t let him go.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hanged if I don&rsquo;t believe it myself.&rdquo; &ldquo;Stewart, I swear that I am on the level. I didn&rsquo;t know. It was just an infernal coincidence,&rdquo; rejoined Sidway huskily. &ldquo;Okay then. Let&rsquo;s shake on it.... Maybe your infernal coincidence will turn out well for me and this ranch problem. I&rsquo;ve a hunch it will.&rdquo; Sidway appeared too poignantly affected to voice his manifest relief and gladness. Gene&rsquo;s conviction was that the young man felt too strongly for the mere misunderstanding. There was more behind it. Gene liked this cowboy, and that Nels did also added a good deal of satisfaction. &ldquo;What&rsquo;d you say yore fust name was?&rdquo; drawled Nels. &ldquo;Lance. It&rsquo;s screwy, I know. Bad as Umpqua.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not so orful bad.... Gene, yore supper bell has rung. I&rsquo;ll look after Lance. He can hev supper with me, an&rsquo; the bunk room next to mine.... Whar&rsquo;ll I hev Jose put his hawse?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not in the pasture tonight. Better in the barn.... Say, wouldn&rsquo;t it be funny if we could bed him down in Madge&rsquo;s car?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not so damn funny in the mawnin&rsquo;.&rdquo; Dusk was settling under the pines when Gene mounted the slope to the house. A ruddy glow faded over the peaks in the west. Coyotes were wailing somewhere low down. Plodding up the low ascent, Gene was revolving in mind the events of the day. He had to side with the cowboy against Madge. She was willful and spoiled. That came out when she could not have her own way. He recalled the tone of her voice, her imperious look, her temper; and he shook his head sadly. But what could have been expected of the girl, their only daughter, adored and petted, born with a gold spoon in her mouth, and left a million when she was fifteen? But Gene reflected that despite these faults she seemed to be irresistible. If only one or more of his own besetting sins did not crop up in her! The cowboy Lance was in love with her&mdash;there was no doubt about that, even if he had not found it out yet. Gene, remembering that Madge had always had what she wanted, was heartily glad that Sidway had been strong enough to brook her will. He had good stuff in him, that lad. Gene&rsquo;s yearning for a son, long buried, had a rebirth. If Lance Sidway turned out as fine as he promised he would come somewhere near the ideal Gene had dreamed of. By the time Gene had washed and changed, the second supper bell rang. He found Madeline and Madge waiting. The girl wore white, some clinging soft stuff that made Gene catch his breath. He thought of what little chance he had or Sidway or Nels, or any man, to resist this lovely and bewildering creature. Not a trace of the recent mood showed on her face. &ldquo;... and he wouldn&rsquo;t part with Umpqua, the sap!&rdquo; she was saying to her mother. And as Gene entered she extended a hand to him with a radiant smile. &ldquo;Dad, I&rsquo;m telling Mom about my hero and his wonderful horse. What a liar he is! He found out who I am and where I lived. I can&rsquo;t get what possessed him to deny that. He needn&rsquo;t. It was a clever stunt. Intrigued me. After I fell so hard for him that day he rescued me!... Well, I fell for his horse a thousand times harder. Oh, Mom, what a beautiful horse! Has it all over Cedar and Range and Bellefontaine&mdash;all of them. I wanted Umpqua so badly that I could have murdered the cowboy... . At first I had a kick out of it. Never occurred to me that I couldn&rsquo;t buy the black. But it turned out that I couldn&rsquo;t. Then I lost my temper. I&rsquo;m afraid I reverted to feline type with claws out. Did Mr. Sidway jar me? I&rsquo;m constrained to admit that he did. Made me feel selfish, mean, rotten. Which I was. But I&rsquo;d do worse to get that horse. And all the time I was so mad I had a sneaking respect for Lance, though I hated him. He&rsquo;s just swell, Mom. Don&rsquo;t you agree, Dad?&rdquo; &ldquo;Rather cottoned to him myself,&rdquo; replied Gene to this long monologue. &ldquo;So did Nels, the traitor,&rdquo; she retorted. &ldquo;I am indeed interested to meet this paragon among cowboys. And see his Umpqua,&rdquo; said Madeline, with a smile. &ldquo;Madge, in the succeeding days of trial I might be of service to you. I&rsquo;ve had some experiences!&rdquo; Madeline threw a laughing glance at her husband. &ldquo;Mom darling!&rdquo; expostulated Madge. &ldquo;Falling for Mr. Sidway doesn&rsquo;t mean a thing in the world. I&rsquo;ve done that nineteen times this last semester.... But will I give that big boy a ride? It&rsquo;ll be a kick. And I&rsquo;ll have that horse if&mdash;if...&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, if what, my girl?&rdquo; taunted Gene. Madeline neither reproved her nor showed surprise, but she remarked that cowboys must have vastly more than horses to be eligible for marriage. &ldquo;But I could divorce him next day,&rdquo; Madge flashed bewilderingly. * * * * * Next morning Gene was out early enough to catch Nels and Sidway at a sunrise breakfast. &ldquo;Wal, look who&rsquo;s heah,&rdquo; drawled the old cowman. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, Gene. You ain&rsquo;t got oot this early fer years.&rdquo; &ldquo;Neither have you, old-timer,&rdquo; returned Gene, jocularly. &ldquo;Shall we put it down to our lately acquired cowboy, Mr. Sidway?&rdquo; &ldquo;You shore can. The son-of-a-gun kept me up till eleven o&rsquo;clock tellin&rsquo; stories, an&rsquo; then, by thunder, he rustled me oot before sunup.&rdquo; Manifestly Nels and the newcomer had gotten along famously. Sidway appeared fresh and eager, and having donned his old outfit, he looked a lithe and striking rider. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t call me Mr. Stewart,&rdquo; Gene replied to his greeting. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m Gene, or Boss, or Stewart.&rdquo; &ldquo;Okay, Boss. I sure appreciate falling in with you. And I&rsquo;m asking if I may have the day on my own.&rdquo; &ldquo;On your own? What do you mean?&rdquo; inquired Gene, puzzled. &ldquo;If you give me the day, I&rsquo;m pretty sure I can tell you where your cattle have been rustled lately.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gene, he made some such crack as thet to me,&rdquo; drawled Nels. &ldquo;Jest young hot blood. But I don&rsquo;t know.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sidway, are you hinting that you can find out what Nels and I and Danny Mains couldn&rsquo;t?&rdquo; &ldquo;No, I&rsquo;m not hinting. I&rsquo;m telling you,&rdquo; replied the cowboy, with an engaging smile. &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t lack nerve,&rdquo; returned Gene, shortly. &ldquo;Boss, I don&rsquo;t mean to be fresh. I just think you men have been hunting for rustlers in an old-fashioned way.&rdquo; &ldquo;Old-fashioned?&rdquo; echoed Gene, while Nels ha-ha&rsquo;d vociferously. &ldquo;Listen, young man, rustling is rustling. Cattle don&rsquo;t fly. They have to be driven. On their hoofs. And hoofs leave tracks.&rdquo; &ldquo;Only so far. I&rsquo;ll bet you tracked yours as far as a macadamized road, and no farther.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, that&rsquo;s true. Or I should say, Jose and Manuel tracked them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then what?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal,&rdquo; interposed Nels, &ldquo;Them two riders split an&rsquo; rode east and west fer twenty-odd miles, an&rsquo; never found the place where them hoof tracks left thet highway.&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell!&rdquo; ejaculated Sidway, clapping his hands. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s exactly what I wanted to be sure of. Saves me the trouble.&rdquo; &ldquo;Of what?&rdquo; &ldquo;Listen, son,&rdquo; returned Nels, his drawl more pronounced than ever, and very patient. &ldquo;Shore you&rsquo;re talkin&rsquo; to a couple of old cowmen, oot of date, an&rsquo; I reckon pretty dumb, as you youngsters say. Will you talk a language we know? These heah modern days air hell on speed, shore, but cattle cain&rsquo;t be drove on a cement road fer hundreds of miles.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dog-gone!&rdquo; complained Nels, turning to Gene. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I was kinda takin&rsquo; to this lad.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, he&rsquo;s got something on us,&rdquo; declared Gene. &ldquo;See here, Lance, just how were my cattle driven along the highway?&rdquo; &ldquo;Simple as a, b, c.&mdash;In trucks.&rdquo; Nels swore, and dropped one of his galvanized utensils on the floor. &ldquo;Gene, it&rsquo;s shore simple. But we&rsquo;d never guess such two-bit rustlin&rsquo; of twenty or forty-odd haid would be done thet way.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, I&rsquo;ll be damned. Sidway, how&rsquo;d you find that out?&rdquo; added Gene. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve seen inside one of these cattle trucks. Fact is I drove one of them,&rdquo; explained the young man. &ldquo;Yes? Wal, I reckon you&rsquo;ll be tellin&rsquo; us next you&rsquo;re one of these newfangled rustlers,&rdquo; drawled Nels, dryly. &ldquo;I might have been without knowing, if the truck had been loaded.... On the way over here to Arizona I went broke at Douglas. Hung around for any kind of a job. Well, it came along, and it was to drive a big canvas-covered truck to Tucson. I was paid a hundred bucks, and told to expect to be held up somewhere along the road. I was held up all right, a little ways out of Tucson, by a gang who expected just what I&rsquo;d figured out&mdash;that the truck was full of booze. But it wasn&rsquo;t&mdash;and were they burned up? These highjackers corroborated my suspicions.... All right now. To come short with it, by figuring, and asking questions I found out that a string of trucks go through Douglas east about once in six weeks. Presumably they go east loaded with bootleg liquor and come back west loaded with bought or stolen cattle. My hunch is that this gang used to buy cattle for a blind to their real operations, but finally turned to rustling. Just easy money!... On the other hand it may be that only one of the trucks is loaded with booze, and possibly it may come west, along with the cattle. We can safely gamble, however, on this&mdash;that your cattle have been stolen in this manner.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, what air we comin&rsquo; to?&rdquo; ejaculated Nels, scratching his white head. &ldquo;Gene, shore we can look forward to be robbed by airplanes next.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fake rustling, likely,&rdquo; added Gene. &ldquo;What&rsquo;d the money for a few cattle mean to these bootleggers?&rdquo; &ldquo;Fork your horse and hit&mdash;No, let Umpqua rest. Tell Jose to saddle Range for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m on my way,&rdquo; replied the cowboy, and he strode out of Nels&rsquo; bunkhouse. Presently he passed off the porch carrying his saddle and accessories. Nels went on wiping his utensils. Presently Gene said: &ldquo;What do you know about that?&rdquo; &ldquo;Gene, that feller proves how much old-time cowboys like us want to figger on new angles. Since the war, you know, we&rsquo;ve jest been goin&rsquo; back. Smart, this boy Sidway. If we only had Ren oot here now!&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. We know Ren Starr,&rdquo; replied Gene, ponderingly. &ldquo;Gene, did you ever know me to be fooled aboot a cowboy?&rdquo; &ldquo;You mean his being straight?... I can&rsquo;t remember one, Nels.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if this Oregon lad can ride an&rsquo; shoot, he&rsquo;ll be a Gawd-send to you.&rdquo; After that encomium from the old cattleman Gene felt himself convinced. He talked a while longer with Nels, went to the store with him, and presently set about an inspection tour of the barns and sheds and corrals, the reservoir, the lake, the irrigation ditches, the fences, and all pertaining to the ranch. He had divined that Madge would be coming to him presently and he wanted to be well posted. This survey was a melancholy task. He saw now why for years he had neglected it. In some degree verdure around the water hid the rack and ruin of the wooden structures. Those built of adobe were also damaged. And in fact repairs were badly needed everywhere. Upon returning to the store Gene found Madge there with Nels. She wore overalls, high-top boots and spurs, and a blue sweater with a red scarf. The mere sight of her flushed and disheveled, and clad as she was, chased away Gene&rsquo;s gloom. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, Dad,&rdquo; she drawled. &ldquo;I caught Nels drinking red likker.&rdquo; &ldquo;She did, at thet,&rdquo; admitted Nels, ruefully. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m darned if she didn&rsquo;t ask me for some.&rdquo; That was a touchy point with Gene, which he passed by. &ldquo;How&rsquo;d you find your horses?&rdquo; he asked. &ldquo;Cedar wild as a March hare. Bellefontaine as sweet as ever. Range was gone, and I learned from Jose that our new cow hand took him,&rdquo; returned Madge, and the dangerous tone of the last words were not lost upon Gene. He hastened to explain that he had told Sidway to saddle Range. &ldquo;Oh.... What&rsquo;s the matter with your own horses?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sold most of my saddle stock. Your horses are fat and lazy, Madge. They need to be worked out.&rdquo; &ldquo;Indeed they do. That was okay, Dad. I thought Mr. Sidway, seeing he is so fresh, might have taken Range on his own.... Dad, his horse Umpqua came right to me. Oh, I was tickled pink. He likes me. I&rsquo;ll have no trouble winning him from his owner. And will I do it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, that&rsquo;d be a dirty trick.&rdquo; &ldquo;So it would be. But I&rsquo;m crazy about the horse&mdash;and Nels said I couldn&rsquo;t do it. Added fuel to the flame!... Besides,&rdquo; she pouted, &ldquo;Nels has fallen for that cowboy in a most unaccountable way.&rdquo; &ldquo;So have I, Madge.&rdquo; &ldquo;I can account for it in more ways than one. Let me tell you just one.... First thing this morning he told Nels and me how we had been losing stock so mysteriously.&rdquo; &ldquo;How?&rdquo; she queried, suddenly intent. Gene liked that instant response. &ldquo;Driven away in trucks. That was a new one on us. It floored Nels. Sidway...&rdquo; Here Gene briefly told her about the cowboy&rsquo;s experience on the trip over, and how swiftly he had put two and two together. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s gone off down the valley to get a line on horse tracks.&rdquo; &ldquo;I told you he was a regular guy,&rdquo; declared Madge, enthusiastically. &ldquo;Yes, you did. But it turned out&mdash;you don&rsquo;t like him,&rdquo; said Gene, casually. &ldquo;Unfortunately, it did. You saw me kiss him. I was just delighted. If it had not been for him I&rsquo;d have had to go to court.... But my feelings don&rsquo;t matter. If Sidway doesn&rsquo;t start something round here he may be a big help to you and Nels. Between the two of us, we will put this ranch on its feet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal now, lass. What you mean&mdash;start somethin&rsquo;?&rdquo; interposed Nels, greatly interested. &ldquo;Nels, you old spoofer. You know what I mean.&rdquo; &ldquo;He&rsquo;s already started somethin&rsquo; with thet hawse.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll say. But I meant particularly what invariably happens to fresh cowboys when I&rsquo;m here.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. An&rsquo; thet poor devil will hev to go draggin&rsquo; himself off withoot his hawse&mdash;an&rsquo; his heart.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels! You&rsquo;re the same old darling!&rdquo; she cried, gleefully as she left them. Presently she turned a happy face over her shoulder. &ldquo;Dad, it&rsquo;s swell to be home!&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh! that&rsquo;s good, Madge. It&rsquo;s sure swell to have you.&rdquo; Madge looked in at the open shed where they had parked her car, then crossed the court and disappeared up the path. Gene observed that Nels&rsquo; eyes had never left her while she was in sight. &ldquo;Gosh!... Nels, I wouldn&rsquo;t be in that poor devil&rsquo;s boots for a lot.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you jest bet I would,&rdquo; averred Nels. &ldquo;If I know cowboys, this Sidway feller will give Majesty a run for her money.&rdquo; &ldquo;Humph! He doesn&rsquo;t strike me as the fortune-huntin&rsquo; breed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, you&rsquo;re a sharp old rascal. I&rsquo;ve relied upon you for years. But let&rsquo;s not get sold on this stranger so pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;m sold now&mdash;same as Majesty is. Reckon it&rsquo;d be a good idee for you to keep yore haid.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge sold on Sidway?&rdquo; ejaculated Gene. &ldquo;Shore. Only she has no idee atall aboot it yet.&rdquo; &ldquo;You romantic old geezer!... It&rsquo;d just suit you now&mdash;if Madge would take to a man of our kind, wouldn&rsquo;t it?... Well, to be honest, I&rsquo;d like it, too. But I think that&rsquo;s only a dream. Madge will marry some city man, tire of us and our simple life here on the open range&mdash;and go for the fleshpots of Egypt.&rdquo; &ldquo;Natural for her Dad to hev sich pessimistic ideas. But ump-umm!&rdquo; &ldquo;Why natural?&rdquo; demanded Gene. &ldquo;&rsquo;Cause you reckon she has inherited a lot of yore no-good blood.&rdquo; &ldquo;Right again, old-timer. I am afraid.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet lass will turn oot like her mother. But I ain&rsquo;t sayin&rsquo;, Gene, thet we won&rsquo;t hev hell with her before she turns oot.&rdquo; * * * * * During the afternoon Gene persuaded Madge to drive him up the old road toward the foothills, where was located the big spring that fed the lake and provided irrigation for the ranch. As the road was rough they did not get back until toward the end of the afternoon. Passing through the little Mexican village, the inhabitants of which had once depended solely upon the ranch, Gene said to Madge: &ldquo;Thanks, daughter. You&rsquo;re almost as good a driver as you are a horseman. I&rsquo;ll stop off at Danny Mains&rsquo; and walk home from there.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh&mdash;oh!&rdquo; said Madge, presently. &ldquo;Look who&rsquo;s here.&rdquo; Then Gene espied Sidway, on foot, leaning on the gate talking to Bonita Mains. Range, bridle down, stood near by. There was no more denying the cowboy&rsquo;s demeanor than Bonita&rsquo;s delight. The dusky-eyed maiden radiated charm and coquetry. That Sidway was not in the least embarrassed by their arrival somehow gave Gene a tingle of expectancy and satisfaction. The car stopped. Gene stepped out. &ldquo;Thanks, Bonita. I&rsquo;m glad to see you. Introduce me to your boy friend.... Oh, it&rsquo;s Mr. Sidway. I thought I knew that horse. How&rsquo;d you like him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not so much. He&rsquo;s cranky, contrary. Spoiled by girls, I expect,&rdquo; replied Sidway, coolly. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s fine. I&rsquo;d rather you didn&rsquo;t ride him.&rdquo; Gene spoke up: &ldquo;Bonita, please call your father.&rdquo; And as the girl flashed toward the house, Gene turned with interest to Madge and the cowboy. She was quietly lighting a cigarette. But Gene had never seen her eyes as magnificent as now. Sidway, however, had stepped away from the gate to bend eagle eyes down upon the sage. &ldquo;Boss, look at that car,&rdquo; he said quickly. &ldquo;Hitting only the high spots!&rdquo; Gene espied a speeding black car appearing to run away from a long trail of dust. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s sure coming,&rdquo; agreed Gene, puzzled. Drivers did not race on that rough road for nothing. &ldquo;Hope it&rsquo;s my mail,&rdquo; spoke up Madge. &ldquo;I left orders for it to be sent out.&rdquo; At that juncture Danny Mains came out, his homely weatherbeaten face wrinkled in a huge smile. Gene called his attention to the car. Mains took one look and then said: &ldquo;Darn fool&rsquo;ll break his neck, ridin&rsquo; like thet.&rdquo; Then he greeted Madge and the cowboy. Bonita, bright-eyed and self-conscious, came out to join her father and gaze down the slope. &ldquo;Ren Starr!&rdquo; she cried. Gene was quick to detect a note of fear stronger than the surprise in her exclamation. Sidway must have caught it too, for he turned a narrowed gaze upon the girl. Then the group watched the racing car until it passed out of sight under the slope. Bonita, with troubled face, left them to enter the house. Gene, attending to Danny&rsquo;s speculation about Starr still had an ear for a byplay between Sidway and Madge. &ldquo;Awful pretty girl&mdash;this Bonita,&rdquo; the cowboy was saying. &ldquo;Swell kid. On the make, too. But am I telling you?&rdquo; retorted Madge. &ldquo;You appear to be. I didn&rsquo;t get that about her.&rdquo; &ldquo;And you such a fast worker&mdash;Well!&rdquo; &ldquo;I&mdash;I like your horse,&rdquo; went on Lance, evidently no match for her at repartee. Her voice had a cutting edge. &ldquo;But you said Range was cranky.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sure. Can&rsquo;t a fellow like cranky horses&mdash;and girls, too, for that matter?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know anything about such fellows.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? I&rsquo;ll bet what you don&rsquo;t know wouldn&rsquo;t fill a book.... When is your college crowd coming?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, you are interested? My sorority sisters, I suppose?&rdquo; &ldquo;No.... Just the crowd. When? The time?&rdquo; &ldquo;That can hardly concern you, Mr. Sidway. But they arrive on the twentieth.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thank you. I wanted to know because I&rsquo;d like to help your father a little. Then I&rsquo;ll beat it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh!&mdash;I get you. Dad thought you had taken to him and the ranch.&rdquo; &ldquo;I had. You see, I just left Hollywood. I was fed up on a lot of glamour gals and pretty boys. And I&rsquo;m leery of a college outfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Indeed. Mine would not embarrass you, Mr. Sidway. Certainly my girl friends do not aspire to collect cowboys.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah! Too slow, I suppose. Prefer gangsters, eh?&rdquo; &ldquo;You can&rsquo;t kid me, Miss Majesty Stewart.... Listen, let me tell you something while I&rsquo;ve a chance. Your dad is swell. A grand guy. And if you were a credit to him you&rsquo;d not have this crowd of yours out here now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh!&mdash;And&mdash;why?&rdquo; gasped Madge, as if stifled. &ldquo;Because he&rsquo;s in trouble&mdash;deep&mdash;without your fast crowd to make it worse.&rdquo; At that moment the humming car sped over the brow of the slope to draw swiftly up to the waiting group. The driver was Ren Starr. As he stopped, Gene espied the tip of a rifle barrel sticking above the door, and in the back seat a pile of duffel, topped with a saddle and bedroll. &ldquo;Howdy, folks,&rdquo; he said, laconically. &ldquo;Heah we all air.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Ren, you look like bizness,&rdquo; returned Danny Mains, soberly. &ldquo;Glad to see you, Starr,&rdquo; added Gene. &ldquo;Boss, you got another new cowboy. Right heah an&rsquo; now.... Ah, Miss Majesty, I shore am glad to see you back home. Hope it&rsquo;s fer good.... An&rsquo; heah&rsquo;s my new pard, Lance Sidway.&rdquo; &ldquo;Darn glad to see you, Starr,&rdquo; rejoined Sidway, eager and puzzled. Gene swore under his breath, and feeling a handclasp on his arm, he turned to see Madge, pale, with dilating eyes of purple fire, close behind him. &ldquo;Wal, you gasoline hound!&rdquo; declared Danny Mains. &ldquo;Back on the job.&rdquo; &ldquo;Starr, you&rsquo;ve more hunch than that. Spill it,&rdquo; said Sidway. &ldquo;Shore. I seen a rider&mdash;stranger&mdash;who&rsquo;d been hangin&rsquo; aboot all day&mdash;go into thet lunchroom. He was the go-between. An&rsquo; then I beat it fer heah.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jump in, Honey Bee,&rdquo; she said, brightly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m too late for my date here and must rush back to campus. Where can I drop you?&rdquo; &ldquo;But, baby, you can&rsquo;t flag me that way,&rdquo; retorted Uhl, getting in beside her. &ldquo;I know where there&rsquo;s a speak close.
