Let&rsquo;s have a drink&mdash;and a chin.&rdquo; &ldquo;Indeed, I&rsquo;m sorry, but I simply haven&rsquo;t the time. I can&rsquo;t even run you downtown. I&rsquo;ll have to drop you at the first streetcar corner.&rdquo; &ldquo;Me ride on streetcars? That&rsquo;s a kick.... What&rsquo;s the dope on this newspaper stuff about you this morning?&rdquo; &ldquo;Damn tough break! I wasn&rsquo;t to blame for that campus riot. But I&rsquo;ll tell you.... Say, Thursday. Give me a ring, and I&rsquo;ll meet you,&rdquo; said Madge, and she slowed up and halted on a corner. Uhl left the car ungraciously, plainly against his will. That steely something about him which had appealed to Madge did not affect her again. &ldquo;Yeah? I&rsquo;ve called you twice and nothing doing. That doesn&rsquo;t go with me, sweetheart. I don&rsquo;t wait on no girl.&rdquo; &ldquo;Perhaps you&rsquo;ve found me a new species, as I you,&rdquo; returned Madge. &ldquo;Sorry. Bye. I&rsquo;ll be seeing you.&rdquo; &ldquo;She&rsquo;d better be,&rdquo; Madge heard, and then she had flashed across the intersection. The dumb cluck! Whatever had possessed her to let him sit down beside her that day at Andr√©&rsquo;s? Madge reflected she was not so much to blame as Dixie Cune. But before she had circled several blocks to get back to the parking place she had forgotten Uhl. Then, half an hour late, she drove to the rendezvous, and did an unprecedented thing&mdash;she waited half an hour longer for this Oregon boy who loved his sister and his horse Umpqua. Not until Madge thought of how long she had waited, how much she wanted to see him again, did she realize that her weakness for new faces, new adventures, had in this instance established an extraordinary parallel. How nice he was! How different! And evidently he had not felt that way about her. Madge suffered a new sensation akin to pique, and that melted into disappointment. Here was one fellow who would not call her up to make a date. He did not even know her name. Why had she not told him and the phone number of the house? Nevertheless on the way back to campus her injured feelings were assuaged by an inexplicable premonition that she would meet him again. Fraternity Row appeared to be more than usually crowded with cars. All the houses had several parked out in front. Madge pulled into the driveway and ran into the living room. All her senior sorority sisters, except Maramee, were there, with two boys from the Tau Phi house. The circle did not, as was its wont, let out a whoop as she entered. They all looked pretty glum and their greetings were forced. Madge now prepared for the worst. &ldquo;That sharp-voiced boy friend of yours just called up, Madge. Uhl&rsquo;s the name. Has he a string on you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I was foolish enough to let him think so,&rdquo; rejoined Madge. &ldquo;You all look like death&rsquo;s-heads. What&rsquo;s the worst? I can take it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Majesty, I just came from campus,&rdquo; replied Rollie Stevens. &ldquo;They&rsquo;ve called a meeting for tomorrow morning. Looks like curtains for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;I met the Dean just now,&rdquo; said Pequita Nelson, reluctantly. &ldquo;She is afraid&mdash;because you already were on probation.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, if you hadn&rsquo;t pulled so many stunts this semester!&rdquo; exclaimed her roommate and her closest friend, Allie Leland. &ldquo;They&rsquo;ll hate to expel you&mdash;at this late date&mdash;graduation right at hand&mdash;and you an honor student. But ever since you were elected in your junior year you&rsquo;ve&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t heap it on, Allie.... I&rsquo;ve been a damn fool. But I had a lot of fun. For myself I wouldn&rsquo;t care&mdash;much&mdash;only it&rsquo;ll reflect on the house. And if Dad and Mom see the papers&mdash;Oh, that&rsquo;ll hurt.... After all, I didn&rsquo;t egg on the students to throw that party.&rdquo; &ldquo;I can vouch for that, Majesty,&rdquo; admitted Rollie gloomily. &ldquo;I did. I collected some of the boys and we went to the President. He was swell. But...&rdquo; &ldquo;Rollie, that was darling of you. I&rsquo;m obliged. If it were up to him I&rsquo;d come through. Mad Everett, however, has it in for me.... How about a bull session this time tomorrow? About my ranch party.... Rollie, be sure my boy friends come.&rdquo; &ldquo;Humph! In that case we&rsquo;ll have to hold your session in the chapel. You couldn&rsquo;t park them here.&rdquo; &ldquo;Rollie Stevens, I said my boy friends,&rdquo; retorted Madge. &ldquo;Am I one&mdash;or just a messenger boy?&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re number one, when you&rsquo;re nice.&rdquo; &ldquo;It was never obvious to me, Majesty.... Then I&rsquo;m to page Barg&mdash;Dawson&mdash;Nate&mdash;Brand?&mdash;Here my judgment, not my imagination, halts shrinkingly.&rdquo; &ldquo;You left out Snake!&rdquo; protested Madge. &ldquo;That muscle man?&mdash;Majesty!&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? I get you. But I like him. I wonder if I have a horse that can hold him up.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;d take an elephant,&rdquo; chimed in Allie. &ldquo;But, Majesty,&rdquo; importuned Pequita, eloquently. &ldquo;Will footballer and cowboy mix?&rdquo; Rollie threw up his hands and departed, and presently Madge and her roommate were upstairs on the third floor, in their light colorful room. &ldquo;Who&rsquo;s him?&rdquo; asked Allie. &ldquo;Him!&mdash;Why the wonderful fellow who slugged the cop and saved me from being pinched.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hadn&rsquo;t heard of him. Madge, you can dig up more romance and more trouble than any girl on campus.&rdquo; &ldquo;Rather a doubtful distinction, Allie, darling. If they expel me I&rsquo;ll have to think about myself at long last.&rdquo; &ldquo;They won&rsquo;t. Why, they&rsquo;d be afraid the senior class would walk out on them!&rdquo; Dinner that night was not the usual merry gathering. Madge seemed the only cheerful girl in the house. She felt a thickening atmosphere of disaster. Her friends knew, perhaps, what she could only anticipate, and they were stricken. Soon after supper Madge phoned to break an engagement and went to her room and presently to bed. When Allie came in and crept into Madge&rsquo;s bed and into her arms to weep unrestrainedly&mdash;then Madge made certain of the worst. She did not sleep well until late. At breakfast she missed the girls. Summoned to the office on campus she went with her chin up and her face tranquil, but inwardly she felt a little sick. She had an interview with the Vice-President, and it was short. The directors of the college had expelled her. They had to take into account the fact of her former derelictions and that she had been on probation. To overlook this last escapade would be establishing a precedent that would have a very bad effect. Madge accepted the decree gracefully, without a word of self-defense, and left the embarrassed official to go out, and cross the campus for the last time. For her own sake Madge did not care particularly. She was tired of college, and traditions, such as graduating and receiving a degree, had never held any significance for her. She felt she had absorbed everything that this university could give her that would ever have any meaning in her life. When she crossed the row she did not look back at the campus, and knew that she never would again. Nevertheless, up in her room alone Madge shed some bitter hot tears. She had loved that room, and to realize its intimacies, its joys and sorrows, its plans and stunts, were over forever struck her with her first deep grief. But in an hour Madge, to all appearances, was her old self again, and had turned her facile mind upon the problem ahead of her. There was trouble out in her Arizona home. Long ago she had sensed that. And it was high time she returned there to take up the burden, whatever it was, for her beloved parents. Madge knew she was going home to stay. A trip now and then to the coast, and perhaps an occasional one East, would suffice her. Before she had come to college the lonely range, the stately Spanish ranch house, the horses, and her lovely soft-spoken, stately mother, and her stalwart father, had filled her life with action and excitement and love. Now that she was a woman, they would be all the more to her. And somewhere there was a man like her Dad... but she dismissed that disturbing thought. Allie came in at the lunch hour to disrupt Madge&rsquo;s concentration on a long-considered task, that of making a list of things to select and order for the ranch. Once given up to that occupation, Madge found it absorbing. &ldquo;Honey,&rdquo; she begged of Allie, &ldquo;fetch me up some food. Anything.&rdquo; A long while seemed to have elapsed when at four o&rsquo;clock Madge tripped downstairs, in a new and striking gown, to meet the bull session. Rollie had them all there, even to the hulking Snake Elwell, whose rosy cherub-like visage for once wore an intelligent expression. As Madge stood in the wide door the faces of her friends flashed expectantly and tragically at her. &ldquo;Friends, Romans, Countrymen&mdash;don&rsquo;t look like that!&rdquo; she cried, gayly. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s over. And I can take it!... I&rsquo;ve asked you all here to talk over my cherished dream&mdash;to have you out to the ranch. Do I need to sell the idea to any one of you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Okay then. Here&rsquo;s the dirt. I want you to arrive on the twentieth or as near that date as possible. There are eleven of us. Allie will come in my little car, Nate driving; unless darling, you&rsquo;d prefer Brand?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ow! Ow!&rdquo; squealed Nate. &ldquo;That is a hot one. Majesty, damn your honest tongue!&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll match you for that honor,&rdquo; spoke up Brand. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re on&mdash;if you got a nickel to toss.&rdquo; When that dire contingency was settled in Nate&rsquo;s favor Madge went on, consulting her notes. &ldquo;The rest of you can come in two cars, that is, by expressing most of your baggage. Jot this down... Bolton, Arizona. And ship your baggage three days ahead, so it will be there.... Rollie&rsquo;s big car for one. Dawson has one. And Snake, whose big bus is it I&rsquo;ve seen you in&mdash;with a red-headed girl?&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, it belongs to Bu&mdash;and I can&rsquo;t get it unless I bring her along,&rdquo; said Elwell, awkwardly. &ldquo;Ha! Ha! Snake&rsquo;s on the spot,&rdquo; shouted Rollie. &ldquo;Well, he can&rsquo;t wiggle like a snake through this field,&rdquo; declared Madge, laughing. &ldquo;Bu who?&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, never mind.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, it&rsquo;s Beulah Allen,&rdquo; declared one of the boys, sotto voce. &ldquo;That number!&rdquo; ejaculated Rollie, aghast. &ldquo;Swell kid, Majesty&mdash;but I&rsquo;m afraid she would disrupt the sweet tenor of our Tau Phi constancy.&rdquo; &ldquo;You telling me?&rdquo; retorted Madge. &ldquo;I know Beulah. Snake, it&rsquo;s up to you. Do you want her, for herself and not for her car?&rdquo; &ldquo;Go into a huddle, Snake,&rdquo; taunted someone. &ldquo;Madge, do you like Bu?&rdquo; countered the athlete, earnestness stronger than his confusion. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll take a chance, and Madge, you&rsquo;re a peach,&rdquo; declared Elwell, in red relief. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s that,&rdquo; said Madge, when the roar subsided, and she checked her list. &ldquo;You can fight among yourselves who&rsquo;s going to ride with whom.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, sweetest, get down to it&mdash;Clothes!&mdash;What&rsquo;ll we need to wear?&rdquo; burst out Maramee Joyce. &ldquo;Your ruling passion, Maramee, for once will be foiled.... We&rsquo;re on a ranch you know&mdash;grand place to wear out all your old clothes. Mostly outdoor stuff, both heavy and light; your riding togs and don&rsquo;t forget a bathing suit. In fact, girls, I want you&mdash;if you have to come without a stitch to your backs,&rdquo; declared Madge, warmly. &ldquo;Suits us swell!&rdquo; yelled the boys. &ldquo;And Majesty&mdash;&rdquo; added Rollie, &ldquo;we&rsquo;re tired of pink teas and cakes, you know.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sending over cases and cases,&rdquo; replied Madge, radiantly. And when the row had subsided she continued: &ldquo;I can supply anything in the world but happy hearts. You must bring them. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been hours on end making up lists. It&rsquo;ll take two full days to shop. Oh, some job! And then I&rsquo;m off. No more dates. The sooner I go the better, dear friends.... That&rsquo;s my say&mdash;for my&mdash;last bull session!&rdquo; She fled in a silence that hurt. Early and late for three days Madge shopped in the Los Angeles stores. At night she packed, with Allie to help, and sometimes Pequita or Selma. What a relief when the expressman took her trunks away! At length all her tasks were done, even to the packing of bags in her car. Next morning when she awoke the sun was flashing on the vine-covered walls of the chapel. With Allie in her pajamas, Madge tiptoed down the stairs, passed the closed doors of her sisters, and hurried to the garage. Her last load of baggage was stowed away. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t make me&mdash;cry&mdash;&rdquo; she whispered, huskily, to the weeping mute Allie. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve got to drive some.... Tell them all&mdash;good-by... darling&mdash;I&rsquo;ll&mdash;be&mdash;seeing...&rdquo; Madge drove out with blurred eyes that did not clear until she rounded the corner and turned her back to the campus forever. An early rising student halloed, but she did not look. The constriction in her throat eased as she threaded into the traffic. It was over&mdash;college and all that went with it. Madge did not deceive herself. All these lovely contacts would sever and fade away in the conflicting tides of new and wider life. Not to press down on the accelerator seemed harder to resist than ever. In one of the suburban towns Madge stopped for breakfast and to send telegrams home. To her sorority sisters she wired: &ldquo;All&rsquo;s well. I&rsquo;ll be seeing you.&rdquo; Soon then she faced the long orange-grove&mdash;and vineyard-bordered road, and here she drove the powerful car up to sixty along the straight stretches. Madge loved to drive, to speed, to feel the impact of wind, to see the narrow thread of road flash under her, and the blurred greens pass by. By ten o&rsquo;clock she was in Banning, where she halted for fuel. The service man paid Madge and her car an admiring gaze. A hot furnacelike breath waved into Madge&rsquo;s face up from the long Gorgonio Pass, as she left Banning. Once, however, under way again, the pressing air felt cool to her. At Indio she had a bite to eat and a malted milk, then leaving her car at a service station she crossed out of the copper sun to the shade of cottonwoods. These green full-foliaged trees, like the pines, were reminiscent of her ranch, and the ranges of the West. Palms and orange trees belonged to California, somehow removed from Arizona. A sultry breeze blew off the desert, tinged with an acrid dust, that felt gritty to her moist hands. Gloves were almost intolerable, and the skin of her lips and cheeks felt drying up. Nevertheless she welcomed this dry heat. Soon she would be across the desert, up on the sage ranges of Arizona, where the air was different. The sun could shine down hot, but in the shade the air felt cool. Then in her beloved home state there was never that copper sky or the blurred distance which she saw now. San Jacinto stood up shrouded in heat; smoky veils curled up from the gray weed-spotted desert; the ragged line of the barren ranges stretched south to fade in the distance; the pale expanse of the Salton Sea gleamed in the hollow of the valley, its salty shore line ghastly and weird. Madge walked a few minutes under the cottonwoods, despite the discomfort. The next two hundred miles and more would be the most trying of this drive. To traverse a long stretch of flat country below sea level was far from a joy ride in June. Madge, rested from nervous strain, went back to her car to resume her journey. From Indio to El Centro Madge reveled in speed and the rare delight of catapulting through space. Beyond El Centro, where she halted for a little while, sunset caught her in the dunes, those exquisitely graceful curves and mounds and scallops of sand, beginning to color with opal tints, and shadow darkly away from the sun. This five-mile stretch was the only one on the journey that she longed to acquire for Arizona. Through that stately region Madge drove at a snail&rsquo;s pace, and rediscovered her love of color and symmetry and solitude. She felt like a Navajo at sunset, part of the nature she watched. When dusk mantled the black lava point of Pilot Knob, and dim purple Picacho, Madge sighted the lights of Yuma and soon the broad Colorado, chafing in sullen flood away into the darkness of a bend toward the south. In a few more minutes she crossed the bridge into Arizona, and drove to the Alcatraz, a new hotel where she had stayed coming and going on her visit home the preceding summer. Her head and eyes ached, and her body was stiff from the long drive. And hungry and thirsty as she was she enjoyed the luxury of a bath and change before going downstairs. Apparently there were only a few guests at the hotel. After finishing a light dinner Madge went out to walk up and down the street and send some telegrams. The night was sultry and warm, with no air stirring, but she required exercise more than comfort. She walked to a corner and back. Mexicans with sloe-black eyes passed her, and a group of the lofty statured Yuma Indians who wrapped their long hair in a coil on top of their heads and set a cake of mud upon it. The glaring twin lights of cars loomed and passed by. Madge did not succumb to an impulse to walk down to the main street. Yuma was fascinating at any hour, especially after nightfall. A long black car had drawn up to the curb before the hotel. Madge, as she passed it, heard a low exclamation, then quick footsteps. A slim hand with fingers like steel clutched her arm. &ldquo;Gold-top, what you doing here?&rdquo; called a sharp voice, cold as ice. Madge knew to whom it belonged before she turned to see Uhl, bareheaded under the electric light, his eyes glittering from his pale face. &ldquo;Oh!&mdash;hello&mdash;this is a surprise,&rdquo; replied Madge, haltingly, as her wits leaped to meet the situation. &ldquo;You alone?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. On my way home,&rdquo; she said, slowly, fighting a confusion of thought. She did not like his look nor the hard clutch on her arm. &ldquo;Home! Say, what kind of a twist are you? Told me you lived in Santa Barbara.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did I? Will you be good enough to let go my arm? You hurt.&rdquo; &ldquo;Come for a ride,&rdquo; he returned, in a voice that brooked no opposition to his will and he almost dragged her toward the big black car. &ldquo;No, thanks,&rdquo; rejoined Madge, as, supple and strong, with one wrench she freed herself. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m tired. Drove all day. See you tomorrow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Like hell you will! Same as phone calls. I&rsquo;m seeing you right now. Get that, baby!&rdquo; He was neither angry nor insolent. His face had the clear cold chiseling of a diamond. Madge was not afraid of him, but she realized that she should be. She wavered between turning her back upon him and asking him into the hotel. The important thing was to get in off the street, and acting upon that she said: &ldquo;I won&rsquo;t talk here. Come in for a moment.&rdquo; Uhl hung close to her, hand at her elbow, and did not speak while they went through the lobby. He steered Madge into a back room with subdued lights, where several couples sat at tables, drinking. Here Uhl led her to a seat at a corner table and ordered cocktails from the attendant. &ldquo;What kind of a deal are you giving me, sister?&rdquo; he began, forcefully, as he leaned toward her across the table. All about him was cold, suspicious, repellent. &ldquo;Deal?&rdquo; she queried, playing for time. &ldquo;You knew I fell for you. I told you. And you met me, danced and drank with me. Then when I get stuck deep you try to pull this &lsquo;I&rsquo;ll be seeing you&rsquo; gag. That doesn&rsquo;t go. See?&rdquo; &ldquo;Mr. Uhl, I seem to see that you have misunderstood me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? Nothing doing, eh?&rdquo; &ldquo;If you wish to put it that way.&rdquo; He controlled what must have been a murderous fire, judging from the instant lowering of his blazing eyes and the quiver of his slim hands. Strangely, this reaction of his again restored a certain fascination he had exerted over Madge, she felt that it was almost an attraction of repugnance. She had had too much adulation, too many at her feet. There was in her, unconsciously, a primitive longing. &ldquo;Baby, I&rsquo;ve gone nuts over some dames,&rdquo; began Uhl deliberately, apparently having suppressed his violent feelings. &ldquo;But none ever held a copper to you. I&rsquo;m horribly in love with you, sweetheart.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I&rsquo;m sorry you&rsquo;ve allowed it to go so far,&rdquo; murmured Madge. &ldquo;Any girl would be flattered and.... But, you see, I&rsquo;m engaged.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? What&rsquo;s that to me?&rdquo; &ldquo;I can&rsquo;t imagine, I&rsquo;m sure. But I know a girl can&rsquo;t accept serious attentions from one man when she&rsquo;s engaged to another.&rdquo; &ldquo;The hell dames can&rsquo;t. They do. They all do... Aw, Beauty, don&rsquo;t be such a plaster. I&rsquo;m crazy about you.... If you&rsquo;re alone here tonight let me....&rdquo; Madge felt his slim hand slide upon her knee under the table. His eyes, gray as molten metal had a hypnotic power. For a moment she felt paralyzed. Then her rigidity broke to a start, to a stinging heat, to an insupportable sensation. That tearing thing seemed to actuate Madge more than Uhl&rsquo;s outrage. In a fury she kicked out with all her might. And her onslaught sent the man sprawling over his chair. Madge almost overturned the table as she leaped up. Wheeling she fled from the room, and never stopped until she backed up against her locked door. &ldquo;Serves you damn&mdash;right!&rdquo; she panted, passionately. &ldquo;Playing with a heel like him!... Are you ever&mdash;going to think?&rdquo; Madge hardly had time to think then. Hurried steps in the hall preceded a knock on the door. Another knock, louder, followed, and the handle of the door was tried. &ldquo;Madge!&rdquo; &ldquo;Who is it?&rdquo; she called. &ldquo;Bee. Let me in. I want to square myself.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll take your word for it&mdash;from that side.&rdquo; &ldquo;I was out of my head. I&rsquo;ll come clean. You&rsquo;re not like other dames.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, Mr. Uhl. You discovered that rather late. But I take the blame for your mistake.&rdquo; &ldquo;Will you let me in?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. I&rsquo;m going to bed.&rdquo; &ldquo;That wouldn&rsquo;t make any difference to me. I want to talk to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, I don&rsquo;t want to listen.... But I&rsquo;ll see you in the morning. At breakfast. Eight o&rsquo;clock,&rdquo; she replied, thinking that might be a way to get rid of him. &ldquo;Okay, baby. But don&rsquo;t string me again.&rdquo; Uhl&rsquo;s voice had an ominous ring that jarred unpleasantly upon Madge&rsquo;s ruffled nerves. Uhl was dangerous. Recalling his bragging, the roll of bills he exhibited, his extraordinary intimation of some kind of power, which Madge now analyzed as underworld, she realized that she might be in actual peril and by no means should she encounter him again. She determined to be a hundred miles and more beyond Yuma by eight o&rsquo;clock next morning. Following that decision her first impulse was to telephone the desk to leave a call for five o&rsquo;clock, but she thought better of it. She always awakened early, provided she went to bed early. This she did at once, and eventually fell asleep. The roaring of a motor truck awakened Madge. She hardly seemed to have done more than close her eyes. Ruddy light on the desert ridges attested to sunrise. Her wrist watch said ten minutes to five. By five o&rsquo;clock she was at the desk downstairs, paying her bill. She told the night clerk, in case anyone asked, that she had received a telegram which recalled her to Los Angeles. In another ten minutes she was speeding east on the Arizona highway. The morning was fresh and cool. A line of fire rimmed the ranges. No cars, no curves on the empty black road ahead gave full rein to her desire and her need to drive fast. Sixty miles an hour had been her conservative limit, except a few times on short favorable stretches. Here Madge had the course and the incentive to increase her speed to seventy and more. The motor droned like a homing bee. It ate up the miles. At eighty miles on the indicator Madge tasted the last complete and full elation of the speedster. The telegraph poles were almost as fence posts. Madge never looked back once. She drove under the urge that Uhl was pursuing her and that she could run away from him. At Gila Bend where the highway forked she slowed down and took the right branch, then opened up again. Mohawk, Aztec, Sentinel&mdash;the stations that she knew well were overtaken and passed by. At Casa Grande, while the car was being refueled and gone over, she had breakfast and lunch in one. From there Madge again faced the waving colored desert, with its black buttes and red ranges, ever growing rougher. But she took little account of it or of the hours. She was bent on a record drive. Tucson for once was not interesting, only as a service station; and the quaint Tombstone and bustling Bisbee only obstacles on the road. Late that day, which had flashed by like the hamlets, Madge drove into Douglas with the most perfect satisfaction for a driving mania she had ever experienced. She stopped at an automobile camp, had her dinner at a lunch counter, and by eight o&rsquo;clock she was in bed, her eyelids heavy. Her last thought was of what she would do on the morrow to the hundred-odd miles between Douglas and Majesty&rsquo;s Rancho. Next morning she was on the way by six o&rsquo;clock, after having breakfasted. She would have made the run to Bolton in an hour but for the fact that she was held back by a detour, and some heavily laden, slow-moving canvas-covered trucks that simply stuck to the middle of the road. They annoyed her so that she thought that she would not soon forget them, nor one of the drivers, a swarthy visaged fellow who glared at her when at last with incessant honking she forced him over. At Bolton Madge tarried long enough to leave orders with the stationmaster about her baggage and freight due to arrive. Then she refueled again, and drove on, beginning to feel strangely happy&mdash;with the journey almost ended, with turmoil behind her, and rest and home almost gained. Madge, surmounting the slope, abruptly to face the vast purple valley, with its arresting black knoll topped by the gray mansion which was her home, and the grand uplifting range beyond, stopped the car to gaze as one in transport. How astoundingly familiar! She had only to see to realize that all these blue distances and splendid landmarks were veritably a part of herself. But absence and life too full for memory had come between them, and her thought and her love, now suddenly leaping to vivid and poignant life again. The morning was still fresh; shadows still lingered under the ridges; every wash and rock shone white; the waves of sage rolled on and on, in an endless sea, dotted with cattle; the league-long slope up to the knoll had a beautiful sweep; and there peeping out of the black pines, lonely and superb, stood the mansion of the old Dons. As a fitting background the mountains loomed impressively, white-slided and black-canyoned, upflung in rock battlements to the blue sky. &ldquo;Oh, darling, darling home!&rdquo; cried Madge, in rapture and reproach. &ldquo;I have been faithless. But I have come back. For good! Forever, to you&mdash;to my home&mdash;to the love and life I shall find here!&rdquo; The moment of exaltation was difficult for Madge, inasmuch as she had a vague realization that she was not big enough, not good enough for this noble country, nor for the devoted mother and father who awaited her coming. But she had a clear conception that if she were true to the emotion this magnificent scene had aroused in her heart, she would grow far toward being worthy of it. That thought persisted helpfully, too strong even for her misgivings. She drove on. The descent into the valley afforded swifter travel. She had left rocks and ruts behind. Soon she was down to a level, on a white road, six inches deep in dust; and here she cut loose again with a shriek of joy, to shoot ahead of the yellow clouds that puffed up from the wheels. The wash in the center was sand and she hit that at a fifty-mile clip, to veer and leap and plunge, the wheel lurching under her strong grip, and roar safely across to another strip of dust. Beyond that the long slope of yellow road was like a country lane, with its two narrow wheel strips, its weedy center, its hard ground. Madge hummed up that, out of the grass and sage, into the pines, and on up the shaded brown-matted aisle to the green oval before the stately mansion. And on the porch, telescope in hand, her eyes deeply dark with joy stood her mother. Madge pressed the brake so forcibly that the great car, answering with a grinding scream on gravel, stopped so suddenly that she and the bags had a violent jerk. Madge threw off gloves, goggles, hat all in one swift action, and leaping out she ran up the steps into her mother&rsquo;s arms. * * * * * &ldquo;Madge! How you rave!&rdquo; exclaimed her mother, her voice low. &ldquo;But it&rsquo;s worth a great deal to have you home&mdash;and hear you talk like that.... I hope it&rsquo;s not all&mdash;let me say, remorse.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, child.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well?&rdquo; &ldquo;I understand, Madge. You need not tell me anything. I went to college once. And all these years you&rsquo;ve been away&mdash;these hard and changing years&mdash;I&rsquo;ve tried to keep abreast of the times, even if it was only through newspapers and magazines and books. Most of all, your letters have told me. I know what has gone on, what has come to pass, even if I have trouble grasping it.&rdquo; &ldquo;My dear,&rdquo; replied her mother, quietly, after a moment fraught with suspense for Madge, &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t think you should tell your father&mdash;all that.... And there&mdash;his step in the patio!&rdquo; * * * * * After luncheon Madge leisurely unpacked her numerous bags and suitcases, stopping dreamily at intervals, or finding some excuse to go to her mother. During all this while the desire to see Nels and her horses grew stronger, until at length it was too demanding to resist. It need not have been anything to resist, if she had been able to put aside the strong desire to change to riding garb, and vault upon Cedar once more. Nels surely would not have liked her in these new English riding breeches, so tight and leggy, and that first day home she could not don overalls and boots. Wherefore at length she went down the old back road, now an overgrown trail, to gray ruin of ranch buildings and corrals. Nels was her second father. He had taught her to ride, to shoot, to rope&mdash;all the tricks of the range. He had told her the terrible stories about her famous father, and that lovely and appalling romance of her mother and all about ranch and cowboy life, even before she was old enough to understand. Meeting Nels proved to mean more, to be a deeper experience, than even her memories had anticipated. The change in him was not indefinable. Madge felt the warm blood recede from her cheeks. The years had told on Nels. Not until she sat with him on the top bar of the corral fence to see her horses did the zest and stir of home-coming return. What a glossy, long-maned, plume-tailed, racy and thoroughbred troop of horses! Cedar, gray as the cedars for which he was named, pranced before her, soft-eyed, whinnying, high-stepping and sensitive, knowing her yet not sure. And Range, the long rangy sorrel, red as fire in the rays of the setting sun; and Bellefontaine, that dainty proud little mare, sure of Madge, poking her nose up for sugar; and Blackboy, like shining coal in his well-groomed hide, and Sultan, the roan, and Dervish and Arab, twin whites, as perfect as the movie horses, but not so tame, and Leatherstocking, a range cow horse, loved despite his plebeian blood, and Pinto, a mustang&mdash;all her own, and the corral full of shaggy jealous horses and colts, snorting and kicking&mdash;all of them brought home to Madge the stinging truth of her return. But it was when she walked up the lane with Nels to encounter a Mexican lad leading a black horse that Madge&rsquo;s emotion flooded to a bursting pitch. A strange horse, a grand horse, dusty and lame, bearing a cowboy&rsquo;s accouterments, in one inconceivable flash, added a bitter drop to the sweet cup which she had just tasted. Screeching to Nels, importuning the lad, this got Madge nowhere. Full tilt then she ran around the corner of the store, straight into the arms of a clanking cowboy, gorgeously arrayed&mdash;a strangely familiar cowboy whose face went white and then a dusky red. As if by magic Madge confronted her campus champion, and under this last straw her burdened feelings burst in glad amaze and gay delight, to a welcome she did not consider and a kiss she could not recall. Could she have paid him a more flattering compliment for his cleverness in contriving to be there at her own home? In the following exchange of words, in this cowboy&rsquo;s well-simulated protest and confusion, Madge experienced a reaction which inwardly she divined was something more, something deeply and inexplicably glad. And that, at his denial, submerged this sweet self. What was the matter with the fool? To be sure there stood her dad, his piercing eyes hard to meet, and his slight amused smile disconcerting. But even so, Madge argued to her ruffled vanity, her welcome, her kiss should have inspired him to confront half a dozen fathers and confess his duplicity. It was the horse Umpqua that saved the embarrassing situation for the moment, and which precipitated a really serious one. Madge left them, furious with the cowboy. She spied upon the group below from the covert of pines, curious even in her anger to see what Sidway would do. There was no doubt about the sincerity of his purpose, when he went to his horse. He meant to ride away. And would have done so but for her father and Nels. &ldquo;Can&rsquo;t he take it, the big handsome stiff?&rdquo; muttered Madge, in her angry surprise. &ldquo;What&rsquo;d he beat me here for if not to make a hit with me? It did!... And&mdash;I was hit before.... Oh, the dumbbell! To spoil it all!... Now I&rsquo;ll have to jolly him along and play with him to get that horse&mdash;when I really liked him... how much? ... Am I burned up?&rdquo; By the time Madge had told the story, with reservations as to her own uncertain feelings, to her mother, her resentment had gone into eclipse. She could not bear ill will. And she had developed a merciless truth about herself. &ldquo;What is young Sidway like?&rdquo; asked her mother, presently. &ldquo;Oh, swell!&rdquo; &ldquo;That is rather an ambiguous term these days.&rdquo; &ldquo;Mom, I&rsquo;d hate to spoil a pleasant surprise for you. Also I&rsquo;d like to get your reaction to Sidway before I tell you mine. I may be&mdash;I must have been all wet, if you get what I mean.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t, my dearest,&rdquo; replied her mother, with a bewildered smile. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;m sure you like him.&rdquo; Madge leisurely changed for dinner, and after catching herself approving of the white image in the long mirror, she had the grace to be ponderingly amused at that part of her thought which conceived a possibility of Sidway seeing her thus. No telling where that Hollywood extra might bob up! She arrived late in the dining room, yet in time to hear her father say: &ldquo;He&rsquo;d have gone, too, Madeline, if Nels and I hadn&rsquo;t believed him when he swore he didn&rsquo;t come here on account of Madge.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, Dad Stewart!&rdquo; declared Madge, in mock solemnity. &ldquo;So that cowboy put it over?&rdquo; &ldquo;Daughter! You look like your mother&mdash;the first time I ever saw her in white!&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, thank you, Dad! Then I must look just stunning.&rdquo; &ldquo;You do.... Madge, young Sidway didn&rsquo;t put it over. He told the truth. Nels believed him. So did I. He hadn&rsquo;t the remotest idea that my daughter, the girl Ren Starr told him about, was you&mdash;the girl he&rsquo;d actually met and befriended.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dad, he&rsquo;s a liar. I wouldn&rsquo;t believe that on a bet,&rdquo; declared Madge. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet you find it out and you&rsquo;ll be sorry.&rdquo; &ldquo;Preposterous! So he put up a big bluff to you and Nels?... Dad, that cowboy is right up to date. He knows his stuff.... What I can&rsquo;t get is why he was ashamed of his clever stunt?&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, it&rsquo;s conceivable that he seemed ashamed because of his innocence when all of you believed in his deceit,&rdquo; observed her mother. &ldquo;Oh, my adorable parents! What you must learn about us! Mr. Sidway&rsquo;s ears must be burning. Let&rsquo;s forget him.&rdquo; Nevertheless, despite her suggestion, Madge had no slight difficulty in conforming to it. She succeeded presently by launching into an account of what she wanted done to the road, and the patio, and the long-deserted rooms in the west wing, and about more servants from the Mexican village&mdash;all in the interest of her guests who were to arrive on the twentieth. She was indeed not disappointed in her anticipation that both of her parents seemed heartily interested in her summer party, with no wish but to contribute in every way to its success. If they appeared a little bewildered several times during the discussion, and at a loss for words, Madge put that down to her elaborate and extravagant plans. They stayed up late, at least for them, and when Madge bade them good night, she went to her rooms thinking how perfectly splendid they were, and that she was happy, and the most fortunate girl in the world. Next morning she satisfied a craving that had long beset her&mdash;that of getting into the saddle again. To find Range gone gave quick rise to her temper, which strangely did not greatly fall when her father explained that the cowboy had merely obeyed orders. She had resolutely to keep her eyes off Umpqua or she would have yielded to riding him again, which pride bade her forego. That afternoon Madge found great enjoyment in driving her father high up the old road, to an elevation that permitted their gazing down upon the ranch and the house, and the flowing sage flats with their speckled knots of cattle. While her father puttered around the boxed-in spring and repaired the outlet, Madge sat at ease, lulled by the heights and the depths of this wide-spreading land. &ldquo;No wonder the lake and reservoir went almost dry,&rdquo; said her father, when he came back to the car. &ldquo;Two thirds of the water leaking away.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I forgot to look at the lake. Will it be full again by the time my friends arrive? We&rsquo;ll want to go in bathing. Dad, is there any sand near enough to haul?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sand. The wash down below is all sand. Fine and white, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell! I&rsquo;ll want a nice sandy beach for us to lie around on in the sun.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s an easy one, Madge.&rdquo; The end of a perfect day just had to have a drawback. When Madge caught sight of Lance Sidway leaning over the gate, apparently deeply interested in Bonita Mains, she fell victim to a most disconcerting irritation. The cowboy, as she drove up to a halt, did not noticeably move, until Bonita blushingly drew back, to come out of the gate. Then he stared at Madge, rather satirically she thought. Was there not a single man in the whole vast earth who did not fall for every pretty girl he saw, regardless of her color? It had not taken Lance Sidway long to contact the little half-breed coquette of the range. Madge chalked up another mark against the cowboy. The succeeding moments, outside of Danny Mains&rsquo; kindly welcome&mdash;Madge did not take Bonita&rsquo;s to heart&mdash;should have bored Madge, if she had been running true to form. But she was off her stride and she knew the reason, and therefore looked at that reason with rather scornful eyes. Her mental alertness quickened, however, when Ren Starr&rsquo;s car was sighted, and Bonita precipitately fled. The stab Sidway had given Madge, along with the look he gave her, silenced Madge, and gave rein to whirling thoughts that refused to recognize her wrath. She nursed it, yet succumbed to curiosity, and got out of the car to listen to the short talk of the men. Rustlers! Verily her home-coming had not been dull. Then the men fell back from Starr&rsquo;s car, Sidway to leap astride Range and gallop off, Mains to run into his house, and her father to get in with her, his fine face stern and dark, somehow recalling her fear of him when she was a little girl. &ldquo;Step on it, Madge.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Dad! What is it? I heard some of your talk. Trucks! Rustlers!&rdquo; &ldquo;You bet you did.... Madge, these boys have made me feel like my old self. Sidway got a line on these cattle thieves. All in a day! Won&rsquo;t Nels jump at that? Won&rsquo;t he be pleased? And Starr ran out with news of a truck raid on our cattle tonight.&rdquo; &ldquo;Truck raid! Who ever heard of such a thing?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sidway got it, just like that. Keen as a whip, that cowboy. And Starr&rsquo;s a running pard for him.... Madge, I have a hunch things will look up for us this summer.&rdquo; &ldquo;I must tell Nels first.&rdquo; &ldquo;That old&mdash;ranger! Oh, Dad, he&rsquo;ll be for guns! horses! ropes!&rdquo; &ldquo;You bet. Madge, before the night is over we&rsquo;ll hang some of these truck drivers and cattle-raiding greasers.&rdquo; &ldquo;I want to go!&rdquo; &ldquo;Nonsense, child. You might get hurt.... Let me out here. I won&rsquo;t be up for supper. Tell Mother.&rdquo; Naturally Madge expected her mother to be greatly perturbed, but was agreeably surprised. &ldquo;This will wake up your father and Nels, too,&rdquo; rejoined her mother, with satisfaction. &ldquo;Madge, your father and Nels and Danny Mains will be a match for all the crooks in the Southwest. Don&rsquo;t worry about them. Rustlers will give them just the impetus they need. And cattle raising will probably be the better for it.&rdquo; Nevertheless Madge did worry. She read and worried and waited until long after her mother had retired. Then when she did go to bed she could not sleep. She listened. But there were no sounds except the lonesome chirping of crickets and the murmuring of running water. This Lance Sidway had certainly injected some vim and vigor into the dead old ranch. His fine eyes, shadowed, troubled, and then blazing with scorn, haunted her as did that taunt about gangsters. She hated him, but she deserved it. Her conscience wrung that from her. Indeed he had kept the rendezvous that day, and bad luck have it! he had seen Uhl meet her, get into her car. And being Hollywood-wise he had caught the cut of that gentleman all in a few minutes. Not this fact, but his scorn was what galled Madge. Still if he had been so burned up by her friendship with an underworld character, why had he learned her name, where she lived, and conceived the brilliant idea of meeting her at her own home? The answer was that no matter whom she knew, what she had done, he must have conceived more than a mere interest in her. But was that the answer? Madge conceived the idea that there was a remote possibility it was not. &ldquo;Wal, I reckon you fellers better have a smack of grub an&rsquo; a cup of coffee with me,&rdquo; Nels drawled, as Stewart ended his brief story. &ldquo;Reckon I hed, at thet,&rdquo; rejoined Mains. &ldquo;My two Bonitas would be too interested in thet confab we hed by the house.&rdquo; &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll all sponge on you, Nels,&rdquo; said Stewart. &ldquo;Pitch in, fellows.&rdquo; &ldquo;All set &rsquo;cept fryin&rsquo; some more ham. You cut it, Danny. ... Whar&rsquo;s Sidway? Come heah, cowboy.... Gene, this truck rustlin&rsquo; is shore two-bit stuff. Kinda oot of our experience. Let&rsquo;s get the cowboy&rsquo;s angle on it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Suits me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sidway, you oughta be up on this automobile cattle stealin&rsquo;. What&rsquo;ll we do?&rdquo; &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll intercept this raid, of course,&rdquo; replied Lance, realizing that he was on the spot and forcing a calm and serious front when inwardly he was quaking. It reassured him that his wide sombrero hid his face. &ldquo;Hev a slice of ham. I can cook, cowboy.... Wal, how&rsquo;d you intercept it?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d like Starr in on this with me,&rdquo; replied Sidway. &ldquo;By all means. You young bloods put your heads together,&rdquo; said Stewart. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m with you, pard, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve got some ideas,&rdquo; returned Starr, nonchalantly. &ldquo;But I cain&rsquo;t talk an&rsquo; eat.&rdquo; Lance thought with all his might. It was a situation in which he wanted to make good. But the fact that these old cattlemen, who had fought rustlers and Mexicans for over a score of years, put the responsibility up to him and Starr seemed more stultifying than inspiring. &ldquo;Starr, what hour of the night will that highway be most free of traffic?&rdquo; asked Lance. &ldquo;About three in the mawnin&rsquo; thar&rsquo;s a quiet spell. Sometimes fer two hours not a darn car goes by.&rdquo; &ldquo;That will be the time the thieves will load,&rdquo; concluded Lance. &ldquo;I agree. And even then it&rsquo;s pretty risky. Old-time rustlers wouldn&rsquo;t be that brazen,&rdquo; said Stewart. &ldquo;Boss, we gotta deal with a new breed of criminal,&rdquo; added Starr. &ldquo;Like as not the drivers of these trucks won&rsquo;t be regular range characters. These men will be city crooks, stealing cattle as a blind. They are gangsters. Probably hop-heads.&rdquo; Stewart&rsquo;s leonine head swept up and he transfixed Lance with penetrating eyes. &ldquo;Cowboy, I heard you use that word hop-head less than an hour ago?&rdquo; he queried, sharply. &ldquo;Yes sir... You&mdash;you may have,&rdquo; replied Lance, startled. But despite his qualm he kept his head. &ldquo;I can explain it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Very well. Go on. What&rsquo;s a hop-head?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s the name underworld characters give to men addicted to opium or heroin. They smoke or eat opium. Heroin they mostly inhale by smelling from the back of their hands. It&rsquo;s a powder.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, the hell you say, cowboy?&rdquo; ejaculated Nels. &ldquo;Them hop-heads must be kinda tough nuts, eh?&rdquo; &ldquo;Brutal killers. No mercy! No respect for law. Rats, the police call them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, they&rsquo;ll shoot if they&rsquo;re cornered, an&rsquo; if they have machine guns, it&rsquo;ll jest be too bad,&rdquo; interposed Starr. &ldquo;They must be ambushed, or at least surprised,&rdquo; went on Lance. &ldquo;They&rsquo;ll be waiting or driving along slow at the place nearest the highway&mdash;most convenient to pick up the cattle.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s less than forty miles from where we&rsquo;re talkin&rsquo;,&rdquo; said Starr. &ldquo;I spotted the blackest bunch of cattle on my way oot. I can drive fairly close. We&rsquo;d hev to walk the rest of the way.&rdquo; &ldquo;I was going to suggest that,&rdquo; resumed Lance. &ldquo;But not to drive too close. These raiders, whose job it is to round up the cattle, could hear a car.... They will be mounted&mdash;acquainted with the range&mdash;probably living on it.&rdquo; &ldquo;In cahoots with the truck drivers. Pard. You said a mouthful.&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon he did thet same little thing,&rdquo; observed Mains, in dry subtlety. &ldquo;Wal, Gene, the kids ain&rsquo;t so pore,&rdquo; said Nels. &ldquo;If yore through eatin&rsquo; an&rsquo; talkin&rsquo; let&rsquo;s go. When we git down thar on the flat we can figger the rest.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the hurry, Nels?&rdquo; asked Stewart. &ldquo;Wal, I ain&rsquo;t trustin&rsquo; them hop-haids to wait till mawnin&rsquo;. Why, I reckon they&rsquo;d jest as lief rob us in broad daylight.&rdquo; Soon after that the five men, armed to the teeth, passed down the road in Starr&rsquo;s car, driving without lights. They had reached the village, passed the main street, when approaching Mains&rsquo; house, Sidway&rsquo;s keen eyes, accustomed to the dark, espied two mounted riders close to the fence. &ldquo;Hold it, Ren,&rdquo; he whispered.... &ldquo;There!... Down the walk past Mains&rsquo; house. Two horsemen!... They&rsquo;re moving.&rdquo; &ldquo;My gosh, I see them,&rdquo; replied Starr. &ldquo;Gone now. Listen. ... Heah them hoofs? Good fast trot.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boys,&rdquo; interposed Mains, &ldquo;it ain&rsquo;t nothin&rsquo; to see hawsemen around heah. They ride up bold as hell an&rsquo; then again they sneak up like Injuns. Bonita&rsquo;s the reason.&rdquo; &ldquo;Drive on, Ren,&rdquo; said Stewart, presently. Below the village some few hundred yards Starr steered off the road down the slope. In the dark he had to go very slowly, a procedure difficult to accomplish on account of the grade. A vast dim emptiness stretched away under the stars. Far down, double pin points of light, moving along, attested to the presence of a car on the highway. Lance asked Stewart how long that road had encroached upon his range. &ldquo;Six or seven years, if I remember. Used to bother old-timers. But that feeling has gone into the discard with the cattle business.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;ll pick up again and be better than ever,&rdquo; declared Lance. &ldquo;Ren, stop every little way, so we can listen.&rdquo; It was a silent night, not yet cooled off from the day&rsquo;s heat. The rustle of sage and the low hum of insects accentuated the silence. Stars were growing brighter in the darkening blue. Gradually the men ceased talking. Once down on the flat Starr had easier driving. Presently he ran out of the sage into the wash where on the sand and gravel the going was smoother and almost noiseless. Starr must have halted a dozen times at Nels&rsquo; order and the five had listened intently before Lance heard cattle bawling. &ldquo;Reckon we&rsquo;ve come fur enough. What say, Gene?&rdquo; queried Nels. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s pile oot.&rdquo; &ldquo;I think I know about where we are,&rdquo; said Gene, peering about in the gloom. &ldquo;Still pretty far from the road, that is, where this wash goes under. But the road curves in to the west.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cattle all aboot,&rdquo; observed Nels. &ldquo;An&rsquo; they ain&rsquo;t skeered, thet&rsquo;s shore. Let&rsquo;s mosey on till we heah somethin&rsquo;.&rdquo; Guardedly and slow, with senses alert, the men zigzagged through the sage, working southwest. Grazing and resting cattle grew more numerous. After what Lance believed was several miles&rsquo; travel Nels halted them near a rocky mound. &ldquo;Fur enough, till we heah or see somethin&rsquo;. You cowboys climb up thar.&rdquo; The eminence appeared to be rather long and higher toward the west. Lance signed to Ren that he would climb the far point. He did not, however, get to do it, for a hist from Starr called him back. Lance joined Nels and Stewart who stood under Starr. &ldquo;... Aboot a mile from the highway,&rdquo; Ren was saying, in guarded voice. &ldquo;Three big double lights comin&rsquo; from Bolton. Trucks. They&rsquo;re close together. Movin&rsquo; slow.&rdquo; &ldquo;How do you know they&rsquo;re trucks?&rdquo; asked Stewart. &ldquo;Cars an&rsquo; lamps an&rsquo; such hev been my job fer a year an&rsquo; more, Boss. These belong to trucks, an&rsquo; you bet the ones we&rsquo;re expectin&rsquo;. Jest creepin&rsquo; along. An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s a level road.&rdquo; &ldquo;How fur away?&rdquo; queried Nels. &ldquo;Cain&rsquo;t say. Mebbe three miles, mebbe six.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, down along here about even with where we are now there are several benches that run close to the road and break off in banks. It would be a simple matter for trucks to be backed up against these banks and loaded. In some places you wouldn&rsquo;t even need a platform. And my cattle are tame.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, we&rsquo;re some sucker cattlemen,&rdquo; drawled Nels, and sat down with his back to the bank. &ldquo;Set down an&rsquo; let&rsquo;s wait fer Starr to find oot somethin&rsquo;. Sidway, you got sharp ears. Go off a ways an&rsquo; listen. It&rsquo;s a still night. Listen fer cattle thet air disturbed.&rdquo; Lance did as he was bidden, conscious of growing excitement. These ranchmen evidently gave him credit for more and wilder experience than he had had. He felt that he must rise to the occasion. Presently there would be some sharp and critical work for all of them and he nerved himself to cool hard purpose. From time to time he heard Starr&rsquo;s low voice. It was some time, however, fraught with suspense, before Lance&rsquo;s range-trained ear caught the faint trample of hoofs and occasional bawl of cattle. Whereupon he ran back to report. &ldquo;Good!&rdquo; declared Stewart. &ldquo;How about you, Starr?&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon I heahed but wasn&rsquo;t shore. I am now. Not so all-fired far, either.... Boss, jest wait, I&rsquo;m watchin&rsquo; them trucks.&rdquo; Starr did not speak again, and the others listened intently. The faint sounds of moving cattle augmented. Presently the cowboy whispered sharply: &ldquo;Boss, the trucks hev stopped&mdash;jest a little to the right of us.... Lights go out!... No, by damn&mdash;the trucks air turnin&rsquo;... backin&rsquo; off the road this way, or I&rsquo;m a born fool.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, look like it&rsquo;s all set,&rdquo; said Nels, getting up. Stewart followed his example. &ldquo;Them rays of light flash across the highway,&rdquo; went on Starr. &ldquo;&rsquo;Pear to be linin&rsquo; up.... Fust lights gone out!...&rdquo; In another moment Lance was gliding cautiously along at Stewart&rsquo;s heels. They progressed fifty steps or more when Stewart halted to listen. &ldquo;But Nels didn&rsquo;t say what to do!&rdquo; whispered Lance. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a cinch we&rsquo;ll break up the raid. But our object is to capture at least one each of the riders and drivers.&rdquo; Lance silenced his misgivings and conjectures, and transferring his rifle to his left hand he drew his gun. Stewart, he had observed, packed two guns, and Lance thrilled at the way he wore them. So different from the movie bad men! They stole along slowly, avoiding the larger sagebrush, careful not to scare cattle, listening at intervals. The hum of a motorcar off to the east distracted Lance&rsquo;s attention from the now audible moving herd. Presently he saw the lights and he and the rancher watched them grow and pass not far below, and go on out of sight. That car was making fast time. No doubt it did not see the trucks. Soon Lance followed Stewart out of the sage upon the highway, black and glistening under the stars. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re farther this way than we thought,&rdquo; whispered Stewart. &ldquo;That bunch of cattle are down to the road.... Hear that?... We&rsquo;ve got to hand it to these truck rustlers for nerve. Right on the highway! Not leary of noise.&rdquo; &ldquo;To hell with the ranchers, eh?&rdquo; replied Lance, with a little husky laugh. He felt the heat throb in his pulsing veins. &ldquo;They wouldn&rsquo;t put a guard out, as the old rustlers used to.... Let&rsquo;s hurry along.&rdquo; Stewart strode so swiftly that Lance could not hear anything while they were moving. But presently they halted; the trample of hoofs and bawl of cattle became plainly audible. On the third halt Lance distinctly heard the thump of hoofs upon a board floor. &ldquo;&mdash;&mdash;!&rdquo; swore Stewart. &ldquo;Loading already! Won&rsquo;t that make Nels snort? It should be getting hot over there. But Nels would move slow.&rdquo; After another hundred steps or so Stewart led off the highway into the sage. Lance divined that the rancher wanted cover to drop behind in case the truckmen flashed their lights. Nevertheless it was not long before Stewart went down on all fours to crawl. This was tremendously exciting to Lance. The thump of solid hoofs on wood drowned all the other sounds except an occasional snort or bawl. The cattle were being moved with amazing celerity and little noise. &ldquo;Beats me,&rdquo; muttered Stewart, then crawled on. They had scarcely gotten even with a point opposite, where a high black bulk loomed up squarely above the horizon and marked the position of the trucks, when huge glaring lights gleamed out of the darkness. Lance flattened himself beside Stewart. A low fringe of sage on their right saved them from detection. But Lance&rsquo;s throat contracted. A harsh low voice, a sudden bursting of an engine, into whirring roar, a grind of wheels left no doubt that a truck was starting. It moved quickly down to the road, and turned so that the lights swerved to the right, leaving the men in darkness. Then the truck stopped and the driver called. Stewart jerked Lance to his feet, whispering: &ldquo;We&rsquo;ve got to move. Careful now. Keep your head.&rdquo; Lance over Stewart&rsquo;s shoulder saw the big gun go prodding into the driver&rsquo;s side. &ldquo;Agg-h!&rdquo; he ejaculated, and lifted his hands off the wheel. A quick grating footstep on the other side of the car caused Lance to crouch. A man came swiftly round the front. &ldquo;Beat it, Bill&mdash;we&rsquo;re held up!&rdquo; rasped the driver. But this man cursed and swept up his arm. Lance having him covered, had only to pull the trigger. His shot preceded the others only by an instant. Lance saw his action violently break, his gun burst red. A crash of bursting glass, a thud of bullets preceded Stewart&rsquo;s staggering away from the truck to fall. The driver, with hoarse bellows of alarm, shoved his power on so quickly that the cattle in the truck banged against the gate. Then the truck roared down the road. The horror that gripped Lance at Stewart&rsquo;s fall nearly overcame him for a moment. Then strident yells, the flashing of lights and roaring of engines added to his fighting fury. Nimbly he leaped beyond the broad flares. And as the second truck whizzed down upon the road he emptied his gun at the front of the first one. The splintering crash of glass, the lurching of the car, the loud yells told that his bullets did some execution. Both trucks gained the road. As they roared on shots rang out from the bank. Then Lance, resorting to his rifle, aimed above the red rear light of the last truck, and sent ten shots after it. Lowering the hot rifle he stood a moment, shaking, wet with cold sweat, realizing all shots had ceased. He caught a clatter of rapid hoofs, the crowding of cattle, then a ringing voice: &ldquo;Hey, over there,&rdquo; called Nels. &ldquo;What&rsquo;n hell was yore hurry?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels! Come&mdash;quick! Stewart&rsquo;s...&rdquo; yelled Lance hoarsely. &ldquo;Keep your shirt on, cowboy,&rdquo; intercepted the cool voice of Stewart. Then Lance saw his tall dark form against the lighter gloom. &ldquo;Oh&mdash;Ste&mdash;wart. I was afraid,&rdquo; gasped Lance. &ldquo;Hello. Where are you, fellers?&rdquo; shouted Danny Mains, and then followed Starr&rsquo;s cheery voice. &ldquo;Busted, by thunder!&rdquo; &ldquo;Here,&rdquo; called Stewart. Presently the three loomed on the road. &ldquo;Gene, you let &rsquo;um git away,&rdquo; protested Nels, hopping mad. &ldquo;Trucks vamoosed in spite of us. How about the riders?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, we was creepin&rsquo; up behind, all set, when you opened up the ball.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did you identify any of them?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell, we didn&rsquo;t even see them. Slick an&rsquo; fast ootfit, Gene. Makes me more curious.&rdquo; &ldquo;I ain&rsquo;t so damn curious as I was,&rdquo; growled Danny Mains, enigmatically. &ldquo;Pard, you shore done a lot of shootin&rsquo;,&rdquo; declared Starr, peering into Lance&rsquo;s face. &ldquo;Here&rsquo;s what happened,&rdquo; explained Stewart. &ldquo;Sidway and I got here just as that loaded truck came off the sage. When it turned on the road, we jumped and ran. It stopped. I opened the door and stuck my right gun in the driver&rsquo;s ribs. He yelled in spite of that. Then his pardner came running. Bill, the driver called him. Bill sure saw me. For he came up with a gun. I threw my left on him but it struck the car door, low down. Sidway shot this fellow&mdash;broke his aim&mdash;or sure as God made little apples he&rsquo;d have killed me. At that he hit me. His bullet knocked me flat.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gene! You shot? Whar? Not a body hit?&rdquo; exclaimed Nels. There was a moment&rsquo;s silence, during which the cold began to creep into Lance&rsquo;s marrow. &ldquo;Bored him plumb center,&rdquo; replied Stewart, grimly. &ldquo;He stood just inside the light. I saw him drop like a sack. He&rsquo;s lying here somewhere.&rdquo; Starr produced a flashlight and with the two men began searching the immediate space, while Lance fought the strangest, most sickening sensation of his life. &ldquo;Heah!... Daid, I&rsquo;ll tell the world!&rdquo; rang out Starr. &ldquo;Lousy-lookin&rsquo; little bastard! One of them hop-haids.&rdquo; &ldquo;Search him, Ren, and drag him off the road.... Sidway, you&rsquo;re too damn good a shot. Dead men tell no tales.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if you ask me, our new cowboy is a man after my kind. Gene, don&rsquo;t call him fer shootin&rsquo; fast an&rsquo; straight.&rdquo; &ldquo;I was kidding. But at that I wish Sidway had only crippled him... . What&rsquo;d he have on him, Ren?&rdquo; &ldquo;Automatic tight in his mitt.... Watch. Knife. Cigarettes.... An&rsquo; this wad of long green. Fellers, will you look at thet! A hundred dollar bill on the ootside!&rdquo; Lance gradually dragged himself closer to the trio, and discerned Starr on his knees beside the dead man, a slack spare figure, terribly suggestive, showing in the flashlight a crooked visage, ghastly in hue and contortion. &ldquo;Wal, you lousy hop-haid,&rdquo; broke out Starr, in genial levity, &ldquo;bumped up agin the wrong hombre, didn&rsquo;t you?... Lay hold, Danny, an&rsquo; help me haul him over heah.&rdquo; The cold grip on Lance&rsquo;s internals slowly lessened; and he helped it pass by a desperate effort to conceal what he felt to be his squeamishness before these ranchers. He thought he could get by in the dark. &ldquo;Sidway must have stung more than this hombre,&rdquo; Stewart was saying to Nels. &ldquo;He shot the front glasses out of both cars. And he sure cut loose with his rifle as they drove away. I heard the bullets hit that last truck.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, thanks to him, it didn&rsquo;t turn oot so pore. Mebbe thet money will more than pay fer the cattle they rustled.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, reckon I&rsquo;ll hev to drive back to Bolton in the mawnin&rsquo; an&rsquo; report this execution to the sheriff,&rdquo; said Starr, joining them. &ldquo;Yes. And I might have to see a doctor.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lemme look.&rdquo; Starr flashed his light upon the side of Stewart&rsquo;s face which he turned for inspection. Lance saw with concern that the rancher was bloody enough to have been struck by a load of shot. The cowboy wiped the blood off, and peering closer he ran his fingers over Stewart&rsquo;s cheek and temple and neck. &ldquo;Hell! You ain&rsquo;t been shot atall, Boss. Jest a blast of glass, I reckon.... Leastways I cain&rsquo;t find any bullet hole.... Gosh, I&rsquo;m glad them glass bits missed yore eye.&rdquo; &ldquo;Feel here&mdash;back of my ear.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ah-ha!&mdash;Shore, he creased you thar.... Hot as fire, huh. Thet was made by a bullet, Boss.&rdquo; &ldquo;A miss is as good as a mile.... Sidway, I owe you something.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, no!&mdash;He&mdash;he just missed you. I was too&mdash;too slow,&rdquo; replied Lance, thickly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been shot at before, boy. I saw him jerk and his gun spurt up. He&rsquo;d have bored me.&rdquo; &ldquo;How aboot moseyin&rsquo; along? Thet&rsquo;s a long tramp fer a man who never trailed rustlers on foot,&rdquo; said Nels, plaintively. As they moved up the highway Starr dropped back to Lance&rsquo;s side. He put a hand on the other&rsquo;s arm. &ldquo;Pard, you didn&rsquo;t tell me you was some punkins with a gun.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, I hope this night&rsquo;s work will end the truck rustling,&rdquo; said Lance, lamely. &ldquo;That&rsquo;ll be swell. I&rsquo;m glad. We&rsquo;ll get along fine, Ren.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh! I jest happened to think!&rdquo; ejaculated Starr, stopping in the middle of the highway to take a pull at Lance. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d give a heap to be in yore boots.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why&mdash;what&rsquo;s hurting? Don&rsquo;t you think, usually?&rdquo; &ldquo;Will you be sittin&rsquo; pretty with Majesty Stewart?... Fust stunt&mdash;right off&mdash;savin&rsquo; her dad&rsquo;s life! Pard, she adores him. My Gawd, the luck of some gazabos!&rdquo; &ldquo;Lord!... Starr, if you&rsquo;re my pard don&rsquo;t tell&mdash;her&mdash;please,&rdquo; exclaimed Lance, his weakness making him prey to another emotion. &ldquo;Wal!&mdash;Why, shore, Sidway. I won&rsquo;t tell her. But how about Gene? An&rsquo; thet gabby old Nels&mdash;Pard, if I was you I&rsquo;d shore want her to know.&rdquo; &ldquo;We&rsquo;ve clashed, Ren. She misunderstood my coming here. Thinks I&rsquo;m a liar. Laughed at me&mdash;when I denied it.... Vainest girl I ever met!&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell! What of thet,&rdquo; returned Ren, bluntly. &ldquo;She&rsquo;s also the loveliest, the sweetest, the finest an&rsquo; squarest. Get thet, buddy?&rdquo; &ldquo;Forget it, pard. You&rsquo;re a little upset. I ain&rsquo;t wonderin&rsquo; at thet. You reckoned Stewart was daid an&rsquo; seein&rsquo; him come back to life would excite anybody, outside the fight.&rdquo; Starr gave Sidway a friendly pat on the back and then let him alone. Presently they reached a culvert over the wash, and turning here, they followed the pale line of sand into the sage. The sand dragged at Sidway&rsquo;s feet, but the exertion helped restore his equilibrium. The distance back to the car seemed interminable and proved how, on the way down, the excitement had made it short. They found the car at length and were soon bumping over the uneven ground. Starr had no incentive now to drive slowly and noiselessly. He certainly gave his passengers a rough trip back to the ranch. Lance went to bed at once. For half an hour Nels and Mains, dressing Stewart&rsquo;s wounds in the adjoining room, kept Lance awake, thinking one moment and going over the adventure the next. When quiet settled down, he soon fell asleep. Upon awakening, Lance heard Starr and Nels talking while they got breakfast. Presently Starr pounded on the wall between, jarring the house. &ldquo;Hey, Oregon, air you daid?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m up,&rdquo; replied Lance. &ldquo;Wal, you&rsquo;re quieter&rsquo;n hell if you air. Waltz out. I gotta rustle to town pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, send for my baggage.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, pard, an&rsquo; what else?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll see.&rdquo; When Lance entered Nels&rsquo; bunkhouse to have breakfast he sensed such a great transformation in himself that he felt certain his friends would exclaim about it. But they did not notice any difference in him. During the meal they did not once mention the affair of last night. All in the day, for them, thought Lance! He essayed a cool and quiet demeanor which he meant to make permanent. &ldquo;Nels, what&rsquo;ll I do today?&rdquo; he asked. &ldquo;Dog-gone if I know, son,&rdquo; drawled the other, scratching his gray head. &ldquo;They&rsquo;re all goin&rsquo; to town. Go wrangle yore hawse an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll ask Gene when he comes down.&rdquo; Umpqua had made the most of the huge grassy pasture. Lance found him in the extreme far corner, more than a mile from the corrals, and rode him bareback to the corrals. After rubbing him down and saddling and bridling him, Lance led him up the lane to the court. Stewart, his head swathed in white bandages, stood by Madge&rsquo;s car talking to Starr. As Lance passed the open door of the store he heard Madge&rsquo;s rich voice, breaking with a singular note, and it gave him a wild impulse to run. Starr hailed him, and then he and Stewart approached. &ldquo;Hope you&rsquo;re okay, Boss,&rdquo; said Lance, eagerly. &ldquo;Mornin&rsquo;, Sidway. Reckon I feel like a nigger who had to have the buckshot picked out of him. Would you like to go to town with us?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not on my own account, sir. Thanks. There&rsquo;s a lot I can find to do here.&rdquo; Lance said this at the same moment he heard Nels&rsquo; clinking slow step behind him and a lighter pace that stopped his heart. But he did not turn. &ldquo;Starr will come back soon,&rdquo; went on the rancher. &ldquo;It might be a good idea for you and him to fix up your quarters. Nels said they had gone to rack.&rdquo; &ldquo;How about the cattle?&rdquo; asked Lance. &ldquo;They have been left free to run the range, and as you saw, have worked low down. Danny and I will be driving a big herd to the railroad soon. Maybe next week. I&rsquo;ll make that deal in town today. As for immediate jobs, I want you and Ren to repair the water flume and the telephone line pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, sir. I&rsquo;ll get started on them today.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, did you make out your list of supplies?&rdquo; &ldquo;Majesty writ it oot fer me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, what have you been tellin&rsquo; that girl?&rdquo; demanded Stewart. &ldquo;Me? Why, Gene, nothin&rsquo; atall,&rdquo; drawled Nels, innocently. &ldquo;You old liar! Look at her!&rdquo; Lance wished to do this with an almost irresistible desire. But he sat down on the edge of the porch, dragging Starr with him, aware that the others had stepped into the store. &ldquo;Ren, you&rsquo;ll not forget my baggage?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, pard. Anythin&rsquo; else? Say how&rsquo;s yore bunkhouse fixed up? I didn&rsquo;t look.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s not fixed up at all,&rdquo; replied Lance. &ldquo;No mattress, no chair, no mirror, nothing to wash in or with. No towels. I&rsquo;ve been using Nels&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, mine cain&rsquo;t be no wuss...&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, buy what you need today,&rdquo; said Madge Stewart, from behind them. She had not gone into the store at all. Manifestly she had heard their talk. &ldquo;Whatever is this ranch coming to?&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, you heah, Miss Majesty. Good mawnin&rsquo;,&rdquo; replied Starr, with confusion, as he stood up to turn toward her. &ldquo;About the ranch&mdash;wal, I&rsquo;d say things was lookin&rsquo; up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lance Sidway!&rdquo; Arising stiffly, Lance wheeled to doff his sombrero and greet her in apparent composure. But the tone of her voice and then the look of her played havoc with all his resolves. At this juncture Stewart and Nels came out of the store. &ldquo;Nels, do you think I dare ride in with Madge?&rdquo; quizzed Stewart. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;d jump at the chance.&rdquo; &ldquo;You old traitor! Why, you never could be hired to ride in a car. Do you remember Link Stevens driving that big white car of Madeline&rsquo;s?&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd, do I? But I&rsquo;ll bet Majesty would hev druv rings around Link.&rdquo; Madge was looking down upon Lance. The fairness of her face appeared enhanced by the scarlet upon her lips. In truth Lance saw that she was pale and that her eyes were unnaturally large, glowing, dilating, with a violet fire. Then she seemed to float down the steps and entwine her arm in Lance&rsquo;s, and lift her lovely face to him, that in the action flushed a hue to match her lips and then went pearly white. &ldquo;You saved Dad&rsquo;s life!&rdquo; Lance had prepared himself for he knew not what, though not for this close proximity, the tight pressure of her arm, the quivering feel of her. &ldquo;Oh, no, Miss Stewart. Somebody has exaggerated.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels told me,&rdquo; said Madge, intensely. &ldquo;I might have known,&rdquo; went on Lance, trying to be cool and nonchalant. &ldquo;Nels is swell, but you know he.... Starr told me what an old liar he is.&rdquo; &ldquo;Heah!&rdquo; yelped Starr. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t you get me in bad. I never said thet...&rdquo; &ldquo;Miss Stewart, please...&rdquo; interposed Lance. &ldquo;You mustn&rsquo;t give me undue credit. I was there&mdash;and I&rsquo;m glad&mdash;I made myself useful. I didn&rsquo;t want to kill the man...&rdquo; Lance spread wide his hands to the watching men, as if to say &ldquo;Now see what you&rsquo;ve done.&rdquo; But it was not the revelation that distracted him. &ldquo;So you&rsquo;re bound to be our good angel!&rdquo; she exclaimed, softly, and shook him gently. &ldquo;Really I&mdash;you... it was&mdash;it&rsquo;s not so much.&rdquo; She led him out to her car and still clung to his arm. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s impossible to thank you,&rdquo; she went on, her voice breaking. &ldquo;I can&rsquo;t even try. But I&rsquo;m unutterably grateful. I&rsquo;ll do anything for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thank you, Miss Stewart....&rdquo; &ldquo;My friends call me Majesty,&rdquo; she interrupted, sweetly. &ldquo;I&mdash;I appreciate your excitement and feeling. It&rsquo;s kind of a tough spot for you. I hoped they wouldn&rsquo;t tell you. But they did... and I won&rsquo;t let you make too much of it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Too much! Aren&rsquo;t you glad?&rdquo; she rejoined, incredulously. &ldquo;Glad? That I was there&mdash;with him? Good heavens! Of course, I am. Greatest kick I ever had!&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels is a sentimental old jackass,&rdquo; burst out Lance. If she would only let go of his arm, move her soft warm shoulder away from his! &ldquo;It bothers you,&rdquo; she asserted, quickly. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll skip it.... Come into town with us.&rdquo; &ldquo;Is that an order?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, no. Just a request.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, but I&rsquo;ve plenty of work to do here.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll say plenty.... Lance, I hated you yesterday.&rdquo; &ldquo;Are you telling me? But really I&rsquo;m sorry I was so rude.&rdquo; &ldquo;I forgive you. Let&rsquo;s be good friends now. You&rsquo;re here, I&rsquo;m here&mdash;and my friends are coming. You&rsquo;ll like the girls. They&rsquo;re peaches. Full of fun&mdash;and great sports. It will embarrass me if we are at odds.&rdquo; &ldquo;How could that be? I&rsquo;m only your father&rsquo;s cowboy.&rdquo; &ldquo;I will be, of course. A friend like Ren Starr,&rdquo; qualified Lance. &ldquo;But I mean more than that. Ren is swell. Only he&rsquo;s a hired hand.&rdquo; &ldquo;So am I. I won&rsquo;t forget my place.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aren&rsquo;t you being just a little snooty?&rdquo; she inquired, subtly changing, and she released his arm. &ldquo;Aren&rsquo;t you kidding me?&rdquo; &ldquo;No,&rdquo; she flashed, loftily. &ldquo;Then isn&rsquo;t that your line?&rdquo; &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t any line, Mr. Sidway.&rdquo; Lance felt utterly helpless in two conflicting ways&mdash;that he simply could not help rubbing the girl the wrong way, any more than he could resist her lovely person and insidious charm. He wanted her to hurry away so that he could think. In another moment she would see that his heart if not his will was prostrate at her feet. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m afraid you had better fire me right here and now,&rdquo; he said, glumly. &ldquo;Perhaps I had,&rdquo; she returned, her purple eyes glowing upon him, as if visioning afar. &ldquo;But Dad needs you. And he wouldn&rsquo;t let you go.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d go anyhow. Just you fire me.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. I&rsquo;ll not do it.... Listen, big boy, you gave me some dirty digs. And I&rsquo;ve been catty. It&rsquo;s fifty-fifty. Here&rsquo;s my hand for a new deal.&rdquo; &ldquo;Miss Stewart&mdash;as Ren says&mdash;you&rsquo;re one grand girl,&rdquo; rejoined Lance, unsteadily. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s unconceivable that I could withhold my hand, if you offered yours. But I can&rsquo;t forget so easily as you evidently do.&rdquo; &ldquo;I see. You can&rsquo;t take it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Do you still believe I found out who you were&mdash;where you lived&mdash;and came out here to&mdash;to...&rdquo; he queried, hotly, and ended, unable to finish. &ldquo;Why, certainly I do.&rdquo; &ldquo;When I swear on my word of honor that I didn&rsquo;t?&rdquo; went on Lance, passionately. &ldquo;Yes,&rdquo; she retorted, almost with like heat. &ldquo;And I&rsquo;d think more of you if you&rsquo;d not lie about it. This word of honor stuff!... I thought it was a swell stunt. I was tickled pink. I&rsquo;ll still think it grand of you if you&rsquo;ll only stop bluffing. What more could you want?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes I have, Lance Sidway,&rdquo; she blazed at him. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve nerve enough to get him, too, at any cost&mdash;unless you show yellow and ride away!&rdquo; Lance bowed and turned away toward the bunkhouses, forgetting the others and afraid to go to Umpqua. He heard her call to her father, and presently the sound of the cars wheeling away. In that moment of passion he divined if he approached Umpqua it would be to ride away from that ranch. And he flung himself upon his bunk to shut out the sunlight. Neither pride in himself nor loyalty to Stewart accounted for that victory over himself. The paralyzing and staggering truth was that he did not ride away because he could not bear to leave this beautiful and tormenting girl. No sooner was the road fit for heavy traffic when it appeared all the trucks in Bolton came along loaded to capacity with the crated furniture and bales and boxes that Madge Stewart had sent from Los Angeles. There were four small trucks and two large ones. The contents, Lance calculated, must have cost the girl thousands of dollars; and the sight of them aroused an unreasonable resentment in him. What business was it of his? Yet he could not help thinking of her all the day and half the night. That fact lay at the root of his intense dissatisfaction rather than her extravagance at such a hard period for her parents. Lance was determined that Madge must not know this. He was always fighting against an acceptance of her faults. On Saturday afternoon of that busy week Lance was glad to see Starr drive up with Stewart and Mains in his car, and an empty truck behind. The road job was finished, very much to Stewart&rsquo;s satisfaction, and he paid off the Mexicans, and sent them back to town. &ldquo;Well, I reckon we&rsquo;re ready for Madge&rsquo;s outfit,&rdquo; said Stewart, eyeing his cowboys. &ldquo;Who is, Boss?&rdquo; queried Lance. &ldquo;Not me, neither,&rdquo; added Starr, making a wry face. &ldquo;All summer long! Gene, they&rsquo;ll drive us nuts.... An&rsquo; shore one of them swell college sprats will cop my girl.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Bonita, you mean,&rdquo; returned Lance, laconically, as he rested against the car. &ldquo;Ren, I haven&rsquo;t noticed&mdash;so much&mdash;that she is your property.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sid, you double-crossin&rsquo; son-of-a-gun!
