I might have knowed it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell kid!&rdquo; &ldquo;Look heah, Sidway, hev you been after my daughter, too?&rdquo; demanded Danny Mains. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve seen a good deal of her while you were away. I knew you didn&rsquo;t approve of Ren.&rdquo; &ldquo;Danny,&rdquo; interposed Stewart, &ldquo;cowboys are the same now as we were. Only a good deal better. I think Bonita is better off for friends like Starr and Sidway.&rdquo; &ldquo;Mischief! Say, Danny, you ain&rsquo;t got me figgered,&rdquo; replied Ren, doggedly. &ldquo;I love Bonita an&rsquo; hev asked her to marry me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren&mdash;So that was it?&rdquo; ejaculated Lance. &ldquo;So that was what?&rdquo; queried Starr, suspiciously. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t want to embarrass you here, pard.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, you&rsquo;ll excuse my cantankerousness,&rdquo; said Mains, simply. &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t hev you figgered.&rdquo; &ldquo;Starr, this here Oregon ladies&rsquo; man is not only stepping on your preserves, but he&rsquo;s kidding you,&rdquo; rejoined Stewart, with a laugh. &ldquo;Hook her up and let&rsquo;s go. Sidway, it&rsquo;s a good job well done.... Oh, yes, I&rsquo;ve a message for you by phone. Madge wants you up at the house to open boxes.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss! I&rsquo;m a tired man,&rdquo; expostulated Lance. &ldquo;And Umpqua needs to be worked over. Up and down this dusty road for a week!&rdquo; &ldquo;Haw! Haw! Haw!&rdquo; laughed Starr, fiendishly. &ldquo;All right. I&rsquo;ll tell Madge not tonight.&rdquo; Starr grinned knowingly at Lance and drove off. From where they had caught up with him it was only a short ride to the ranch. Yet it seemed a long and thoughtful one for Lance. It began to look as if Madge Stewart either meant to try him with odd stable-boy jobs or else she wanted him to be unable to avoid her, as he had tried so hard to do. The former made him furious and the latter made him weak. By the time he had put Umpqua away it was dark, the store was closed, and bright light shone from Nels&rsquo; window and door. A drowsy breeze blew in from the range, moved down by the cooler air on high. Frogs were croaking in the lake. Lance washed his grimy hands and face before he went in. &ldquo;Jest in time, son. Come in an&rsquo; get it,&rdquo; said Nels, cheerily. &ldquo;Pard, what say to a swim in the lake after supper? She&rsquo;s bank full already,&rdquo; suggested Starr. &ldquo;Okay by me. But, boy! that water is cold.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; you from Oregon!&rdquo; It turned out to be warmer water than Lance was used to at home, and he enjoyed the bath. On the way back he realized that Ren had something on his mind. Lance clapped him on the back. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s on your chest, buddy?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s Bonita.&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, Ren, I was surprised at what you admitted to Danny. I had no idea you were serious. I&rsquo;m sorry, old man.&rdquo; &ldquo;You like Bonita?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll say. She&rsquo;s some kid.&rdquo; &ldquo;Pard, did you kiss and hug her?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren! Have a heart. Would you expect me to tell?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, in my case, yes. You see, pard, I want you to help me win thet kid. I cain&rsquo;t do it alone.&rdquo; &ldquo;Okay. Yes I did&mdash;a little. But it was no cinch. And I liked her the better. She&rsquo;s a charming girl, Ren. I honestly think she&rsquo;d make a swell little wife. But there are a lot of guys who&rsquo;re after her, and not all of them with your good intentions.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. But town fellows, too. And I&rsquo;m suspicious of them. I&rsquo;ve a hunch some of them might know something about that rustling.&rdquo; &ldquo;My hunch, too. We&rsquo;ll find out. An&rsquo; pard&mdash;listen! If you&rsquo;ll help me with this black-eyed little girl I&rsquo;ll shore play yore game with yore proud Majesty.&rdquo; &ldquo;My God!&mdash;Ren, have you gone nutty?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. I&rsquo;m cool as a cucumber right this heah minnit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why, hell, yes. Pard, I seen her look at you thet day, an&rsquo; if she&rsquo;s not stuck on you&mdash;mebbe unknowin&rsquo; to herself&mdash;then I am nutty an&rsquo; what hev you?&rdquo; &ldquo;You are&mdash;Ren&mdash;you are,&rdquo; replied Lance, frantically. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll make that Bonita kid think you&rsquo;re a prince. But, pard, forget your pipe dream about the other.&rdquo; &ldquo;Faint heart never won fair lady,&rdquo; returned Starr, lightly. That night Lance had a dream of a cherub-faced cowboy leading him along flower-strewn trails to a bower where a goddess with golden hair awaited him with white arms extended. At breakfast he was silly enough to tell Ren and Nels about that dream. &ldquo;Dreams come true, sometimes,&rdquo; declared Ren, stoutly. &ldquo;Hey, Nels?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore they do. An&rsquo; this one of Lance&rsquo;s has a pertickler bearin&rsquo; heah,&rdquo; observed Nels, without glancing at Lance. &ldquo;Yeah? And how d&rsquo;you figure that?&rdquo; asked Lance, scornfully. Nels and Ren both laughed and Lance&rsquo;s face crimsoned. Luckily at that juncture a message came from Stewart requesting the cowboys to come up to the house, not dressed in their Sunday clothes. &ldquo;That means work. And it&rsquo;s Sunday!&rdquo; complained Lance, honestly and fearfully divided between bliss and panic. &ldquo;Aw, you know you&rsquo;re piflicated,&rdquo; replied Starr, cryptically. &ldquo;I am&mdash;sure, but what is piflicated?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, as I remember from past mournful experiences, it&rsquo;s a kinda prolapsus of the gizzard. Heart trouble, pard.&rdquo; Upon ascending to the ranch house the cowboys, with Nels accompanying them, found a busy and excited household. Front porch and part of the patio were packed with crates, bales, boxes, trunks, packages. Stewart, in his shirt sleeves, apparently in a trance, was helping the beaming Danny Mains carry furniture into the house. Mrs. Stewart, flushed and radiant, was bustling about with the servants. Bonita was there, red-lipped and pretty, her arms full of linens. And Madge, in slacks and a backless waist, cool and sweet and smiling, evidently was boss. She put Lance and Ren to opening crates. For a couple of hours they worked diligently at this job, and after they had unpacked everything, they carried boards and boxes and burlap and paper out to be hauled away on the morrow. The next job was to move things into the patio rooms. Lance observed that these were light, high-ceiled rooms, with colored adobe walls, and shiny floors. He had a glimpse of Madge&rsquo;s rooms, and was reminded of some of the luxurious motion-picture sets he had seen in Hollywood. At noon they had lunch on the patio porch, and it was a merry occasion. Lance, looking at Mrs. Stewart, no longer wondered how Madge had come by her beauty. She was a western girl, but her mother&rsquo;s eastern breeding and distinction had been augmented in her. Lance had to admit that parents and daughter made a delightful trio, and also that the girl&rsquo;s singular zest and enthusiasm motivated them. After luncheon everybody worked harder than ever. Nels was wearing his long spurs, that stuck in dangerously as he knelt to hold a pillow in his teeth and slide it down into a slip. Nevertheless in housekeeping matters, Nels proved serious and efficient. Madge presently gave Starr and Bonita tasks to perform together, while Stewart carried and unrolled rugs and his wife oversaw his disposition of them, and the moving of furniture. Lance propounded the question as to whether he had naturally gravitated to tasks with Madge or she had arranged it that way. But the time came when he no longer doubted. That fixed his distant exterior, but it added more and more to the tumult within. The tasks she set him, and completed with him, would never be remembered. As the minutes sped by he seemed hardly conscious of anything besides her presence&mdash;her intense and zestful activity, her requests and suggestions, and her talk in between, the intimacy that she seemed not to notice but which he felt so subtly, the play of her lovely features and the changing expressions in her violet eyes, her laugh, her smile, her grace, her disheveled golden hair falling over her face to be brushed back with a beautiful hand. And at last, how it came about he could not say, they had finished all present tasks, and she was offering him a cigarette. &ldquo;No thanks,&rdquo; he said, easily. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t you smoke?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sure. Once in a while.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lance, you&rsquo;re one swell assistant. I think I&rsquo;ll promote you, if...&rdquo; &ldquo;To what?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, major-domo or gigolo&mdash;or just cavalier.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;ll be all I can do to make good as a cowboy. That is&mdash;here!&rdquo; &ldquo;How modest and cool you are! I&rsquo;d like it, if I could be sure you were sincere. You don&rsquo;t ring true, darling.&rdquo; He had no reply for that. They had come out into the patio, and Lance was walking slowly toward the back, with apparent composure and the respectful demeanor of a cowboy toward the boss&rsquo;s daughter. She walked with him, cigarette in hand. Somewhere near by Starr and Bonita were quarreling, but Madge gave no sign she heard them. Lance felt that he must escape at once, or he would betray what he knew not. Yet the bitter paradox was that another self of him longed to linger there. &ldquo;Oh, I forgot. There&rsquo;s a nice room at that end of the patio. I want you to have it.&rdquo; &ldquo;But, I&mdash;thank you, Miss Stewart. I have my bunkhouse lodgings.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. I peeped in there one day while you were out. Nels showed me. Rotten for even a cowboy. And you&rsquo;re a gentleman, Lance.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks again. But I&rsquo;m satisfied down there.&rdquo; &ldquo;I expect you to help entertain my guests.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why should you be so surprised? Because you&rsquo;ve been rude to me and I&rsquo;ve been selfish?&mdash;That&rsquo;s nothing. We can give, and take it, too.... These boys and girls will be tenderfeet. I must have some real western he-man around on occasions&mdash;especially where my horses are concerned. I&rsquo;d like to trust you with that job.&rdquo; &ldquo;But your father wants Ren and me to ride the range, build and dig and what not.&rdquo; &ldquo;Are you giving me an order?&rdquo; &ldquo;You are very&mdash;kind. But in that case I must refuse.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t you like me?&rdquo; she demanded, incredulously. &ldquo;Miss Stewart, that is a personal matter,&rdquo; he replied, looking straight ahead. &ldquo;If you put it that way, I&rsquo;m afraid I must be rude again. You are mistaken,&rdquo; he rejoined, and his voice sounded curiously strange to his throbbing ears. But he was telling the truth. He did not like Madge Stewart because he loved her. The silence grew almost unbearable. He steeled himself against sarcasm, anger, wounded vanity. When they reached the wide green-bordered exit from the patio, when she stopped, he simply had to look at her. The last thing he expected was to see her eyes brimming with tears. She looked by him, out over the slope to the range. Her wide eyes were softly blurred, dark with pain. &ldquo;Lance, I can take it,&rdquo; she said, presently, and lifted her cigarette to her lips. It had gone out. &ldquo;Have you a match?&rdquo; He produced one, struck and held it for her, and she blew a cloud of smoke, with apparent unintention, into his face. When the blue cloud cleared away he could not have believed she could possibly have looked hurt. &ldquo;Anyone can start something. But it takes a real sport to finish.&rdquo; Did she refer to an affair with him or the acquisition of his horse, or was it the passionate pride of a woman in herself? He answered by saying that he feared he, at least, had undertaken something he could never finish, and bidding her good night he left her. On the way down he took a short cut off the back road into a trail, and lingered on a secluded spot, from which he could see the flaming range on fire with sunset. The critical hour of his life had struck. He loved this girl, and the emotion seemed a coalescing of all his former fancies and loves, magnified into an incredible passion too great to understand or hate or resist. It did not require to be brooded over and analyzed and made certain. Like an avalanche it fell upon him. It was too terrible in its fatality, too transporting in its bliss, too great to be ashamed of. But it must be his secret. He swore he would die before he would let this man hunter and love taster, who like a savage princess exacted homage, this frail, spoiled, lovely creature know that he loved her. Before dusk settled down thickly, Lance had fought out the battle, losing in his surrender to the catastrophe that had overtaken him, but victor over his weakness. Nevertheless he realized anew that he should straddle Umpqua and ride away before another day dawned. As he was not big enough to do that he did not blind himself to the peril that played and led him on. No matter how troubled and hopeless he felt when he lay down at night, when morning dawned, with all the exquisite freshness and sweetness of this country, and the golden light over the range, he seemed to be transformed, renewed, to be glad of life and youth, and the nameless hope that beckoned him on. Returning from his tasks at the corrals he found Madge and Nels puttering around her car. She wore a blue hat, blue gown, blue gloves&mdash;everywhere she appeared as blue as her eyes. &ldquo;Heah, son, come an&rsquo; help,&rdquo; called Nels. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know a darn thing aboot these enjines.&rdquo; Madge&rsquo;s look of annoyance vanished as Lance strode up to the car. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the matter?&rdquo; &ldquo;The damn thing won&rsquo;t start,&rdquo; she said, smiling. &ldquo;You have a mechanic here. Cars are Starr&rsquo;s specialty.&rdquo; &ldquo;He&rsquo;s gone. Will you oblige me?&rdquo; Lance leisurely lifted the hood of the engine, to see at a glance what was wrong, and in a moment adjusted it. &ldquo;What for?&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, it might go on the blink again.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not a chance.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right. Then&mdash;just for the ride,&rdquo; she returned. Lance returned her look with a feeling that he knew he was the only man on earth who could have refused her and who must suffer the anguish of despair because he had done so. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sorry, Miss Stewart. I&rsquo;ve no time. Your father relies on me for certain things. And I&rsquo;m glad to relieve him of many labors&mdash;if I can&rsquo;t of worries.&rdquo; &ldquo;Noble of you, big boy!... Strike a match for me. I have my gloves on,&rdquo; she rejoined, and leaned over the door with the cigarette between her lips. Lance had to step close, and he executed her request, but to save his life he could not have stilled the quiver of his hand as he held the match. She could not have seen it, however, for her unfathomable eyes were fastened upon his face. Then, with a merry good-by to him and Nels, she drove away. Not until she had gone down the slope did Lance realize he had watched her. Evidently Nels had done the same. &ldquo;Wal, son, she&rsquo;s kinda set on disturbin&rsquo; you,&rdquo; he drawled. &ldquo;Nels, I&rsquo;ll never last here,&rdquo; he replied, poignantly. &ldquo;Shore you will. We all like you, cowboy, an thet applies to Majesty Stewart, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell no!&rdquo; ejaculated Lance, borrowing Starr&rsquo;s expressive language. &ldquo;Sidway, I&rsquo;ve knowed thet girl since she was borned,&rdquo; declared Nels. &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t figger her atall. You made her mad at you fust off. I reckon thet was good, if you&rsquo;re as nutty aboot her as Starr swears you air.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, did he tell you that? I&rsquo;ll sock him, by thunder!&mdash;Isn&rsquo;t there anything or anybody on this ranch but that girl?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, she seems to be the center of things, whirlin&rsquo; them like a dust devil.&rdquo; All day long Lance glanced up from this and that task to see if there was a car raising the dust down on the valley road. Nevertheless he accomplished three days&rsquo; work in one, so strenuously did he apply himself. When he went in to supper, Starr sat there, owl-eyed and pretending innocence. His entrance evidently disrupted some kind of a eulogy Nels was delivering to Stewart. &ldquo;Sidway, I&rsquo;ve got to hand it to you,&rdquo; said the rancher, warmly. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re a glutton for work.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t hate work, sir.&rdquo; Starr sat up in mild reproof. &ldquo;Heah, you queer duck from Oregon, you aint no hawg fer eatin&rsquo;, I&rsquo;ll tell you thet. An&rsquo; if you keep on doin&rsquo; ten greasers&rsquo; work on an empty stummick you&rsquo;ll be an angel in heaven.&rdquo; &ldquo;Been off my feed lately,&rdquo; Lance admitted, after the laugh subsided. &ldquo;Sidway, are you any good at figures?&rdquo; asked Stewart. &ldquo;My accounts are in a tangle. I never was any good at them. Nels can&rsquo;t add two and two to make four. And Starr never went to school.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, Boss! The hell I didn&rsquo;t. I can read an&rsquo; write some.&rdquo; &ldquo;Stewart, your daughter can, I&rsquo;ll bet. It must have cost you a lot to educate her. Why not make her your bookkeeper?&rdquo; &ldquo;I wouldn&rsquo;t have Madge see what a poor businessman I am for anything.... Can you straighten out my accounts for me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Be glad to, Boss. I had a course in bookkeeping. I&rsquo;m not so hot at it, but ordinary figures are not beyond me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, I&rsquo;ll bet our new range hand is hot all around,&rdquo; interposed Starr. &ldquo;Ren, he started well. But any fool can start. It&rsquo;s stickin&rsquo; to the finish that counts.&rdquo; The cowman&rsquo;s lazy drawl of humor recalled Madge&rsquo;s subtle expression of the very same thought. &ldquo;Shall I come up right away?&rdquo; asked Lance. &ldquo;No. I&rsquo;ll fetch the books down. I had to hide them from Madge. She came home full of the Old Nick. She&rsquo;d be curious, and maybe offended, if she found out about it.&rdquo; When Stewart was gone, Ren stared solemnly at Lance. &ldquo;Pard, did you get thet? Full of the Old Nick!&rdquo; &ldquo;Rest! You cain&rsquo;t have a rest. Never again so long as you live! Thet&rsquo;s what Bonita did to me, only not anyways so bad.... Pard, I smelled hard likker on Majesty&rsquo;s breath!&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? When did she come home?&rdquo; &ldquo;Couple hours ago. She was sweeter&rsquo;n a basket of roses. But I got a scent like a wolf&rsquo;s.&rdquo; &ldquo;What of that? She drinks. All these college girls drink. It&rsquo;s nothing. No more than cigarettes.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. I read the magazines an&rsquo; go to the movies. But, pard, it&rsquo;s different in Majesty&rsquo;s case. It worries me an&rsquo; Nels.&rdquo; &ldquo;And why? You&rsquo;re wasting your time.&rdquo; &ldquo;Son,&rdquo; interposed Nels, gravely. &ldquo;Gene Stewart was the hardest drinkin&rsquo; cowboy on this range. He was a drunkard. For ten years he swore off. Thet was on account of Madeline. Then he drank again&mdash;oncet in a long while&mdash;an&rsquo; he does now. When he said Majesty come home full of the Old Nick, me an&rsquo; Ben hed the same idee. Did he mean drink? An&rsquo; is he afeared his girl has inherited his weakness for strong likker?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, fellows, the boss didn&rsquo;t mean that,&rdquo; expostulated Lance. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sure he didn&rsquo;t. He just meant mischief.... My God, that&rsquo;d be a tough spot for Stewart! He&rsquo;s one grand guy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you said it. Pard, if she asked you to ride in town with her how&rsquo;n hell did you keep from it?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve got a job and a sense of responsibility. Besides, she just wants to make a&mdash;a chump out of me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Son, don&rsquo;t you let her,&rdquo; declared Nels. &ldquo;Wal, I hope to Gawd you can keep from it,&rdquo; added Ren, fervently. &ldquo;Say, Sid, there&rsquo;ll be high jinks goin&rsquo; on if them girls air the least like Majesty. Comin&rsquo; day after tomorrow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lord help us!&rdquo; &ldquo;Son, it shore looks like you&rsquo;d hev to be the Lord. Fer all of us air under her thumb,&rdquo; said Nels, so very earnestly that Lance could not laugh. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s telling me, Nels,&rdquo; replied Lance, and went to his own room. He stumbled over something soft, then ran into a chair that had not been there in the morning. He could not locate his table. Even in the pitch blackness the room felt different and smelled differently. &ldquo;What in the deuce has come off?&rdquo; he muttered, and taking out his matches, he struck one. This colorful clean-smelling room could not be his. Yes, it was&mdash;because he heard Nels and Ren talking through the partition. Burning his fingers in his astonishment he struck another match to light the lamp. But where was his plain, cheap, stinking lamp? Here was a shining one of brass with a big white globe. Rugs on the floor, curtains at the two windows, a dresser with a fine mirror, pictures on the walls, a new three-quarter bright-blanketed bed where his bunk had been, a washstand with colored ware upon it, and towels the quality of which no cowboy before had ever felt, a comfortable Morris chair beside his table, and... but his roving gaze encountered a striking photograph in a silver frame upon his table. Majesty Stewart! With a groan he took the picture and fell into the chair, to stare down upon the lovely face, the speaking eyes, the bare neck of this girl who had bewitched him. &ldquo;Damn you! damn you!&rdquo; he whispered, softly. It seemed a long while before he became aware of whispers and low laughs in Nels&rsquo; room. &ldquo;Hey, pard, air you daid?&rdquo; came through the wall. &ldquo;No! But I wish I were,&rdquo; shouted Lance. At that juncture Stewart stamped into Nels&rsquo; room. Lance hid the picture, and hurried out, and into the ranchman&rsquo;s presence. &ldquo;Here you are, Jack-of-all-trades,&rdquo; said Stewart, gayly, and he opened a ledger on Nels&rsquo; table. &ldquo;Balanced proper up to this page and date. And there&rsquo;s a year and more of figures. Sidway, if you make sense of these I&rsquo;ll be obliged.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll hop to it, Boss.&rdquo; It was midnight when Lance straightened out those accounts. The last entry was of seven hundred and thirty steers sold at thirty-five dollars a head&mdash;payment not yet received. Among the bank statements, papers and correspondence were a batch relative to Madge Stewart&rsquo;s income and expenses. Over a period of time a yearly income of sixty thousand dollars had diminished until at the present it had shrunk to a few thousands. The correspondence indicated that from time to time bonds and stocks had been transferred from Madeline Stewart&rsquo;s account to that of her daughter. &ldquo;Gosh! I wonder if Stewart really meant me to see these,&rdquo; pondered Lance. &ldquo;All as plain as print! Mother and father sacrificing themselves to the extravagance of spoiled daughter! And she doesn&rsquo;t know it!&mdash;Can you beat that?&rdquo; Lance&rsquo;s troubled mind yielded to the exhaustion of a hard day and toilsome hours with figures, and he slept. Ren&rsquo;s pounding on his door awakened him. After breakfast Stewart appeared and Lance brought him the ledger. &ldquo;All done, sir, and not so bad except for&mdash;for these,&rdquo; said Lance. &ldquo;Accounts, you know, of your daughter.&rdquo; &ldquo;Evidently you did. Of course, I went over them. I&rsquo;m sorry, sir.&rdquo; &ldquo;If she ever found this out...&rdquo; &ldquo;Stewart, she won&rsquo;t from me,&rdquo; interrupted Lance, hurriedly, hoping to relieve the rancher of embarrassment. &ldquo;And&mdash;as for your own accounts, sir&mdash;they&rsquo;re not so bad as you led me to believe. When you receive the money for that batch of cattle sold the other day, you can pay your debts and have around five thousand dollars left.&rdquo; &ldquo;No!&mdash;Sidway, you&rsquo;re&mdash;you&rsquo;re.... it&rsquo;d be too good to be true.&rdquo; &ldquo;Maybe you did not figure out just what you&rsquo;d receive for the cattle.&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t at that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, it comes to twenty-five thousand, five hundred and fifty dollars. Quite a lump sum, sir.&rdquo; &ldquo;I must have made a big mistake on the wrong side.&rdquo; &ldquo;You evidently did.&rdquo; &ldquo;I was thinking most of Madeline,&rdquo; replied Stewart, his dark eyes softening. &ldquo;Sidway, I reckon Starr is right about you.&rdquo; &ldquo;That knock-kneed windbag! Now what&rsquo;d he say?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t remember it all. One thing stuck, though. He called you a whiz.&rdquo; Lance felt that he had never received a compliment that had pleased him more. He went to work that day and drove Starr to distraction and dragged him home a cripple. &ldquo;Look at me, Nels,&rdquo; whined Ren, wet with sweat, grimy and ragged. &ldquo;Hey, Sid, who&rsquo;s this heah nigger you fetched in?&rdquo; drawled Nels. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s me, Nels. Me!&mdash;Worked to death by thet fiend. An&rsquo; what I hate most in all this world is diggin&rsquo; postholes!&rdquo; &ldquo;We finished that corral, didn&rsquo;t we? Now we can have some peace when Manuel and Jose wrangle Miss Stewart&rsquo;s horses.... Oh boy&mdash;tomorrow!&rdquo; &ldquo;Me for the hills!&rdquo; ejaculated Ren. * * * * * Lance was waiting for Starr the following night at sunset. Inside Nels was banging pans in unusual excitement. &ldquo;Pard, what&rsquo;n hell&rsquo;s wrong with you?&rdquo; demanded Ren, staring. &ldquo;Behold a&mdash;a&mdash;devastated man!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, whatever&rsquo;n hell thet means you shore air it.... So you obeyed them orders?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. They were Stewart&rsquo;s.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; you had to ride in town with Majesty&mdash;all alone&mdash;and meet her friends&mdash;all them peaches we jest know air comin&rsquo;&mdash;an&rsquo; be a swell lady&rsquo;s guy?&rdquo; &ldquo;You said it!&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd, how tough! Jest the rottenest break ever. Wait till I bed down my hawse an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll be ready to be deevasstated.&rdquo; After supper Ren got up to help Nels with the dishes and he said: &ldquo;Okay now, pard. Shoot! I reckon I&rsquo;m strong enough now.&rdquo; &ldquo;What do you want to know?&rdquo; &ldquo;Tell us aboot Majesty&rsquo;s outfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, the boys are all nice clean-cut college chaps. You&rsquo;ll like them, especially the big football player, Snake Elwell. He&rsquo;s a regular fellow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, nix on the fellers. It&rsquo;s the gurls Nels an&rsquo; me want to heah about.&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell. But thet ain&rsquo;t tellin&rsquo; us how they stacked up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Can&rsquo;t you wait till you lamp them?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. Me an&rsquo; Nels hev artistic feelin&rsquo;s.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, here goes&mdash;the way I got it. Allie Leland first, evidently Madge&rsquo;s best friend, a slim stylish girl with gray eyes, the peach of the bunch, I&rsquo;d say, though not in looks. Next Maramee Joyce, brown beauty built like Jean Harlow, a knockout. Next a little southern girl, looks like sixteen but must be twenty-two. Dark, vivacious, with a smile that would drive any man nutty, and a sweet southern accent. Nels will fall for her. Then Pequita Nelson. Part Spanish, Ren. Creamy olive skin, great dreamy sloe-black eyes, willowy and graceful. Blue-blood, pard.... Then Selma Thorne, a blonde that, if you never saw Madge, would do the trick. And last Beulah Allen.... Whew! Ren, here&rsquo;s a peach that&rsquo;s a composite of honey, dynamite and autumn leaves of red and gold. Pretty! Why, she&rsquo;s so pretty I couldn&rsquo;t take my eyes off her. Red-headed, roguish-eyed, and a shape! What&rsquo;s more she&rsquo;s a devil clear down to her toes.&rdquo; &ldquo;Pard!&rdquo; gasped Ren, utterly fascinated. &ldquo;What&rsquo;n hell air we up agin? It was bad enough with only Majesty heah. We shore air a deevasstated outfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, you should have heard the whoop those college tenderfeet let out when they saw Bonita.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, I&rsquo;ll be liable to shoot a laig off one of them,&rdquo; growled Ren. * * * * * Work on the ranch for Lance and Ren, except an occasional and brief overseeing of laborers brought out from town, practically ceased. Their jobs took on manifold aspects. They had to drive and to ride, especially the latter. The only girl guest who knew anything about horses was Dixie Conn. Madge&rsquo;s horses were all too spirited for tenderfeet. Lance and Ren disagreed as to Madge&rsquo;s own ability to handle several of her mounts. &ldquo;Say, Ren, you&rsquo;re all wet,&rdquo; protested Lance. &ldquo;She was a swell horsewoman once. Nels vouched for that. But she has been to college for four years. She&rsquo;s forgotten a lot. Besides she&rsquo;s out of condition. She&rsquo;s soft, if you get what I mean.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cain&rsquo;t you lay off yore grouch?&rdquo; complained Ren. &ldquo;Majesty is okay.&rdquo; &ldquo;So far as looks are concerned, yes. She looks grand. And that&rsquo;s all you see. Ren, you ought to be back at that garage.&rdquo; The expression of Starr&rsquo;s face became so peculiar, and a giggle of Bu Allen sounded so gleeful that Lance turned toward the wide stable door. Madge had entered and she had heard him. Likewise had Rollie Stevens and Nate Salisbury, who were with her. The others appeared coming down the lane. The purple fire in Madge&rsquo;s eyes was no new catastrophe for Lance. As a matter of fact, he had never seen it blaze for anyone or anything except him. Nevertheless this time, as always, it stimulated him to battle. Perhaps he labored under the delusion that he was right, but so long as he believed so he would not give in. &ldquo;Ren, saddle Dervish for me,&rdquo; ordered Madge, quietly. Lance stepped forward and laid an ungentle hand on the cowboy. &ldquo;Miss Stewart, please forgive my interference. But you should not ride Dervish&mdash;just yet. You&mdash;he...&rdquo; &ldquo;I heard you express your opinions to Ren,&rdquo; she interrupted, in a tone that made Lance feel as if he were the scum of the earth. &ldquo;You can save your breath.&rdquo; &ldquo;That I won&rsquo;t do so long as I am a cowboy on this ranch,&rdquo; replied Lance, coolly, as he found himself. &ldquo;I have a duty here&mdash;to your father&mdash;and through him to you.... Dervish is a bad actor. He has not been worked out. Besides he does not like you, Miss Stewart. It&rsquo;s dangerous for you to mount him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Majesty, listen to Sidway,&rdquo; interposed Rollie, his fine face earnest. &ldquo;That horse looks skittish to me.&rdquo; Dixie Conn backed up Stevens, and the other girls apparently fell in line. This, Lance knew, was only adding fuel to the fire. He believed that if they only had advised it, Madge would have been amenable. But Lance Sidway was waving a red flag in her face. &ldquo;Ren, do as you&rsquo;re ordered,&rdquo; said Madge. &ldquo;Miss Majesty, mebbe Lance is right about this.&rdquo; &ldquo;I know I&rsquo;m right,&rdquo; Lance said, earnestly. &ldquo;Nels agreed with me. He saw you on Bellefontaine the other day, and Bell isn&rsquo;t half the horse this Dervish is. May I repeat what Nels said to me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Why, yes, if it pleases you so much,&rdquo; rejoined Madge. &ldquo;He told me to keep you off the wicked horses if I could.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels! The old traitor! He taught me to ride.&rdquo; &ldquo;He said also that when you were sixteen there wasn&rsquo;t your beat in this state.... Coming from Nels, Miss Stewart, that is the very highest compliment.&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t say that. I think, though, you act like sixteen or under.... Will you force me to go to your father?&rdquo; &ldquo;You wouldn&rsquo;t dare!&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, I would.&rdquo; &ldquo;Go ahead. It&rsquo;ll be a relief to be rid of you. By the time you find Dad I&rsquo;ll be far out on the range.&rdquo; &ldquo;Miss Stewart, he will hold me responsible if you are thrown.&rdquo; &ldquo;So that&rsquo;s it? Thinking of your job! It&rsquo;s not too sure, at that.&rdquo; Lance gave up, and went back to saddling Pinto. Starr, at Madge&rsquo;s order, led the slender racy Dervish out of his stall. Lance heard the cowboy curse under his breath. He also heard Allie Leland, and some of the other girls, taking his part against Madge. And Lance&rsquo;s ears burned with something besides resentment. All these guests of Madge&rsquo;s had been fine to him, and Bu Allen more than friendly. Lance put Pequita Nelson up on Pinto, and hurried on to saddle Leatherstocking. The young men were having the fun of saddling their own horses. Lance liked this bunch of college boys better than he had anticipated. At last all the girls were up except Madge and she was leading Dervish out into the open. Ren was with her. Lance hurried to get astride Umpqua. The others, except Allie Leland, rode out toward the range. &ldquo;Sidway, go with the others,&rdquo; called Madge. He waited to see her put a foot into Starr&rsquo;s hand and go sailing upon Dervish. She was not in the least afraid of him. There was a red flush in her cheeks, a smoldering fire in her eyes. Lance had to admire her for more than the superb and lovely figure she made on the roan. Then Madge and the Leland girl passed him to join the others. Dervish acted all right, Lance thought, but Madge was holding him in. But could she hold him if he broke into a run or could she stay on if he wanted to pitch? Lance gambled that she would fail in the latter event, anyway. Starr joined him and they loped to catch up with the others. &ldquo;Ren, you four flusher, why didn&rsquo;t you stand by me?&rdquo; queried Lance, with irritation. &ldquo;By thunder, I should of,&rdquo; he replied, contritely. &ldquo;But Majesty always kids me along&mdash;makes me reckon I&rsquo;m a helluva feller.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? Well, if you&rsquo;d come strong, we might have avoided a risk.&rdquo; &ldquo;She&rsquo;ll ride thet nag.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh, I hope so. She handles him great. But Ren, damnit! That&rsquo;s a horse&mdash;a mean horse! And she can&rsquo;t weigh more than a hundred and ten.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fifteen, pard, and she&rsquo;s strong. I&rsquo;ll admit, though, I forgot she hadn&rsquo;t been ridin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right, let&rsquo;s go. I&rsquo;ll try to make Madge think I don&rsquo;t care a damn if she breaks her neck.... Fact is, I&mdash;I don&rsquo;t. But I&rsquo;ll keep an eye on her.&rdquo; &ldquo;Me too, pard. Ain&rsquo;t they a swell lookin&rsquo; outfit? I wish Bonita was heah. Majesty said I could ask her. But I knowed I couldn&rsquo;t see anyone else, then... hey!...&rdquo; Lance rode away from the loquacious cowboy and steadied down conscientiously to his job. Once out in the grass and the sage there was much less danger of accidents. The girls had listened to reason, if their hostess had not. And except Dixie Conn, they were all too scared of horses to try any stunts. Dixie and Madge forged ahead, and Lance kept a position that would enable him to overtake Dervish, if he bolted. But nothing happened across the sage flat to the pine knoll five miles away. Madge led them up to the top of that, then down, and over the rolling range land toward the foothills. Half an hour of lope and trot brought them to the slope. &ldquo;Majesty,&rdquo; screamed Maramee Joyce. &ldquo;For Pete&rsquo;s sake&mdash;hold on! I&rsquo;m dying!&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve got that&mdash;awful stitch in my&mdash;side,&rdquo; cried Selma Thorne. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll rest,&rdquo; replied Madge, merrily. &ldquo;But how in the world will you girls ever make it up into the mountains?&rdquo; &ldquo;But you&rsquo;re&mdash;not going soon?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ah! this is swell, Majesty!&rdquo; &ldquo;Majesty, let&rsquo;s leave these tenderfeet behind on that trip,&rdquo; suggested Snake Ewell, good-naturedly. &ldquo;What? You big hunk of protoplasm!&rdquo; exclaimed Bu Allen, her pretty face scarlet, her roguish eyes snapping, her red hair disheveled. &ldquo;Why, astride that horse you look as much like Sidway as I do like a rodeo queen.&rdquo; At length when they were rested Madge gave the word: &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s go! And step on it!&rdquo; As they swept off with merry screams and shouts Lance, with an eye ever on Dervish, saw that he meant business. He balked. And when Madge laid on the spurs he began to pitch. Lance in a few jumps had Umpqua beside her, but as he reached for the roan&rsquo;s head Madge cried: &ldquo;Let him alone!&rdquo; &ldquo;But he&rsquo;ll pile you up.&rdquo; &ldquo;He will not!&rdquo; As bucking horses went, Dervish would have been mean for any rider. But to Lance&rsquo;s surprise Madge stayed in the saddle. Bent double, red-eyed and infuriated, the roan bucked all over the flat, and failed to dislodge the girl. She had her spurs dug into him and sat her saddle as if a part of it. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re riding him, cowgirl!&rdquo; yelled Lance, carried away with her spirit and the spectacle she made. Then Dervish, succeeding in getting the bit between his teeth, bolted away across the valley, in the opposite direction from the ranch. It took only a glance to see that the roan was a runaway horse and that he would eventually get the best of his rider. Lance spurred Umpqua after him. By now the others were a couple of miles distant toward the ranch, and they were not yet aware of Madge&rsquo;s predicament. The roan was fast. Lance had to urge Umpqua into his top speed to gain at all. And he saw that it was going to be a race. Madge fought her mount with all her might. If she heard Lance yell to let the horse run she gave no sign. The girl had evidently been jolted by the bucking, and now she was spending the last of her strength. She would be thrown. Then after a grueling run Lance drew close to the roan. Madge showed signs of distress. She was beginning to sway. &ldquo;Drop your bridle!&rdquo; yelled Lance. &ldquo;Grab the pommel! Hold on!&rdquo; She heard him and obeyed. That saved her from an immediate spill. Umpqua thundered closer and closer until his nose passed the roan&rsquo;s flank. But again Madge was swaying. She was near a fall and the ground was rocky, and rough with hummocks. Desperately goading his horse, Lance gained inch by inch, until he stretched out a clutching hand. She had the sense to shake her feet loose from the stirrups. But that loss of stability broke her seat in the saddle. She was in the air when Lance caught her in a grip of steel and swung her up before him. &ldquo;Oh!&rdquo; she screamed, wildly. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re tearing my flesh!&rdquo; Lance let her go, to slip her into the crook of his arm, and hold her across his saddle. Umpqua was excited, too, and hard to slow down. &ldquo;Whoa! Steady, old boy!&rdquo; called Lance, over and over again. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ve got her.... There, Ump!&mdash;Easy now&mdash;easy!&rdquo; At length Lance halted the horse and then turned his attention to the girl. Her face lay high up on his left arm, near his shoulder, and it was white. The lipstick on her strained lips made a startling contrast. &ldquo;Gosh&mdash;I&rsquo;m&mdash;sorry I had to hurt&mdash;you,&rdquo; he said, haltingly. &ldquo;But I&mdash;couldn&rsquo;t help it. That damned&mdash;roan can run. Lucky to catch you&mdash;at all.&rdquo; &ldquo;How strong you are!&rdquo; she exclaimed, her eyes, darkly dilated, upon him. &ldquo;You had the muscles of my back. I&rsquo;ll bet I can&rsquo;t wear my new formals very soon.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shall I get down? Can you ride my horse?&rdquo; asked Lance, hurriedly. &ldquo;I feel very comfortable where I am.... Lance, I deserve it. I was wrong&mdash;bullheaded&mdash;vain. You were right.... Now does that soothe your wounded vanity?&rdquo; &ldquo;My feelings don&rsquo;t count. But I don&rsquo;t remember that vanity entered into it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Damn you anyway, cowboy!&rdquo; she exclaimed, broodingly, passionate eyes upon him in speculation. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s not very kind,&rdquo; returned Lance, beginning to weaken under another kind of strain. She was resting in his arms, her head now on his shoulder. A little color began to creep into her cheeks. Lance almost collapsed under a terrific longing to kiss her. &ldquo;Miss Stewart, I had a hunch about this horse.&rdquo; &ldquo;For the love of Mike stop calling me Miss. Why don&rsquo;t you cuss me out?&rdquo; she replied, hotly. &ldquo;To tell the truth I&mdash;I don&rsquo;t know why,&rdquo; answered Lance, lamely. He sensed that fatality for him consisted in being with her, and this close contact was insupportable. If he did not get away from it instantly he could not answer for himself. She was in his arms and if she did not like it, she was acting a part. Then Lance saw Starr approaching in a long sweeping gallop, and the others a mile or more behind. &ldquo;They&rsquo;re coming,&rdquo; said Lance, in relief, as he carefully slid with her out of the saddle. &ldquo;Can you stand?&rdquo; &ldquo;I can&mdash;if you hold me.&rdquo; And she swayed against him. &ldquo;Miss... Madge, you&rsquo;re not hurt so&mdash;bad,&rdquo; he protested. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s what you say. My back is broken.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nonsense!&rdquo; cried Lance, in alarm, and he turned her round to feel of her. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s just bruised&mdash;sore. I must have pinched you.&rdquo; She squealed as he felt to see if he had broken a rib. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re a swell western girl&mdash;I don&rsquo;t think. Can&rsquo;t you take it?&rdquo; &ldquo;God only knows!&rdquo; he responded, with an inward groan. &ldquo;Here&rsquo;s Ren. And the others are coming. Sit down. I&rsquo;ll ride after Dervish.&rdquo; He found upon releasing her that she could stand easily enough. &ldquo;What&rsquo;n hell come off?&rdquo; shouted Starr, as he leaped from his saddle. &ldquo;Only me,&rdquo; laughed Madge. Lance thankfully galloped off to catch Dervish, now contentedly grazing half a mile away. What a girl! He was slipping&mdash;slipping. Then his softer agitation burned away in a tumult, some of which emotion was wrath. He had not had half a chance. To save her life, or at least, a nasty spill, was just his hard luck. All in a second to find her in his arms! Hell! What was a girl like that for? Her lying lovely eyes would make an imbecile out of a cigar-store Indian. Yet there seemed to be something so sweet, so square about her. If only she had not hated him, maybe he never would have known of her other nature! But that would have been worse. At length he caught Dervish, and by the time he had returned to the waiting group he was outwardly his cool self again. &ldquo;Did you beat him?&rdquo; asked Madge. &ldquo;No. I never beat horses.&rdquo; Ren glowered at the sweating roan, as he stood meekly, his racy head drooping. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll beat him some day, believe me.&rdquo; &ldquo;I wonder&mdash;why is he so kind to dumb brutes,&rdquo; said Madge, cryptically. &ldquo;Listen, friends. Sidway saved my neck. After I refused to listen to him&mdash;insulted him! I am a cat. Now you shall see me apologize.&rdquo; She turned to Lance in one of her bewildering flashes. &ldquo;Lance, I am sorry. I beg your pardon. You were swell to keep your temper&mdash;and stay a gentleman. I&rsquo;ll tell Dad. And I&rsquo;ll not get on Dervish very soon again&mdash;if ever.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s just fine,&rdquo; replied Lance, heartily. &ldquo;Now you ride Umpqua home. He&rsquo;s gentle and easy.&rdquo; &ldquo;What will you do?&rdquo; she asked. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll ride Dervish. He&rsquo;s worked off his edge now.&rdquo; He helped her mount and shortened the stirrups while the others expressed their relief in various ways. The girls, especially, had been frightened. The boys, except Rollie Stevens, quickly recovered their spirits. Then just as the cavalcade got into motion, Bu Allen said slyly: &ldquo;Lance, if I ride Dervish some time will you be a hero for me?&rdquo; The door opened to disclose Beulah Allen on the threshold. She wore a henna gown that matched her hair, cut to expose her creamy arms and neck. Her charm appeared considerably magnified. &ldquo;Good evening, Lance. Here I am,&rdquo; she said, archly. Lance awkwardly returned her greeting, then: &ldquo;So I see. Who&rsquo;s with you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m alone. I had a scrap with Snake, so I thought I&rsquo;d hunt you up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell.&mdash;Only, what&rsquo;ll Snake do to me?&rdquo; &ldquo;He hasn&rsquo;t any strings on me. We&rsquo;ve been engaged several times and broken it off as often. Tonight is the last.&rdquo; Lance had arisen, and now he stood looking at her, fully aware of her seductiveness, and half inclined to yield to it. &ldquo;How swell you are here! Madge got a kick out of dolling up these rooms. Isn&rsquo;t she a peach? Always playing Santa Claus!&rdquo; &ldquo;Indeed, she&rsquo;s very kind. Which reminds me&mdash;in the excitement today I forgot to thank her.&rdquo; &ldquo;She hates to be thanked.... Aren&rsquo;t you going to ask me in?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. But I&rsquo;ll come out,&rdquo; replied Lance, and taking his sombrero he joined her and led her off the porch. She took his arm and remarked that the night and the full moon were made for love. &ldquo;Yeah?&mdash;But how about a guy, who if he fell, would be down for good?&rdquo; &ldquo;You?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m telling you. Beulah, you&rsquo;re one attractive kid. I like you. I&rsquo;ll be glad to help you with your riding&mdash;as you asked me. But don&rsquo;t get me in bad with Snake Elwell. He might beat hell out of me.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know about that. Snake can run with a football. But he gets hurt easily. Always crippled.&rdquo; &ldquo;You little devil!&rdquo; laughed Lance. &ldquo;Honest now, isn&rsquo;t Snake in love with you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes,&rdquo; she admitted, reluctantly. &ldquo;But he&rsquo;s not alive.&rdquo; &ldquo;Beulah, I&rsquo;ve met a lot of young fellows. Elwell is not flashy. He&rsquo;s a rough diamond. He&rsquo;s a regular guy. If you liked him well enough to be engaged to him you oughtn&rsquo;t to play fast and loose with him.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;What do you call this? Coming to my quarters after me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Lance, if you must be serious, I came because I felt a little out of it tonight. There&rsquo;s an odd girl, you know. Snake belongs to the same fraternity as the other boys. But I don&rsquo;t belong to Majesty&rsquo;s sorority.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I see.... But she wouldn&rsquo;t slight you?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. She&rsquo;s a thoroughbred&mdash;a real sport. And I&rsquo;d be crazy about her if she&rsquo;d let me. She&rsquo;s just a little aloof with me. And I&rsquo;m as proud as the dickens. So when Snake made me mad, I beat it to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sure flattered. Let&rsquo;s walk down to the village cantina, and have a Mexican cone.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hello, Bonita. Ren is looking for you,&rdquo; hazarded Lance, with a meaning glance. When her dusky eyes dilated widely he knew he had hit some kind of a mark. Before he and Beulah had finished their ice cream, Bonita left with her escort. The incident determined Lance to pay more attention to Danny Main&rsquo;s pretty daughter. It developed that Beulah had an intense interest in motion pictures, about which Lance talked at length, and in fact all the way up the hill to the ranch house. She led him in through the corridor to the brightly lighted living room, where Madge and part of her guests sat at two card tables, and the others grouped around Stewart and his wife. Most of them were dressed in white. Lance had to bear the sight of Madge supremely lovely in filmy blue. Their entrance put a stop to games and conversation. Beulah, flushed and radiant, made the most of the situation. It invoked various greetings, all full of fun and interest. &ldquo;Beulah, you look stunning,&rdquo; observed Madge. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s your recipe for such glamour? My cowboy!... Where&rsquo;d you pick him up?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Lance came up after me,&rdquo; returned Beulah, sweetly. &ldquo;He took me to the village&mdash;for a cone.&rdquo; &ldquo;For a which?&rdquo; &ldquo;Cone?&rdquo; &ldquo;You mean ice-cream cone?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. It was swell. I&rsquo;d have liked one of those Mexican drinks we had in town, but I guess Lance doesn&rsquo;t buy drinks for girls.... We saw that pretty Bonita. Say, she&rsquo;s got rr!&rdquo; &ldquo;Come on, Barg,&rdquo; spoke up Nate Salisbury. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s drive down and grab a cone.&rdquo; And the two young men went tearing out. &ldquo;Mr. Sidway, do you play bridge?&rdquo; asked Madge, politely. &ldquo;I tried it once. Didn&rsquo;t get the hang of it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Poker.&rdquo; &ldquo;Of course. Ren plays poker. We&rsquo;ll have you up some night.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks a lot. I&rsquo;ll hate to take your money. But I&rsquo;ll come.... I&rsquo;m glad to see you okay, after your run on Dervish.&rdquo; &ldquo;I may look okay in front.... See, you ironfisted cowboy!&rdquo; And Madge arose to turn her back. The V-shaped opening in her gown extended clear to her waistline. About half way down, disfiguring her lovely back, showed black and blue marks of a ruthless hand. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sorry!&rdquo; burst out Lance, his surprise and regret checking other feelings. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s terrible.... But, Miss Stewart, how could I help it?&rdquo; Madge&rsquo;s slow smile might have promised much. However, at this point, Stewart called the cowboy: &ldquo;Sidway, I&rsquo;ve had several versions of this runaway. Madge wouldn&rsquo;t say anything. From the looks of her back, though, I&rsquo;d say you laid hold of her hard. Nels whitewashed the accident. Starr didn&rsquo;t see it, nor did the others. Come now, what&rsquo;s your story?&rdquo; &ldquo;Mr. Sidway, take this chair here,&rdquo; interposed Mrs. Stewart, beckoning Lance to a seat beside her. He felt the penetrating kindness of her eyes. &ldquo;Thank you.... Well, really, there wasn&rsquo;t much danger. For a western girl!&rdquo; replied Lance, deprecatingly, with a casual gesture. &ldquo;Dervish began to pitch. Mad&mdash;Miss Stewart stayed in the saddle. Then he nailed the bit and lit out. Ren had gone with the outfit. I chased Dervish across the flat. He can run, that roan. I caught up with him&mdash;and grabbed her&mdash;a little roughly, I&rsquo;m sorry to say.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did you forget my instructions?&rdquo; queried Stewart, his kind eyes twinkling. &ldquo;I forbade Madge to ride Dervish. And I told you not to let her get on any bad horse. You&rsquo;re a judge of horses, aren&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not so good. I&mdash;I&rsquo;m afraid I forgot, sir,&rdquo; returned Lance, not meeting Stewart&rsquo;s eye. &ldquo;Dad, your cowboy is a liar,&rdquo; spoke up Madge, in her rich voice, that now had a little ring. &ldquo;In fact he&rsquo;s an awful liar.... He advised me not to ride Dervish. He insisted that I must not. He made me furious by threatening to hunt you up. But you know your little Madge, Dad.... Dervish worked out fine as long as I held him in. We rested a while. When I mounted again he began to pitch. He made me see red, and hurt me&mdash;took it all out of me. Finding he couldn&rsquo;t unseat me, the devil beat it up the valley. I stayed on somehow. After a while I heard Umpqua pounding at my heels. And Sidway yelled: &lsquo;Let him run! Hold on!&rsquo; But for that I&rsquo;d have gone off. The rocky ground scared me stiff. My arms went dead. I lost my stirrups. Just then, as I pitched out of the saddle, Sidway caught me. He certainly put his brand on me.... But, Dad, he saved me broken bones, perhaps a crushed face&mdash;maybe my life!&rdquo; Lance groaned in spirit to be thus made out a hero, yet her eloquence radiated through him, and added another link to the chain that was fettering him. &ldquo;Sidway, modesty is a becoming trait, but hardly justifies your lying to your boss to save his willful and wayward daughter,&rdquo; said Stewart, mildly. &ldquo;Dad, don&rsquo;t rub it in,&rdquo; called Madge, mirthfully. Then, taking up her cards, &ldquo;Where was I, Allie?&rdquo; The card players settled down to their game again. Mrs. Stewart began to ask Lance about Oregon, and she was so gracious and interested that he found himself telling her of his boyhood home, of his mother and sister, about that sister&rsquo;s malady, and how he had left college to take Umpqua to Hollywood, how wonderfully the great horse had made good, how he loved him and would not part with him for the world, and finally how he had set out for Arizona and New Mexico. &ldquo;Majesty Stewart! You trumped my ace!&rdquo; exclaimed Rollie Stevens, incredulously. &ldquo;She&rsquo;s transported!&rdquo; declared Allie. &ldquo;Listening to Mr. Sidway,&rdquo; chimed in Maramee, with a giggle. &ldquo;Majesty, you&rsquo;re not very flattering to us.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, take a coat or wrap, if you&rsquo;re going into the patio,&rdquo; advised her mother. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s one in the hammock, Mom.&rdquo; Rollie Stevens called forcefully after them. &ldquo;Majesty, I&rsquo;ll cut in&mdash;what do you call it&mdash;pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;He will, the sap!&rdquo; whispered Madge. &ldquo;But we&rsquo;ll fool him.&rdquo; The patio was silver-bright under a full moon. The fountain tinkled, there was a stir of leaves, and peep of sleepy birds. Madge caught up a white coat from the first hammock and gave it to Lance. He helped her into it, and turned up the wide collar, and buttoned the upper buttons, his fingers clumsy, while she stood still and gazed up at him with eyes he felt but dared not meet. Then she took his arm and led him along the wide porch, where the shadows of foliage played black on the tiles. Lance was helpless in the thrall of the moment. &ldquo;Lance, it&rsquo;s coming to you right now&mdash;while I&rsquo;m hot under the spell,&rdquo; declared Madge. &ldquo;Beulah Allen has fallen for you. They all saw that. I saw it long ago. What did you do to her? She sailed in positively regal. That was for my benefit, Lance Sidway. Only yesterday I told the girls you didn&rsquo;t neck. What a liar you&rsquo;ve made me out!... They all like you. Dad doesn&rsquo;t throw a fit over every fellow who comes along, not even a cowboy. And Mom!... Young man, do you know you couldn&rsquo;t pull a greater stroke here than that? I listened. It couldn&rsquo;t be a line. All that about your sister! Oh, Lance.... Mom likes you! That is the last straw! My lovely patrician mother!&rdquo; &ldquo;She was just&mdash;moved by my&mdash;my service to you,&rdquo; said Lance, unsteadily. &ldquo;No. Don&rsquo;t start that stuff. This is serious,&rdquo; she rejoined, and halting beyond the last archway, she turned to him in the white moonlight. In that light, shining from the pale oval of her face, her eyes held the sum of all beauty. &ldquo;Isn&rsquo;t it a pity&mdash;I don&rsquo;t like you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Maybe that is lucky for me,&rdquo; he returned, huskily. &ldquo;Lance, are you engaged to any girl in Oregon?&rdquo; &ldquo;No&mdash;indeed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Are you fancy-free?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes,&rdquo; he lied, glibly enough. &ldquo;You made a play for Bonita. Oh, I know. She gave you away&mdash;and herself. I was brazen enough to pump her.... Lance, do you know Ren Starr has a terrible case on Bonita?&rdquo; &ldquo;I found that out pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;You were only playing with her?&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t admit that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aren&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not since I found out about Ren.&rdquo; &ldquo;Listen, these college friends of mine, particularly Barg and Nate, are nuts over that little Mexican hussy. She&rsquo;s half white, yes, but the Latin blood dominates.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bonita isn&rsquo;t quite all that,&rdquo; rejoined Lance, stoutly. &ldquo;She is. And I&rsquo;m a jealous cat. But all this is for Ren&rsquo;s sake.... You seem to be as big as that mountain there. Are you big enough to play Ren&rsquo;s game&mdash;to keep these college devils away from her? They&rsquo;re on the make. One or the other, most likely Nate, will get her.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m afraid I&rsquo;m not quite so&mdash;so big as that,&rdquo; answered Lance, led on and on by the deadly sweetness of her, and by the infernal power of his bleeding vanity. She released his arm and averted her face. Like a cameo the perfect profile shone as if cut out of marble. The night breeze stirred her golden hair. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m disappointed in you&mdash;again.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why should you be, Miss Stewart?&rdquo; he queried, stiffly, fighting a struggle almost vain. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m human&mdash;the same as you. Just no good!&rdquo; &ldquo;How dare you!&rdquo; she cried, wheeling with a startled movement. &ldquo;Smile&mdash;when you say that.&rdquo; But Lance did not smile. She had wanted to be serious and he had told her the truth. Without a word she left him standing there. Lance stepped into the black shadow of the wall, his thoughts whirling, his conscience stinging, his judgment at fault, his love valiantly championing this perverse and wayward beauty. A thousand wild queries did not lodge in his mind, let alone find an answer. There was not any answer to anything. Why had she asked those direct thought-provoking questions? How easy to escape from her if she were only like Beulah! But Madge Stewart had the insidious power to make men believe in her sincerity. Her look was enough to lift any poor masculine fool to the seventh heaven&mdash;to be convinced that he was the one man! Lance&rsquo;s endless ravings were disrupted by approaching voices. Two people were coming down the patio path. Then Madge&rsquo;s silver laughter, a little mocking, froze Lance to the spot. They came clear to the inside wall. &ldquo;Majesty, you drive me mad,&rdquo; came in Rollie Stevens&rsquo; subdued voice. &ldquo;You know you have no use for that cowboy. You told me so. Yet for days now you&rsquo;ve been rotten to me, on his account! Oh, I get it! Sidway hasn&rsquo;t fallen for you&mdash;and that&rsquo;s piqued your vanity. Besides you want his horse. Why don&rsquo;t you give the fellow a break? He&rsquo;s a real man. He&rsquo;s not a sap. But pretty soon he&rsquo;ll fall for you, even if he knows you&rsquo;re not on the level with him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Rollie, I might be in earnest,&rdquo; she scoffed. &ldquo;Rot! Why, Majesty Stewart, only a month ago you said you&mdash;you might marry me.&rdquo; &ldquo;That was a month ago, darling. An age!&rdquo; &ldquo;Majesty, you can&rsquo;t marry a cowboy,&rdquo; he expostulated, incredulously. &ldquo;Rollie, I hadn&rsquo;t thought of that. But&mdash;why couldn&rsquo;t I?&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re a lady of quality, a talented girl. Why he&rsquo;s not of your class. Admitting Sidway is a fine chap&mdash;I like him, Majesty&mdash;you couldn&rsquo;t marry him. Oh, to talk of it is preposterous.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right. Skip it.... Rollie! don&rsquo;t kiss me right here in the moonlight.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet he did,&rdquo; he returned, hoarsely. &ldquo;Who?&rdquo; &ldquo;Your cowboy!&rdquo; &ldquo;He never even thought of it.&rdquo; &ldquo;My word!&mdash;Majesty, can you expect me to believe that?&rdquo; &ldquo;No, I don&rsquo;t.&rdquo; A slight scuffle followed, a protest from the girl, then the soft sounds of kisses. &ldquo;Rollie, you needn&rsquo;t tear my clothes off. Pick up my coat. And remember, my back is too sore for hugging.&rdquo; &ldquo;Darling&mdash;it maddens me&mdash;to taste your lips. I&rsquo;m just wafted...&rdquo; &ldquo;New kind of lipstick! All over your face. And mine too.&rdquo; &ldquo;Majesty, honest to God&mdash;didn&rsquo;t that Sidway even kiss you?&rdquo; &ldquo;No, Rollie. He didn&rsquo;t even try, I&rsquo;m ashamed to admit.&rdquo; &ldquo;He could have kissed you! All the boys kiss you! It was campus talk!&rdquo; &ldquo;You jealous sap! Surely he could have&mdash;and they do. I rather like it. And besides what&rsquo;s a kiss?&rdquo; &ldquo;You know what it leads to, Majesty Stewart.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? Well, it never led me anywhere in particular yet, except to muss my dress and make-up, as you&rsquo;ve done.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sorry. But you drive me wild.... Kiss me good night, sweet. A real one, like you used...&rdquo; &ldquo;There, little boy. Let us go back. I am cold.&rdquo; Then soft footfalls and subdued voices faded away. Lance plunged down the trail like a blind man. He had his answer. * * * * * Every morning Lance awakened under the shadow of impending calamity. What was going to happen next? Or what would Madge be up to doing? It did not make the slightest difference to Lance, only he seemed to be the one doomed to encounter her in moments of stress. Things happened to Madge&rsquo;s guests. Pinto ran off with Beulah; Pequita, who was a poor swimmer, fell or was pushed off the platform into the lake, and nearly drowned; Maramee was kicked in the ankle so badly that she could not ride; Allie lolled around all day on the sandy beach and was blistered by the sun. In spite of the fact that Stewart insisted on Lance&rsquo;s keeping a close watch upon the girls, accidents happened. The boys went off on larks of their own contrivance, and on one occasion became lost less than ten miles from the ranch. Another time they slipped off to town and did not show up at the ranch until late the next day, for which jaunt Madge called them &ldquo;a lot of bums.&rdquo; When, however, the least little thing or something more serious, such as Dervish&rsquo;s bolt, happened to Madge, Lance seemed always to be on the spot. This morning he was morosely counting the occurrences, and wondering if the last two had been strictly bona fide, yet nursing a sense of guilt because of his doubt. There were endless jobs. He was laboring on a corncrib. Ren had taken the boys fishing. And just about the time Lance forgot his woe, there came a wild clamor of screams from the big barn. &ldquo;Sounds scary. Now what the hell?&rdquo; he muttered, darkly, and strode for the barn. The first screams might have been mirth, but those following sent Lance into a run. He dashed up the runway. The girls were in a pandemonium of fright, shrieking, pale as death and wild-eyed. It could not be an ordinary circumstance. Missing Madge from the group Lance yelled: &ldquo;Shut up!&mdash;Where&rsquo;s Madge? What&rsquo;s the matter?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh!&mdash;Oh!&mdash;she&rsquo;ll be killed!&rdquo; &ldquo;Save her, cowboy&mdash;for God&rsquo;s sake!&rdquo; &ldquo;If she falls... it... it&rsquo;ll be terrible!&rdquo; &ldquo;Where is she? What is it?&rdquo; yelled Lance. &ldquo;Girls, if you tell him&mdash;I&rsquo;ll hate you,&rdquo; cried Madge, piercingly from somewhere. &ldquo;Lance Sidway, you get out&mdash;of here! Don&rsquo;t you dare look!&rdquo; At that juncture Beulah Allen ran to Lance, &ldquo;She was swinging on the hay rope&mdash;from one loft to the other.... Sitting on the noose!... Something gave way&mdash;and up she went bang against the roof.... There!&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s out for you, Bu Allen!&rdquo; raged Madge. Then Lance saw Madge up under the roof, hard against the wheel. The noose evidently slipped from her hips, up to her armpits, and had stripped her that far. But Lance saw only those terrible eyes and the scarlet face. &ldquo;Madge, you&rsquo;re in great danger,&rdquo; flashed Lance.... &ldquo;Grab the wheel!&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d rather die&mdash;than&mdash;have you...&rdquo; Frantically he began to drag huge armloads of hay from the loft, flinging them to the girls. They worked with a will. Then Lance dashed to the windlass. It was an old-fashioned hay-fork contrivance, and the heavy reel, owing no doubt to a swinging rock wired on it for a balance, had slipped to jerk her aloft. &ldquo;All right,&rdquo; shouted Lance, as soon as he had loosened the rope and taken a strong grip. &ldquo;Let go the wheel up there.... Down you come.... Hang on to the rope now.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re squeezing me&mdash;to death,&rdquo; cried Madge. Madge reached the floor and the rope went slack. A chorus of tender and commiserating exclamations came from the girls who had surrounded Madge. Bu Allen met Lance with a twinkle in her eyes. Lance made no effort to approach the circle around Madge, who evidently lay prostrate on the hay. He had been forced to expend breath as much through emotion as effort. Bu put a sympathetic hand on his arm. Lance received the impression that the girl, despite her sophistication, was someone to like. &ldquo;Girls&mdash;is&mdash;she hurt?&rdquo; panted Lance. &ldquo;Not that we can tell,&rdquo; replied Allie, who knelt on the hay. Maramee had Madge&rsquo;s head in her lap. Her abbreviated costume had been decently arranged. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;s that dragon killer?&rdquo; asked Madge, her voice weak, but spirit apparently undaunted. Bu Allen dragged Lance over to the pile of hay, where Madge lay, white as a sheet. &ldquo;You would!&rdquo; she exclaimed, with inscrutable eyes on Lance. &ldquo;Would&mdash;would what?&rdquo; &ldquo;Be the one to catch me in that stunt. I used to do it when I was little. It never occurred to me to look at the windlass. I&rsquo;m an idiot. ... Sidway, please promise you&rsquo;ll not tell Nels or Dad, or even Ren.&rdquo; &ldquo;It never happened, Miss Stewart,&rdquo; rejoined Lance, soberly. &ldquo;Help me to the car,&rdquo; she replied, and with Allie and Maramee&rsquo;s assistance arose painfully to her feet. &ldquo;The knot on that damned noose stuck into my back.&rdquo; The girls helped Madge in, and then piled in themselves, with Allie at the wheel. Madge leaned over the door and took hold of the dusty edge of Lance&rsquo;s vest with unsteady fingers. &ldquo;Did I thank you?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. But that&rsquo;s not necessary,&rdquo; returned Lance, hurriedly. &ldquo;I ought to be decent enough.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, you said it,&rdquo; chimed in Beulah. &ldquo;She&rsquo;s just waiting for something big,&rdquo; added Allie softly. &ldquo;Anyway, Lance, we all thank you from the bottom of our hearts.&rdquo; &ldquo;Are those orders?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, they&rsquo;re orders.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks. I&rsquo;ll not be present next time. And I won&rsquo;t attend your funeral!&rdquo; The car rolled away. * * * * * Lance felt inclined to the conviction that it was his careful avoidance of the girls the next few days which kept him out of hot water. Still he had to hear about their mishaps and stunts from Ren, who had been relegated to the job, and who raved rapturously through supper, and then long afterward, to Lance&rsquo;s disgust. It did not help Lance&rsquo;s mood to realize that he listened keenly when he might have gone out of hearing. On the third night, however, Ren, for some reason appeared very glum and silent. Nels ventured a few sly queries. And when Lance added: &ldquo;Has our poodle had his tail pulled?&rdquo; Ren stalked out and stamped to his room. &ldquo;Wal, he ain&rsquo;t often like thet,&rdquo; said Nels, ponderingly. &ldquo;Reckon one of us ought to make a move, anyhow.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll go, Nels,&rdquo; returned Lance, and lighting a cigarette he went out. Approaching Ren&rsquo;s door and seeing that the light was out, Lance knocked and said: &ldquo;Sorry, old man. I was only kidding.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, I know that. It&rsquo;s okay,&rdquo; replied Ren, gruffly. &ldquo;Little off your feed, Ren?&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon.... An&rsquo; I was made out a turrible sucker today!&rdquo; &ldquo;By whom?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, who&rsquo;d you think?&rdquo; &ldquo;Bonita?&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet little hussy!&mdash;Say, she&rsquo;s lost her haid over them boys. I cain&rsquo;t do a damn thing about it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t try. They&rsquo;re only in fun, Ren.&rdquo; &ldquo;Like hell they air!&rdquo; ejaculated the cowboy, bitterly. &ldquo;Ren, I lay off Bonita for your sake. Maybe I shouldn&rsquo;t have. She liked me. And I&rsquo;m your pal, you know.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re damn right you shouldn&rsquo;t. Them boys hev been chasin&rsquo; Bonita an&rsquo; her friends pretty hard lately, an&rsquo; unbeknown to Majesty.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s not so good, Ren.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good!&mdash;It&rsquo;s pretty bad, if you ask me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, I&rsquo;ll walk down and give the kid a spiel, Ren.... But, I forgot. Who made the sucker out of you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Never mind now, pard. I don&rsquo;t want to hear you whoop.&rdquo; Lance strolled off the long porch across the square, and down the road toward the high wall of poplar trees that marked the village. The night was close and warm. Merry voices up by the lake attested to the presence of night bathers. The strumming of a guitar and the lilt of a Spanish love song suited the summer night. At the corner Lance turned left to go down the long avenue of poplars. Lance passed the deserted adobe houses, then the lighted store, and beyond that the noisy cantina. Here there was no one out under the vine-covered trellis. Peeping in he saw a number of Mexicans, but no girls or white men. Lance crossed the street, and in the deep shadow of the other lane of poplars, he went slowly on toward Danny Mains&rsquo; house. When he got to the corner he halted in the shadow. The gate was beyond a little ways. Lance thought he would hang around a little before he went in. There were both lights and music in the Mains&rsquo; cottage. Presently three bareheaded girls appeared, scarfs round their shoulders. The foremost was Bonita. They hesitated, whispering excitedly, and were evidently expecting someone. When Lance called Bonita she gave a start and then approached slowly, while the other girls hung back. He met her at the gate. &ldquo;Hello, kiddo. Where are you going?&rdquo; &ldquo;You look it. Sweet as a wild rose!... Bonita, have you gone back on Ren and me?&rdquo; &ldquo;No indeed. But I never see you&mdash;and him so seldom. He&rsquo;s jealous. Tries to boss me. I won&rsquo;t stand it, Lance.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t blame you. Has he asked you to marry him yet?&rdquo; &ldquo;He has not,&rdquo; she retorted. &ldquo;But he did say I was so&mdash;so bad he wasn&rsquo;t sure he wanted me to.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, that&rsquo;s a tough one.... Bonita, have you been stepping out a little lately?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not so very. Tonight&rsquo;s the first time I&rsquo;ve consented to go to town. Francisca and Maria have both been. I&rsquo;m scared. If Daddy finds it out he&rsquo;ll whip me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Stay home. Come with me to see Ren. He&rsquo;s blue. Let the others go.&rdquo; &ldquo;Listen, honey. Now don&rsquo;t be a little chump. It&rsquo;s all right to go if you refuse to drink. You&rsquo;ll enjoy the movies and dancing.&rdquo; &ldquo;They all drink like fish.&rdquo; &ldquo;Right.&rdquo; And Lance laughed at the frank girl. He drew her close to him and kissed her. &ldquo;Bonita, get a load of this. I like you myself&mdash;more than these college guests of Miss Stewart&rsquo;s. But Ren loves you, dearly. I know it. And I&rsquo;d hate to see you two fail to make a go of it. Now be a good kid. Promise me. I&rsquo;ll make a date with you for tomorrow night, like this, so we can talk.&rdquo; &ldquo;I promise, Lance,&rdquo; she replied, happily, her hands on his arm. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll not drink tonight. If you can&rsquo;t patch it up between Ren and me, it&rsquo;ll not be my fault.&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell! You&rsquo;re okay, Bonita, and Ren is a crabby old sourdough. We&rsquo;ll fix it.... Hello!&mdash;a car?&rdquo; &ldquo;They&rsquo;re coming. Perhaps it&rsquo;d be just as well if you weren&rsquo;t seen.&rdquo; Lance gave her dark head a pat and hurried back into the shadow of the trees. But the car did not come close to the house, and Lance could not ascertain to whom it belonged. The girls ran out and were taken in with merry greetings. As the car went on, Lance thought he saw a figure hanging on behind. Presently, being certain of this, he took to the road and strolled down the hill. He knew that if someone had stolen a ride, he would not stick on for long, not on desert roads at the speed these fellows liked. This car, however, did not appear to open up, until it reached the level valley floor. Lance kept on, presently reaching the level, where the dry wash and the sand made rough going for a space. At length he gave up and was about to turn back when he almost bumped into someone sitting on the low bank of the road. A little peal of silvery laughter magnified his start. &ldquo;Lance Sidway! I was just gambling with myself how soon you&rsquo;d arrive,&rdquo; said Madge Stewart. &ldquo;Well, I&rsquo;m damned!&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s a tough break. You&rsquo;re not alone?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, I am. Allie was in the plot with me. She was to hang onto the other side. Either she did not get on or she was jolted off. You didn&rsquo;t see her along the road?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. She couldn&rsquo;t have come. I saw only one person hanging on the back of that car.&rdquo; &ldquo;You saw me? When?&rdquo; &ldquo;When the car stopped outside Mains&rsquo; house. I was with Bonita. The other girls waited in the yard.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh! I see. Johnny on the spot!&mdash;Did you get who the boys were?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. That didn&rsquo;t interest me particularly. Bonita told me these boys were too swift for her. What Bonita does is probably none of my affair, but I am interested because of Ren. So I urged her not to drink and she said she wouldn&rsquo;t. Bonita is easily influenced if you go about it right. I&rsquo;ve been Ren&rsquo;s friend, with her, if you know what I mean.&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t until now,&rdquo; returned Madge, bluntly. &ldquo;We girls thought the boys were going to the cantina with the girls. I think it lousy of them, especially of Barg Hillcote. Just engaged to Maramee! There I go spilling my insides.... Lance Sidway, men are all rotten.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah!&rdquo; answered Lance, uncertainly. She had begun to strike him rather singularly, as she had not moved, and she sat leaning back on both hands with one leg up over the bank. In the starlight he could see her lovely face and speaking eyes. &ldquo;Maramee would break her engagement with Barg for this. I would. And she&rsquo;s been so happy.... I hope to goodness Allie didn&rsquo;t get on and fall off.... Surely she&rsquo;ll come soon.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s wrong with you?&rdquo; Lance said suddenly, and he leaned close to Madge, peering at her. &ldquo;Guess I got hurt,&rdquo; she replied, with great inscrutable eyes meeting his. &ldquo;Where?&rdquo; &ldquo;My foot. This one. Thought at first I&rsquo;d sprained my ankle. But I&rsquo;m not sure.&rdquo; &ldquo;Let me see.&rdquo; Lance stepped up on the bank and knelt. She had taken off her shoe and stocking. Her white foot and leg gleamed in the starlight. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t touch it!&rdquo; she cried. But he went right on until she screamed out. &ldquo;All right, all right, touchy!... Let me see you move it.... Flex it!&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I can do that. It doesn&rsquo;t hurt.&rdquo; &ldquo;Your ankle is okay. You&rsquo;ve sprained your instep&mdash;or something. But if you keep off it and use hot water frequently, you&rsquo;ll be all right tomorrow or next day.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll have to walk back home.&rdquo; &ldquo;No you won&rsquo;t. You can&rsquo;t. Let me get your car.&rdquo; &ldquo;But I don&rsquo;t want anyone to know about this, and they will if I come in the car. I&rsquo;ll have to sneak in by the west wing to my room. I&rsquo;ll have to walk.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nonsense. I can easily carry you.&rdquo; She laughed outrageously. &ldquo;But I can. I&rsquo;m strong,&rdquo; protested Lance, earnest, amazed, solicitous. &ldquo;I can throw a hundred pound sack of grain all over the place.&rdquo; &ldquo;Strong? I know you&rsquo;re a perfect Hercules, Mr. Sidway,&rdquo; she said, tauntingly. &ldquo;But I won&rsquo;t have you packing me around.&rdquo; Very carefully she stepped, and moved up the slope. Every time her injured foot touched the ground it must have pained greatly, Lance knew. He put a hand under her arm and half lifted her along. They came into a trail, and that appeared to be easier for Madge. When they arrived at the pines, however, she was tottering. But this girl was the kind that could not quit. &ldquo;Why won&rsquo;t you let me carry you?&rdquo; he asked, suddenly. &ldquo;You did once.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s why.&rdquo; &ldquo;If you&rsquo;re not the queerest, screwiest girl, I&rsquo;ll eat my hat!&rdquo; declared Lance. &ldquo;Yes, when you haven&rsquo;t one!&rdquo; she retorted. When she started on, Lance knew she would not make it much farther. And he bided his time, hot and perplexed. Finally she swore and sobbed almost in the same breath. Without a word more he picked her up in his arms and went on. Shifting his hold, so she would carry more comfortably for her, he said: &ldquo;There. Isn&rsquo;t that better? I hardly feel your weight.&rdquo; &ldquo;Better, indeed. But I fear&mdash;riskier,&rdquo; she returned in a queer voice. Lance had to look at her. Before that, all had seemed well. He was relieved to save her pain. Her face lay high up on his right arm, almost on his breast, turned toward him somewhat, and its lovely proximity grew suddenly exciting. She was looking at him with eyes whose expression he could not fathom. &ldquo;Riskier!&mdash;What do you mean?&rdquo; he demanded. &ldquo;You see I am utterly helpless. You might get a cave-man notion... . Really that wouldn&rsquo;t be so bad. But you probably just kissed Bonita...&rdquo; &ldquo;I did. For Ren&rsquo;s sake&mdash;mostly.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ye gods and little fishes!... And no doubt it was Bu Allen last night. She came in with her lipstick all smeared up. Radiant. Bold as the very devil. And she didn&rsquo;t deny it when we kidded her about you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Miss Stewart, I did not see her last night,&rdquo; protested Lance. &ldquo;That would have depended entirely upon her.&rdquo; &ldquo;How chivalrous! If she had been suffering for contact or release&mdash;or what have you, why you&rsquo;d have been a perfect necker.... Lance, you give me a pain in the neck.&rdquo; &ldquo;I know. But why&mdash;why?&rdquo; he demanded, furiously. &ldquo;It must be because you&rsquo;re a liar.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, Madge Stewart, you give me worse than that&mdash;and it&rsquo;s because you&rsquo;re no good.&rdquo; &ldquo;Let me down. You said that before. I&rsquo;ll die before I&mdash;I&rsquo;ll...&rdquo; &ldquo;Bunk! You can die all right, after I get you home. I hope you do. I hope you choke on your terrible tongue.&rdquo; Anger and intense mortification, and some other emotion began to augment in Lance&rsquo;s consciousness. &ldquo;The girls think you&rsquo;ve the sweetest disposition&mdash;that you&rsquo;re the swellest fellow. My God!&rdquo; And she uttered a tinkling little laugh that cut into Lance like icy blades. &ldquo;Your boy friends think the same of you. But they&rsquo;re a lot of sapheads. They don&rsquo;t know you.&rdquo; &ldquo;You do!&rdquo; &ldquo;Bet your&mdash;sweet life I do. Better than you have any idea,&rdquo; he panted. &ldquo;Rest here, young Lochinvar! or you&rsquo;ll fall. I think, after all, you&rsquo;re not so strong. This magnificent frame is pithy&mdash;like your head.&rdquo; Lance groaned under the excess of his burdens. Halfway up to the house, in one of the little bench parks under the pines, he sat down on a boulder to regain his equilibrium, but he did not let go of Madge.
