He could feel the throb of her against his throbbing. And all at once he happened to think of what she had told Rollie that last night. Under the galvanizing stress of the idea that leaped out of it he arose like a giant and a fiend. He wrapped his long arms closer about her and drew her wholly against his breast. Madge seemed totally calm. Then Lance kissed her, not with any particular feeling, but merely as a preliminary. &ldquo;I thought it was about time,&rdquo; muttered Madge. Then, staggering on under the pines, he kissed her cheeks, her eyes, her hair, her neck&mdash;and when at last she protested, Lance stopped her mouth with his, in an endless passionate kiss which magnified all he had ever bestowed in his life. &ldquo;Damn&mdash;you!&rdquo; she panted, as he moved a moment to breathe. And she began to pound him&mdash;to tear at his hair. &ldquo;You insult me...&rdquo; &ldquo;Mine ought to be&mdash;as good as any of those guys&mdash;and cleaner, by heaven! and fresher&mdash;from lack of promiscuous practice.&rdquo; And bending over, squeezing her face up immovably, he began to kiss her lips like a madman. His kisses choked off her scream. After one frantic and tense struggle she collapsed in his arms. And he kissed her for every step, on under the pines, out upon the drive, almost to the front archway. Keeping outside the drive he passed this, and once in the shrubbery he began again his ravenous tasting of her lips, as if his appetite grew with what it fed upon. But not until he rounded the west wing and reached her window did he realize that her face, her lips, her body had changed. Her eyes were closed tightly&mdash;heavy eyelids dreamy, long curled lashes on her cheeks; her lips bowed, open, sweet with a strange fire; her breast pressed on his. Not until Lance lifted her into the open window did he realize that she had an arm round his neck. He lowered her carefully to the floor. Then he leaned on the sill, spent and devastated. She stirred, and sat up, and laboriously climbed upon the bed. Lance, watching her, expected, yearned for a scourging, bitter enough even for him. But she just looked at him. In the starlight he saw her face as only he would carry it in his heart forever. &ldquo;Majesty,&rdquo; he began, in husky whisper, &ldquo;I...&rdquo; but he could not go on. Not all at once could her wit and intelligence throw off that spell. She found herself rubbing her stockingless leg and ankle. The tightness around her foot, the heat, meant injury in some degree, but she felt no pain. Over and above these sensations thundered the truth, clearing in her mind. She had ranged the gamut of incredible feeling&mdash;from pique, surprise, shock, fury to a sudden overwhelming tumult of love, of her willful changing moods, her wounded vanity, her temper and upflaring hate, and her softening doubt, her endless misgivings and suspicion, that had kept her up and down for days, only to have this shameful assault leave her undone, madly in love at last, stricken forever. &ldquo;I&mdash;I can take it!&rdquo; whispered Madge, with her fluttering hand on her hot lips. She did not weep. She asked no quarter. She had her just deserts. But she was not as he believed&mdash;that shot through her with a passionate pang. From the very beginning everything had worked to her detriment. Her imperious demand for that horse? No&mdash;that was not the first. Her meeting with the gangster Uhl! That had started her wrong with this Oregon boy. And every single thing afterward had gone wrong&mdash;her tempers and her tricks, her insincerity and subterfuge, her nasty tongue and open satire. He must have overheard her saying she rather liked being kissed. That night when Rollie had met her after Lance had infuriated her! But there was nothing to be ashamed of about that. It was true, only on the moment she was torturing Rollie. The cowboy had something these other boys lacked. His recent treatment of her was wholly at variance with that, and seemed inexplicable to Madge. He did not want to kiss her. He did not approve of her. He despised her. He must have possessed some kind of a masculine trait that made her kissing promiscuity intolerable and abhorrent. He was avenging the throng of boys she had kissed and forgotten. There were a thousand slants and angles to this outrageous assault upon her&mdash;only one terrible revelation accruing from them! The doom of love, that she had trifled with so regally and callously, had fallen upon her. How impossible to understand! If Lance Sidway had entered her room that moment and snatched her up.... But he had not known she had been, at the last, taking his kisses and spending her soul in exchange. And suddenly Madge was possessed of an insane rage. She wanted to kill him. It would not be enough to have him horsewhipped and driven away. He must not live to kiss girls like Bonita and have the sunlight shine in his hazel eyes for some other.... &ldquo;Oh, nuts!&rdquo; burst out Madge, baldly, suddenly sick of herself, so weary that the fury drained out of her. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve put this day of reckoning off long enough!&rdquo; Her exclamation must have been heard, for clicking high heels sounded on the stone corridor. &ldquo;Majesty!&rdquo; called Allie, in great eagerness. &ldquo;Did I hear you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I shouldn&rsquo;t wonder. I was cussing. Come in. I&rsquo;ve a story to unfold. What happened to you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Me!&mdash;I was thrown in the dust,&rdquo; whispered Allie. &ldquo;Blinded. I couldn&rsquo;t see a thing. The car was gone and you with it. I felt my way back to my room and washed the dust from my eyes. After that I walked under the pines&mdash;down the road&mdash;watching for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Funny you didn&rsquo;t see that cowboy carrying me or hear him kissing me. Must have sounded like a decisive battle of the world!&rdquo; &ldquo;Be careful, darling. I&rsquo;m a cripple. I fell off the car, too. Hurt my foot.... Help me into my bathroom.&rdquo; There between the two of them, Madge boiled out the pain in her foot, and bound it up, to find then that she could walk without limping. She sent Allie out to find where everybody was, and bade her return to sleep with her. Madge found it good to stretch out in bed, in the dark, and wonder. Presently Allie came back, to feel her way to Madge&rsquo;s side. It was a mutual emotion that caused them to seek each other&rsquo;s arms. &ldquo;Your mother has gone to bed, I think,&rdquo; whispered Allie. &ldquo;Snake was playing checkers with your dad. I told them you were tired. Your dad seemed concerned. &lsquo;Madge tired? that&rsquo;s unheard of.&rsquo; He looked worn himself, poor dear. Majesty, do you know I&rsquo;ve an idea he worries about you and us....&rdquo; &ldquo;Skip it!&mdash;Where were the girls?&rdquo; &ldquo;In their rooms, lolling, fussing, gossiping. Except Bu. She&rsquo;s down the hill looking for cowboys, so Dixie said.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh!&rdquo; cried Madge, poignantly. &ldquo;What ails you, darling? You act kind of queer and talk worse. You&rsquo;re burning up. I&rsquo;m afraid you&rsquo;ve got fever.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fever!&mdash;Ha! Ha!&mdash;Yes, I&rsquo;ve got galloping fever... . And the boys?&rdquo; &ldquo;Down playing pool. Wouldn&rsquo;t take the girls.&rdquo; &ldquo;Frame-up to shield that trio of heels!&rdquo; whispered Madge. &ldquo;All in the know about that date.... Darling, get a load of this. Barg and Dawson and Brand were in that car. And down below they took in Bonita and two of her friends. They went to town.&rdquo; &ldquo;Majesty! Not really.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll say. What do you think of them, Barg especially?&rdquo; &ldquo;For Barg?&mdash;Lousy is too lovely a word. That dirty little bum! Just finally won Maramee, and he pulls such a stunt. Maramee is so happy. She thinks Barg is perfect. It&rsquo;ll break her heart.&rdquo; &ldquo;She must never know. Don&rsquo;t you breathe it.&rdquo; &ldquo;But, darling, they don&rsquo;t mean any harm.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who don&rsquo;t?&rdquo; &ldquo;Why, Barg and the boys.&rdquo; &ldquo;Majesty! Someone will hear you. Cowboy! You mean Sidway? It&rsquo;s none of his business.&rdquo; &ldquo;Isn&rsquo;t it, though? Ren Starr is his pal. Ren has been at Bonita&rsquo;s feet for long. And Lance has been courting her for him. Would he be sore? He was sore tonight, maybe somewhat because I... Allie, that cowboy would call the boys down to the barn...&rdquo; &ldquo;But, honey, nothing terrible has happened yet. It won&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;Majesty, are you crazy? Such talk! What happened?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ha! Ha! So you get that? What wonderful perspicacity, darling! Never mind about me&mdash;about the dual rotten nature that has turned on me ... but what did Lance Sidway mean? Tell me that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, it&rsquo;s beyond the bounds of human possibility that he doesn&rsquo;t love you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why?&rdquo; &ldquo;Because he&rsquo;s a man. And you&rsquo;ve placed him on the damnedest spots. Seeing you every day and every way.... Why, Madge. I was sorry for him the other day at the lake. You in that indecent bathing suit&mdash;the boys wallowing you all over the sand! And he stuck there to save our lives if we got cramps!&rdquo; &ldquo;I saw the look in his eyes. You know he has beautiful eyes, when they&rsquo;re soft.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re as sentimental as Maramee, and as gullible. That cowboy hates my very insides.&rdquo; &ldquo;I can&rsquo;t believe that.&rdquo; &ldquo;But, sweet, listen. For God&rsquo;s sake use some brains. Isn&rsquo;t it conceivable that I should finally fall foul of a real man who sees through me&mdash;who has my number&mdash;whom I can&rsquo;t fool or intrigue or fascinate or seduce&mdash;who has fine ideals, and who consequently despises me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, it&rsquo;s conceivable. That&rsquo;d be a horrible misfortune. ... But if Sidway wasn&rsquo;t mad about you he couldn&rsquo;t act as he does. Actions, my proud savage! Actions! Any boy or man can rave. But it&rsquo;s actions that count. He&rsquo;s done something, hasn&rsquo;t he?&rdquo; &ldquo;He&rsquo;s done me wrong,&rdquo; wailed Madge, fighting vainly against the sweet madness of Allie&rsquo;s loyal convictions. &ldquo;From&mdash;what?&rdquo; asked Madge, faintly. &ldquo;From seeing you&mdash;from your chin down&mdash;without a stitch!&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, no! He&mdash;he didn&rsquo;t see me... he swore he didn&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Allie! I&rsquo;m so horribly afraid he&rsquo;ll turn out big and fine and noble&mdash;despite all&mdash;that tonight.&rdquo; &ldquo;He was indicating his sex.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh!&rdquo; &ldquo;You are a traitor, Allie Leland.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. You&rsquo;re that, Madge, to yourself.&rdquo; &ldquo;You are a queen and a law unto yourself. Sidway will not bend to you.&rdquo; Spent, but still unconvinced, Madge lay there in her friend&rsquo;s arms. &ldquo;Yes, once. The first time. That campus day.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then go back to that. Even if he hates you unmercifully, he&rsquo;ll come around. After we are gone&mdash;and we shouldn&rsquo;t drag this grand vacation out selfishly....&rdquo; &ldquo;Gone! I couldn&rsquo;t stay. Yet I must. This is home. I owe it to Dad and Mom. But&mdash;alone on the range with that eagle-eyed cowboy! Mom says he is like Dad used to be! Dad is hipped on him. And Nels.... Oh, what is the use?&rdquo; &ldquo;Majesty, it&rsquo;ll all work out. But I&rsquo;m afraid you must suffer more.&rdquo; &ldquo;Have a heart, will you? For Pete&rsquo;s sake! I&rsquo;ve been dying for weeks.&rdquo; &ldquo;Darling, compose yourself and get to sleep,&rdquo; begged Allie, tenderly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m dead tired. But sleep! What&rsquo;ll I do when I wake up?&rdquo; &ldquo;Of course.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why be just the same as if nothing had happened.&rdquo; &ldquo;You callous woman!... Allie&mdash;I think&mdash;I guess...&rdquo; faltered Madge, finally weakening to tears... &ldquo;I&rsquo;m licked.... I&rsquo;m afraid&mdash;he&rsquo;ll go&mdash;away!&rdquo; * * * * * Golden sunlight streaming in at Madge&rsquo;s window seemed inconsistent with the gray gloomy void she wanted to believe was her lot. Allie had gone out in her dressing gown to fetch some coffee and toast. Madge&rsquo;s foot felt stiff, but it was not going to incapacitate her in the least. What she wanted most right at that moment was to be down at the corrals. Would Lance be such a coward as that&mdash;to run off for fear she would betray him? What kind of girls had he known anyway? She would not have hurt him in her father&rsquo;s estimation for anything in the world. She was consumed with a desire to see Sidway this morning. To see if the monster resembled in any degree her conception! He should be haggard, drawn, after a sleepless night, burdened by guilt, unable to look anyone in the eye. Allie returned, escorted by a bevy of bright-eyed girls, all of whom had been in the kitchen. &ldquo;Lazy girl! It&rsquo;s ten o&rsquo;clock,&rdquo; said Maramee, whose sweet face appeared so gay and happy that Madge wondered at the credulity of human nature. They all came in, their colorful print dresses bright around Madge&rsquo;s bed. &ldquo;Where are Dixie and Bu?&rdquo; asked Madge. &ldquo;Horse mad. Dixie loves to sit on the corral fence and Bu is crazy to ride everything.&rdquo; &ldquo;She&rsquo;ll get piled up,&rdquo; declared Madge, severely. &ldquo;Humph! Bu&rsquo;s been piled up, as you call it. But she picks herself up and yelps for more. The cowboys get some kick out of her.&rdquo; After a little Madge inquired for the boys. Gone, hours before, off on a hiking trip! &ldquo;Not really?&rdquo; ejaculated Madge, her cup halted halfway to her lips. &ldquo;Not Barg and... and...&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, Barg,&rdquo; declared Maramee, happily. &ldquo;He poked his head in my window and tossed wild roses on my face, to awaken me. Whispered he&rsquo;d rather have stayed home with me. Oh, he was darling.&rdquo; &ldquo;To be sure. Barg&rsquo;s a darling, all right....&rdquo; Madge was interrupted by the arrival of Dixie Conn, flushed and breathless, no doubt from a climb up the hill. &ldquo;Majesty, I thought you were indisposed or something. You just look stunning,&rdquo; said the southern girl. &ldquo;Yeah? Thanks, Dix. But you&rsquo;re looking through rose-colored spectacles.... Where&rsquo;s Bu?&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge&mdash;Girls! That outsider has shown us up. She&rsquo;s dishonored the fair name of our sorority.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, for the love of Mike&mdash;what now?&rdquo; &ldquo;Bu is riding Umpqua right this minute. You know we all tried to coax Sidway to let us ride him. Same as Majesty tried to buy him. Nothing doing! And now she&rsquo;s down there having a swell time on that grand horse. The cowboy is teaching Bu to jump over logs and ditches, and what have you? Was I jealous? All the same I had to hand it to Bu. She looked great. How&rsquo;d she ever put that over with Sidway?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve a hunch and I&rsquo;m going to try it,&rdquo; said Selma Thorne, subtly. &ldquo;Say, don&rsquo;t imagine I let any grass grow under my feet,&rdquo; declared Dixie. &ldquo;I went up to the cowboy, raved about his horse and complimented Bu. Then I said, with all I&rsquo;ve got, old dears, &lsquo;Lance, I&rsquo;d almost sell my soul to ride Umpqua!&rsquo; He said, &lsquo;Why didn&rsquo;t you tell me? I can&rsquo;t read your mind. Umpqua loves girls. I&rsquo;d be only too happy to put you up on him. Hang around, till Beulah is through.&rsquo; I wasn&rsquo;t dressed for riding as you see, so I asked if I might go down early tomorrow. Then he looked troubled. I hadn&rsquo;t noticed that. Why, he looked just wretched. And he said: &lsquo;Yes, by all means, if I&rsquo;m here. But I expect Miss Stewart to fire me this morning!&rsquo;... Majesty, darling, what has he done now? But no matter, don&rsquo;t fire Sidway.&rdquo; They fled in a fiendish clamor and Madge hid her face in her pillow. It was a bad moment. There were many impetuses toward a magnificent fury, which she viewed with her mind, one after another. But she could not surrender to the one thing that had crushed her&mdash;the perfectly inconsequential and natural circumstance of Lance putting Bu Allen up on his horse. The absurdity of her childish pique gradually faded in the stern realization that her happiness, her future, and the welfare of her dear parents, so fatefully bound up in her, were at stake. Well might it be too late! But she would humble herself, crucify her selfish imperious side, absolutely refuse catastrophe. If she had been half as nice to Sidway as Bu Allen had been she would not now be in such extremity. Madge prostrated herself before her love, which was to betray her pride and spirit. It was too great a thing to deny any longer. But by surrendering she gained some aspect of the wit and self-control she needed at this trying time. Three betrothals among her guests attested to the success of their sojourn at her ranch. That Beulah and Elwell would make a go of it there was no reasonable doubt. Madge decided to shorten and intensify the remaining stages of their entertainment and center her energies upon the trip into the mountains and the dance she had long planned. This decision would change for her, and therefore her guests, the idle languor of the summer days. To that end, an hour after she left her room, she approached her parents, finding them in her mother&rsquo;s room. Evidently Madge had interrupted a serious talk, and having changed the direction of her mind she looked at them penetratingly, conscious of her neglect this exciting summer. &ldquo;Darlings, am I intruding?&rdquo; she asked, halting in the doorway. Her mother&rsquo;s sweet response and the light that her presence always brought upon her father&rsquo;s dark face assured Madge of her welcome, and that indeed she had been remiss. &ldquo;Mom, you can hide trouble, but Dad can&rsquo;t,&rdquo; said Madge, going to them, and she found that this was not a new thought, only one put aside because it hurt. An unaccountable aloofness, arising from her shame, kept her from sliding upon the arm of Stewart&rsquo;s chair. When had she done that? How little she had seen of him for a month and more! His reserve betrayed it. &ldquo;Has my crowd gotten on your nerves?&rdquo; she asked. &ldquo;They have been somewhat trying,&rdquo; replied Mrs. Stewart, with a smile. &ldquo;But that was only because of our difficulty in adjusting ourselves to excitement and mirth and&mdash;well, the life they brought with them. I like them all, Madge. Your favorite Allie is mine, too. And the boys are fine. I&rsquo;m glad you had them all here.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dad?&rdquo; queried Madge, poignantly. &ldquo;After they&rsquo;re gone, I&rsquo;ll tell you, lass,&rdquo; replied her father, then hastily: &ldquo;Oh, I like them all right. I just mean I must get hold of myself.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? I&rsquo;m afraid I&rsquo;m answered.... Has Rollie Stevens been nagging you about me?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. That young man steers a little clear of me. But he has approached Madeline.&rdquo; &ldquo;He has told me he wanted to marry you, darling. Three separate times. And has taken occasion to tell about the Stevens, their position, wealth, and all that. Very correct and a fine young fellow. But, Madge, he wouldn&rsquo;t care to live out here.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll tell the world he wouldn&rsquo;t,&rdquo; retorted Madge, with a laugh. &ldquo;And I wouldn&rsquo;t have him if he would. So skip that, Mom.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, then&mdash;so you intend to stay home&mdash;a while?&rdquo; asked Stewart, a little huskily, gazing away from her out of the window. &ldquo;Dad!&rdquo; If she had followed her swift impulse it would have been to throw her arms around his neck. But she could not do it. Her intuition grasped something strange here. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m going to pack this crowd off sooner than I had expected. And after that I&rsquo;m going to stay home for good.&rdquo; It was her mother Madge looked at, and she divined that whatever had been her thoughtless failings and deplorable shortcomings, they had never changed that faithful heart. If she had lost her father, through the years of absence, and his inability to understand her when she did come back, she divined that would not be a permanent estrangement, because she was kind and loving, and if she made amends for her wildness and settled down to a real love of him, and her future at the ranch, all would be well. Her quiet talks with Nels, too few and far between, had played no small part in the awakening of her conscience. Yet remorseful as she felt, her temper would admit of no reason that she knew why she should arraign herself at these odd moments. It was on account of that cowboy, and because she had been so unaccountably a prey to love for him. She had always known she must love some man with all her being, desperately, once and for good and she had always been looking for him. That might account somewhat for her endless interests. &ldquo;Dad, what&rsquo;s on your mind?&rdquo; asked Madge, after this flashing pageant of thought had left her composed, once more in a way to win back her old confidence. &ldquo;Nels told me you were worrying over money troubles.&rdquo; &ldquo;The gabby old woman!&rdquo; ejaculated Stewart, impatiently. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t be angry with Nels. I coaxed it out of him. I&rsquo;ve intended to go at him again&mdash;but I&rsquo;ve been so busy with these friends. Besides, Dad, I&rsquo;ve troubles of my own.&rdquo; &ldquo;You have? No one would guess it. You are the happiest, gayest, most thoughtless of all these young people.&rdquo; To Madge&rsquo;s amaze Stewart abruptly took her in his arms and clasped her so closely that she could not breathe. And over her he said to her mother. &ldquo;Madeline, Nels knows our girl better than we do.&rdquo; Then he kissed her hair, her cheek, and rushed out. &ldquo;Mom!&rdquo; she cried, going to her mother. &ldquo;What have I done?... Is it?... Oh!&rdquo; &ldquo;Darling, your conscience and your heart have spoken,&rdquo; replied her mother, earnestly. &ldquo;I knew they would. I have never doubted. It is no small thing for a rich and popular girl to return from college, from a great city, to the old-fashioned life of a ranch. Don&rsquo;t distress yourself further now. Devote yourself to your friends. When they are gone we&rsquo;ll face our problems. You have eliminated the only one that concerned me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Mother! Whether or not I loved you&mdash;and my home?... I&rsquo;ll never forgive myself.&rdquo; &ldquo;What is it you girls say? &lsquo;Skip it!&rsquo;... Madge, you will not accept young Stevens?&rdquo; Madge did not need to avert her eyes, because they were blind with tears. &ldquo;No, Mom. I like Rollie, and I&rsquo;ve played with him. He has done the same with other girls while courting me. Rollie is a playboy. He couldn&rsquo;t stand this lonely range. But I can, Mother!... And I want a handsome brute who will beat me!&rdquo; &ldquo;The latter is inconceivable,&rdquo; returned her mother, mildly. &ldquo;I hope no such contingency arises. I do not want to see the ranch blown to bits or be shaken by some cataclysm.&rdquo; &ldquo;You overrate me, darling. I&rsquo;m a very meek little girl this morning.&rdquo; &ldquo;You are certainly strange.&rdquo; Madge&rsquo;s original idea had been to ask her father&rsquo;s advice about taking her guests to the wild fastness up in the Peloncillo Hills, famous as a stronghold of the great Apache chief, Cochise. Before her school days Madge had ridden to this place with her father and the cowboys. She had never forgotten it, and it had been one of her cherished hopes to give her friends a camping trip there. For some inexplicable reason she found that she was cooling on the project, but she was too stubborn and fearful to analyze the cause. To abandon the trip after having exalted it continually for weeks did not quite suit Madge. She would have welcomed a reasonable excuse for not going, and as she considered the plans, that idea amplified. If she remembered correctly the ride up to Cochise&rsquo;s stronghold was long and arduous, and not for tenderfeet. That very fact had been an incentive. She had vowed that her friends would get one experience of the real thing. Whereupon Madge, feeling that there was safety for her in numbers, filled her car full of girls and drove down to the store. Nels was there, chipper as a grasshopper, and ready to sell the girls anything from cigarettes to calico. Three separate times Madge&rsquo;s contingent of friends had bought the store out, to Nels&rsquo; joy. &ldquo;Where are the cowboys?&rdquo; asked Madge. &ldquo;Lance is diggin&rsquo; postholes. An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s a job he hates as turrible as any other cowboy. Ren says every time Lance does somethin&rsquo; awful he goes oot an&rsquo; digs postholes.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sort of a penance?&rdquo; &ldquo;Must be. I seen Ren aboot somewheres a minnit ago. I&rsquo;ll yell fer him.&rdquo; It developed that Ren was very easy to locate and soon stood, sombrero in hand, his sunburned face beaming, before Madge and her friends. &ldquo;Mawning, cowboy. Where&rsquo;s your side partner?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Miss Majesty, he&rsquo;s drunk or crazy or somethin&rsquo;,&rdquo; replied Ren, with a grin. &ldquo;Woke me up before daylight, an&rsquo; heah&rsquo;s what he said, kinda loud an&rsquo; ringin&rsquo;. &rsquo;ren, I&rsquo;m goin&rsquo; out to dig postholes fer thet new fence. If anybody about heah wants to hang me or hawsewhip me, I&rsquo;ll be out there. Savvy?&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;How very thoughtful of him,&rdquo; remarked Madge, resisting a deep vibration that was more than thrill. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s he&mdash;done now?&rdquo; &ldquo;Dog-gone if I know. But it musta been turrible. I says &lsquo;Lance, you think you&rsquo;re funny?&rsquo; An&rsquo; he says &lsquo;About as funny as death!&rsquo; An&rsquo; he stamps off, without any grub. Why, he&rsquo;s worryin&rsquo; pore Nels to death.&rdquo; &ldquo;How would you and Sidway like to do me a great favor?&rdquo; &ldquo;Job or jest fun?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;ll be a job. No fun at all! I want you to truck your horses as far as you can from town toward the Peloncillo Hills. Find the old trail up to Cochise&rsquo;s stronghold, and fetch me a report on it and the camp site.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d like it swell, Miss Majesty, an&rsquo; I reckon Lance would about pass out to get away fer a spell. But, excuse me, what&rsquo;s the big idee? I was huntin&rsquo; deer up there last fall. I can tell you most anythin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, be very serious now. Think of my friends. Is that trip going to be a safe and comfortable one for them?&rdquo; And Madge gave Starr a look that had passed from her to him on former occasions. Ren suddenly looked blank and dropped his head. &ldquo;Hell no! it&rsquo;s neither one or the other. But thet&rsquo;s why it&rsquo;d be grand.&rdquo; Ren hated to abandon the idea. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m a little afraid of it. You see, Ren, I was sixteen when I made it first, had been riding horses all summer and I was fit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lousy! What&rsquo;s he mean by that?&rdquo; returned Madge, on fire in an instant despite the fact that Sidway&rsquo;s inelegant remark was in line with her designs. &ldquo;Did I ask him that? He told me a lot of terrible stuff. Said we were all too weak-kneed and soft-bottomed. That&rsquo;s just what he said, the bum. He thinks we&rsquo;re a lot of swell kids, but no good for the West. And that goes for you, Majesty.&rdquo; &ldquo;I am quite aware in what poor opinion Sidway holds me,&rdquo; rejoined Madge, cool once more, and her contrariness was such that now she felt a mounting desire to go and show him how soft-bottomed she was. &ldquo;Ren, you take Sidway, and leave at once. Find out all about the trail and Cochise&rsquo;s stronghold. Good and bad. Then upon your return you will report to me and all the crowd, after which we&rsquo;ll vote to decide whether to go or not.&rdquo; &ldquo;Very wal, Miss Majesty, I&rsquo;m on my way,&rdquo; replied Ren. &ldquo;Madge, you&rsquo;re a whiz! Of course, we&rsquo;re on to you. But we think this investigation will make the trip irresistible.&rdquo; &ldquo;At least it&rsquo;d be upon your own heads,&rdquo; warned Madge, then calling Ren back she met him halfway, to ask: &ldquo;Will you let me know if this plan is acceptable to Sidway?&rdquo; Ren regarded her, comically dumfounded. Madge averted her face slightly to go on: &ldquo;You see, Ren, he may leave any minute. The more I&mdash;I need him the more contrary he grows.&rdquo; She managed that demurely, but she was not smiling. Starr&rsquo;s tanned face brightened. &ldquo;Miss Majesty, between you an&rsquo; me we know Lance is daid plumb nutty, an&rsquo; what it&rsquo;s all about. Fer a time there I kind of feared you wasn&rsquo;t on to him. Wal, I am an&rsquo; so is Nels. If you don&rsquo;t believe me go to Nels. Shore I&rsquo;m a pore pard to double-cross Lance this way. He&rsquo;d kill me if he ever found out.&rdquo; &ldquo;Found out what, Ren?&rdquo; queried Madge, cool and sweet, mistress of herself again, but there was an incredible and unbelievable tumult within her being. &ldquo;You surprise me, Ren.... And why?&rdquo; went on Madge, unable to resist these precious and unreliable words from Lance&rsquo;s friend. &ldquo;Wal, you gotta figger thet out fer yourself. An&rsquo; if you cain&rsquo;t, why go to Nels. I&rsquo;ve talked too much. Thet son-of-a-gun has eyes like gimlets when he&rsquo;s close an&rsquo; telescopes when he&rsquo;s far. He might be seein&rsquo; us right now. Anyway all he does is watch fer you, Majesty.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren! What on earth for?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s not because you&rsquo;re nice to look at.... I&rsquo;ve peeped through a crack in the wall between our rooms&mdash;an&rsquo; seen him porin&rsquo; an&rsquo; sighin&rsquo; over a picture. He acted like a man who couldn&rsquo;t help lookin&rsquo; when he hated to. Thet picture is one of you, Majesty, fer I sneaked in an&rsquo; took a peep. He keeps it under a book in his table drawer. Now don&rsquo;t ask me no more. I feel pretty yellow. But I&rsquo;d never give him away onless fer my hunch thet you like Lance a little. Don&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Like&mdash;Lance?&rdquo; repeated Madge, and all her blood seemed rushing to her head. &ldquo;Ren, if Lance cannot trust you, how can I?&rdquo; &ldquo;Doesn&rsquo;t make sense. But you can.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll trust you. Yes, I do&mdash;like Lance,&rdquo; returned Madge, and to save her life she could not have made it casual. She went back to the house with the girls, playing her gay part, but there had come a complete reversal in her emotional reaction. Ren had vindicated her own deep convictions&mdash;that even if Sidway did hate her, he liked her too, against his better judgment and will, surely. Once more alone in her room she endeavored to stem this tide of overwhelming love, that was so great and so humble at the mere words of a sentimental cowboy. It frightened Madge&mdash;that flood of feeling. The sweetness of it warned her that this was not the time for surrender. But she had a tiny nucleus of hope around which to build. If she could only clasp to her breast this humble spirit! Time, days and days, would be her ally. * * * * * It was getting along into August, with touches of color beginning to show on the hills. Stewart advised Madge that if she was contemplating a camping trip up in the Peloncillos not to waste any more time. And she confided that she was pretty sure that would fall through. Snake Elwell had to return to college soon for fall practice; Allie had planned to motor east with her parents; and the rest of the party were beginning to think of the city. Nevertheless they were enthusiastic over the prospect of that horseback ride. &ldquo;Majesty, they won&rsquo;t say a darn thing,&rdquo; burst out Maramee, and the others chimed in with gay sallies. At last Madge reached them, and with a hand on Stewart&rsquo;s arm, she faced Sidway, and the grinning Starr. She was keen to feel something proven in them. &ldquo;Boys&mdash;it took you&mdash;long enough,&rdquo; she panted, and smiled upon them. &ldquo;Wal, Miss Majesty, you gave us all the time there was,&rdquo; replied Ren. Sidway&rsquo;s hazel eyes, dark and intent, appeared to pierce through Madge. Not for weeks had she met his full gaze like this, and despite the scattering of her wits, she realized the searching nature of his look, as if he were striving to divine her wishes. &ldquo;Miss Stewart, it was well you sent us,&rdquo; said Sidway, simply. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sure it spared you and your friends a real ordeal!&rdquo; A groan ran through the listening party. &ldquo;Real ordeal! What do you mean?&rdquo; &ldquo;Too severe a physical strain for tenderfeet. A motion-picture crowd would shy at this one&mdash;and they do things.... But it can be done, Miss Stewart, and I&rsquo;m bound to admit, it&rsquo;d be the trip of a lifetime.&rdquo; &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t advise it?&rdquo; &ldquo;I do not.&rdquo; &ldquo;Would you take the responsibility if I insisted?&rdquo; asked Madge. &ldquo;Yes, if your father insisted, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dad, are you with me?&rdquo; Madge transfixed Sidway with a troubled passionate gaze. She did not want to undertake this trip. She rejoiced that Sidway was making it impossible. But there was something about him that dared her to see if she could prevail upon him. She realized that until she could conquer such weakness, she would never be at her best with him. &ldquo;Lance, you&rsquo;re on the spot,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;Heavens, when haven&rsquo;t I been?&rdquo; he ejaculated, and joined Stewart in a laugh. Their understanding and good feeling seemed manifest. Then he bent a glance upon Madge, so clear, so frank, yet so supremely doubtful of her, that she writhed inwardly under it. She divined a thrust aimed at what must be his conviction of a vulnerable point in her which she had no idea she possessed. &ldquo;Shoot!&rdquo; she said, with all her disdain, but she felt dismay before she had been attacked. This cowboy must know something about her, to her discredit, or he could not have affected her that way. &ldquo;It may seem superfluous&mdash;to you,&rdquo; he said, coolly. &ldquo;But have you considered the expense?&rdquo; &ldquo;Expense!&rdquo; echoed Madge. That was the last question she would have expected. &ldquo;Yes. Perhaps you have not thought of that.&rdquo; &ldquo;I had not. Usually I don&rsquo;t consider what my plans cost.&rdquo; &ldquo;Exactly. That is why I presume to mention it.... This trip would cost a great deal. A gang of laborers would be needed on the trail. Two weeks&rsquo; work at least. The cabin up at Cochise&rsquo;s stronghold has gone to rack and ruin. It would have to be repaired. There are no tents and tarpaulins at the ranch, nor cooking utensils. You would require a complete new camping equipment. We have packsaddles for only a few horses; and, well, would you expect to have this camp on the scale on which you do everything?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m afraid I would.&rdquo; &ldquo;Of course. Then it would be necessary to buy twenty new packsaddles and at least ten pack animals. That would entail hiring half a dozen extra riders.... So you see, Miss Stewart, it is quite a big undertaking.&rdquo; &ldquo;I see all right,&rdquo; replied Madge, dubiously. To the credit of her friends, they at once turned thumbs down upon the whole proposition, and were so nice and fine about it that Madge regretted her subterfuge. But what was Lance Sidway aiming at? She believed his report implicitly. A half or a quarter of these obstacles would have sufficed. He believed that no matter how unfavorable his report or how exorbitantly the trip would cost, she would decide to go willy-nilly. Then he believed other things that mystified Madge. For an instant she had a bothersome thought that he might feel contempt for her because expense had never meant anything to her. A rebellious impulse to do the very thing he expected died in its infancy, somehow hastened to its death by the singular, almost mocking light in Sidway&rsquo;s hazel eyes. In a flash she saw how she could amaze and undeceive him. &ldquo;Thank you, Sidway. I&rsquo;ll abandon the camping trip solely upon your report,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;You have been very conscientious and dependable. I appreciate it.&rdquo; If Lance&rsquo;s scarcely veiled surprise proved Madge&rsquo;s intuition close to correct, his relief and gladness, that warmed out the coldness of his face, augmented the thought-provoking power of that moment. Madge conceived, too, an impression that Sidway&rsquo;s feelings were reflected in her father&rsquo;s dark face. Could these two possibly have an understanding? Madge drove the perplexing thought away. &ldquo;Friends, it&rsquo;s off, our mad ride up into the wilds,&rdquo; declared Madge. &ldquo;Some other summer! Instead I&rsquo;ll throw the biggest party ever.&rdquo; Pandemonium broke out among the boys and girls. When they ceased mobbing Madge she suddenly found Sidway towering over her, a stranger to whom her whole being seemed to leap. He broke off and squeezed her hand and strode away toward the bunkhouses. Madge stood a second, aware of the cramped fingers she could hardly straighten, and wondering what were the words he had left unsaid. It was not her fault if he had not had a glimpse of all her sides. Madge went back to her father. &ldquo;Dad, have you and Lance framed me?&rdquo; she asked. &ldquo;My dear, I&rsquo;ve had no part in this&mdash;this, whatever you&rsquo;d call it,&rdquo; he laughed. &ldquo;Honest, Madge. It looked as if I might have been in cahoots with Lance to queer your trip. But he never told me a thing. Nor did Ren. I think he carried it off very well indeed. Your mother will be pleased.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah?&mdash;What&rsquo;s this Machiavelli mean by pleasing you and Mom? Looks like deep stuff to me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, he&rsquo;s just a nice boy, who disapproves of you a lot.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dad, he said some strange things, for him. Nearly crushed my poor hand. Look! Then he beat it. He ran off before I could even answer.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bu, a girl has to have something beside a gown to get married.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not that one. You don&rsquo;t even need a slip.&rdquo; And so mad were they all that only Madge noted the omission of a man. The thing struck a fatalistic chord in her. She had everything&mdash;wonderful parents, lovely friends, wealth, education, ranch, horses, cars, all to make any girl happy&mdash;except a man to marry. But that was the last thing in the world to occupy her mind now. Anyway it was a calamity she could remedy this very night, provided she beat down her obsession for one unappreciative, unresponsive cowboy. Still it had been ingrained in Madge&rsquo;s girlish dreams that no one save a cowboy like her father could ever have her. Toward the end of that long day Madge slept, and was awakened by Allie and Maramee. They informed her that the lights were lit, the many tables set, and guests were arriving. Madge sent them off to dress and flew to her bath. She was in the midst of her make-up task when they returned, formal and elegant, to draw encomiums from Madge. &ldquo;Girls, we&rsquo;ll knock &rsquo;em for a loop tonight,&rdquo; said Madge, gleefully. &ldquo;We?&rdquo; chirped Maramee. &ldquo;Yes, us,&rdquo; declared Madge. &ldquo;Darling, I think you mean him,&rdquo; retorted Allie. They brushed her hair until it sparkled with glints of fire, and then by some magic of deft feminine hands they encased her in the blue and gold gown. For jewels Madge wore a string of pearls, the gift of her Aunt Helen, so beautiful and valuable that she had not risked it out of the safe for years. Allie was silent, gazing raptly at her, but Maramee raved on and on. &ldquo;Once in my life!&rdquo; was all Madge whispered to the image shining from her long mirror, and either she meant that she was satisfied or that she would play that beauty to the limit. Madge went to her mother&rsquo;s room, to be admitted. Her father was there, lean and dark and handsome in his black suit. &ldquo;Oh, Mom, but you are a lady of quality!&rdquo; cried Madge, a rush of warm sweetness piercing her trance. &ldquo;Dad, isn&rsquo;t she just stunning?&rdquo; Both her father and mother appeared incapable of speech on the moment, but their eyes would have gratified a far vainer girl than Madge. &ldquo;I wanted you to see me first.&rdquo; And she whirled for their benefit. &ldquo;Now, darlings, this is my party. I&rsquo;ve had it coming to my crowd for a year. Unknown to them it is my farewell to them&mdash;to college&mdash;to that kind of life. Whatever we do, don&rsquo;t be shocked.&rdquo; And she ran out, through the living room, into the corridor where she encountered Sidway. In his dark garb he looked so slim and different that she did not recognize him at first glance. &ldquo;Oh!&mdash;how stupid of me! It&rsquo;s Lance.&rdquo; And she halted under the colored lights. He started and backed partly against the balustrade, while a dazed and frowning expression altered his face. Then it vanished as he leaped erect, to utter a queer little laugh and make her a profound bow. &ldquo;Lance. Do you&mdash;like me?&rdquo; she queried softly. &ldquo;Majesty, I used to believe you were a mistake of evolution, but now I know it was God.&rdquo; &ldquo;Is that a compliment or a slam?&rdquo; &ldquo;Pardon me. I&rsquo;m in urgent search of your father. I just found out that the balance of his cattle herd were just rustled. And I&rsquo;m going to find out who stole them and where they were driven.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Lance, how dreadful. But must you tell him tonight?&rdquo; wailed Madge. &ldquo;Come to think of it, no,&rdquo; he returned, brightly. &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t even told Ren. Poor kid! It&rsquo;s going tough with him. Love is a terrible thing!&rdquo; &ldquo;I hope you please,&rdquo; he said, rubbing his hands together. &ldquo;Corvalo, I&rsquo;m bound to be. Remember, serve champagne to my party in the living room. Wine to the other tables. As for the punch, it must have an awful wallop. But not an immediate kick. Use creme de menthe to flavor only&mdash;to make them like it&mdash;leading them on. A soft sweet tasty punch&mdash;flowers and music leading to a precipice. Get me, Corvalo?&rdquo; He departed with shining eyes and beaming face, as if that order had been one to his liking. Madge went in search of her friends. They had rounded up the boys, who looked cool and natty in white flannels. Their various comments were incense to Madge&rsquo;s heart. Rollie Stevens said: &ldquo;Murder in the Rue Rancho this night!&rdquo; &ldquo;Come, let&rsquo;s make the rounds,&rdquo; said Madge. &ldquo;At least I can speak to all these strangers.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lamping you will be enough,&rdquo; declared Barg. Madge found it easier to be courteous and friendly than she had anticipated. The delight of the Mexicans especially pleased her. There were ranch people she remembered, and apparently all the cowboys on the range. &ldquo;Pack of wolves!&rdquo; averred Rollie. &ldquo;They&rsquo;d eat you alive. Good thing you&rsquo;re under my wing.&rdquo; The long patio made a colorful and beautiful spectacle. A row of tables extended down the center. Benches and chairs lined the walls. The waxed floors, built in for the occasion, shone iridescently. Colorful lanterns hung from the center of the arches. The gorgeous Spanish and Indian decorations lent a richness and legend to the old rancho. Madge slipped a hand over her heart to still its beating, its muffled pain. What was this pang stealing into her happiness? Madge, with her college guests, and her father and mother, sat down to dinner in the living room. That table from its hothouse orchids, its silver plate and crystal, to the rare and savory dishes of the sumptuous dinner, excelled anything the ranch had ever known. Stewart appeared to be staggered with its magnificence. But as the dinner wore on he fell under the spell of his wife&rsquo;s pleasure and Madge&rsquo;s rapture, and the continuous merriment and wit of the college crowd. Snake Elwell and Bu Allen were the first couple to begin dancing. Bu looked ravishing in a white gown that threatened to split at every move. Allie, usually the sweetest of girls who never made a criticism or caustic comment, spoke right out: &ldquo;Some hot little cookie!&rdquo; And Madge&rsquo;s father, who heard it, surrendered unconditionally to this group of young moderns. Madge&rsquo;s keen eyes did not miss anything. Once she saw Sidway and Starr, flushed of face and fire-eyed, peep into the living room. She also observed that her father did not drink his champagne. With dinner at an end, the dancing set in continuously, with only short intermissions. Madge loved to dance, and the first hour passed by on wings. When her crowd happened to congregate, someone remembered the punch, whereupon they flocked to the living room. The long table had been cleared, and moved back to the wall. In the center an enormous bowl of silver and crystal shone resplendent, full of a twinkling liquid that had life and color. An attendant stood ready to serve. Curious and gleeful, Madge drained her cup, tasted and wondered, and listened for comments. She alone knew that innocent-looking punch was loaded with dynamite. &ldquo;Say, Madge, where&rsquo;d you hit on this concoction? Pretty nifty,&rdquo; observed Rollie Stevens, who considered himself a connoisseur. &ldquo;New to me, Rollie.&rdquo; &ldquo;Soft and minty,&rdquo; interposed Brand. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet it&rsquo;ll lead you on.&rdquo; &ldquo;Tame, if you ask me,&rdquo; said Allie, loftily, and that from her was a source for mirth. Allie could not stand liquor at all. &ldquo;Swell punch,&rdquo; observed Elwell. &ldquo;What do you say, Bu?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hand me another,&rdquo; replied the redhead. &ldquo;Majesty, are you kidding us with this stuff?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sorry, Brand. But this is my home, you know. And remembering your capacities I wanted something weak.&rdquo; &ldquo;Weak or not let&rsquo;s have another.&rdquo; Madge finally dragged her friends out. While dancing and resting the next hour she contrived to keep tabs on that punch bowl. Just as she had suspected, her friends were succumbing to this insidious drink. Once with Rollie she almost burst in upon her father, Nels, Danny Mains, Starr and Sidway, but she drew her escort back behind some decorations in the corridor. With intense interest and fiendish glee she watched them and listened, holding Rollie back with imperious hand. Manifestly this group of gentlemen had been in there before. &ldquo;Gene, we shore lived too soon,&rdquo; drawled Nels, regretfully. &ldquo;Shades of Monty Price and Nick Steel!&rdquo; ejaculated Stewart. &ldquo;Nels&mdash;Danny, what would our old pards have thought of this drink?&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd, I dunno.... Fill me up another, waiter.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, this heah punch is nectar an&rsquo; honey an&rsquo; hell all mixed up together,&rdquo; said Starr. &ldquo;Where does the hell come in?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I hadn&rsquo;t noticed thet until this last drink, which was my sixth.... Pard, how many have you had?&rdquo; &ldquo;Enough,&rdquo; replied Sidway, tragically. &ldquo;Why, you dawg-gone kill-joy! Cain&rsquo;t you hold your likker?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, I can&rsquo;t hold this liquor&mdash;and I can&rsquo;t take it!&rdquo; &ldquo;Lissen, pard. I&rsquo;m gonna put a couple of these under Bonita&rsquo;s belt,&rdquo; whispered Ren, behind Danny Mains&rsquo; back. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t. You&rsquo;ll lose her right then.&rdquo; &ldquo;Umpumm! Thet&rsquo;s when I&rsquo;ll win her. Pard, Bonita is funny tonight. Been cryin&rsquo; an&rsquo; turrible upset.&rdquo; &ldquo;Come here, you geezer!&rdquo; And Sidway dragged Ren out of the room. &ldquo;Gene, any drink that can make an old man young again is one to tie to,&rdquo; said Nels. &ldquo;I agree with you. But, pards, even if my daughter hadn&rsquo;t sprung some destroying drink on us, I&rsquo;d have to get drunk with you for old times&rsquo; sake.&rdquo; It came in the nature of a surprise. Bu Allen sat down on the floor, a cup of punch in her hand, and turned a somersault. She did not spill any liquor. The boys and girls howled at the sight. Thus encouraged, she turned somersaults all across the living room. Nels and Danny Mains were in hysterics; Ren Starr whooped like the cowboy he was; Sidway strode out of the room. Stewart, his face like a thundercloud, threw up his hands like a man who had been vainly fighting facts, and lunged out into the patio. Madge, frightened at the lightning of his eyes, watched him disappear with a sinking of her heart. Had she gone too far? But she had not known Beulah Allen would disgrace her party. And if Snake Elwell had not violently jerked the girl to her feet and dragged her out Madge felt that she would have had to adopt extreme measures. That event saw the disintegration of the party. The dancing grew desultory, except in the patio where the range guests still held forth. Finding Barg and Maramee asleep in each other&rsquo;s arms in a corner, and some of the other couples fading from the living room to the benches, Madge realized her party was about over. And it had been a failure. She knew when she had had enough to drink. But in her bitterness, she overstepped her habit. With Rollie she drank two more cups of punch. And as she went outdoors with him, wrapping a mantle around her bare shoulders she realized two things&mdash;that Rollie was pretty drunk and that a gaiety had overcome her gloom. Good to have the blue devils fade away! Lance Sidway had not come near her! To hell with him! Rollie was a pal, and on the way out under the pines, Madge not only permitted his extravagant embraces but returned his kisses. She felt just on the verge of being giddy and dizzy. But she did not want to think. After all she could do worse than marry Rollie Stevens. In an open space, shaded by spreading pines and surrounded by low foliage, they found a bench covered with blanket and pillows. The moonlight streaked through rifts in the branches to lend a silver glamour to the glade. Rollie sat down and drew Madge upon his lap. At first she felt silly and soft at his love-making, and experienced a pleasant glow of excitement. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re going to marry me,&rdquo; he said, thickly, between kisses. &ldquo;Is that so? Who told you?&rdquo; laughed Madge. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m telling you,&rdquo; he replied, more violently. &ldquo;Rollie, you&rsquo;re drunk.&rdquo; &ldquo;If I am it&rsquo;s your fault.&rdquo; &ldquo;You all fell for that punch. My secret, Rollie!&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah?... Your line, Madge&mdash;secrets! I&rsquo;ll give you another one.&rdquo; The edge on his voice, accompanying some rough handling of her awakened Madge to the situation. But her lackadaisical good nature was such that she made only feeble resistance to his ardor. &ldquo;You love me&mdash;don&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; he demanded fiercely. &ldquo;&rsquo;Course I love you&mdash;Rollie&mdash;as a pal&mdash;old friend, and what have you?... but...&rdquo; &ldquo;Nuts! I&rsquo;m tired of that dope.&rdquo; And the hot kisses upon her mouth and neck grew more violent. Madge was no longer returning his kisses. From that to remonstrating with him was only a short step. It appeared to inflame him. Locked in his arms she was at a disadvantage. A rattling of her pearls alarmed her. The fool would break the necklace. &ldquo;Let me&mdash;go!... You&rsquo;re drunk&mdash;boy.... this is ...&rdquo; &ldquo;So&rsquo;re you&mdash;drunk,&rdquo; he panted, and pushed her back off his lap upon the cushions. Madge&rsquo;s utterance was stifled by his kisses. She twisted her face away. But Rollie only grew more violent. Furiously she flung him aside, and sprang off the bench. In the dark she fell over someone she took to be Rollie and had to clutch his arm to regain her balance. He appeared to be sitting against a tree trunk. But there at the end of the bench was Rollie, mumbling and cursing. She managed to arise despite a paralyzing dismay, that gave place to a terrific shame and rage. He rose rather slowly and pulled himself erect. A slant of moonlight fell across his face. It was ashen white, and out of it glittered eyes as black as coal and as sharp as daggers. &ldquo;Yeah, it&rsquo;s me. Who else in hell could have such rotten luck?&rdquo; he returned, with exceeding bitterness. &ldquo;Lance Sidway! you waylaid me!&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t flatter yourself,&rdquo; he flashed, hotly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d left your drunken outfit. On the way to my lodgings, I stopped here to&mdash;to smoke. But after I&rsquo;d finished, I lingered, like the sap I am. I saw you coming and made sure you&rsquo;d pass. But you didn&rsquo;t. Ha! Ha!&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, you lie! And you laugh at me!&rdquo; exclaimed Madge, beside herself with rage. &ldquo;No, I don&rsquo;t lie,&rdquo; he retorted. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;ve the laugh on you, Madge Stewart.&rdquo; Rollie had clambered up, hanging to the bench, evidently more than ever under the influence of liquor. &ldquo;Whosis?&rdquo; &ldquo;Rollie, it&rsquo;s Lance Sidway. He was sitting here all the time,&rdquo; declared Madge. &ldquo;Keep your hands off me,&rdquo; ordered the cowboy, shoving him back. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sorry. But I wasn&rsquo;t to blame. I didn&rsquo;t spy on you. You get that?&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re a liar, Sidway. You&rsquo;re always spying on Madge. You&rsquo;re stuck on her.&rdquo; Rollie lunged at Sidway who avoided him, backing against the bench. &ldquo;Let him alone, Rollie. You&rsquo;re drunk,&rdquo; cried Madge. Sidway had no recourse but to stave off Stevens&rsquo; blows. Finally a hearty slap in the face changed the cowboy&rsquo;s tactics. He seized Stevens by the arms and shook him violently. Then he shoved him back. &ldquo;Stevens, I warn you. Lay off me, or I&rsquo;ll sock you.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d shoot you if I had a gun.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, if my back were turned. You&rsquo;re one swell flop, Stevens. ... Stay away from me, I tell you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lance, get away from the fool!&rdquo; implored Madge, who was if anything more infuriated with Stevens than Sidway. &ldquo;Sure, you would ask that. Me run, to save this guy&rsquo;s face.&rdquo; &ldquo;It might save my good name.&rdquo; &ldquo;You can&rsquo;t save a rotten egg, Madge Stewart. I tell you I&rsquo;m the insulted one here and I&rsquo;m getting sore.&rdquo; When Stevens belligerently confronted Sidway again, it was to meet no resistance. The cowboy stood motionless in the moonlight, his arms lowered. But to Madge he looked formidable. &ldquo;You&mdash;insufferable cow hand!&rdquo; shouted Stevens, furiously and he struck Lance twice in the face. &ldquo;Okay, Rollie. Now let&rsquo;s see if you can take it,&rdquo; rejoined Sidway, grimly, and he swung hard on the collegian. The blow sounded solid, meaty, and Stevens went down with a thud and did not move. &ldquo;There! Sorry to mess up your lover, Miss Stewart, but as you saw, I couldn&rsquo;t avoid it.&rdquo; &ldquo;He lies so still... he&rsquo;s so white,&rdquo; cried Madge, in alarm. &ldquo;I hope the sucker croaks,&rdquo; rejoined Sidway, brutally. &ldquo;What&rsquo;ll I do?&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, you might hunt up your dad and Nels, tell them what this guy tried to do to you&mdash;and watch them hang him.&rdquo; &ldquo;What a beast you are, Lance Sidway! It was bad enough to sit there, like a cheap eavesdropper, and listen, let alone...&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell! I tell you I&rsquo;m innocent. I didn&rsquo;t look. I didn&rsquo;t listen&mdash;at least until you got so raw in your love-making...&rdquo; &ldquo;But you should have made your presence known at once,&rdquo; cried Madge, poignantly. &ldquo;Right. I&rsquo;m damn sorry. But I was scared, confused... It wasn&rsquo;t easy&mdash;for me&mdash;Madge Stewart.&rdquo; He choked over the last utterance, and gazed down upon her with eyes of terrible reproach, which might have softened Madge but for her own insupportable emotions. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s no excuse for a gentleman,&rdquo; she retorted. &ldquo;No! But for God&rsquo;s sake, do you think you were a lady?&rdquo; &ldquo;And I&rsquo;m a poor, miserable, crawling louse!&rdquo; he ejaculated, in desperation. &ldquo;I regard the appellation as fairly felicitous.&rdquo; &ldquo;And you&rsquo;re Majesty Stewart, a law unto herself, a lady of quality, a princess who can do no wrong?&rdquo; he burst out, passionately. &ldquo;Listen, you!&mdash;That college bum there was not drunk. But his decency, if he had any, was gone. And you were not drunk, either, but your dad would have despised you if he had been here in my boots.&rdquo; &ldquo;Rollie forgot himself&mdash;I confess... But I didn&rsquo;t...&rdquo; &ldquo;Bah! Why, for a real man you&rsquo;d have been a push-over,&rdquo; retorted Sidway, hoarsely. Madge slapped him viciously across the lips. The next instant his open hand cracked along her cheek and head, and but for the bench would have upset her. Nevertheless, almost blinded by stars and shock, Madge slapped him again, with all her might. &ldquo;Regular cat, eh?&rdquo; he burst out, huskily. &ldquo;But you can&rsquo;t make a dog out of me.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&mdash;don&rsquo;t&mdash;have&mdash;to...&rdquo; panted Madge. He seized her in powerful hands, hard and hot, and dragged her into a ray of moonlight. &ldquo;Majesty&mdash;what a travesty that name is!&mdash;Madge Stewart, you&rsquo;re going to hear the truth once in your life.&rdquo; He was suddenly so strangely different, so grimly righteous and ruthless, so white and fire-eyed that Madge sustained a sinking of her heart. She tried to retort with some further insult, but failed of coherence. He shook her as he had shaken Stevens. &ldquo;Majesty Stewart! One swell girl, they all think. Proud, blue-blooded, rich. What a mistake! Why you are as false as hell. It was low-down enough before I caught you tonight. Thank God it was I instead of your dad who caught you. He&rsquo;s had enough of you to stand.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sidway, what do you&mdash;mean?&rdquo; whispered Madge, and slipping out of his nerveless grasp she sank upon the bench. &ldquo;I mean your splendid father and your loving mother are too damn good for you, Madge Stewart.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lance, I&mdash;I know that.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you don&rsquo;t know what you&rsquo;ve put them through.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh!&mdash;Not&mdash;not money trouble?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, money!&rdquo; he bit out, bending over her. Madge moaned. This it was then that had vaguely haunted her, the conscience which she would not face. She felt it in this man&rsquo;s intensity, in the bitterness of his voice, the fire of contempt in his eyes. This something had given him power over her, and her spirit seemed to be fainting. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s fate that I have to tell you this,&rdquo; he went on, swiftly. &ldquo;Your dad gave me his books to straighten out. He did not know that in the book he had left your bank statements, checks and what not. I went over these, too. And that is how I learned of your rotten extravagance and the way your parents have ruined themselves for you.&rdquo; Madge sank down to hide her face in the pillows. The blow had fallen. And of all blows it was the mortal one which could crush her. &ldquo;And now, angel-face,&rdquo; whispered Sidway, almost spent, &ldquo;your father is ruined&mdash;and who&rsquo;ll pay for this party? Would you like Nels and Ren and me to chip in our savings....&rdquo; Madge stretched out a shaking importunate hand that silenced him. And it seemed amidst the knell of pride and happiness that had fallen in ruins about her, she heard Sidway&rsquo;s swift footfalls fading away. Brutally he had made impossible any longer stay at Stewart&rsquo;s ranch. That long-deferred break seemed an unutterable and immense relief. But his conscience flayed him. &ldquo;What for?&rdquo; he whispered, huskily. &ldquo;Why do I feel this&mdash;this.... It was&mdash;coming to her!&rdquo; He was glad that he had had the courage to tell her. If she had a grain of good in her the truth would bring it out. Then why this stab in his heart, this clamor of furies in his ears, this still small voice? He would have wanted to tell her like an impassive destiny, letting the iron consequences fall. And he had sunk to the level of a man like Uhl. Perhaps even that philanderer would have been more of a gentleman. Lance struck the low of misery. Then attending to his smarting lip he found it cut and bleeding. How about that? And the stinging blows might as well have been re-enacted. He had struck her, a hard openhanded slap that had staggered her. Suddenly it all flashed clear. Jealousy had been at the root of that incredible passion. Loving Madge Stewart to distraction, his damnable fate had been to be compelled to cower there in the shadow, seeing, hearing the kisses she had lavished on that college fellow. Lance tried to blot out the sight. That had seemed a sickening mortal blow, but it was his vile speech to her that stuck like a hot blade in his side&mdash;the jealous false word for which she had struck him across the lips. At last Lance uncovered the real trouble. Lance stood up, shivering a little at the cool air and the indifferent stars. This was the end of his secret love affair. And there would never be another, he was certain. It did not seem possible that any man, much less he, could see Madge Stewart as he had seen her, and carry her in his arms, and kiss her with such abandon, and then fall in love with another woman. Lance strode down to the bunkhouse, his mind trying to take up the threads of the information he had forced from Bonita. There was a light in Nels&rsquo; cabin. Lance&rsquo;s watch said that morning was less than an hour away. He burst in upon the old cattleman, who was in the act of undressing. &ldquo;Nels, are you sober?&rdquo; demanded Lance. &ldquo;Hello, son, what&rsquo;s ailin&rsquo; you&mdash;all white an&rsquo; eyes aburnin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell to pay, Nels. Are you sober enough to get me straight?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sober?&mdash;Dog-gone! I don&rsquo;t know. Thet shore was some punch. I jest couldn&rsquo;t stop drinkin&rsquo; it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell drink, all right. What&rsquo;d it do to Danny and Ren and Stewart?&rdquo; &ldquo;Plenty! Now, listen. Pack me some biscuits, meat, dried apples&mdash;anything you can dig up pronto. Put it in a saddlebag. I&rsquo;ll wrangle my horse. And you be sure you sober up while I&rsquo;m gone.&rdquo; &ldquo;Son, I reckon I savvy,&rdquo; drawled Nels. Lance went into his cabin, and hastily changed into his riding garb, buckled on his gunbelt, and hurried out, to jerk a bridle off a peg on the porch. The night before, because of the strange horses that had arrived, Lance had put Umpqua in the barn. The moment Lance&rsquo;s step sounded on the runway, Umpqua nickered, and stamped his hoofs. Lance looped the bridle round his neck, led him out, and filling a nosebag with grain, he put that over Umpqua&rsquo;s head. Then leading the horse he hurried back to Nels&rsquo; cabin. There he saddled Umpqua, but left the cinch loose. He decided before seeing Nels to go into his cabin thinking hard what to take. It was necessary to light the lamp. A blanket, his fleece-lined coat, his rifle and some shells, his gloves, money and matches&mdash;these he thought would be about all. Then he remembered Madge&rsquo;s photograph. He would take that, for the chances were against his returning, or ever seeing her again. Fortunately the picture fit inside his coat pocket. He wrapped it in a silk scarf and carefully put it away. Funny, he thought, if a rustler&rsquo;s bullet pierced that lovely likeness of Majesty Stewart before it pierced his heart! But even so it could not hurt any more than she had hurt him. Then he extinguished the lamp and went out. The east was breaking gray. Dawn was not far off. Umpqua was pitching the nosebag to get the last of the grain. &ldquo;Nels, come out,&rdquo; he called. &ldquo;Heah I am. Been waitin&rsquo;, son, kinda worried.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, Nels,&rdquo; replied Lance, receiving the saddlebags. &ldquo;Nothing to worry about&mdash;much.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. Wal, you act kinda queer. I&rsquo;ve spent my life with range fellers. An&rsquo; if you&rsquo;re not drunk on thet punch, you&rsquo;re shore drunk on somethin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeh? Well, what, old wiz?&rdquo; rejoined Lance, his swift hands at work over the saddle. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re leavin&rsquo; Majesty&rsquo;s Rancho.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ha! Gee, Nels, you&rsquo;re keen. I rather snicker to snort that I am.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; on account of Majesty?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, on account of Majesty!&rdquo; ejaculated Lance, flippantly. &ldquo;Aw!&mdash;Did you quarrel?&rdquo; &ldquo;Look where she split my lip. That little lady has a sock.&rdquo; &ldquo;Son, don&rsquo;t tell me she&mdash;she hit you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll tell the world she did.&rdquo; &ldquo;What in Gawd&rsquo;s name for?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, it&rsquo;s too long a story. I deserved it and I took it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lance, you&rsquo;re uncommon bitter.... I don&rsquo;t mind admittin&rsquo; thet I had it figgered you&mdash;you was in love with Majesty.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, damn your lunatic hide!&rdquo; burst out Lance. &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t mind admitting!&mdash;Say, you lying old matchmaker, you&rsquo;ve been driving me nuts! You haven&rsquo;t given me any peace for weeks. You wouldn&rsquo;t even let me sleep. &lsquo;Ain&rsquo;t I kinda in love with Majesty? She shore is sweet on you!&rsquo;... What kind of talk!&mdash;Now, listen, for once, for good and all. &lsquo;Kinda in love with Majesty?&rsquo; Ha! Ha! Ha!... I love that good-for-nothing angel so terribly I&rsquo;m dying for her. Do you get that? I&rsquo;m stark staring mad about her. I&rsquo;d shoot myself if I hung around here any longer. So I&rsquo;m beating it. Now, take a load of that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Son, it&rsquo;ll be the turriblest mistake if you run off now,&rdquo; replied Nels, awed and moved. &ldquo;Fer Majesty&rsquo;s jest as turrible in love...&rdquo; &ldquo;Skip it. You&rsquo;re balmy. You&rsquo;re nuts. You&rsquo;re crazy,&rdquo; retorted Lance, wrenching the words out. It was insupportable to listen to such raving from this simple old man. &ldquo;Listen to this. All the cattle Stewart had left were rustled last night, right after dark. Must have been rounded up in the daytime.&rdquo; &ldquo;What?&rdquo; roared Nels, changing magically. &ldquo;Why&rsquo;n&rsquo;hell didn&rsquo;t you tell us?&rdquo; &ldquo;Your darling Madge begged me to keep it till tomorrow. Well, that&rsquo;s today.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who told you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Bonita.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. How&rsquo;d you drag thet oot of her?&rdquo; &ldquo;I threw a couple of those punches into her, danced with her, took her out. Well, she spilled the beans, on conditions.&rdquo; &ldquo;What conditions?&rdquo; &ldquo;Never mind them, Nels. I won&rsquo;t tell you. And you&rsquo;re not to give Bonita away to Stewart or Ren.&rdquo; &ldquo;Humph! You cain&rsquo;t fool them.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s thet long gray hill southeast of heah. Aboot ten miles, closest. Separates the range from the foothills of the Peloncillos. There&rsquo;s a cattle trail straight down the valley across the border. Rustlers used it years ago.&rdquo; &ldquo;Stewart&rsquo;s cattle ought to be around that divide by now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, an&rsquo; then some. What&rsquo;s yore idee, son?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m going to find out.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good. But don&rsquo;t let yourself be seen from the ridge top. Those rustlers will figger thet the cattle won&rsquo;t be missed right off. But they&rsquo;ve got sharp eyes. With two days&rsquo; start they&rsquo;d aboot get acrost before we could haid them off.... I&rsquo;ll get Stewart an&rsquo; Danny, an&rsquo; Starr drunk or sober, an&rsquo; hit this cattle trail. Meanwhile you locate the rustlers, then ride on in to town. Don&rsquo;t lose no time gettin&rsquo; an ootfit of cowboys&mdash;or any land of a posse, an&rsquo; ride hell fer leather to haid these greasers off.&rdquo; &ldquo;Use my own judgment as to where I&rsquo;ll cross the ridge?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. But if you&rsquo;re smart an&rsquo; lucky you can cross by the Cochise Trail. Only don&rsquo;t ride down into thet valley onless you&rsquo;re ahaid of the rustlers.&rdquo; &ldquo;Okay, Nels. I&rsquo;m on my way,&rdquo; replied Lance. &ldquo;Nels, that outfit might get suspicious or something, and hit up the Cochise Trail. Tell Gene and Ren to look for my tracks on that trail, crossing the valley.&rdquo; Then vaulting astride he rode across the square, down by the sleeping village, out upon the shadowy gray range. It was almost daybreak when he struck the wash. By the time a ruddy light showed over the dark mountain barrier Lance had struck the fresh cattle track. It crossed the highway and headed straight for the low slant of gray that marked the northern end of Gray Ridge Divide. Satisfied and thrilled, Lance swerved off the trail to make a short cut, intending to climb the ridge ten miles or so toward the south. It was then only that his mind reverted to the tragedy of his leaving. Madge haunted him, her lovely face white and tragic, her big eyes, and most of all, after he had told her that she had ruined her father, the way she sank down, crushed by amaze and shame. Lastly that imploring hand, mute appeal for mercy. Would she like him and Nels and Ren to donate their wages?&mdash;those had been his last words. Too late! Lance writhed in his saddle. As the dawn slowly flamed to a glorious sunrise and the broad daylight drove away his morbid thoughts, he could not understand how he had been so base. He felt that he would be driven to go back to the ranch and explain to Madge how his jealousy and passion had made him a coward and a cad. That would be a betrayal of his love, a refuting of all his scorn. As he rode along, his brooding self-reproach and remorse augmented his error and mitigated Madge&rsquo;s faults. If it turned out that he was to be the means of saving her father&rsquo;s cattle, and perhaps getting himself shot in Madge&rsquo;s interest, that would be all right with him, if only she could know of his repentance. Lance crossed the valley obliquely and headed up the ridge about five miles south of the point. The sun was high in the heavens when he gained the summit. He took care not to show his horse or himself on top of the ridge. There were rocks and scrub cedars all along, affording good cover. Lance dismounted to reconnoiter. He had to walk a long way north on the ridge before he discovered the cattle. They had been driven into the head of the narrow valley between the foothills and the ridge, and were grazing. The distance was not quite too far to distinguish horses and riders, but Lance had to wait a good while before he made sure he saw them. They should have traveled down the valley to a point almost equal with his position. Lance lingered there until he saw the herd move down the valley toward him. Then he retraced his steps. Arriving at the spot where he had left his horse, Lance sent a keen gaze back across the valley toward the ranch. He espied puffs of dust some miles out from the highway, about on a line with the cattle trail. He concluded Gene and his riders were in pursuit of the rustlers. &ldquo;Okay,&rdquo; soliloquized Lance, with satisfaction. &ldquo;Pretty lucky for me to worm this out of Bonita. Poor kid! All to save her good-for-nothing brother! Well, I&rsquo;ll keep my word.&rdquo; &ldquo;My best bet is to go down to the range and look for a cattle outfit between here and town,&rdquo; Lance told himself, and after thinking of every angle possible he decided to put his idea into effect. There were several ranches along that slope of the ridge, and he might be fortunate enough to meet some riders. To this end Lance headed down the slope. Lance had enough on his mind to make the miles and hours seem short. Umpqua walked and trotted under the hot sun. As long as he had soft ground to travel on he would not tire. Late in the afternoon Lance arrived at the last ranch along the ridge, and ascertained that an outfit of cowboys had left not long before to round up some cattle south of Bolton. This was good luck, indeed, and Lance set off with high hopes. These riders would very likely camp outside of Bolton. It was sunset when Lance caught up with a trio of cowboys leading three pack horses, and half a dozen extra mounts. Lance joined them with a greeting and pulled Umpqua to a walk. &ldquo;Howdy,&rdquo; returned a lean towheaded rider, fastening penetrating eyes upon Lance. &ldquo;I seen you coming &rsquo;way back. Jest about in a hurry, wasn&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll say. You&rsquo;re the Bar X boys from Spencer&rsquo;s ranch, aren&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, we&rsquo;re some of them.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m Sidway, riding for Gene Stewart.&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckoned you was. My handle&rsquo;s Tim Sloan, an&rsquo; my pards are brothers, sons of Spencer.&rdquo; &ldquo;Your boss told me you were bound south of Bolton to round up some of his cattle.&rdquo; &ldquo;We air, if we can locate them. But I reckon they&rsquo;re rustled across the border. Some two-bit outfit been workin&rsquo; the edges of the range lately.&rdquo; Lance lost no time accounting for his presence, and the lean rider was so interested that he reined his horse, and halted the cavalcade in the middle of the road. &ldquo;Hell you say!&mdash;Boys, you hear thet?... How far back air these raiders with Stewart&rsquo;s cattle?&rdquo; &ldquo;Over the ridge in the valley, halfway at the least.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; when was it you sighted them?&rdquo; &ldquo;This morning around ten. Stewart will be behind them, keeping out of sight. And it&rsquo;s my job to get some riders to head them off from this end.&rdquo; &ldquo;We&rsquo;re with you, Sidway.... Boys, like as not this same outfit has been runnin&rsquo; off our stock.&rdquo; &ldquo;Purty shore, I&rsquo;d say,&rdquo; replied one of the brothers. &ldquo;But we&rsquo;d help you out if there wasn&rsquo;t a chance.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, fellows. I&rsquo;m relieved.... And now, Sloan, what do you advise?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, them stolen cattle won&rsquo;t get nowhere near this end of the valley tonight. My idee is to camp here outside of town, an&rsquo; be off before daylight in the mawnin&rsquo;. How&rsquo;s thet suit you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Fine. It&rsquo;s just going to work out great.&rdquo; Before dusk settled down the riders halted just on the edge of Bolton near a clump of trees Lance remembered having passed on the trip to the Peloncillos. &ldquo;Sloan, will we eat in town?&rdquo; queried Lance, as he dismounted. &ldquo;No. Boss won&rsquo;t stand for thet. We&rsquo;ll throw up some grub here. But we&rsquo;re out of coffee an&rsquo; butter.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll buy some. Do you think I ought to notify the sheriff?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell no. This deal is a cinch, an&rsquo; thet old geezer would hawg all the credit.&rdquo; Lance strode off into town, his mind thronged with thoughts. He seemed on the verge of an adventure much to his liking. Stewart and Nels were sure to like him better than ever. And proud, wild, volcanic Madge Stewart would surely be indebted to him, whether or not she ever confessed it. Lance had a desire to telephone the ranch. At that hour, with Stewart and his men absent, it was ten to one that Madge would answer. How coolly he could make his report, not omitting subtly to augment the dangers! Did that violet-eyed girl have a heart? Lance had to admit that she had, but he had never touched it. His bitter and final resolve of last night still held, yet he was conscious of a rending of spirit at the thought of keeping it, and leaving Majesty&rsquo;s Rancho forever. At Smith&rsquo;s Store on the highway Lance purchased butter and coffee, and several cakes of hard chocolate, one of which he put in his pocket. While the clerk was wrapping Lance&rsquo;s purchases the proprietor accosted him. &ldquo;Hey, Sidway, when did you leave the ranch?&rdquo; &ldquo;Before daylight this morning. Rode down the ridge looking for cattle.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then you don&rsquo;t know Stewart&rsquo;s phone is out of connection? I suspect it has been cut.&rdquo; &ldquo;Indeed I don&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, something&rsquo;s wrong. This morning Mrs. Stewart phoned in an order. I expected some things she wanted by express, so did not call until after they arrived. Then I couldn&rsquo;t get an answer.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s not strange. One of the old telephone poles may have toppled over,&rdquo; replied Lance, thoughtfully. &ldquo;Yes, it might, but it didn&rsquo;t,&rdquo; returned Smith, bluntly. &ldquo;Yeah? How do you know?&rdquo; &ldquo;Mike Scanlon was in not ten minutes ago. He&rsquo;d been out for a load of dead aspen wood. He said that when he was cuttin&rsquo; it, out there along the creek, he saw a big black car dustin&rsquo; along toward town hell-bent fer election. An&rsquo; it stopped down the road eight or ten miles. Mike forgot about thet until he got near to the highway. Then he tangled up in a wire thet turned out to be Stewart&rsquo;s. It had fallen across the road. Hadn&rsquo;t been cut long, for Mike saw the bright end, where it had been clipped. He thought somebody in thet big black car had done it. Not half an hour ago! Damn queer, don&rsquo;t you call it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Where does this Mike Scanlon live?&rdquo; &ldquo;Up at the end of town, on the other side of the highway. Ask Meade, the garage man.&rdquo; Lance, hurrying along past the bright red and yellow lights, pondered this news. It clamped down upon him with a presagement far out of proportion to probabilities. Apparently Stewart&rsquo;s telephone wire had not been cut until late in the day. That seemed to preclude any possibility of the rustlers being accountable. A big black car! Lance wanted to talk to Mike Scanlon about that car. He passed the last bright neon lights. Meade&rsquo;s garage appeared to be deserted. Just at that moment a big black car, with headlights dark, moved slowly down the back road. Lance wanted a look at that car. It was strangely familiar in line and build. He swerved off the highway, crossed the open space to the back road. &ldquo;Hi there. Hold up,&rdquo; he called boldly. Manifestly the driver heard him for the car came to a halt. The street lamp behind Lance caught the gleaming faces of men in the front seat. &ldquo;Stick &rsquo;em up, cowboy!&rdquo; cut the air with deadly menace. As Lance threw up his hands he recognized that voice. It belonged with the car. &ldquo;Okay, Uhl. Up&mdash;they are,&rdquo; he replied quietly. &ldquo;Come close.&rdquo; Lance walked to the automobile halting abreast of the front seat. Uhl had his hand in his coat pocket and he was leaning over the door. Lance knew that he faced a concealed gun and that he had to think quickly and right. Uhl was bareheaded. His clean-cut visage shone pale and cold in the light. The driver hunched down over the wheel, as if ready to race. The engine purred. Then Lance caught the gleam of a machine gun on the lap of a man in the back seat. Between him and another man shrank a girl with face as white as chalk and great dark eyes. Lance recognized her with a terrific stop of his heart.
