For an instant he seemed to reel dizzily, then the cold sickening freeze of his very marrow quickened to a hot gush of blood, and his faculties cleared to a magnified intensity. &ldquo;Cowboy, you&rsquo;ve been here on Cork&rsquo;s snatch racket?&rdquo; queried Uhl, sharply. &ldquo;Yes.&rdquo; &ldquo;What held him up?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know.&rdquo; &ldquo;We beat him to it! Who wised you and why&rsquo;re you looking for me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Want to tip you off. You cut the wire too late. Sheriff here has blocked you as far west as Tucson and as far east as El Paso. Posse down the road waiting to blow your tires into smithereens.&rdquo; Uhl burst into vehement curses: &ldquo;Raggy, &mdash;&mdash; &mdash;&mdash; your dumb soul! You &mdash;&mdash; &mdash;&mdash; &mdash;&mdash; hop-head! I ought to bump you off for that loss of time back there.... What&rsquo;ll we do?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shoot our way through,&rdquo; rasped the driver. Lance interrupted in ringing low voice: &ldquo;Might be okay for Bolton but points farther on the highway will be blocked. No chance in a million. Just as bad east. The wires are hot.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fox, what&rsquo;s the dope?&rdquo; flashed the leader. &ldquo;Are you asking me?&rdquo; curtly retorted one of the men in the back. &ldquo;Didn&rsquo;t I warn you against this racket? I advise hiding along the railroad track and hopping a freight.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re no fox. You&rsquo;re a rabbit.... Cowboy, what&rsquo;s your tip?&rdquo; &ldquo;Beat it for the hills pronto,&rdquo; exclaimed Lance, hurriedly. &ldquo;You can&rsquo;t get through this town by car.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hills!... I get it. But horses, food, blankets&mdash;where can they be found?&rdquo; &ldquo;Cowboy outfit just out of town. You can buy what you need from them and be on the trail in a jiffy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good. Where&rsquo;ll we go?&rdquo; &ldquo;Up in the Peloncillos. Rough wild country. You couldn&rsquo;t be tracked. You can hide for days. As soon as you get your dough you can ride down across the border into Mexico.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good tip, cowboy. What about this bus?&rdquo; &ldquo;Send your driver on the ridge road. Give him water and grub. When morning comes he can drive off the road into the cedars beyond the point. And hide there. He could get out later.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oke. Will you guide us?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sure. If you slip me enough.&rdquo; Uhl&rsquo;s gun gave out a metallic clink as he drew his coat over the door. Producing a roll of bills he handed one to Lance. &ldquo;Here&rsquo;s a grand.&rdquo; &ldquo;Make it two, Uhl. And promise of more if I get you through,&rdquo; demanded Lance, lowering his hands. &ldquo;Okay, you chiseler. Jump on the running board and tell the driver where to go.&rdquo; Lance ran around to the other side of the car and caught on. He directed the driver down the road and away from the town. A campfire blazed among the trees. In a reaction of feeling Lance could scarcely hold on. He imagined he was in a dreadful nightmare. But the car was moving. In the back he saw a gangster on this side with a machine gun across his knees, the same as the other. And on the floor lay another man. Lance puzzled over that. By sheer luck and wit he had met a tremendous situation. Anything to keep Uhl from carrying Madge off to the cities! She would be worse than lost or dead. He divined that Uhl would never release her. If he could steer them up into the hills, Stewart would be on his trail in another day. It was the only chance. &ldquo;Here we are,&rdquo; called Lance, as the car approached to within fifty feet of the campfire, out in the shadow. &ldquo;Fox, you and Flemm get out and stick up this bunch,&rdquo; ordered Uhl. It was done almost in the twinkling of an eye. Uhl got out and faced the cowboys. Sloan&rsquo;s comrades, especially the cook, looked comical in their maze, but Sloan himself grew pale and grim. &ldquo;No holdup, cowboys. I want to buy horses and stuff to go up in the hills. Here&rsquo;s a grand.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s thet?&rdquo; queried Sloan. &ldquo;Ten hundred smackers&mdash;a thousand dollars, you dumbbell.&rdquo; &ldquo;What do you want fer thet much?&rdquo; &ldquo;Five saddle horses, some packs, and whatever else we need.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a deal.&rdquo; Uhl stuck the bill into the cowboy&rsquo;s shirt pocket. &ldquo;Line them up, Flemm, and keep &rsquo;em covered.... Come here, cowboy.&rdquo; Lance strode into the campfire light, quite prepared for the profane ejaculations of Sloan and the Spencer brothers. &ldquo;Pick out what we want damn quick.&rdquo; &ldquo;Uhl, we&rsquo;d save time by having these cowboys help me saddle and pack. Two of your men can keep them covered,&rdquo; suggested Lance. &ldquo;Oke. Step on it,&rdquo; rejoined Uhl, then repaired to the car. He opened the back door. &ldquo;Come out, baby.&rdquo; Madge descended from the car, clad in white slacks and a white sport coat. She made a step toward the campfire, when Uhl seized her roughly. &ldquo;Say, you move only when you&rsquo;re told,&rdquo; ordered the gangster, harshly. &ldquo;Honey Bee Uhl talking&mdash;and you get it.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right. But keep your hands off me,&rdquo; flashed Madge, with a passion that told her spirit had not been weakened. And she twisted free. &ldquo;Oke, baby. But you might as well get used to them.... Raggy, throw that college bloke out. Then you grab some eats and drink and beat it.&rdquo; Lance was as amazed as the other cowboys to see a limp young man pulled out of the car. He appeared dazed or injured, but he sat up, to disclose the handsome pale features of Rollie Stevens. &ldquo;Get up and come to the fire,&rdquo; ordered Uhl. And he pushed Madge along ahead of him. &ldquo;Now sit down, both of you. In a minute I&rsquo;ll talk ransom money to you.... Raggy, don&rsquo;t forget to put our bags out of the car.&rdquo; Lance tried to see and hear everything from where he saddled Umpqua. The other cowboys were saddling and packing with extreme celerity under the guns of the two gangsters. Lance, thinking to have Madge ride his horse, shortened the stirrups. If a chance offered he might shoot one or more of these fellows and leap up behind Madge to make their escape. In a very few minutes six saddle and two pack horses were ready to travel. He searched for an extra rope and canteen, to tie them on his saddle. He heard the car roar and roll away up the ridge road. Hurrying back to the campfire he said crisply: &ldquo;Ready, Uhl.&rdquo; &ldquo;My God!&rdquo; cried Rollie Stevens. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s Sidway. Madge, look!&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve had the pleasure,&rdquo; returned Madge, with infinite scorn. &ldquo;Fetch those cowboys here,&rdquo; called Uhl. When Sloan and his two comrades were lined up in front of the gangster, he asked, indicating Sloan, &ldquo;What&rsquo;s your name?&rdquo; &ldquo;Tim Sloan.&rdquo; &ldquo;Get this dope, cowboy,&rdquo; went on the gangster, deliberately. &ldquo;In the morning you notify Stewart I&rsquo;m holding his daughter for fifty grand....&rdquo; &ldquo;My father can&rsquo;t raise that,&rdquo; interposed Madge. &ldquo;He is practically ruined. But I can raise half that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Baby, will you keep out of this?&rdquo; retorted Uhl, then turning to Sloan again he resumed, &ldquo;Notify Stewart I want fifty grand for her, and the same for her boy friend. If my orders are not obeyed, we&rsquo;ll rape the girl, and then kill them both. No bluffing. Send one man on our track with the money. Get that, cowboy?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore&mdash;I get it,&rdquo; replied Sloan, huskily. &ldquo;Fox, you keep these fellows covered until we&rsquo;re all in the saddle and out of the light.... Sidway, you lead the way with the pack horses. I&rsquo;ll follow with the dame. Fox, you and Flemm drive Stevens between you. Let&rsquo;s go.&rdquo; &ldquo;Uhl, I&rsquo;ve selected an easy-gaited horse for Miss Stewart,&rdquo; spoke up Lance. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a tough trail.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah? Bet she&rsquo;ll stand it better than any of us. I haven&rsquo;t been in a saddle half a dozen times in my life.... Which horse? Come on, baby.&rdquo; Lance led them over to Umpqua, and took from the saddle the fleece-lined coat he had untied. &ldquo;Get into this. It&rsquo;ll be bitter cold when we&rsquo;re high up,&rdquo; he said, and held the coat for her. If he had not been under stress of strongly suppressed emotion he might have recoiled from her convulsed white face and magnificent eyes. But her look of horror and hate strangely changed. &ldquo;What can&rsquo;t be true, baby?&rdquo; interposed Uhl. &ldquo;Miss Stewart, it happens that I am,&rdquo; replied Lance. &ldquo;Hurry into this coat.... You&rsquo;ll find gloves in the pocket.&rdquo; Lance blindly held the coat for her and then plunged away. Mounting Sloan&rsquo;s horse he drove the two pack animals into the road, and headed for the dark hills. In a moment or more he recognized Umpqua&rsquo;s gait behind him, and presently heard the other horses following. It was done, and his heart seemed to descend from his throat and settle where it belonged. A cool wind blew down from the heights. The stars blinked as if incredulous. His jumbled thoughts began to straighten out. No use to marvel at where he found himself&mdash;at the unaccountable fate which had finally placed it in his power to save Madge Stewart, her honor and her life, and the happiness of her parents. Somehow he would do it. All these dove-tailing angles could not be merely coincidences. They fitted, and he felt that he would solve the problem. But he must be governed by cool judgment instead of emotion. To this end he brought sternly to bear all the mentality of which he was capable. And out of the welter of thoughts he fixed upon a determination to be alert and ready on the instant to seize any opportunity to escape with Madge. It would come inevitably. These tenderfoot gangsters, unused to horses and pains, climbing into the wild rugged hills, would sooner or later provide that opportunity. But if it did not come before Uhl resorted to violence with the girl then Lance must be quick to kill him, and call upon her to run for her life while he fought it out with the others. That settled Lance into a cold and calculating mood which transformed him into another man. He was dealing with a matter of life and death&mdash;with vicious degenerates from the underworld of crime. Tim Sloan had a rather complex problem to solve, as far as Lance&rsquo;s connection with Uhl was concerned; but any cowboy would obey the gangster&rsquo;s orders, and then let Stewart decide. Lance knew what would happen and he would not have been in Uhl&rsquo;s boots for a million dollars. Stewart and his men would be Indians on the trail of this gang, and they would shoot them down from ambush or surprise them and hang them from the pines. All Lance&rsquo;s faculties must be concentrated on his task of saving Madge from these merciless fiends. Several miles out, the road swung to the south, and the Cochise Trail branched off around the lower point of the ridge. The black hills loomed high. A brightening to the east heralded a rising moon. Lance did not need the repeated calls from Uhl to &ldquo;step on it,&rdquo; and he led across the valley at a trot. The pack horses, with light burdens, did not hold up the progress. In short order Lance reached the point where the trail started up the slope. He dismounted here to wait for the others. Umpqua was close behind. Lance broke the tip of a cedar bush with a vicious twist. He prepared this first sign to make his trail easy to follow. &ldquo;How you riding, girl?&rdquo; queried Lance, as Umpqua came up. &ldquo;Swell. I like Umpqua in spite of the bum who owns him. It&rsquo;s going to be some romantic ride,&rdquo; replied Madge, mockingly. Uhl arrived next, straddling his horse as if he were on stilts. &ldquo;What you gassing about?&rdquo; he demanded. &ldquo;I asked Miss Stewart about her saddle cinch,&rdquo; returned Lance. &ldquo;Yeah? And what did that little dame say?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ask her.&rdquo; Uhl did and was promptly told to go where it was hot and that if he wanted to keep her from talking he would have to gag her. Stevens rode up then, accompanied by his captors. He appeared to have recovered somewhat and sat his saddle upright. The other gangsters, packing their bags and machine guns in front, looked as if they would have been glad to get down and walk. &ldquo;We begin to climb here,&rdquo; said Lance, &ldquo;and I&rsquo;ll tighten your cinches.&rdquo; When he worked back to Madge and made a motion at her cinch she interrupted him. &ldquo;Keep your black hands away from me. I don&rsquo;t want to be soiled. If my cinch needs tightening, I&rsquo;ll do it,&rdquo; and her voice rang with contempt. &ldquo;Black?... Oh, I see. Gosh, how dumb I am!&rdquo; declared Lance, lowering his bare hands. &ldquo;Listen, you all. This trail is steep. Give your horse his head. Lean forward in bad places and hang onto his mane. When I stop to rest my horses you do the same. That&rsquo;s all.&rdquo; He slapped the pack animals up the trail, and mounting he followed them. Umpqua, with a loose bridle, kept right on the heels of Lance&rsquo;s horse. When Lance turned to look back he saw Madge almost close enough to touch. The other four riders came on in close single file. Lance zigzagged after the pack horses, and forbore gazing back again. But he thrilled at her nerve. She was not in the least afraid of Uhl. Lance let the pack horses initiate the rests. They were well-trained animals. Beyond the first foothill yawned a shadowy cedar flat, which led to another slope, long and gradual. When he surmounted it to the summit a full moon soared white above the black domes, transforming night into a silvery luminous day. &ldquo;Beastly trail,&rdquo; declared Madge, sarcastically. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll have to send a gang of laborers up here for two weeks to work on it. Big expense, though.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll say,&rdquo; replied Uhl, taking her literally. &ldquo;Tough as nuts. But, baby, you ride like one of those circus girls in tights.&rdquo; &ldquo;Miss Stewart, you&rsquo;d better not go to such expense,&rdquo; interposed Lance, satirically. &ldquo;Oh, are you a monumental liar?&rdquo; burst out the girl. &ldquo;Shut up gabbing to him,&rdquo; ordered Uhl. That significant speech for once silenced Madge, and it almost drove Lance into throwing his gun on the egoistic crook. He had kidnaped Madge for more than ransom. Lance rode on into an up-an-down cedar country with an occasional pine tree heralding the approach to the heights. The air began to have a cool edge. The moon climbed toward the zenith. Presently the trail led into a narrow canyon. It was long and tortuous, heading at last into a mountain meadow, where traveling was comfortable for a while. A black belt of pines loomed ahead, shining in the moonlight. Lance kept eye and ear keen for his followers. Madge appeared to ride easily, but the others were growing crippled. They shifted from side to side in their saddles, let their legs hang, and grumbled intermittently. Once, under the dark pines, Lance was seized by a savage and desperate impulse. Here was the place to shoot Uhl and ride off with Madge. He almost surrendered to it. He was sure of killing the gangster, sure of Umpqua, but some hitch he could not anticipate might give the other gangsters a moment to rake forward with those machine guns. Lance refused the chance. A better would offer, and he importuned patience. The forest belt gave way to rough rocky country where Madge, if she had not bestrode a grand horse, would have suffered considerably. The horses labored slowly over shale and up loose slides and through thick brush that tore at them. The moon reached a point overhead; the air had a bite in it; coyotes mourned lonely cries; the night grew far advanced. Here Uhl at last fell off his saddle and walked behind Madge leading his horse. The other gangsters cursed and raved for a halt. &ldquo;Sidway, for God&rsquo;s sake, have a heart!&rdquo; yelped Uhl, finally. &ldquo;Aren&rsquo;t we far enough? Can&rsquo;t we camp here?&rdquo; &ldquo;No water. No grass. You must go on,&rdquo; replied Lance. &ldquo;But we&rsquo;ve rode&mdash;a hundred miles&mdash;already,&rdquo; panted the gangster. &ldquo;Seems like, maybe. But we&rsquo;re not twenty miles from town. Better get on your horse again.&rdquo; Uhl obeyed groaning. Lance would not let them rest, and presently divining that Uhl was dependent upon him and knew it, he turned a deaf ear to appeals and curses and threats alike. And he led on and up through increasingly rough country, until Uhl, with a bellow, fell off his horse. &ldquo;Sorry, Uhl,&rdquo; said Lance. &ldquo;You almost made it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Made what?&rdquo; &ldquo;Camp at Cochise&rsquo;s stronghold. Not much further. And a swell place. Water, wood, grass. A log cabin.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gimme a&mdash;drink&mdash;Fox,&rdquo; panted the gangster. &ldquo;I can walk&mdash;the rest.&rdquo; &ldquo;I wouldn&rsquo;t ride no furder for Al Capone,&rdquo; retorted Flemm, doggedly. &ldquo;Well, you can rot here&mdash;for a little shot,&rdquo; snapped Uhl, and he labored to his feet. &ldquo;Beat it, cowboy.&rdquo; Lance led on, riding with his hands in his pockets. On the heights it was cold. Madge would be warm, all except her feet. Stevens appeared to be sagging in his saddle, but Lance could not summon any sympathy for the collegian. After more weary miles of travel Uhl burst out: &ldquo;Baby, what kind of liar did you call Sidway?&rdquo; &ldquo;Monumental. But that&rsquo;s weak. He&rsquo;s a colossal liar!&rdquo; &ldquo;Shall I bump him off?&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, you&rsquo;re a hellcat, ain&rsquo;t you? But I&rsquo;ll tame you.... Hold up, you guide. I want to get on again.&rdquo; The last miles of that uphill ride were dragging and cruel to the gangsters. Even Stevens, hurt at the outset in some way, endured the ordeal better. When Lance led into the beautiful wooded park, which inclosed Cochise&rsquo;s stronghold, the moon was low and dawn not far away. He halted the cavalcade under some spreading pines. Lance&rsquo;s hands were so stiff from cold that he could scarcely start a fire. But that once done, he gathered firewood, and soon had a blaze. White and silent now Madge leaned against a tree. Lance threw his saddle, then flew to the packs. In a few moments he had them off the horses. He carried one to the fire and threw out blankets. Uhl knelt, his shaking hands to the fire. The other gangsters stood over the blaze, guns in hands, still wary and watchful. Their chief might have trusted Lance, but they did not. &ldquo;Majesty&mdash;aren&rsquo;t you&mdash;frozen?&rdquo; asked Rollie, his teeth chattering. &ldquo;Come to the&mdash;fire.&rdquo; &ldquo;My feet&mdash;are ice,&rdquo; she whispered. &ldquo;Here,&rdquo; cried Lance, sharply. &ldquo;They can&rsquo;t be frozen. It&rsquo;s not cold enough.... Sit here, on this blanket. Lean against the pack. Put this blanket over you.... Never mind, I&rsquo;ll take off your shoes.&rdquo; Her thin shoes and stockings afforded little protection against this frosty air. Her little feet did feel like blocks of ice. &ldquo;Rollie, throw a blanket round you and sit close to her,&rdquo; went on Lance. No one seemed to oppose him, and he caught Madge&rsquo;s great dark eyes upon him. Then Lance leaped to throw the other pack, and unsaddle the horses. He turned them loose. The luxuriant grass and good water in the mountain park were the equal of any pasture. There was not much likelihood of their straying soon, and Umpqua at least would stay. Lance went back to the fire. Madge was asleep, her fair face drooping upon Rollie&rsquo;s shoulder. He too had sunk into weary slumber. On the other side of the fire Uhl lay covered with his head on a log, dead asleep. Fox appeared to have crawled under a pack canvas. Flemm sat on his guard, his machine gun at rest, his eyes like gimlets. &ldquo;Cowboy, stretch yourself right there,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Me an&rsquo; Fox will have a go at this job.&rdquo; Lance dragged his saddle close, and wrapping himself in his blanket he lay down to make up a little for the loss of two nights&rsquo; sleep. His last thought was a wondering if he dared risk a snap shot at Flemm, and then kill Fox and Uhl as they lay. Sleep claimed him before he could decide. Daylight had come when he was awakened by a sound of wood being dumped upon the fire. Fox had taken Flemm&rsquo;s place on guard. The others were locked in slumber. Lance fell asleep again and when he awakened the sun was high. Uhl sat huddled near the fire, his pale face showing the havoc of extreme exertion and privation. Behind him the fox-featured guard paced to and fro, gun in hand. Lance threw off his blanket and arose to his feet, cramped with the cold. &ldquo;Good morning. Kind of brisk up here on top,&rdquo; he said, cheerfully. &ldquo;Brisk?&mdash;Ha! I damn near froze to death,&rdquo; ejaculated Uhl. Lance spread his hands to the blaze, and casually looked about. The third gangster evidently was hidden under the tarpaulin. The two victims of the kidnapers were asleep. All Lance could see of Madge was her disheveled golden hair. &ldquo;Uhl, it&rsquo;s only a little way to the log cabin,&rdquo; said Lance. &ldquo;Much better place to camp. Hadn&rsquo;t we better move over? Then I&rsquo;ll cook some meat and make some hot coffee.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oke, cowboy. Step on it. I&rsquo;ll follow with these duds.... Fox, kick Flemm out of his sleep.... Baby, wake up and get wise. This is the last time you&rsquo;ll ever sleep with any man but me.&rdquo; Lance, with murder in his heart, lifted a pack upon his shoulder, and stepping into the trail he strode for the clearing. He could see it through the big pines, a beautiful glade, with its frosted grass glistening under the sun. Sight of deer made him think of his rifle. That was in his saddle sheath. There might come a chance later to use it. Umpqua whistled from some point near at hand. Lance saw no sign of the other horses. A huge pine tree with wide spreading branches, and some high gray rocks marked the site of the log cabin. Its open door stared like a black curious eye, wondering what was to happen there. All around the stately pines stood up and beyond them rugged crags. This spot had once been the stronghold of the Apache chief, Cochise, at which time the trail was known only to the Indians. Depositing the pack under the pine, Lance hurried back for another load. Halfway he met the gangsters. Lance swerved off the trail into the brush. He had a reluctance to meet Madge Stewart face to face. Yet the part he was playing sustained him with a kind of rapture. Perhaps he was afraid she might see through him. Most certainly he must look a queer kind of villain. Returning to the glade with the second pack, which he had opened, he set that down with the other, and then proceeded to build a fire. This done, he went back to fetch his saddle and the blankets that had been left. Flemm, the meanest looking of the gangsters, manifestly distrusted Lance, and for that matter, the situation itself. He sat apart, holding the machine gun across his knee. Lance spread the tarpaulin on the grass and proceeded to empty the contents of a pack. &ldquo;Rollie, you&rsquo;re one of these worthless rich guys, I know,&rdquo; said Lance, not without sarcasm. &ldquo;But if you&rsquo;d condescend to help me, we&rsquo;d have breakfast sooner.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d starve to death before I&rsquo;d associate with you in any way,&rdquo; declared the collegian. &ldquo;Yes, and you&rsquo;d let Madge starve, too. If you and she were left alone on your own, she&rsquo;d soon get your number.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t address me again, you two-faced scoundrel. You&rsquo;re a dirty rat, Sidway, and your pleas won&rsquo;t get you anywhere with Miss Stewart or me.&rdquo; &ldquo;What would you call yourself for night before last?&rdquo; queried Lance, in bitter scorn. Stevens&rsquo; pale face flamed red, but it was fury more than shame that strangled his speech in his throat. &ldquo;Cowboy, you said a mouthful,&rdquo; interposed Uhl, caustically. &ldquo;What&rsquo;d this swelled-up sap pull on my baby?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know whom you mean,&rdquo; snapped Lance. &ldquo;Well, then, Miss Stewart.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s none of your business.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah?&mdash;See here, cowboy. Don&rsquo;t let this dame work on you. Flemm swears you&rsquo;re sore because we beat you to this snatch.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bee, I tell you he&rsquo;s not on the level,&rdquo; interposed Flemm. &ldquo;Sidway, you&rsquo;re not on the spot with me,&rdquo; went on Uhl. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve done me service and I&rsquo;m for you. But don&rsquo;t strain things on account of a pretty skirt. That doesn&rsquo;t go with me.... What&rsquo;d this swell sap do to Madge Stewart that burned you up?&rdquo; Lance was quick to see how jealousy and heat had imperiled him, and therefore the girl. Despite his calculations he had erred. &ldquo;Chief, you wouldn&rsquo;t think it much,&rdquo; Lance said, with an outright laugh. &ldquo;I happened to see Stevens trying to take liberties with Miss Stewart.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did he get away with it?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll say he didn&rsquo;t.&rdquo; The gangsters&rsquo; explosion of mirth was no compliment to Madge. Lance resisted a strong impulse to look at her then. No doubt she would be something unforgettable to see. &ldquo;Skip the dirt about me, you poor fish!&rdquo; she interrupted shortly. Suddenly Lance felt her close to him, and then heard her light step. He was on his knees on the tarpaulin fumbling among cans and parcels, and he was aware that his hands shook. &ldquo;Did anyone see the sack I had with me last night? Coffee and butter.&rdquo; &ldquo;If it was a snake, it&rsquo;d bite you,&rdquo; spoke up Madge, sweetly. &ldquo;There, under your nose!&mdash;Lance, can I offer my services as assistant cook?&rdquo; Lance sustained a slight start. &ldquo;Are you&mdash;any good?&rdquo; &ldquo;Can you mix biscuit dough?&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell. Nels taught me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Go to it. Here&rsquo;s flour, salt, lard, pan. Fetch some water. I&rsquo;ll heat the Dutch oven.&rdquo; Uhl showed interest in the proceedings. The other gangsters grinned sardonically. Stevens sat dejectedly with his face in his hands. Lance cut strips of bacon into a skillet all the while bafflingly aware of the going and returning of the girl, and then her agitating presence close beside him, on her knees. The shine of her hair, the fragrance of her, the vitality of her, and more than all that nameless and irresistible attraction seemed propelled into his senses. Hurriedly he arose to rake red coals out of the fire, to put on the oven to heat, and the coffeepot to boil, to fetch more firewood, and to find other tasks. It was the eyes of Uhl and his allies fixed upon the kneeling girl that drove Lance to look at her. She had thrown off the heavy coat, and knelt in her thin slacks, her round, gold-tanned arms bare, her beautiful voluptuous form revealed in all its devastating allurement. Lance cursed under his breath. This gangster was a ghoul for both money and flesh. Madge was in deadly peril, yet seemed oblivious of it. Lance&rsquo;s calculation had been that by midday Stewart would be on his trail. That would fetch him to the mountain clearing late the coming night or at dawn. What might happen before then? No matter what happened, Stewart and his men could be expected to waylay or ambush the gangsters and kill them. No doubt Tim Sloan and the Spencer boys would be with them. It was all up with Uhl right that moment. They would not escape these relentless rangers. Gene Stewart would experience a throwback to his wild frontier days. But they were hours and miles away. And Lance had to escape with Madge before nightfall. These thoughts revolved in his mind as he performed the tasks of cook around the campfire. Presently breakfast was ready, and it was Madge, not Lance, who called: &ldquo;Come and get it!&rdquo; Kneeling over the fire had caused her cheeks to burn red, and it was only in her deep somber eyes that there was any sign of physical or mental distress. They all sat or knelt to eat and drink, except the cursed Flemm, who patrolled his short beat. Fox brought him food and drink. &ldquo;Baby, I didn&rsquo;t think it was in you,&rdquo; declared Uhl, devouring one of the hot biscuits. Then Lance discovered that as she did not reply, nor deign to glance at the gangster, he took offense at her indifference. Evidently he had a tremendous egoism. &ldquo;Get this, baby,&rdquo; he flashed, in cold passion. &ldquo;Soon as I thaw out and get some sleep, I&rsquo;ll change your damned snooty manner.&rdquo; With that he stamped away into the pine brush beyond the clearing. Stevens appeared to shrivel up at the significance of that threat. Madge gazed intently at Lance, her wonderful eyes, hypnotic in effect, searching his very soul. She was delving into his depths. What did she imagine she saw there? She was strangely uncertain of her convictions about him. Her present bad conceptions might be warring with good ones of the past. Lance nearly betrayed himself before her tremendous appeal. But he was aware that the beady and fox-eyed gangsters were watching, too. Kneeling once more, Lance bent over the utensils, and began collecting them preparatory to washing. Presently Uhl returned. &ldquo;Flemm, I&rsquo;ll give you a rest for half an hour. Then I want to sleep. God, that sun feels good!&rdquo; Then he turned to Madge. &ldquo;Baby, you can go into the cabin.&rdquo; She hurriedly acted upon the order. &ldquo;Uhl, hadn&rsquo;t I better look up the horses?&rdquo; queried Lance. &ldquo;Horses? I forgot them.&rdquo; &ldquo;They&rsquo;ve strayed. I didn&rsquo;t see any tracks going down the trail. So they must be around. You realize how important horses are, don&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;By God, I do now!... Here, Stevens, you wash up that mess. Cowboy, find those horses.&rdquo; Lance, making a show of anxiety and hurry, strode off. Circling the clearing he found Sloan&rsquo;s roan and near by his own horse Umpqua. No sign of the other animals. Lance did not bother to hunt tracks. He made a detour and came up within sight of the camp, and sat down on a log to peer through the foliage. He could see the cabin. There, watching intently, he brooded over the situation. Presently he saw Flemm rejoin Uhl. The three gangsters held a colloquy, which was unintelligible to Lance. But they did not appear concerned. Once Fox pointed at Stevens, who knelt with his back turned, laboring over his task. Again Uhl made a passionate gesture toward the cabin, at which Flemm threw up his hands in resignation. Then Uhl lay down on a blanket in the sun and went to sleep. Lingering there for some time Lance finally retraced his steps. &ldquo;Found only two horses,&rdquo; he informed Flemm. &ldquo;The rest have wandered off. I&rsquo;ll have to saddle up to find them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wait. If we waked up the boss now he&rsquo;d bump you off.&rdquo; &ldquo;But every hour they may stray farther away.&rdquo; &ldquo;Okay by me. I&rsquo;d a hell of sight rather walk.&rdquo; Whereupon Lance proceeded to wipe the utensils for Stevens. &ldquo;Rollie, this is a tough break,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t take it so hard. You&rsquo;ll come out okay, except for loss of some dough you won&rsquo;t miss.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t mind the money. I fear for Majesty. It was all my fault&mdash;that we were caught by these ruffians. I persuaded her to come out&mdash;lied to get her, in fact. And we were held up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sidway,&rdquo; interrupted Flemm, sarcastically, &ldquo;you don&rsquo;t strike me as a snatch scout.... Cheese it!&rdquo; Lance wisely refrained from further talk, though he gnashed his teeth. When the chores were finished he cut and packed firewood, mostly bark from dead trees. After that he cut great armloads of spruce boughs and dragged them to camp. &ldquo;Sid, there&rsquo;s somethin&rsquo; rotten about you, but it ain&rsquo;t in this camp stuff,&rdquo; commented Fox. &ldquo;Ha! He learned all that in Chi,&rdquo; laughed Flemm. &ldquo;Say, tenderfeet, if you&rsquo;re stuck up here for a week, you&rsquo;ll appreciate soft beds,&rdquo; replied Lance. &ldquo;Week! What in hell&rsquo;s eatin&rsquo; you? Two days is my limit,&rdquo; retorted Fox. Lance strode off, ax in hand, groaning over a thought of what complications would evolve among these violent men in another day. He cut armloads of spruce, and packing that back he approached the door of the cabin, and without a glance at the gangsters, made bold to enter. To his amaze Madge had been waiting, surely watching for him, for she leaped at him. He let the load of spruce fall with a sodden swish. Her extraordinary loveliness must have been due to intense spirit and emotion. Her face was like a pearl&mdash;her eyes glowing purple. &ldquo;Are you crooked or honest?&rdquo; she added. &ldquo;Crooked&mdash;as hell!&rdquo; he gasped. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll try.... Keep your nerve. Watch!&rdquo; he whispered, huskily, and turned to stride out. Before facing the gangsters he thought it best to go into the woods and cut more boughs. Recovering his poise he packed another huge load back to camp. Presently he said to Flemm. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s getting late in the afternoon. I ought to be wrangling the horses.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah. An&rsquo; what&rsquo;s that?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hunting them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Set down an keep your shirt on. Or you might peel potatoes an&rsquo; what have you.&rdquo; The gangster leader roused out of his slumber with surprising quickness, and sat up, blinking. &ldquo;These guys won&rsquo;t let me hunt for the horses. I found only two. I ought to ride around these woods and find them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hop to it, cowboy. But don&rsquo;t forget we want supper soon.&rdquo; Lance ran to get his saddle and bridle and blankets, tingling with the vibration of his nerves. All day he had pondered over the need to saddle Umpqua. Once astride the horse he felt that the critical hour was near at hand. Riding off out of sight he returned to the point where he could watch the camp. The heat of the day was waning, and sunset burned in the west. Lance saw Uhl, bare-headed and coatless, get up to go toward the cabin. And he went in! That was a signal for Lance to ride back toward the camp. He had to meet the crisis. Terror and panic gave way to fury, and by the time he had reached the clearing he was steel&mdash;cold and tight in mind and body. Boldly he rode to the big pine opposite the cabin, and there halted. Flemm and Fox were watching him curiously. With warning gesture, Lance pointed down the trail toward the opening into the clearing. Both gangsters were impelled to leap up and look. On the moment Lance heard Madge&rsquo;s ringing voice: &ldquo;No!... Bee Uhl, I&rsquo;ll pay the ransom. But....&rdquo; &ldquo;Baby, you started it. You got to come through. No dame who ever lived can play with me,&rdquo; he replied, in cold passion. &ldquo;No matter now. You&rsquo;ll come across.&rdquo; Lance leaped off Umpqua and ran over to the excited gangsters. His manner would have struck anyone into amaze and fear. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;s Uhl?&rdquo; he queried. &ldquo;He said he was goin&rsquo; to love his baby,&rdquo; replied Fox. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s eatin&rsquo; you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I rode up high back there on the slope. And I saw two horses down where the trail comes up. First I thought they were our horses. But they had riders and were coming this way.&rdquo; &ldquo;Riders! You mean men on horseback?&rdquo; &ldquo;I sure do.... There may be more than two. Looks damn bad. You better sneak down the trail, keeping out of sight, and make sure.&rdquo; &ldquo;What of?&rdquo; &ldquo;Who it is and what they want.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fox, you go,&rdquo; ordered Flemm. &ldquo;Okay. But what&rsquo;ll I do!&rdquo; &ldquo;Hold them up. An&rsquo; use your gray matter.&rdquo; Fox looked to his machine gun, and ran out to the trail, which he entered, and glided along till he reached the green foliage where he soon passed out of sight. Lance stepped up on the pack beside Flemm. &ldquo;There! Look!&rdquo; he whispered, tensely. &ldquo;That little open place, beyond the yellow pine. See!&rdquo; &ldquo;My eyes must be damn poor. I see nothin&rsquo; but green,&rdquo; growled Flemm. &ldquo;Okay! then see stars,&rdquo; hissed Lance, and swung his heavy gun on the gangster&rsquo;s bare head. Flemm fell soddenly. Whereupon Lance sped across the space to the side of the cabin, listening, watching the door. He heard a scuffle, then swift light footfalls, then panting breaths, and&mdash;: &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not&mdash;afraid of you&mdash;Bee Uhl!&rdquo; &ldquo;Swell! I like my dames to be wildcats,&rdquo; replied Uhl, with something exultant in his voice, no longer cold. &ldquo;Make me fight for it, eh?&rdquo; &ldquo;You lousy bum! Fight you? I can whip you,&rdquo; cried Madge, hotly. Lance made for the door. He heard thudding footfalls, a cry, and then a rip of cloth. &ldquo;Let go!&mdash;Oh, you beast!&rdquo; &ldquo;Baby&mdash;I&rsquo;ll strip you&mdash;right now,&rdquo; panted the gangster. Lance leaped into the doorway, gun leveled. Uhl had Madge backed against the wall. The gangster&rsquo;s clawing hands held strips of her clothing. The girl, half-naked, was warding him off, like a tigress at bay. The gangster froze a second, then sprang into convulsive action, to catch the girl and get her in front of him. But she was as strong as he and far more supple. A short struggle ensued, the end of which came when Uhl made the blunder of striking her down. Then, even as he half turned, his thin face gray, his eyes hot and clear as molten steel, Lance leaped to get in better line. When his gun boomed the gangster appeared to be propelled against the wall. It upheld him a moment. A great bloody blotch came as if by magic. Lance thought he had shot away half of Uhl&rsquo;s face. He stuck there an instant, a ghastly spectacle, then slid sideways to the floor. Madge lay apparently unconscious, a bruise on her white temple and a red welt across her bare shoulder. Lance snatched up a blanket, and lifting her flung it around her and carried her out the door. Flemm lay as Lance had last seen him. Far down the trail Fox appeared running toward camp. Lance took a long shot at him for luck, then sheathing his gun, he stepped to the snorting Umpqua, and kicked the stirrup around. &ldquo;Steady, Ump! It&rsquo;s me. Hold, you fool horse!&rdquo; With Madge in his left arm Lance mounted and drew her across his saddle. Umpqua needed no urging. As he plunged away a rattle of gunshots blended in a continuous volley, and a rain of bullets whistled and ticked through the trees, and pattered on the cabin. But in a few jumps the horse was behind the cabin, and out of danger. Lance held him to a lope along the wall of foliage, into the woods. &ldquo;Wal, Boss, you was loco. Never seen you so oot of your haid.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hello, old-timer. Been having crazy dreams. Must have been that punch concoction Madge sprung on us.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wait till you see Ren.... Pile oot, Gene, an&rsquo; throw on your ridin&rsquo; things. We&rsquo;ve got work on hand.&rdquo; &ldquo;Uhuh. So that was the big idea. What&rsquo;s up?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sidway just left on the trail of your cattle. Rustled last night before the ball opened.&rdquo; &ldquo;By jacks!&mdash;He wanted to tell me last night. But Madge wouldn&rsquo;t let him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Might hev spoiled her party.... We&rsquo;re to hit Sidway&rsquo;s trail pronto. Danny is wranglin&rsquo; the hawses. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve throwed some grub together. Come along, Boss. We hev Sidway to thank fer somethin&rsquo; like old times.&rdquo; Nels clinked out into the patio where his musical footsteps died away. Gene quickly dressed in his range garb, and slipped a comb and toothbrush into his pocket. His gunbelt had ample shells, and his rifle was down at Nels&rsquo; bunkhouse. Then he went to his wife&rsquo;s room and poking his head in the door he awakened her: &ldquo;Sorry, dear. Nels just called me. We&rsquo;re going after a bunch of strayed stock. May not be back for a day or two.&rdquo; &ldquo;So this is what young Sidway had to tell you?&rdquo; she replied quickly. &ldquo;Good guess, Mom. Go back to sleep, and don&rsquo;t worry about what that cowboy starts. He&rsquo;s a finisher.&rdquo; Going out through the patio Stewart saw two of Madge&rsquo;s guests, boy and girl, asleep in each other&rsquo;s arms in a hammock. They were half covered by a colorful blanket. It was a pretty picture, and Gene thought the girl was Maramee. &ldquo;Some party!&rdquo; he muttered, as he strode on. &ldquo;But dog-gone-it! I had a good time watching them.... Only&mdash;my girl worries me!&rdquo; Clear daylight had come when Stewart reached the bunkhouses. Four horses saddled and bridled stood at the rail. He found his men inside eating. Danny looked grim and dark as he bent over his plate. Starr appeared drunk. &ldquo;Boss, throw some hot cakes under yore belt.... Ren, you drink thet hot coffee or I&rsquo;ll pour it down you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels&mdash;old manz&mdash;I want drink.&rdquo; &ldquo;I gave you a bracer.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, you&rsquo;re drunk,&rdquo; said Gene. &ldquo;Whosh drunk? I ain&rsquo;t so.... It wash just thet peach juice las night.&rdquo; Nels forced the cowboy to drink the coffee, and stuffed some biscuits and cold meat in his pocket. &ldquo;Boss, let&rsquo;s get goin&rsquo;,&rdquo; interposed Danny Mains, darkly. &ldquo;If I don&rsquo;t miss my guess we&rsquo;ll hev hell catchin&rsquo; Sidway. Thet boy&rsquo;s another Nick Steele.&rdquo; &ldquo;Right,&rdquo; agreed Nels. They dragged Starr outside and threw him on his horse. &ldquo;Can you stick on?&rdquo; queried Stewart. &ldquo;You insolt me,&rdquo; protested Ren, swaying in his saddle. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll hold him on, Gene,&rdquo; said Danny, &ldquo;till he sobers up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bosh, whash in &rsquo;ell was thet punch as night?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know, Ren. It sure gave me a hell of a nightmare.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m agonna get thet mixtoure from Miss Madge&mdash;an&rsquo; make million bucks. Washen thet heavern best drink ever?&rdquo; &ldquo;A most deceiving one, Ren. It led you on.&rdquo; &ldquo;Whash wuz in it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Dynamite, greased lightning, sweet cider and aqua fortis.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell you shay?&rdquo; They rode down past the village, Stewart and Nels gradually drawing ahead, while Mains came along behind steadying Starr in his saddle. &ldquo;Nels, what&rsquo;s the deal?&rdquo; asked Stewart. &ldquo;Wal, some Mexicans sloped off with the rest of yore an&rsquo; Danny&rsquo;s cattle. Gosh, but Danny is sore! They was slick aboot it, when every last person in the country was heah last night. I&rsquo;ve a hunch how Sidway got wise to the deal. He&rsquo;s a clever boy, Gene. But how he found oot ain&rsquo;t nothin&rsquo; to us. The thieves drove the cattle acrost to the valley behind Gray Divide. An&rsquo; they expect to work them by easy stages down acrost the border. It&rsquo;d been a cinch but fer Sidway.... Wal, he&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; to locate them, then ride on into town an&rsquo; get help. In the mawnin&rsquo; he&rsquo;ll haid the rustlers off in thet narrow valley. Our part is to ride in behind the ridge an&rsquo; trail them down, keepin&rsquo; oot of sight. By this time tomorrow I reckon we&rsquo;ll be smokin&rsquo; them up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hardly, in that open valley,&rdquo; replied Stewart. &ldquo;They&rsquo;ll see us and slope. Which suits me, though I&rsquo;d like to throw a scare into them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, we&rsquo;ll get the cattle back, an&rsquo; mebbe thet&rsquo;ll end this two-bit rustlin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, as long as westerners raise cattle out on the range there will be stealing.&rdquo; &ldquo;Kind of a disease.... Look, Gene, heah&rsquo;s the trail, plain as plowed ground. An&rsquo; there&rsquo;s the track of Sidway&rsquo;s hawse.&rdquo; &ldquo;I see.... Nels, do you remember when I gave my great roan Majesty to Madeline and beat it for Mexico to get myself shot?&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd, yes,&rdquo; declared Nels, fervently. &ldquo;All thet past seems to be gettin&rsquo; clearer in my memory.... Gene, I&rsquo;ll bet Sidway gives Umpqua to Madge.&rdquo; &ldquo;Not that cowboy. He&rsquo;s got too much spunk. He&rsquo;s onto Madge,&rdquo; replied Gene, a little sadly. &ldquo;Wal, I reckon thet won&rsquo;t make no hell of a difference. There ain&rsquo;t a man in the world who could resist thet girl long.&rdquo; &ldquo;If you put it that way,&rdquo; agreed Stewart, pleased with the old cattleman&rsquo;s hint. Once across the highway the four riders settled down to a steady trot, and in two hours had reached the rocky point of the ridge. They rode around cautiously. The gateway to the valley was wide, and on the ridge side thick with sage and brush. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s hold up heah,&rdquo; suggested Nels, and reined in. &ldquo;Ren, air you so bleary-eyed yet you cain&rsquo;t see nothin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, I can see a hawse ten miles an&rsquo; a steer more&rsquo;n thet&mdash;an&rsquo; a gurl with a red bonnet twice as fur,&rdquo; declared Starr, swaggeringly. &ldquo;All right. Climb up the slope heah an&rsquo; see if you can spot the cattle down the valley.&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd!&mdash;Climb in these high-heel boots?&rdquo; &ldquo;Come on, Ren. I&rsquo;ll go, too,&rdquo; offered Stewart. They did not ascend the rough brushy slope more than a hundred feet when Starr made good his brag. Then Gene saw a long black band, moving like a snake, down the valley. &ldquo;Eight or ten miles?&rdquo; asked Gene. &ldquo;More&rsquo;n thet, Boss. Jest moseyin&rsquo; along.&rdquo; They retraced their steps and reported to Nels. &ldquo;Wal,&rdquo; said that worthy, &ldquo;I reckon we better keep travelin&rsquo;. We want to be on their heels when Sidway haids them back in the mawnin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, don&rsquo;t I remember there&rsquo;s good water down this valley?&rdquo; asked Gene. &ldquo;Shore you do. Nice rocky creek haids aboot where them cattle air now.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;ll be far enough for us. We&rsquo;ll camp there.... Walk your horses and keep your eyes peeled.&rdquo; They rode on in single file, somewhat separated. The sun rose hot; a flock of buzzards circled high over the locality where the herd moved, indicating death to a calf or heifer. Coyotes slunk through the sage, another indication of meat on the move; the black domes of the Peloncillos sank behind the gray foothills. Stewart&rsquo;s memory was busy. He recalled his early days with Nick Steele and Monty Price, and others of Stillwell&rsquo;s famous cowboys. And that reminded him of Madeline&rsquo;s brother Al, who had married Florence Kingsley, and had moved to Colorado to take charge of a ranch she had inherited. They had prospered. Stewart wondered if it would not be a good plan to ask financial assistance of Al. Something had to be done as soon as Madge&rsquo;s guests left, or he would lose the ranch. Madge would have to be told of the impending ruin, and Stewart hated the thought. But he must do it. Madge was wonderful, adorable, irresistible, but she was on the wrong track. Hours wore away while Stewart revolved memories and problems and hurts over and over in his mind. Some time late in the afternoon Stewart and his men arrived at the head of the creek and halted to make camp there. It was an ideal spot, with grass and sage, and cottonwood trees, and dead cedars near by on the slope. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll boil a pot of coffee,&rdquo; drawled Nels, &ldquo;an&rsquo; what with our meat an&rsquo; biscuits we won&rsquo;t fare so bad.&rdquo; &ldquo;Rustle then, for I&rsquo;m almost asleep this minute,&rdquo; replied Gene. &ldquo;We ain&rsquo;t none of us had a damn wink of sleep,&rdquo; added Starr. &ldquo;Why, Ren, I had to kick you onmerciful this mawnin,&rdquo; protested Nels. &ldquo;Thet wasn&rsquo;t sleep. I was unconscious from Majesty Stewart&rsquo;s punch.... Boss, don&rsquo;t you never let thet gurl make thet drink again. My Gawd! if college eddication is responsible fer thet&mdash;wall, when I marry Bonita an&rsquo; we hev a dotter, she ain&rsquo;t goin&rsquo; to get any modern schoolin&rsquo; atall.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, your philosophy is sound,&rdquo; declared Stewart, ruefully after the laugh had subsided. &ldquo;But it can&rsquo;t be adopted.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; why&rsquo;n th&rsquo;ell not?&rdquo; &ldquo;Because these days girls will do as they please whether they have education or not. They are going to have equal rights with men.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Gene, thet&rsquo;ll make a better world,&rdquo; interposed Nels. &ldquo;Nels, never in yore born days, did you know anythin&rsquo; aboot wimmen, much less a dotter,&rdquo; observed Danny, pessimistically. &ldquo;Dan, do you mean a dotter is a turrible burden?&rdquo; &ldquo;More&rsquo;n turrible&rsquo;n awful.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; if you had it all to live over again would you kept single, so you couldn&rsquo;t hev no dotter?&rdquo; &ldquo;I ain&rsquo;t sayin&rsquo; thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; you, Gene, would you rather never hev had Madge?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, old pard, ten thousand times no,&rdquo; burst out Stewart, somehow glad to express himself. &ldquo;Madge has been a joy. And will be forever.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thar yore talkin&rsquo;. She&rsquo;s life&mdash;beautiful life&mdash;an&rsquo; thet cain&rsquo;t be perfect.&rdquo; They talked and had their leisurely and frugal meal round the little smokeless campfire, while the sun set, and shadows appeared under the slopes. Stewart made his bed with saddle and blankets, and scarcely had he stretched himself when a subtle glue touched his eyelids. Late in the night he awoke, saw Danny replenishing the fire, fell asleep again, to be roused at dawn by an ungentle boot. &ldquo;Come an&rsquo; get it,&rdquo; said Nels, cheerily. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve a hunch we&rsquo;ve a day ahaid of us.&rdquo; Before broad daylight they were on the move. When the sun arose, Danny and Ren rode up the slope to locate the cattle, but failed to do so, owing to a projecting cape that ran down into the valley. This was some miles ahead. Before they reached it Ren sighted dust clouds. &ldquo;On the run already,&rdquo; declared Nels. &ldquo;Looks like it,&rdquo; admitted Stewart. &ldquo;They&rsquo;re pretty far yet, Boss,&rdquo; added Ren. &ldquo;Wal, there ain&rsquo;t no use in our haidin&rsquo; the cattle off, when we want them to come this way.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. But how about the rustlers?&rdquo; &ldquo;If they got haided off below, we won&rsquo;t see hide nor hair of them.&rdquo; They rounded the cape and rode at a trot fully five miles farther before Ren sighted cattle. They were headed up the valley and evidently had been running, but had now slowed down. They rode on, keeping sharp lookout for riders on the slopes, and presently had to take to higher ground to let a scattered herd pass. &ldquo;Aboot seven hundred, I&rsquo;d say,&rdquo; observed Nels. &ldquo;Reckon thet&rsquo;s all of them. Not winded much. They&rsquo;ll be home tomorrow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wonder where&rsquo;n hell them rustlers rode?&rdquo; complained Ren. &ldquo;I ain&rsquo;t feelin&rsquo; swell, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;d shore like to shoot at somebody.&rdquo; &ldquo;Suits me,&rdquo; replied Danny, relieved. &ldquo;Killin&rsquo; Mexicans even from acrost the border wouldn&rsquo;t set good with my family.&rdquo; &ldquo;Danny, we don&rsquo;t know them rustlers was greasers,&rdquo; declared Ren, too casually. &ldquo;No. But I was afeared they might be.&rdquo; Stewart suggested they ride on to meet Sidway, and whoever he had with him. Very soon Ren sighted three riders, whereupon Stewart ordered a halt. &ldquo;Lance ain&rsquo;t in thet ootfit,&rdquo; declared Starr, presently. &ldquo;Say, they see us, an&rsquo; air ridin&rsquo; to beat the band.&rdquo; Stewart was curious about the three horsemen who were evidently in a hurry to reach them. In very short order they arrived, three lean cowboys, ragged and dusty and hard-eyed. Stewart recognized them. &ldquo;Howdy, boys. Where&rsquo;s Sidway?&rdquo; Stewart thought Sloan&rsquo;s intent eyes searched his with undue fire, and he wondered what was coming. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, all,&rdquo; returned Sloan. &ldquo;Stewart, you don&rsquo;t &rsquo;pear all het up aboot this raid&mdash;or nothin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m well satisfied, thanks to you boys. Did you get a line on the rustlers?&rdquo; &ldquo;They seen us far off an&rsquo; bolted. We couldn&rsquo;t make them out.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right, that&rsquo;s that. Where&rsquo;s Sidway?&rdquo; rejoined Stewart, sharply, suddenly sensing some untoward circumstance. &ldquo;By now Sidway must be at Cochise&rsquo;s stronghold, guidin&rsquo; some gangsters who&rsquo;d kidnaped your girl Madge an&rsquo; a young fellow.&rdquo; A blank silence ensued. But amaze did not long obstruct Stewart&rsquo;s faculties. He had sensed catastrophe. Sloan&rsquo;s tan had lost a shade. His eyes smoldered. Nels flung a clenched hand at him. &ldquo;Sloan, what you sayin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;Listen, all of you. But don&rsquo;t waste no time restin&rsquo; here. Come on. Ride close an&rsquo; let me spill it.... Yesterday a little before sundown, Sidway caught up with us on the road to town. He told us about this cattle steal an&rsquo; asked us to help him haid them back this mawnin&rsquo;. Me an&rsquo; the Spencers was glad to help, of course. We made camp jest outside of town. Jest about dusk, Sidway walked in town to get some coffee an&rsquo; butter. Not long after, but it was dark, a big black car came up, an&rsquo; Sidway was on the runnin&rsquo; board. We was held up by two men with machine guns. Then their boss got out an&rsquo; bought my hawse outfit fer a thousand dollars. They was a gangster outfit an&rsquo; Sidway &rsquo;peared to be on good terms with them. I didn&rsquo;t get wise to the kidnapin&rsquo; till Miss Madge an&rsquo; the young feller was dragged out of the car. Then I seen it plain as print.... Now, to make it short, one gangster drove the car away, up the range road. Sidway an&rsquo; us fellers, under them guns, saddled an&rsquo; packed. Then thet pale-faced gangster ordered me to wait till mawnin&rsquo;, an&rsquo; notify you to send one man up the Cochise Trail with fifty thousand dollars fer Miss Madge an&rsquo; the same for the young feller. An&rsquo; if his orders was not carried out Miss Madge would be raped, an&rsquo; both of them killed.... It was hard fer me to wait till mawnin&rsquo; but with your telephone wire cut, I couldn&rsquo;t find out if you&rsquo;d left, an&rsquo; I knew you&rsquo;d be comin&rsquo; along here after the cattle. So here we are.&rdquo; By the time Sloan had concluded, Stewart&rsquo;s horror had mounted to a ruthless and terrible wrath. Starr&rsquo;s face had grown a leaden white, and he appeared incapable of speech. After a brief paroxysm of emotion Nels interrupted Danny Mains&rsquo; curses with a terse query: &ldquo;Gene, what do you make of Sidway?&rdquo; &ldquo;What do you?&rdquo; countered Stewart, huskily. &ldquo;I got it figgered. When he went in town he seen thet car with Madge in it. He must hev been slick enough to scare them kidnapers off the highway, to take to the hills.&rdquo; Sloan interrupted impatiently: &ldquo;But, Nels&mdash;Stewart, it looked like Sidway was one of thet gang.&rdquo; &ldquo;You all think Sidway was in with the gangsters?&rdquo; queried Stewart. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m afraid, sir&mdash;we do. Why, we stayed awake half the night talkin&rsquo; it over. These kidnapers are pretty smart. They take their time. We figger Sidway must have been sent on ahead&mdash;planned the job&mdash;rode away the day it was done. But we jest couldn&rsquo;t figger this cattle steal in the job atall.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sloan, I&rsquo;ll grant it looked that way to you,&rdquo; rejoined Stewart, tersely. &ldquo;But you don&rsquo;t know Sidway. I tell you the idea is preposterous.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right, sir. I only hope an&rsquo; pray you&rsquo;re right.&rdquo; &ldquo;My&mdash;Gawd!&rdquo; gasped Starr, totally overcome by that information. &ldquo;Boss, thet&rsquo;s it&mdash;one of Nels&rsquo; hunches,&rdquo; affirmed Starr. &ldquo;But, my Gawd, we got to rustle!&rdquo; &ldquo;Sloan, you boys will go with us,&rdquo; asserted Stewart. &ldquo;You bet your life!&rdquo; * * * * * Riders and horses were wet with sweat, and practically spent when that long climb up the mountain had been accomplished by sundown. A halt was made in the deep forest west of Cochise&rsquo;s stronghold, at a point they all agreed was scarcely a half mile from the clearing. &ldquo;Ketch your&mdash;breath, fellers,&rdquo; panted Nels, as he removed his chaps. &ldquo;Men, in case they&rsquo;re here...&rdquo; began Stewart. &ldquo;They air heah,&rdquo; interrupted Nels. &ldquo;Didn&rsquo;t we foller their tracks over two thirds of thet trail? Didn&rsquo;t Sloan ketch two of his hawses makin&rsquo; fer home? Sidway would make the gang stop heah, even if it wasn&rsquo;t the logical camp.&rdquo; &ldquo;All&mdash;right, then,&rdquo; Stewart rasped. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll slip up on them. Then what?&rdquo; &ldquo;Stewart, they&rsquo;re gangsters with machine guns. I&rsquo;d say shoot &rsquo;em down on sight.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell, yes,&rdquo; agreed Starr. &ldquo;Wal, I don&rsquo;t know,&rdquo; added Nels, ponderingly. &ldquo;Shore we never&mdash;dealt with any of this ilk&mdash;before. I&rsquo;d say hold &rsquo;em up. If they don&rsquo;t hold up pronto then bore &rsquo;em.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, it&rsquo;s a better idee at thet,&rdquo; agreed Ren, savagely. &ldquo;But my gun finger is shore itchin&rsquo;.... If we only find&mdash;Madge alive an&rsquo; unharmed.&rdquo; &ldquo;We will&mdash;shore,&rdquo; declared Nels, passionately. &ldquo;I may hev lived to be old an&rsquo; soft, but somehow I gamble on Sidway. He loves thet girl an&rsquo; he&rsquo;ll ootwit the slickest kidnapers there ever was.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, that faith has kept me from collapse. First time in my life I&rsquo;ve weakened. But it&rsquo;s&mdash;my girl!&rdquo; exclaimed Stewart, low and thick under his breath. &ldquo;Come on,&rdquo; ended Nels. &ldquo;Keep even an&rsquo; in sight of each other. No noise!&rdquo; A pine-thicketed slope led up to the gray crags. They entered the mountain enclosure through a gateway between the huge monuments of stone. The clearing lay beneath Stewart&rsquo;s strained eyes, a green and gold park marked by great pines scattered about, and shining with appalling beauty under the sunset glow. A thin column of blue smoke halfway across made Stewart&rsquo;s heart leap. Ren pointed to a roan horse grazing in the open meadow, and Sloan whispered that it was his horse Baldy. At a motion from Nels they stealthily began their approach down to the level. Every few rods Nels halted to listen. Stewart could hear only the sough of the wind in the pines, and the murmur of distant running water. The place seemed locked in an unearthly silence. Suddenly Ren startled Stewart and all of them. He held up a pregnant hand. &ldquo;I heah voices,&rdquo; he whispered. He must have possessed extraordinarily sharp ears, for all of his companions shook their heads. Hardly had they started forward again when the boom of a gun made them statues. They listened with bated breath. &ldquo;No forty-five Colt,&rdquo; whispered Stewart. &ldquo;Sidway packs a forty-four Smith an&rsquo; Wesson. Sounded like it,&rdquo; replied Nels. &ldquo;Come on, we gotta see this.&rdquo; Before they had taken a dozen swift steps a rattling biting volley halted them. The continuous volley appeared to come from their right down the trail. Accompanying the rattling was a swishing cut of bullets through foliage and then a pattering on solid wood. It ceased. And Ren leaped up in the air, trying to see over the green brush. &ldquo;Heah &rsquo;em?&mdash;Hawse hoofs!&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore as Gawd made little apples!&rdquo; ejaculated Sloan. Stewart was quick to catch a soft rapid thud of hoofs, a crash of brush, a cracking of dead twigs, then thudding hoofbeats dying away. &ldquo;Thet was when, Gene!&rdquo; hissed Nels, his gray eyes like points of flame, and he motioned them on. Despite the intense suspense, Nels had the cool judgment to advance very slowly, without the slightest sound. Stewart swallowed his harrowing fears and doubts. Then swift footfalls close in front made him, and all of them, aware of the nearness of the trail. Nels crouched down and stealthily separated the small pines to slip through. Starr followed suit, as did the others to left and right. Stewart saw the roof of the old cabin over the tips of the brush. &ldquo;Flemm!&rdquo; shouted a hoarse voice. &ldquo;What happened?&rdquo; &ldquo;He crowned me,&rdquo; replied another man, hotly. &ldquo;Who? Not Stevens?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. It was that two-timin&rsquo; cowboy. He lied about seeing horses down the trail. Ruse to get rid of you. Then he beaned me.&rdquo; &ldquo;That shot in the cabin?&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t hear any.&rdquo; &ldquo;You know Uhl went in the cabin to the girl?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. I saw that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, there was a shot in there, all right. Sidway went in and bumped Bee off. That&rsquo;s it. For I saw the cowboy come out with the girl in his arms and jump on his horse. I let loose my gun, but I was running&mdash;and didn&rsquo;t connect.&rdquo; The faces of Nels and Ren appeared to shine upon Stewart, a singular transformation from grim dark passion to an ecstasy of gladness. Stewart felt the same so powerfully that he was overcome. But his tremendous relief was counteracted by a hateful query&mdash;had Sidway gotten in that cabin in time? Again Stewart&rsquo;s passion to rend and slay dominated him. He crawled softly after the others, until he bumped into them. They had arrived at the edge of the clearing. Ren&rsquo;s hard hand pressed Stewart&rsquo;s shoulder. Peering through the foliage he saw that they were scarcely fifty yards from the campfire. Two bareheaded young men, with corded livid faces, stood facing each other. Both held machine guns. The taller, a dark-haired individual, was bending his head to the other, no doubt for examination. Beyond them on the ground sat young Stevens, apparently uninjured, but plainly shocked with terror. Then on the moment the gangsters whirled at a piercing shout from the cabin. A third man appeared, a slim-built fellow, with a bloody face. He staggered toward them, a ghastly spectacle, but assuredly instinct with life and desperation. His curses rang through the forest clearing. Then he confronted the astounded gangsters. &ldquo;That &mdash;&mdash; fake cowboy shot me... got away with her!&rdquo; he yelled, wildly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll kill you both&mdash;you &mdash;&mdash; hop-heads! What in hell were you doing?&rdquo; &ldquo;He fooled us, Bee,&rdquo; replied Flemm. &ldquo;Made us believe he saw horses. Sent Fox down the trail. Then he crowned me.&rdquo; &ldquo;I wish to God he&rsquo;d smashed your empty pan!&rdquo; &ldquo;Looks like he emptied yours. Better let us wash you off. Looks like what you used for brains is oozing away.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s only blood. He grooved me... here... Christ, how it burns!&mdash;Wipe me off.&rdquo; Fox laid down his gun and picked up a towel from the pack. He dipped it in a water bucket, and wiped off the blood to disclose to the watchers the visage of a white, hard-faced criminal whose passion and experience seemed greatly beyond his years. &rdquo;HANDS UP!&rdquo; thundered Nels. Uhl and Fox lost not a second in elevating their hands. But Flemm whirled with his machine gun bursting into flame and rattle. Almost instantly his distorted visage went blank and he pitched forward. The machine gun sputtered into the ground, scattering gravel, then fell from the gangster&rsquo;s stretching hands. Stewart saw smoke issuing from Starr&rsquo;s rifle. Then Nels, gun low, ran out, to be followed by the cowboys. Mains came out from the right. When Stewart emerged from the foliage Sloan was disarming the gangsters. &ldquo;Heah, gimme thet rope, Spencer,&rdquo; yelped Starr. Receiving it he spread the noose, and pitched it deftly over Uhl&rsquo;s head. The gangster had courage or else he did not get the significance of Starr&rsquo;s move. &ldquo;Wait, Ren!&rdquo; ordered Stewart, and strode over to Stevens. &ldquo;Are you all right, boy?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes&mdash;sir. I&mdash;guess so,&rdquo; faltered Stevens. &ldquo;Thank God. I was about&mdash;dead of fright.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sidway made off with Madge?&rdquo; &ldquo;He did, sir, but&mdash;but...&rdquo; &ldquo;Was she&mdash;all right... too?&rdquo; queried Stewart, hoarsely. &ldquo;Yeah, that&rsquo;s correct,&rdquo; interposed Uhl, darkly. &ldquo;Sidway belongs to Cork&rsquo;s snatch gang. He tricked me. He wants the ransom and the girl for himself. I&rsquo;ll get him for that if it costs me a hundred grand.&rdquo; &ldquo;Haw! Haw!&rdquo; burst out Sloan, sardonically. &ldquo;Gangster,&rdquo; added Stewart, coldly. &ldquo;If you knew Westerners you would not concern yourself about that.&rdquo; Ren Starr confronted Rollie Stevens. &ldquo;Say, did I heah you make a crack about Sidway bein&rsquo; one of this outfit?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, you did. He&rsquo;s hand in glove with these kidnapers. And he has betrayed them. He&rsquo;s...&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re a thickheaded fool.&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon I&rsquo;ll have to bat you one...&rdquo; &ldquo;Hold on, Ren,&rdquo; interrupted Stewart, sternly. &ldquo;Make allowance for circumstances. It does look strange. But we&rsquo;ll clear it up presently.&rdquo; The gangster Flemm was dead, shot through the center of the forehead. Stewart ordered Sloan to take charge of the machine guns, and Starr to search the gangsters. Nels stood with his gun on Uhl, and not for many years had Stewart seen such an expression on that lean face. Then Stewart strode to the cabin and went in. There was a pile of spruce brush on the floor, but it had not been disturbed. Searching around Stewart saw tracks of Madge&rsquo;s little feet in the dust, and he could read from them that she had run and fought. He also found a splotch of blood in a depression, where no doubt Uhl had fallen, and had lain until he came to. There was little more to be learned in the cabin. The dreadful pang in Stewart&rsquo;s breast did not subside. But how grateful he was that Madge was alive and in the keeping of a man! Upon Stewart&rsquo;s return to the group, Ren pointed to several automatic pistols evidently salvaged from the gangsters&rsquo; effects. &ldquo;You boys can have those. Save Uhl&rsquo;s for Sidway. I imagine he&rsquo;d like to keep it.&rdquo; &ldquo;How about these, Boss?&rdquo; asked Ren, and handed Stewart several wads of greenbacks. On one, the outside wrapper had a denomination of one thousand. &ldquo;Peep into thet, Boss. All the same. What these guys call grands.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well!&mdash;These gentlemen seem to deal in large numbers, said Gene, sarcastically. &ldquo;Stewart, that dough is yours if you pass up this snatch,&rdquo; said Uhl, suavely. He seemed to lack comprehension and fear. And his assurance, even before the grim and silent Nels, was remarkable, and could have come only from supreme egoism and ignorance. Stewart knew that no power on earth or in heaven could stay Nels&rsquo; hand. These vultures had dared to frighten, and probably harm, Nels&rsquo; one treasure, and that had been Madge since she had first ridden his knee. &ldquo;Thanks, Uhl. You can afford to be generous, for you won&rsquo;t need that money where you are going.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, he doesn&rsquo;t get it,&rdquo; declared Starr, contemptuously. &ldquo;Let me hev the fun of tellin&rsquo; him.&rdquo; And the cowboy gave the lasso a jerk that made the noose close up like a snake round Uhl&rsquo;s neck. The gangster spread the noose and flung it off his head. &ldquo;Cowboy, I never forget faces,&rdquo; he snarled. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve put men on the spot for less than that.&rdquo; &ldquo;For the love of Mike!&rdquo; shrilled Starr, astounded and resentful. &ldquo;Boss!&mdash;Nels!&mdash;Danny! Did you heah this guy? He&rsquo;d put me on the spot!&rdquo; Stewart realized that his comrades as well as he were profoundly impressed by this new type of desperado. Absolutely, Uhl was convinced he would get out of this predicament. His ally, Fox, was an older man and less assured. No doubt he had always been a tool. Uhl, young as he was, indubitably had been used to imperious and unlimited power. &ldquo;Say, you opium-eatin&rsquo; nut, air you so dotty you think you&rsquo;ll get out of this?&rdquo; yelled Starr, red in the face. It struck Stewart that the cowboy voiced a tremendous antagonism toward the cold-faced, steel-eyed gangster, while Nels maintained a silent ruthlessness. Stewart knew Uhl was as good as dead, yet had not the slightest divination of it. Stewart felt that he had an intense curiosity about this species of gunman. His own inflexible hate, now that fear for Madge had somewhat subsided, was controllable. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s a scout for Cork. I know that bird. I met Sidway in Yuma. He drove one of my booze trucks.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. One of those trucks you sent back with stolen cattle, eh?&rdquo; &ldquo;Me steal cattle? That&rsquo;s a kick,&rdquo; replied Uhl, with a laugh of contempt. &ldquo;All the same your drivers did, probably on the sly. Sidway drove an empty truck to Tucson. It had been full of cattle. He was held up by men who expected to find bootleg whisky. Later this truck and others like it parked off the highway near my ranch to be loaded with stolen cattle. They got away with one load. Sidway killed one of your men.&rdquo; Uhl gave vent to a rage that convinced Stewart of his innocence of complicity in the raid. Such curses Stewart had never heard in the dens of the frontier. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s more, Sidway is not what you think him,&rdquo; went on Stewart. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s just a slick cowboy. He knew you&mdash;outguessed you&mdash;led you up here to your death.&rdquo; &ldquo;He was going to kidnap Madge himself,&rdquo; snapped Uhl, but his certainty seemed weakening. &ldquo;Your mistake. Why would he want to kidnap her when he&rsquo;s going to marry her?&rdquo; That random shot of Stewart&rsquo;s broke down the gangster&rsquo;s stubborn convictions and betrayed the terrible nature of the man. If he were capable of love for a woman, he must have felt it for Madge Stewart. At any rate Stewart decided the gangster had been obsessed with some violent passion for Madge and that an insane jealousy possessed him. &ldquo;Marry her&mdash;yeah?&rdquo; he choked out, his face purple, his neck convulsed, his eyes not those of a human. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s welcome&mdash;to the rag&mdash;I made her!&rdquo; Stewart knocked him flat, but had the self-possession to turn aside Nels&rsquo; quivering gun. It seemed impossible, however, to control Starr, and suddenly Stewart had no desire to. Starr dragged the gangster to his feet. &ldquo;You bastard,&rdquo; he hissed, his visage gray and set. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll never live&mdash;to brag of thet again!&rdquo; &ldquo;Stand aside, Ren,&rdquo; ordered Nels, piercingly. &ldquo;No, Nels, you ain&rsquo;t gonna bore him,&rdquo; shouted Starr, hoarsely. &ldquo;An&rsquo; we ain&rsquo;t gonna hang him. We&rsquo;ll hang his pard, an&rsquo; make him look on, but by Gawd! I owe somethin&rsquo; to myself heah!&rdquo; Starr slipped the noose over Fox&rsquo;s head and jerking it tight he threw the end of the lasso over a sturdy pine branch, and caught it coming down. &ldquo;Heah, Sloan, an&rsquo; you Spencers! Get in on this. An&rsquo;, young feller, grab hold of this rope behind me, an&rsquo; pull, if you&rsquo;re half a man.... If you don&rsquo;t I&rsquo;ll beat hell oot of you.... PULL!... Ahah! Them yells choked off! Yellow clear through!... There, tie the end, Sloan.&rdquo; Stewart averted his eyes, but he could not escape the grotesque jumping-jack shadow on the ground, or the expulsion of breath from the condemned and executioners, the scrape of boots and jangle of spurs, and lastly the incredible spectacle of Stevens hauling on the lasso. For the moment the collegian had answered to primal instincts, and his red visage was as beastly as those of his fellows. But suddenly Stewart swerved his attention to Uhl. The gangster had watched the hanging of his lieutenant, and his face, his look, his mien were vastly but incalculably transformed from what they had been. &ldquo;What do you think of our necktie party, gangster?&rdquo; demanded Ren, leering at him. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s how we do things in the West.... I&rsquo;m jest damn sorry I cain&rsquo;t hang you an&rsquo; watch you kick. But yore swagger gets my goat. So Mister Bee Uhl, kidnapin&rsquo;&mdash;bootleggin&rsquo; gangster gunman, you&rsquo;re gonna go up agin my game!&rdquo; &ldquo;Heah, Ren&mdash;none of thet. Hang him,&rdquo; said Nels, speaking for the first time. &ldquo;Umpumm, old pard. I wonder you ask it.... Where&rsquo;s thet popgun of his?&rdquo; Ren snatched it up from the pack and held it gingerly in contempt. &ldquo;What do you think of thet toy, Nels? These guys in the movies shoot through their coat pockets. Okay!... Where&rsquo;s his coat?&rdquo; Starr took that up and slipped the little automatic into the right coat pocket. Stewart had answered to the same strange antagonistic passion that beset Starr. No doubt Nels also was under its influence or he would have shot the gangster and made an end of the thing. Nels had been a gunman in his day; and later cowboys, who packed and shot guns, were wont to view with contempt the exploits of the modern killers with their automatic and hidden firing. They were simply murderers. An even break was Greek to them. But Ren wanted the test. &ldquo;Listen to reason, cowboy,&rdquo; importuned Stewart. &ldquo;I savvy you. But even a little risk...&rdquo; &ldquo;Risk, hell? There won&rsquo;t be none. Anyway, Boss, neither you nor Nels must hev this crook&rsquo;s blood on yore hands.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the difference whether it&rsquo;s you or me or all of us?&rdquo; &ldquo;On account of Madge. An&rsquo; if you elected to take him to jail&mdash;why Sidway would ride down there an&rsquo; shoot him in his cell. Thet wouldn&rsquo;t do either, Boss.&rdquo; Nels appeared to be struck mute and Stewart had no ready answer. At that moment Starr reminded Stewart again of Monty Price. The advance of time did not change the hearts of these firebrand range riders. Starr drew his gun, and kept it in his hand while he helped the gangster into his coat. &ldquo;There!... Stewart, you fellers get back pronto.... Now Uhl, don&rsquo;t move a hand.&rdquo; Starr backed away from him for perhaps twenty feet. &ldquo;Turn around, Uhl.&rdquo; The gangster did as he was bidden, exposing a front that was sickening to men who held courage and nerve as Stewart held them. Blood had again begun to stream down the side of Uhl&rsquo;s temple and cheek. &ldquo;Ten&mdash;grand&mdash;if you&rsquo;ll...&rdquo; &ldquo;Bah!&rdquo; interrupted Starr, piercingly. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re talkin&rsquo; to an American cowboy.&rdquo; Starr sheathed his gun and held his hand out, his fingers clutching at the air. &ldquo;Nels, you give the word.... Come on, kidnaper! Let&rsquo;s hev yore game.&rdquo; Stewart&rsquo;s gaze was riveted on the gangster. In a flash he jerked his right hand down into his coat pocket. As the corner of his coat suddenly pointed out to bark and smoke, Starr&rsquo;s gun crashed and in a second again. The gangster&rsquo;s bullet sputtered up dust and gravel. Between the shots his visage underwent an indescribable change, and as he fell the terrible instinct to survive left his body. * * * * * Darkness found Stewart and his men around a campfire in another spot, several rods from the cabin. Nels was cooking some supper. And he was speaking: &ldquo;Wal, Gene, I cain&rsquo;t see any sense in tryin&rsquo; to trail Sidway in the dark.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hawses all in, Mr. Stewart,&rdquo; interposed Sloan. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll have to rest up tonight at least.&rdquo; Stewart endeavored to subdue his impatience and dread, knowing how right his comrades were. &ldquo;Anyway, wait till Ren comes back,&rdquo; added Nels, and presently called them to supper. While they were sitting there the cowboy returned. In the campfire light his face showed white and set, without the violence that had distorted it. &ldquo;I found Lance&rsquo;s trail,&rdquo; he said, eagerly. &ldquo;Used my flashlight. He had Ump goin&rsquo; some when he struck into the trail. But he soon slowed down. I follered the tracks till they turned left off the trail.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;ll he do?&rdquo; queried Stewart, sharply. &ldquo;Beat it fer home. Thet guy an&rsquo; thet hoss&mdash;nothin&rsquo; to it, Boss, if Madge is okay.&rdquo; &ldquo;Does he know how to get down out of here?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, we rode all over when we was up heah.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ren, you take his trail at daybreak,&rdquo; suggested Nels. &ldquo;Shore. I&rsquo;d thought of thet. But mark my hunch, Sid will beat us home half a day.&rdquo; &ldquo;Set in an&rsquo; hev a bite.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, I sorta ain&rsquo;t hungry.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, eat an&rsquo; drink anyhow. It&rsquo;s been a tough day.&rdquo; &ldquo;You boys go after our horses and hobble them out for tonight. And Sloan, you&rsquo;ll hunt up the rest of your horses tomorrow,&rdquo; asserted Stewart. &ldquo;They&rsquo;ll be around close. Seldom thet stock will leave this grass an&rsquo; water.&rdquo; Stevens set propped against a pack, with a blanket around him, his hair damp on his pale forehead. &ldquo;Rollie, you had a hand in that lynching,&rdquo; said Stewart. &ldquo;How you feel?&rdquo; &ldquo;Pretty scared&mdash;and sick yet,&rdquo; he replied, weakly. &ldquo;But it&mdash;isn&rsquo;t my part in that hanging. That was great.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fine. Brace up now. Everything is okay. And we&rsquo;re d&mdash;&mdash; lucky.&rdquo; Very little was spoken after that and nothing at all about the tragedy. The cowboys brought a pile of firewood to last out the night. Stewart asked one of them to fetch the cut spruce in the cabin, and he made his bed upon that. Starr was the only one who did not smoke. He stood back to the fire, his head bent. Stewart appreciated how he felt. The night wind set up its dirge in the pines and coyotes barked off in the distance. Despite his extreme fatigue Stewart could not sleep at once. The stars seemed to mock his troubled mind. She did not wholly lose consciousness, for she felt him lift her and wrap her in a blanket and carry her out. More clearly then she heard a string of shots and the spang and thud of bullets all about, and felt herself swung upon a horse, and the violent jars of her body as he plunged away. A vague, almost blank interval succeeded. When her mind cleared again she was being carried comfortably upon a pacing horse over a level trail. Through the big black pines she saw the stars shining, and then a dim outline of Sidway&rsquo;s face and bare head. That thrilling reality brought back vividly the fight with the gangster, his half stripping her, and the brutal blow he had dealt her, and then Sidway&rsquo;s startling and fatal intervention.
