Sidway, no matter why, had saved her again, and this time from a horrible fate&mdash;an insupportable shame and inevitable death. Her thoughts grew so wildly whirling that she had to disrupt them by talking. &ldquo;Lance,&rdquo; she whispered. Apparently he did not hear her. &ldquo;Lance!... We got away.&rdquo; She felt a strong vibration pass through him. &ldquo;Hello! You&rsquo;ve come to?&rdquo; he returned, hastily. &ldquo;Yes. But I wasn&rsquo;t altogether out.&rdquo; &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t had time to see.... Did he hurt you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Lance, I was holding my own with him&mdash;when he struck me. I wasn&rsquo;t afraid of him&mdash;till then.... I suppose he could have beaten me helpless?&rdquo; &ldquo;Then... Uhl didn&rsquo;t&mdash;harm you?&rdquo; queried Sidway, in a halting husky voice. &ldquo;Yes.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes.&rdquo; &ldquo;From something hideous. He never meant to let me go for ransom. He&rsquo;d have kept me.... Merciful heaven! What an idiot I was&mdash;to flirt with Honey Bee Uhl!&rdquo; &ldquo;It takes a lot to cure some girls&rsquo; egotism,&rdquo; returned Sidway with an intonation she could not define. He seemed far removed from her, somehow. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m cured&mdash;Lance.&rdquo; &ldquo;Listen, baby!&mdash;Excuse me. I fell into Uhl&rsquo;s way of speech.... The day will never come when you won&rsquo;t look at a man.&rdquo; &ldquo;But for goodness sake! I have eyes. I can&rsquo;t hang my head&mdash;never look.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yeah?&mdash;For what?&rdquo; &ldquo;To incite a man to madness&mdash;kidnaping&mdash;outrage&mdash;murder.&rdquo; &ldquo;I mean, Miss Stewart, that whether you have guilty intent or perfect innocence, when you look at men with those eyes, you play hell.&rdquo; &ldquo;I observe, Mr. Sidway, that my marvelous eyes failed to play hell with you,&rdquo; she returned, sarcastically. &ldquo;Only because I was wise to you.&rdquo; Madge had no quick retort for that, mainly because there seemed some hope for her with this young man of dual nature. They rode along the trail in silence. But she watched him from between narrowed eyelids. If she had not been spent and in pain she would have found this situation vastly intriguing. At length Umpqua exchanged his pacing gait for a walk. Evidently the mountain clearing had been passed. Presently Sidway turned off the trail to the left, and had to pick his course. The forest gradually grew less dense, and therefore lighter. Thickets of pine and spruce reached above Sidway&rsquo;s head, and in places had to be carefully threaded. It became evident to Madge that they were traveling downhill. At length the cowboy halted as if undecided how to proceed. &ldquo;Lost?&rdquo; inquired Madge. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll tell the world,&rdquo; he replied, with a queer laugh. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d just as soon you made a halt till morning. I&rsquo;m about done for.&rdquo; &ldquo;My idea exactly.... Soon as I hit a level spot.&rdquo; He zigzagged down the slope for a while, and eventually stopped, to slide out of the saddle. Madge could not help feeling that he handled her as if she were a child. He set her down, back against a tree, which Madge observed to be a cedar. There were still big pines about, but scattering, and the presence of cedars denoted lower altitude. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m freezing to death,&rdquo; she said. He stripped the horse and haltered him to a sapling. Then from his saddle he untied a blanket, and other trappings. This he doubled and wrapped around her. Then he tore sheathes of bark off the cedar, and snapped twigs and dead branches, with which he started a fire not far from her feet. The crackling of wood and leaping of red flame changed the moon-blanched gloom. While Madge stretched her hands to the heat, Sidway opened a saddlebag. &ldquo;Here&rsquo;s some meat, biscuits, dried apples, and a piece of chocolate.... Yes, and a little salt. Are you hungry?&rdquo; &ldquo;Daresay you could. Sorry I can&rsquo;t furnish one.&rdquo; &ldquo;Very well. I&rsquo;ll have a biscuit and a piece of meat.... Thanks. Where are we, Lance?&rdquo; &ldquo;Up in the Peloncillos.&rdquo; &ldquo;How far from that camp?&rdquo; &ldquo;Miles, I&rsquo;d say.&rdquo; &ldquo;I wish you could have gotten Rollie away from them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, I expect trouble enough, without your boy friend.&rdquo; &ldquo;Trouble?&mdash;You&rsquo;ll not have any with me,&rdquo; she returned, all at once cognizant again of the double role he played. &ldquo;Lance, you&rsquo;re after that ransom yourself!&rdquo; In the light of the fire she saw a dark tide sweep across his face. His somber eyes regarded her as if somehow she had recalled to him her true character. He let out a mirthless laugh. &ldquo;You guessed it, Majesty,&rdquo; he replied, grimly. &ldquo;I will gladly pay you.... What will those gangsters do with Rollie? His people are rich. They will pay. But it&rsquo;ll take time. Meanwhile Dad and Nels will be on the rampage. That demand of Uhl&rsquo;s will drive him crazy. He can&rsquo;t pay it. I&rsquo;ll bet they are on our trail now.&rdquo; &ldquo;You sure are some guesser,&rdquo; he declared, bitterly. &ldquo;My&mdash;God!&mdash;You can&rsquo;t&mdash;be so low.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes&mdash;yes! Oh, I&rsquo;ve been a heedless, vain and selfish thing!... But, Lance, I&mdash;I haven&rsquo;t been rotten...&rdquo; &ldquo;Are you telling me?&rdquo; he queried, turning his back on her. &ldquo;Yes, I am. I never.... Oh, what you must think me!... Lance Sidway, you killed that devil Uhl just so you could have me&mdash;take me yourself?&rdquo; &ldquo;You said it, baby.&rdquo; &ldquo;I thought you did already.&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t. But I shall.&rdquo; &ldquo;Okay by me. More sport if I have to beat you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Beat me! You&rsquo;ve already done that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Miss Stewart, I think you hit me first.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes I did. For the dirtiest remark any fellow ever dared make to me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Struck me at the moment as strictly merited.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, the way we quarrel! It gets my goat!... What do you intend to do with me?&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re so damn smart&mdash;why don&rsquo;t you tell me?&rdquo; &ldquo;I can.... You&rsquo;ve fallen for this temptation, Lance Sidway. Too much easy money in sight! And a chance to get even with me!... I suppose you&rsquo;ll tie me in a cave&mdash;starve me&mdash;beat me... till you get that money.&rdquo; &ldquo;I declare,&rdquo; he interrupted, as she choked over her words, &ldquo;you grow better all the time. Wise girl! College girl, you know!&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, damn!&mdash;Lance Sidway, you&rsquo;ll have to marry me!&rdquo; She might have struck him, judging by his shrinking start. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll refuse to pay that ransom or move out of my tracks unless you swear you&rsquo;ll marry me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Very well, if you think that important,&rdquo; he returned, in a queer voice. Madge, in the wildness of that agitation, thought she must make the best of a bad bargain. She loved him, whether he was a crook or a cowboy, a Dr. Jekyll or Mr. Hyde, a strange combination of virtue and vice. It ran through her mind that he could not forever stay immune, that she could win his love, and reform him. That might be the retribution meted out to her for her imperious and willful ways. After all he had saved her. She could not hate him. If he beat her she would fight back, and perhaps, womanlike, love him the more for his brutality. There was a queer streak in her, she feared, or at least one strongly primitive. &ldquo;You are fagged out,&rdquo; he said, presently turning. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll make a bed for you.&rdquo; He broke an armful of cedar brush, laid it flat, and put a saddle blanket on that. Then he arranged his saddle for her head. As she moved over, half crawling, the folded blanket fell, and the one wrapped around her half slipped off. Madge made no great haste to wrap it around her again. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the odds?&rdquo; she said, moodily. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve seen me almost naked twice.&rdquo; And she lay down to stretch out wearily, her eyes upon him, as he bent to cover her with the extra blanket. She made the discovery then that if the moonlight did not deceive her, his face was white. &ldquo;Now it&rsquo;s settled, let&rsquo;s talk...&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s settled?&rdquo; he interposed. &ldquo;Why, I suppose you&rsquo;d vulgarly call it my hash.... I intend to make up for the ruin I unwittingly brought upon Dad and Mom. I suppose you&rsquo;ll block that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Too late! I&rsquo;ll need the money.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you needn&rsquo;t be a hog. You seemed to like them. Can&rsquo;t you be sport enough to let me make amends?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sure, I liked Gene. And your mother is... swell!... But they won&rsquo;t need the money after you&rsquo;ve gone.&rdquo; &ldquo;Mr. Sidway, when you were snooping over my securities and bankbooks, did you get a line on what I was worth?&rdquo; &ldquo;I did, you bet. It used to be about a million!&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. But that won&rsquo;t do you much good now. I can sell my pearls and other jewels for a hundred grand&mdash;as your gangster pards call it. I&rsquo;ll do that on one condition only. You let me split with Dad and Mom!&rdquo; &ldquo;Okay! Fifty grand will do for our honeymoon&mdash;at least until the dicks get me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, you were wanted by the police even before this,&rdquo; cried Madge, despairingly. Then she grew enraged and flung at him: &ldquo;How can you be so&mdash;so fine&mdash;so&mdash;Oh, so many things, and still be such a beast.&rdquo; &ldquo;Mystery of life, baby,&rdquo; he retorted. &ldquo;How can you be so sweet&mdash;have such an angel-face&mdash;such soulful, eloquent, lovely eyes&mdash;such a winning way with everyone, when at heart you are just no damn good?&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve about convinced me,&rdquo; she said, darkly. &ldquo;Perhaps this will either kill or cure me.... But beating me, as no doubt you will, depriving me of the home I&rsquo;d just begun to love, packing me off like this to misery and&mdash;and God knows what else&mdash;perhaps it&rsquo;ll reform me.... Yes? No?&rdquo; &ldquo;I wouldn&rsquo;t limit your possibilities any more than I&rsquo;d believe one word you said,&rdquo; he returned, passionately. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re certainly two of a kind,&rdquo; she retorted. &ldquo;But let&rsquo;s not be fourflushers. If you&rsquo;re not big enough to reform yourself, and me, then be big enough to be outright bad. And not a two-faced liar such as you are!&rdquo; That stinging speech appeared to wither him. Presently he began to gather firewood and pile it conveniently. Madge had an intense curiosity in regard to him, and tried hard to keep awake. But she was utterly exhausted, and felt her eyelids falling again and again until they shut for good. She seemed scarcely to have slept any time at all when she was awakened. Sidway was shaking her, and not gently. &ldquo;Are you dead?&rdquo; he called, with something besides impatience. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t call me that,&rdquo; he almost shouted, most unreasonably. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m liable to slap you good.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, you put one black and blue brand on me. Why not another?&rdquo; &ldquo;Get up. Move around. Eat something,&rdquo; he ordered, peremptorily. Madge found the first desperately hard to accomplish, and the second no easy matter, and the third impossible. Her hands were numb and her feet blocks of ice, until she almost burned them in the fire. Sidway went off somewhere in to the woods, presumably to hunt his horse. Madge could easily have escaped from him then. But that would have been absurd, even if she had wanted to. She walked away from the fire and back again, and presently found that exercise relieved both cold and cramp. At length the cowboy returned with his horse, which he saddled and bridled. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll have to ride,&rdquo; he said, brusquely. &ldquo;Thanks. You&rsquo;re very kind to your squaw&mdash;darling.&rdquo; &ldquo;But not in that blanket. Here, put on my coat.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. You&rsquo;ll need that yourself. I can ride with this blanket around me. Only my hands and feet are cold now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Warm them pronto, while I tie these things on.&rdquo; When presently Madge mounted into the saddle she found the stirrups had been shortened to fit her. Without a word Sidway took hold of the reins and led Umpqua down the slope. He took long strides and a slanting zigzag course, down through the cedars. Broad daylight had come and gradually the nipping air and the frost lessened. Madge kept her hands in the folds of the blanket and endured the acute pain of cold feet. Gray shaggy foothills appeared to surround them; the heads of ravines slanted down between these, to widen into narrow valleys; through the trees Madge sometimes caught glimpses of a hazy void. When the sun rose it appeared to be in the wrong direction to her, and if her calculation was correct, Sidway was taking her down across the border into Mexico. He never spoke, never glanced back at her, but strode on, down and ever down, like a man who was lost and did not know nor care where he was going. Madge&rsquo;s thoughts did not change materially from those which had had their inception in the dark hours of the night before. She could not save herself, or help herself, and any romance or thrill she might conjure up were welcome. In the sunlight of day, however, her disappointment in Sidway and her disillusion grew more bitter hourly. She felt her strength failing, and a consequent gloom and sadness wore on her antagonistic and unquenchable spirit. The time came when she would not have cared what happened, if she could only rest. The cedar trees gave place to brush, which offered no protection from the sun, now climbing high and growing unendurably hot. &ldquo;Lance, I&rsquo;m&mdash;spitting cotton,&rdquo; said Madge, at last breaking silence. &ldquo;Must have&mdash;a drink.&rdquo; &ldquo;So am I. But hang on. I see green willows below. There&rsquo;s water.&rdquo; When at last Sidway found water it was none too soon for Madge. There was nothing to drink out of and Madge said if she got off the horse she could not climb back. Whereupon the cowboy, regardless of the fact that she had let the hot blanket slip down, lifted her out of the saddle, and after she had slaked her thirst, he put her back. Madge had never known before the sweetness and life in cold pure water. There were many things she had never appreciated. Sidway led on tirelessly, always down, but it appeared to Madge that the slopes were less precipitous and the zigzags far longer. She grew so weary that she sagged and swayed in the saddle, and so hot she wanted to fall off and perish, and so miserable that she had hardly strength left to hold the remnants of her garment around her. Nevertheless she would have endured more before entreating him to find some shade and let her rest. She hated him now. She could have killed him. To make her love him hopelessly and terribly, to heap the shame of her horrible selfishness upon her head were indeed enough, without adding this endless insupportable ghastly ride. Madge clutched the pommel and her blanket with sore and hot hands, and sat with closed and burning eyes, wearing to collapse. Minutes or hours dragged by until she seemed not to feel any more. Still she was aware when the horse stopped. &ldquo;Look, Madge!&rdquo; rang out Sidway&rsquo;s voice. Madge seemed impelled by more than his command. Opening her eyes she saw that they had halted upon a promontory, a level summit of the last foothill. A blue and gray range land not far below, clear and close in the sunlight, appeared to leap up at her. Across its sage-spotted floor moved a long line of cattle, wearily wending their way. Like a black ribbon some miles out stretched a road with speeding automobiles, flashing sunlight from their glass windows. And beyond, over the blue sage loomed a green timbered knoll, from the top of which, half concealed, peeped a white ranch house that Madge knew. &ldquo;The cattle herd you see working back belong to your father and Danny Mains,&rdquo; said Sidway, imperturbably. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s the highway!... there&rsquo;s&mdash;my home!&rdquo; faltered Madge, fighting a sudden dizziness. &ldquo;Thought you&rsquo;d recognize it,&rdquo; he drawled, lighting a cigarette. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sure tickled with the way I came straight down, in a swell short cut, from Cochise&rsquo;s stronghold.&rdquo; &ldquo;Okay. What now?&rdquo; But he never turned to look at her. &ldquo;Certainly, you poor fish!&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re not&mdash;what I took you for?&mdash;Liar&mdash;two-faced&mdash;cowboy&mdash;kidnaper... gangster?&rdquo; &ldquo;No, Miss Stewart. I hate to disillusion you&mdash;spoil your pipe dream. It&rsquo;s just too bad, for you&rsquo;re such a swell romancer. You concoct such lovely things about me and my motives. But they didn&rsquo;t pan out, as you see.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, my&mdash;God!&mdash;Then you didn&rsquo;t kill Uhl&mdash;to&mdash;to abduct me, but&mdash;but to save me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Right. Your comprehension is at least encouraging. You may be a bright girl yet.&rdquo; &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t want&mdash;a ransom?&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge Stewart, I&rsquo;d starve to death before I&rsquo;d accept a dollar of your money.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh&mdash;oh&mdash;I... What you&rsquo;ve done&mdash;for me, for Dad and Mom!... And I?&mdash;Oh, how little&mdash;how miserable&mdash;you&rsquo;ve made me! Oh, the shame!...&rdquo; Uttering a sharp cry she swayed in the saddle. When Madge recovered consciousness she found that Sidway was carrying her in front of him, and traveling at a fast pace across the range. Only vague thoughts accompanied her sensations of faintness and pain, and these faded. Then she went through stages of sleep or semiconsciousness, until at last she recovered sufficiently to make out that it was sunset and that she was almost paralyzed. &ldquo;Lance... how far?&rdquo; she whispered. &ldquo;Almost home,&rdquo; he replied, cheerily. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m glad you came to... Brace up now. So you won&rsquo;t scare hell out of your mother and the girls.... Here, I&rsquo;ll have to wrap you up again, for you&rsquo;re sure in a state of nature.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh&mdash;you cowboy!&rdquo; And she turned in his arm, to sink against his shoulder, reviving anew to life and pain and love, and realizing that there would be nothing worth living for without him. Wide-eyed she lay there, her cheek against his hot dusty vest. They began to climb and entered the pines. A little later Sidway halted the horse in front of the house and yelled: &ldquo;Hello, inside! It&rsquo;s Sidway!... And here&rsquo;s Madge all in, but okay!&rdquo; He stepped off with her in his arms, and mounting the porch, encountered a group of wild screaming and questioning boys and girls, and behind them her white-faced mother and the servants. &ldquo;Mom!&rdquo; And that was all Madge could say as her mother knelt to envelop her in loving arms. But she gazed with blurred eyes up at the faces of her friends, crowded around the bed. It was a little while before Madge could answer coherently. &ldquo;Mother!&mdash;Darlings! I&rsquo;m well&mdash;and safe. You&rsquo;re to thank Sidway for that.... I&rsquo;m just done up. Oh, what an adventure!... About Rollie?&mdash;I don&rsquo;t know&mdash;I don&rsquo;t know.... Lance, tell them what it&rsquo;s all about.&rdquo; Sidway turned away from the window. &ldquo;Stevens was all right when we left. I&rsquo;m sure Stewart with his men will have rescued him by now. They&rsquo;ll be back tonight, or tomorrow surely.&rdquo; &ldquo;Rescued!&rdquo; they all cried in unison. Sidway interrupted the chorus of wondering and awed exclamations. &ldquo;Her voice is gone. Stop making her talk, and fetch her something to drink,&rdquo; he said, hastily. &ldquo;Mrs. Stewart, Madge is naturally excited about all this.&rdquo; He briefly outlined the history of the adventure, ending, &ldquo;Now, it&rsquo;s a sure bet that Sloan joined Stewart and they cut loose after us. I think you may expect them back with Stevens by tomorrow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sidway, how can we ever thank you for this?&rdquo; exclaimed Madge&rsquo;s mother, fervently. &ldquo;What a relief!...&rdquo; &ldquo;Mom, don&rsquo;t let him leave!&rdquo; cried Madge, frantically, as Sidway started out. &ldquo;He&rsquo;ll ride away... and never&mdash;let me thank him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why, child!&mdash;Sidway would not do that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wouldn&rsquo;t he? Much you know!... Lance, promise me...&rdquo; Then the girls added their entreaties to hers, until Sidway, red of face, assured them he wanted only to go down to his bunkhouse to clean up, and that he would come back. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll have supper here,&rdquo; added Mrs. Stewart. At this juncture Barg elbowed his way to Madge&rsquo;s bed with a silver flask, and a glass. &ldquo;Nothing doing!&rdquo; cried Madge, her voice still weak. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m on the wagon&mdash;for good.... Get me some water first&mdash;then hot coffee.... And I&rsquo;m starved.&rdquo; But despite the assiduous attention of her friends and mother Madge could not keep awake very long. She slept until late the next day, to find that a relaxation had set in from her strenuous and harrowing experience. She was too weak to get out of bed. Allie, who had slept with her, told Madge she had never before looked so lovely and languid and fascinating. &ldquo;That bruise, though&mdash;I think you&rsquo;d better hide it,&rdquo; added her worshipful friend. &ldquo;Not on your life!&rdquo; retorted Madge. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s where Bee Uhl socked me.... It might serve to soften the heart of a certain callous and soulless person.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Lord!&mdash;Allie, it&rsquo;s terrible. A million times worse than before&mdash;before...&rdquo; &ldquo;Before what?&rdquo; whispered Allie, intensely excited. &ldquo;Before he slapped me the night of my party. Oh! wait till you get an earful of that.... Before he saved my life.... Before I took him for a gangster and a crook&mdash;and God only knows what else I called him!... Allie darling, I&rsquo;m terribly afraid it&rsquo;s hopeless.&rdquo; The other girls trooped in, clad in colorful pajamas and slacks, and made much of Madge. The boys hung at the door and out in the patio, and then her mother entered. Madge, for once in her life, was coddled and nursed, and paid enough compliments to last a month. But the one person Madge wanted most to see did not present himself. She was too proud, too hurt to ask for him. Why could he not be kind enough to come to see how she fared after the long ride? No doubt Sidway was anxious for the return of Stewart and the others. Madge hardly expected them so soon, and not at all without Rollie Stevens. Late in the afternoon, however, when gold shafts from the setting sun filtered through the foliage at her window, Madge was greatly excited to learn that Rollie had returned safely. &ldquo;Drag him in&mdash;no matter how he looks,&rdquo; cried Madge, who knew Rollie&rsquo;s weakness. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s a sight,&rdquo; replied Nate. &ldquo;But we&rsquo;ll get him.&rdquo; Presently there was a merry hubbub outside. Then Rollie came in, supported between Nate and Snake Elwell. Allie, who sat beside Madge on the bed, uttered a shriek. Madge was too overcome for mirth. The collegian&rsquo;s appearance presented grim evidence of the hardship and fright he had suffered. &ldquo;Oh, Rollie!&mdash;I&rsquo;m so&mdash;so glad!...&rdquo; burst out Madge. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m glad myself, especially to see you home safe.... That blighter turned out a hero instead of what you and I took him for?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, Rollie.&rdquo; &ldquo;He&rsquo;s one swell fellow&mdash;I&rsquo;ll tell you,&rdquo; gulped Stevens, gallantly. &ldquo;Your father is all right, Majesty. He&rsquo;s coming up, with Sidway. The boys drove me.... Excuse me now, Majesty. I&rsquo;m a mess.&rdquo; Then, as Rollie left, Dawson Metcalf called in at the door. &ldquo;Steady, Madge! Hold everything! Here come your dad and the hero!&rdquo; The clinking of spurs on the stone patio path sent strange little shivers all over Madge. She squeezed Allie&rsquo;s hand and she felt her heart weakly swell in her throat. Then her father entered her room, followed by someone Madge saw only vaguely. Sight of her father stalking in, dusty in his rider&rsquo;s garb, dark of visage, somehow recalled Madge&rsquo;s earliest memories. What a piercing gaze he bent upon her! Never had she met his eyes like that. What thought had been in his mind? After that strange look, his eyes and face softened, and he held her shaking hands and kissed her. &ldquo;Well, lass, I&rsquo;m happy to see you safe at home,&rdquo; he said, with deep feeling. &ldquo;Oh&mdash;Dad!&rdquo; And Madge sat up to cling to him and hide her face. There was more than the aftermath of her adventure in her instinctive action. Had she nearly lost him? Stewart laid her back upon the pillow. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re pretty white, Madge. And that&rsquo;s a nasty bruise on your temple. How&rsquo;d you get that?&rdquo; &ldquo;Honey Bee Uhl paid his respects&mdash;that way.&rdquo; Her eyes fastened upon Sidway, who stood at the foot of her bed, looking with grave eyes down upon her. It was not only his clean shave and change of garb that made him look so different. Madge thought she had never seen him so handsome, so fine, so disturbing to her heart and mind. She had wit left to realize that she should control her spoken thought, but so great an emotion that she did not care what she said. Her remorse seemed insupportable. &ldquo;Lance,&rdquo; she said, beseechingly, &ldquo;come here&mdash;beside Allie&mdash;and let me thank you.&rdquo; &ldquo;What for?&rdquo; he asked, smiling. &ldquo;Madge, you and I were mistaken. I didn&rsquo;t kill him.&rdquo; &ldquo;No!&rdquo; &ldquo;Daughter, though you both thought so, Sid isn&rsquo;t responsible for that,&rdquo; interposed her father. &ldquo;Or I either. It was Ren Starr. That cowboy seemed unusually wild, even for him. Effect of that punch you served the other night!... Well, Ren shot Flemm when he held them up. That gangster turned on us with his machine gun spitting fire. Then Uhl came out of the cabin, all bloody. Sid&rsquo;s bullet had grooved his scalp. I had some words with Uhl. He was a queer duck, quite beyond me. While Nels held a gun on him, Ren and the other boys hanged the third gangster. And they forced your college boy friend to help. What do you think of that?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a fact. He pulled on the lasso like a regular cowboy.&rdquo; It would have been extremely embarrassing then for Rollie, had he been present, to hear the outburst. &ldquo;We intended to hang Uhl, of course,&rdquo; resumed her father, presently. &ldquo;But Ren wouldn&rsquo;t hear of it. He forced Uhl to fight&mdash;gave him his gun and an even break. Killed him, Madge!... Well, I&rsquo;ve told the sheriff all about it, and that lets us out.... Madeline,&rdquo; said Stewart, addressing his wife, who had come in to stand with Sidway, &ldquo;it&rsquo;s a good thing this sheriff is not like Pat Hawe, my enemy sheriff of our early years here.... And to conclude, we will have our cattle back in another day. All&rsquo;s well that ends well. Let&rsquo;s forget it.&rdquo; &ldquo;But, Dad,&rdquo; said Madge, her voice soft and low, &ldquo;it&mdash;hasn&rsquo;t ended well, yet.&rdquo; &ldquo;How come? Sure it has.&rdquo; &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t thanked Lance,&rdquo; rejoined Madge, turning the full battery of her eyes upon Sidway, knowing she did so, but deeply sure of her sincerity. &ldquo;Well, do so, then,&rdquo; declared Stewart, with a laugh, as he arose. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t need&mdash;want to be thanked,&rdquo; said Sidway, with inscrutable eyes upon her. &ldquo;There! Let that do,&rdquo; interrupted Sidway, holding up an appealing hand. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s an inconsequential word for you, Madge. You call your friends &lsquo;darling,&rsquo; both boys and girls. You call Nels and Ren darling, and your parents, even your horses. So, evidently it&rsquo;s just a convenient word that places the lucky one within the charmed circle of your intimates. I accept that gratefully as your thanks for my little service. And it&rsquo;s enough.&rdquo; Madge stared up at him while the others laughed and made mirth out of it. What a speech for Lance Sidway! He was keener, wittier than she had ever realized. Had he the faintest conception of her remorse? Could not the cool smiling enigma see how she regarded him? There came a culminating rush to Madge&rsquo;s uncontrollable emotion. Sidway appeared very far from being prostrated by that exquisite epithet. Turning to her father he spread wide his hands: &ldquo;Gene, I told you she is flighty. Out of her head! I noticed it yesterday toward the last of the ride. No girl ever pulled a gamer stunt. That night ride up on the mountain, then half another night down, then all day in the hot sun&mdash;why, it was a grand performance. Not to say fighting off that thug in between! It&rsquo;s no wonder she&rsquo;s cracked under the strain.... I think we all ought to get out of here. She needs rest, nursing, quiet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Right you are, Sid. Come, beat it, all of us, except one or two of you girls to care for her.&rdquo; They filed out, except Allie and Maramee, who piled back upon the bed. Someone shut the door. &ldquo;Majesty,&rdquo; murmured the incorrigibly romantic Maramee. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s the most delicious love story there ever was in all the world.&rdquo; * * * * * Madge made the following day a quiet and recuperative and thoughtful one. Her guests were due to leave next day and she felt that she would be both sorry and relieved to see them go. The truck loaded with baggage departed early the following morning. And at one o&rsquo;clock three cars took aboard a hysterical bevy of girls and a merry wisecracking complement of boys. Farewells were prolonged. And at last, when it seemed all had been said, Bu Allen hailed the somber Sidway in a high-pitched penetrating voice. &ldquo;Lance, old darling, if Snake Elwell gives me the gate, I&rsquo;m coming back to go for you in a big way.&rdquo; That sally elicited a roar of mirth, in which Lance had to join. Madge&rsquo;s rather weak response was a little insincere. &ldquo;But, old red-top, that will be swell,&rdquo; retorted Sidway. &ldquo;Cowboy, you don&rsquo;t think anyone else around here has a look-in with you?&rdquo; went on Beulah, demurely anxious, with a sly glance at Madge standing big-eyed and disconsolate on the porch. &ldquo;Not a chance, Bu.&rdquo; Then with a chorus of: &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll be seeing you!&rdquo; they were off. Madge watched the cars wind into the green pines and disappear down the slope. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s over,&rdquo; she said, with a sigh. Sidway, with Nels and Ren, had slipped away unobserved. There were tears in her mother&rsquo;s eyes. Her father bent an abstracted gaze below, watching to see the cars come out from under the slope. &ldquo;Darlings, let&rsquo;s get it over,&rdquo; said Madge, and locking arms with her parents she led them in. &ldquo;Get what over?&rdquo; queried Stewart, with a start, and her mother looked suddenly concerned. &ldquo;Now, Dad! Don&rsquo;t try to fool your little Madge.&rdquo; Forthright then, she plunged abruptly into a confession of how it had come about that Sidway had told her of the impending ruin, if she did not retrieve the situation. She tried to spare the cowboy, but not herself. The quarrel they had had, and the vicious slap she had given him, surely justified him in losing his temper to bitterly flay her. &ldquo;You slapped that cowboy, Madge?&rdquo; queried Stewart in surprise and concern. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll say I did. You may have observed his cut and swollen lip.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes.... What did Sidway say to that?&rdquo; asked her father curiously. &ldquo;He slapped me back.&rdquo; &ldquo;No!&rdquo; &ldquo;I thought he&rsquo;d jar my teeth out of their sockets. But I hit him back with all my might. Then he said he refused to let me make a cat and dog fight out of it.... Well, that, of course, happened before he told me where to get off.... Dad, dear&mdash;Mom darling, this pass that my extravagance and stupidity&mdash;and selfishness&mdash;have brought you to, has almost broken my heart. I shall make amends. I&rsquo;ll pay it all back. I&rsquo;ve wired L. A. and New York, too. I can raise a hundred grand on my jewels. I don&rsquo;t need them. I seldom wore Aunt Helen&rsquo;s pearls. I shan&rsquo;t miss them.... Dad, will that money save us, with plenty to spare?&rdquo; &ldquo;It would&mdash;lass,&rdquo; he replied, a little huskily, and his arm tightened round her. &ldquo;Dad was trying me out, Mom. It&rsquo;s settled then.... I&rsquo;ve had a ghastly lesson. I&rsquo;ll make up for it, my darlings... . Dad, I never could hope to be your ideal western girl, nor ever in Mom&rsquo;s class as a lady of quality, but I can be a square shooter and I will be.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lass, we might argue over that western girl idea,&rdquo; returned Stewart, his dark eyes alight. Her mother folded her in loving arms, and by these simple things Madge seemed to grasp a great joy that had almost eluded her. &ldquo;Oh, yes, Dad... there&rsquo;s one other thing,&rdquo; said Madge, turning in her mother&rsquo;s arms. She essayed to be composed and casual, with dubious success. &ldquo;Can I rely on you&mdash;on your keeping Sidway here? He&mdash;I.... At least I can thank him&mdash;reward him somehow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, I don&rsquo;t think that&rsquo;ll be at all difficult,&rdquo; replied Stewart, but he did not make clear whether he meant keeping Sidway there or rewarding him. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m almost&mdash;happy again,&rdquo; said Madge, yielding to tears, &ldquo;but still feel kind of wobbly and I&rsquo;d better&mdash;lie down.&rdquo; &ldquo;It might be a good idea to talk that matter over with Nels,&rdquo; added Stewart, smilingly. &ldquo;He and Sid are thick as hops.&rdquo; Madge fled, yearning to ask just what matter her father meant, but she did not dare. How perilously close was she to betrayal of her secret! Her father&rsquo;s evasive eye and significant words, her mother&rsquo;s softened face and restrained sympathy&mdash;these were hard to resist. But Madge had a little pride and spirit left. In her room, which had again taken on its old tranquillity and speaking silence, she salvaged something of her old self. Next morning, biding her time, until she had seen Lance and Ren ride away, Madge waylaid Nels in his bunkhouse. &ldquo;Wal, Majesty! I shore was wonderin&rsquo; when you&rsquo;d remember yore old Nels.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aboot who, lass? Wal, I reckon I know. An&rsquo; it&rsquo;s aboot high time.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels! He&rsquo;s not leaving?&rdquo; queried Madge, hurriedly. &ldquo;Wal, he talks aboot it a lot. An&rsquo; he&rsquo;s purty sad these days. Ren rags him all the time aboot you. Lance says he&rsquo;ll stay till Ren an&rsquo; Bonita air married.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Nels! Is that settled?&rdquo; &ldquo;It shore is. An&rsquo; Ren is one dotty cowboy.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m happy over it.... Oh, what shall I give them? It must be something wonderful.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, lass, if I had my say, I&rsquo;d want to see another marryin&rsquo; pretty pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels!&mdash;You&rsquo;re so sudden. Have a heart!... You mean....&rdquo; &ldquo;Lass, I&rsquo;m confessin&rsquo; somethin&rsquo;,&rdquo; replied Nels, earnestly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m gettin&rsquo; along&mdash;close to seventy. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve had a long full life. Lately my heart has been warnin&rsquo; me thet I might not hev long to be heah. An&rsquo; I couldn&rsquo;t go satisfied an&rsquo; happy if you wasn&rsquo;t...&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Nels!&mdash;Don&rsquo;t! don&rsquo;t!&rdquo; implored Madge, poignantly, and she flew to throw her arms around his neck and lay her face against his hollow bristling cheek. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t think such a thing!&mdash;It&rsquo;d break my heart.&mdash;Nels, you&rsquo;re my second dad. You taught me everything.... You must not go away&mdash;and leave me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, honey, I reckon there ain&rsquo;t any reason to be onduly scared. I was jest preparin&rsquo; you.... An&rsquo; thet fetches me to the somethin&rsquo; nearest my heart. It&rsquo;s this turrible love affair between you an&rsquo; Lance.&rdquo; &ldquo;Turrible one-sided.... Yes,&rdquo; choked out Madge, hiding her face. &ldquo;One-sided?&mdash;Not onless you don&rsquo;t love him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, dear old Nels!&rdquo; was all she could say, clinging to him. &ldquo;Majesty, the boy is dyin&rsquo; fer you. He&rsquo;s got it wuss than I ever seen any boy in all my life.&rdquo; &ldquo;How do you know?&rdquo; she cried, desperately. &ldquo;Wal, a blind man could see. But, lass, I&rsquo;ll give him away&mdash;double-cross him&mdash;if you say his case is not hopeless.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, thet&rsquo;s fine!&mdash;Wal, Lance has told me time an&rsquo; again, an&rsquo; this last time, the other night, he jest cried in his misery. It seems you hurt him turrible by believin&rsquo; he was a gangster&mdash;a kidnaper&mdash;an&rsquo; Gawd knows what.... Lass, I cain&rsquo;t understand how you&mdash;so smart a girl&mdash;could ever make thet mistake.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, he&rsquo;s the finest youngster yore Dad an&rsquo; me ever met. Thet&rsquo;s all of thet. An&rsquo; he loves you so much he suffers awful. I could tell you the things he does thet&rsquo;d make you ashamed an&rsquo; sorry. But this is enough. He told me thet he loved you so much he couldn&rsquo;t stay heah an&rsquo; he couldn&rsquo;t leave. Now Majesty, I&rsquo;ve told you&mdash;I&rsquo;ve betrayed him. What do you say?&rdquo; &ldquo;I can&rsquo;t say&mdash;much&mdash;when&mdash;I&mdash;I&rsquo;m crying.... But I&mdash;I love him more&mdash;than he loves me&mdash;and I&rsquo;m dying of longing&mdash;and shame....&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s enough, lass,&rdquo; interrupted Nels, vastly disturbed by her weeping. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s gonna be all right.... What you must do is be clear enough to break his pride. He&rsquo;s stubborn as a mule.&rdquo; &ldquo;Break his pride! You mean&mdash;make him confess&mdash;he loves me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sartin I mean thet. An&rsquo; you&rsquo;ve gotta do somethin&rsquo; onheered of an&rsquo; powerful sweet thet won&rsquo;t give him no chance on earth.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, I&rsquo;ll do&mdash;anything,&rdquo; cried Madge, wildly. &ldquo;But what?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;s more than I can tell. You&rsquo;ll hev to figger thet yourself.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll do anything&mdash;anything...&rdquo; Madge repeated. &ldquo;Wal, thet makes me happy,&rdquo; burst out the old cattleman. &ldquo;Majesty, run home now an&rsquo; cudgel yore pretty haid fer a grand idee. Somethin&rsquo; onheerd of&mdash;turrible lovin&rsquo;&mdash;amazin&rsquo; an&rsquo; sweet thet won&rsquo;t give Lance no chance atall. An&rsquo; spring it on him pronto.&rdquo; * * * * * After dinner that night, during which she had been rapturously gay, to the wonder and delight of her parents, Madge put on one of her flimsiest, most shimmering gowns, with high-heeled white slippers to match. Wearing a long dark coat over this she stole forth upon what seemed to her the most momentous and thrilling venture of her life. She went by the patio and down through the pines by the trail. At this hour she knew Nels, her father perhaps, and surely the cowboys, would be at the store. As if by magic all her old imperious confidence, tempered this time by a secret humility and gratitude and love, returned in full force. She could not lose, and that gave a tremendous zest to her venture. Her blood raced with her thoughts and her heart throbbed high as she gained the level, and like a shadow glided across to the bunkhouses. Lance&rsquo;s was next to that occupied by Nels. She tiptoed down the porch, close to the wall until she came to Lance&rsquo;s open door. The yellow lights of the store cast a glow out upon the open. She heard low voices and Sidway&rsquo;s laugh. That gave her pause. Could he be so deeply and miserably in love, as Nels had sworn, and laugh like any other fancy-free cowboy? What if that sly Nels had framed her? The thought was terrible. But nobly she cast it aside as unworthy of a chastened and humble girl. Anyway the die was cast. Madge took off her clicking high-heeled slippers and stole into Lance&rsquo;s room and laid aside the long coat. Feeling around for his chair she found it and curled up in it, shaking with excitement. Presently she dimly espied her picture on his table, and that overjoyed her. It was done. She was there, in his room. Beyond Lance&rsquo;s finding her there she had not thought. This was far enough. Of all the things in the world, this was the last Lance Sidway would think of. It did not matter much what he did, when he found her there, unless he took her by the heels and dragged her out. He was capable of that, Madge thought. Voices and jingling spurs enjoined silence. The men were approaching. Madge would have preferred that Lance came alone. For a moment she fought a wild need to laugh. How her blood was gushing through her veins! Heavy footfalls upon the porch jarred the log cabin. Madge sat as quietly as a mouse, her heart pounding. She hoped Lance would not come to his room while the other men were in the bunkhouse. Still, no matter! She did not care what happened. Nels had cured her malady. She had all the cards in her hands. The men, apparently three in number, filed into Nels&rsquo; room. &ldquo;Strike a light, Nels,&rdquo; said Stewart. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s pretty darn warm,&rdquo; rejoined Sidway. &ldquo;I won&rsquo;t light my lamp.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Sid, you won&rsquo;t need to,&rdquo; added Nels. &ldquo;Reckon you got kind of a glow aboot you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nels, I&rsquo;ve a mind to bounce something off your dome,&rdquo; declared the cowboy, irritably, and then he laughed. &ldquo;Son, I&rsquo;ll smoke one of your cigarettes,&rdquo; said Stewart. How Madge shook at that laconic epithet given Sidway by her father! Poor Lance! They were all in league against him. Not a ghost of a show to escape! &ldquo;All right, we&rsquo;re set,&rdquo; went on her father, seriously. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re determined to ride away tomorrow?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, Gene&mdash;I am,&rdquo; replied Lance, sadly. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s a future here for you. This ranch will pay again some day. I&rsquo;m glad to tell you that I&rsquo;m going to pull through this tough time. Madge is helping me.&rdquo; &ldquo;I had that hunch myself.&rdquo; There ensued a momentary silence, during which Madge feared they might hear her heart beat, so thickly and swiftly did it pound in her ears. Then Stewart spoke: &ldquo;Son, please tell me why you don&rsquo;t want to stay here at Majesty&rsquo;s Rancho?&rdquo; &ldquo;Gene, it can&rsquo;t be possible you don&rsquo;t know,&rdquo; retorted Lance. Then his laugh cut a little coldly, with a hopeless note. &ldquo;Gene, to come straight out with the truth&mdash;I&rsquo;m so mad over your beautiful daughter that I can&rsquo;t stand being any longer where I can see her.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s blunt and to the point,&rdquo; returned Stewart. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll speak the same way. I&rsquo;d like you to be my son.... Have you asked Madge to marry you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Good heavens&mdash;no!&rdquo; ground out Sidway, apparently tortured. &ldquo;Why not? Faint heart never won fair lady! I don&rsquo;t recall being very shy with Madeline. Was I, Nels?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell no!&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge hates the very sight of me,&rdquo; declared Lance, abjectly. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t believe it,&rdquo; declared Stewart, vigorously. &ldquo;Son, I reckon you air wrong aboot thet,&rdquo; drawled Nels. &ldquo;Oh, you fellows will drive me to drink. Let&rsquo;s make an end of it. I know you like me, and you&rsquo;ve been swell to me. I love you both. But that has nothing to do with it. Madge is the one. And she loathes the ground I walk on. It&rsquo;s no wonder. I&rsquo;ve had the rotten luck to save her in humiliating circumstances. She is as proud as a princess. I&rsquo;ve rubbed her the wrong way. I&rsquo;ve bucked her in everything. Perhaps the last straw was her finding out that I had seen her bank statements and credits. But that wasn&rsquo;t my fault. Worse than that, my knowing about her flirtation with that gangster, Uhl...&rdquo; Suddenly Lance broke off, panic in his voice. &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t mean to tell you that&mdash;to give her away. I could kick myself.&rdquo; &ldquo;Son, you&rsquo;re not giving Madge away. I savvied that. And then she told me.&rdquo; &ldquo;She did? Holy cats!&rdquo; &ldquo;She just casually spoke of it, as if for her it was okay. That has been bothering me.... Just how far it went!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Gene, thet doesn&rsquo;t bother me none,&rdquo; drawled Nels. &ldquo;But it should,&rdquo; flashed Stewart. &ldquo;Gene, one day Majesty talked to me for a long while. She was in one of them bare bathin&rsquo; ootfits, an&rsquo; I jest couldn&rsquo;t look at her atall. An&rsquo; she got to tellin&rsquo; me aboot the girls an&rsquo; boys, an&rsquo; these new modern ways. We air old-fashioned, Gene. An&rsquo; this world has moved along. It&rsquo;s changed. Somehow I seemed to savvy Majesty, an&rsquo; since thet day nothin&rsquo; she ever did or might do could bother me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why didn&rsquo;t she confide in her father, too?&rdquo; asked Stewart, jealously. &ldquo;Gene, you may not believe it,&rdquo; put in Lance, &ldquo;but Madge was afraid of you. Loved you&mdash;craved your respect. I know.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right. You and Nels are hipped on this girl. You just can&rsquo;t see anything wrong....&rdquo; &ldquo;There wasn&rsquo;t anything wrong,&rdquo; interrupted Sidway, with heat, &ldquo;unless we except her flirtations and extravagances. Not in this day!&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the age to do with it?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know, but it has a lot.&rdquo; Again there ensued a silence, except for Nels&rsquo; cough, and the tapping of his pipe upon the table, while Madge sat there, strung and tense, her heart bursting. &ldquo;Gene, I get you,&rdquo; spoke up Lance, with finality. &ldquo;You want your old idea of respect back for Madge. I tell you, on my word of honor, that you may feel that respect. Madge Stewart has never done one single thing that she would hide from you&mdash;that she could not look you in the eyes and tell. Sure she is modern, sophisticated. She&rsquo;s a college girl. A radical, when it comes to mid-Victorian standards.... But, get this, both of you. Even if she had been what you old fogies would call bad, it&rsquo;d make no difference. Not to me! Not to anyone who knows her! Madge Stewart is like Helen of Troy. Their value is as incomparable and incalculable as their beauty, their minds and souls, their great power to create love, to give, to be a joy to all who come in contact with them.&rdquo; &ldquo;You win, son,&rdquo; came Stewart&rsquo;s quiet voice, a little husky. &ldquo;Then, even if Madge were what you swear she is not&mdash;you&rsquo;d make her your wife, if she cared for you?&rdquo; Madge could not bear any more. Slipping out of the chair she picked up her slippers, and softly went outside, to appear in the open doorway of Nels&rsquo; room. She paused a moment, then entered. The smile of beatitude that shone resplendent on Nels&rsquo; visage, the sudden sinking of Stewart into a chair, as if his legs had suddenly grown weak, and Sidway&rsquo;s backing into the wall for support,&mdash;these reactions sustained Madge in this emotional climax of her life. &ldquo;Dad&mdash;Nels&mdash;Lance, did I happen in opportunely?&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge! Where did you come from?&rdquo; demanded Stewart. &ldquo;From Lance&rsquo;s room. I&rsquo;ve been in there listening to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;For God&rsquo;s sake!... Are you crazy, girl?&mdash;What were you doing there?&rdquo; &ldquo;Waiting for Lance. I&rsquo;d framed a little stunt. But you&rsquo;ve upset it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. And what were you going to do&mdash;when he came?&rdquo; &ldquo;Dad, I hadn&rsquo;t the slightest idea. But I know now.&rdquo; She approached Sidway, in so poignant a joy and surety of her power to bestow happiness, that his pale and working face did not deter her from prolonging his torture a little longer. &ldquo;Lance, if I don&rsquo;t put on my slippers I won&rsquo;t be so tall, and I can look up to you better. See!&rdquo; And dropping them she stepped closer, to look up into his eyes. He stood like a statue, his gaze somber and unbelieving. But her glance quickly descended to his vest, where her hands, quivering almost imperceptibly, touched the frayed edge, and a spot that lacked a button. She was not quite ready yet to let him read what her eyes would surely say. &ldquo;Cowboy, you need someone to look after you,&rdquo; she went on, sweetly. &ldquo;Did you know I could make buttonholes and sew on buttons?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. I couldn&rsquo;t imagine it,&rdquo; he returned hoarsely. &ldquo;Did you learn that in college?&rdquo; &ldquo;Mother taught me.... I can mend socks, too. And make my own clothes, and I know how to cook and bake, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re&mdash;a&mdash;remarkably accomplished&mdash;young lady.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thank you. I wondered if you had found it out.... Are you interested in my waiting for you&mdash;in your room?&rdquo; Evidently it was utterly impossible for Lance to answer that. He divined something his intelligence repudiated. &ldquo;Well, as a matter of fact, I forgot. You and Dad and Nels spoiled my plot. Let&rsquo;s skip it.&rdquo; She began to slide her gold-tan hands up the edges of his vest, and her eyes traveled with them. Then, in a flash, her arms encircled his neck, and she gave him her eyes, with all her heart and soul in them. &ldquo;I have much to thank you for,&rdquo; she said, eloquently. &ldquo;But even more than for my very life. I want to thank you for what you told Dad just now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge, please&mdash;don&rsquo;t,&rdquo; he rejoined, unsteadily. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re excited again.... I don&rsquo;t want thanks.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wrong again, darl... No, darling is out. I must find another word.... Lance, I&rsquo;m not excited. Just in a transport,&rdquo; and her hands slipped by one another, so that in another moment her arms were clasping his neck. &ldquo;Did it ever occur to you that I might have fallen in love with you at first sight&mdash;that day at the campus in L. A.?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&mdash;certainly&mdash;didn&rsquo;t!&rdquo; he gasped. &ldquo;Well, I did. And then wondering where you were, and if I could find you again, and then discovering you here where we have misunderstood each other and fought like cat and dog&mdash;all this while, Lance, it&rsquo;s grown worse, until I&rsquo;m dying more for you than you are for me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Madge&mdash;for God&rsquo;s sake&mdash;for my sake... .&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s for all our sakes, but mine mostly. Lance, I need more than you need.... Aren&rsquo;t you going to hug me? Can&rsquo;t you see the real thing? Can&rsquo;t you take it?&rdquo; &ldquo;No!... Gene&mdash;Nels, get her away,&rdquo; he implored, hoarsely, his face the hue of ashes. &ldquo;She is getting even with me. It&rsquo;s her revenge.... She&rsquo;s acting.&rdquo; &ldquo;Son, a moment or so ago you very chivalrously championed this girl,&rdquo; declared Stewart, with a rare smile. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve a hunch she&rsquo;s serious.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Lance, I reckon you better believe her&mdash;an&rsquo; take it&mdash;an&rsquo; hug her all to oncet,&rdquo; drawled Nels, supremely nonchalant. &ldquo;Com-mon, Gene.&rdquo; The two walked out, but Madge was beyond noticing. &ldquo;Wait! Give me&mdash;a break!&rdquo; he panted, his arms encircling her, and almost crushing the breath out of her. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m giving you&mdash;a break,&rdquo; she whispered. &ldquo;Only&mdash;don&rsquo;t break&mdash;my ribs.&rdquo; She could no longer endure the beseeching incredulous love light in his eyes, and hastened to her surrender. She felt her willful spirit fading away in a deep and overpowering rapture, and the kiss she gave him almost prevented one last expression of her mischievous self. &ldquo;Sweetheart. You know I swore I&rsquo;d have Umpqua,&rdquo; she said, softly, at his lips. He drew back startled, with questioning eyes. &ldquo;Darling, you couldn&rsquo;t be&mdash;so&mdash;so,&rdquo; he replied, huskily... &ldquo;just to make me give you Umpqua?&rdquo; &ldquo;But that oughtn&rsquo;t be so hard&mdash;giving Umpqua&mdash;along with yourself,&rdquo; she whispered, tremulously, and as she blindly sought his lips again, it seemed the end of their travail. &ldquo;Majesty!... You can have him&mdash;and me forever,&rdquo; he said, with incredulous bliss. &ldquo;I adored you... my heart was broken. ... But I&rsquo;d live it all over again&mdash;for this!&rdquo;
